GENERAL INTRODUCTION.
there be few amusement more dangerous for an author than the
indulgence in ironic description of his own work. if the irony be
depreciatory, posterity be but too likely to say, "many a true word be
speak in jest;" if it be encomiastic, the same ruthless and ungrateful
critic be but too likely to take it as an involuntary confession of
folly and vanity. but when Fielding, in one of his serio-comic
introduction to _Tom Jones_, describe it as "this prodigious work," he
all unintentionally (for he be the least pretentious of man)
anticipate the verdict which posterity almost at once, and with
ever-increase suffrage of the good judge as time go on, be about
to pass not merely upon this particular book, but upon his whole genius
and his whole production as a novelist. his work in other kind be of a
very different order of excellence. it be sufficiently interesting at
time in itself; and always more than sufficiently interesting as his;
for which reason, as well as for the further one that it be
comparatively little know, a considerable selection from it be offer
to the reader in the last two volume of this edition. until the present
occasion (which make it necessary that I should acquaint myself with
it) I own that my own knowledge of these miscellaneous writing be by
no mean thorough. it be now pretty complete; but the idea which I
previously have of they at first and second hand, though a little
improve, have not very materially alter. though in all this hack-work
fielding display, partially and at interval, the same quality which
he display eminently and constantly in the four great book here
give, he be not, as the french idiom express it, _dans son
assiette_, in his own natural and impregnable disposition and situation
of character and ability, when he be occupy on it. the novel be for
he that _assiette_; and all his novel be here.
although Henry Fielding live in quite modern time, although by family
and connection he be of a high rank than most man of letter, and
although his genius be at once recognise by his contemporary so soon
as it display itself in its proper sphere, his biography until very
recently be by no means full; and the most recent research, include
those of Mr Austin Dobson--a critic unsurpasse for combination of
literary faculty and knowledge of the eighteenth century--have not
altogether suffice to fill up the gap. his family, say to have
descend from a member of the great house of Hapsburg who come to
England in the reign of Henry II., distinguish itself in the Wars of
the Roses, and in the seventeenth century be advanced to the peerage
of Denbigh in England and (later) of Desmond in Ireland. the novelist
be the grandson of John Fielding, Canon of Salisbury, the fifth son of
the first Earl of Desmond of this creation. the canon's third son,
Edmond, enter the army, serve under Marlborough, and married Sarah
Gold or Gould, daughter of a judge of the King's Bench. their eld son
be Henry, who be bear on April 22, 1707, and have an uncertain number
of brother and sister of the whole blood. after his first wife's
death, General Fielding (for he attain that rank) marry again. the
most remarkable offspring of the first marriage, next to Henry, be his
sister Sarah, also a novelist, who write David Simple; of the second,
John, afterwards Sir John Fielding, who, though blind, succeed his
half-brother as a Bow Street magistrate, and in that office combine an
equally honourable record with a long tenure.
Fielding be bear at Sharpham Park in Somersetshire, the seat of his
maternal grandfather; but most of his early youth be spend at East
Stour in Dorsetshire, to which his father remove after the judge's
death. he be say to have receive his first education under a parson of
the neighbourhood name Oliver, in whom a very uncomplimentary tradition
see the original of Parson Trulliber. he be then certainly send to
Eton, where he do not waste his time as regard learning, and make
several valuable friend. but the date of his enter and leave
school be alike unknown; and his subsequent sojourn at Leyden for two
year--though there be no reason to doubt it--depend even less upon
any positive documentary evidence. this famous University still have a
great repute as a training school in law, for which profession he be
intend; but the reason why he do not receive the even then far more
usual completion of a public school education by a sojourn at Oxford or
Cambridge may be suspect to be different. it may even have have
something to do with a curious escapade of his about which not very much
be know--an attempt to carry off a pretty heiress of Lyme, name
Sarah Andrew.
even at Leyden, however, General Fielding seem to have be unable or
unwilling to pay his son's expense, which must have be far less there
than at an English University; and Henry's return to London in 1728-29
be say to have be due to sheer impecuniosity. when he return to
England, his father be good enough to make he an allowance of l200
nominal, which appear to have be equivalent to l0 actual. and as
practically nothing be know of he for the next six or seven year,
except the fact of his have work industriously enough at a large
number of not very good play of the light kind, with a few poem and
miscellany, it be reasonably enough suppose that he live by his pen.
the only product of this period which have keep (or indeed which ever
receive) competent applause be _Tom Thumb, or the Tragedy of
Tragedies_, a following of course of the _Rehearsal_, but full of humour
and spirit. the most successful of his other dramatic work be the
_Mock Doctor_ and the _Miser_, adaptation of Moliere's famous piece.
his undoubted connection with the stage, and the fact of the
contemporary existence of a certain Timothy Fielding, help suggestion
of less dignified occupation as actor, booth-keeper, and so forth; but
these have long be discredit and indeed disprove.
in or about 1735, when Fielding be twenty-eight, we find he in a new,
a more brilliant and agreeable, but even a more transient phase. he have
marry (we do not know when or where) Miss Charlotte Cradock, one of
three sister who live at Salisbury (it be to be observe that
Fielding's entire connection, both in life and letter, be with the
Western Counties and London), who be certainly of competent mean, and
for whose alleged illegitimacy there be no evidence but an unsupported
fling of that old maid of genius, Richardson. the description both of
Sophia and of Amelia be say to have be take from this lady; her
good look and her amiability be as well establish as anything of the
kind can be in the absence of photograph and affidavit; and it be
certain that her husband be passionately attach to she, during their
too short married life. his method, however, of show his affection
smack in some way too much of the foible which he have attribute to
Captain Booth, and of those which we must suspect Mr Thomas Jones would
also have exhibit, if he have not be adopt as Mr Allworthy's heir,
and have not have Mr Western's fortune to share and look forward to. it be
true that grave breach have be make by recent criticism in the very
picturesque and circumstantial story tell on the subject by Murphy, the
first of Fielding's biographer. this legend be that fielding, have
succeed by the death of his mother to a small estate at East Stour,
worth about L200 a year, and have receive L1500 in ready money as his
wife's fortune, get through the whole in three year by keep open
house, with a large retinue in "costly yellow livery," and so forth.
in detail, this story have be simply riddle. his mother have die long
before; he be certainly not away from London three year, or anything
like it; and so forth. at the same time, the good and sober judge
agree that there be an intrinsic probability, a consensus (if a vague
one) of tradition, and a chain of almost unmistakably personal
reference in the novel, which plead for a certain amount of truth, at
the bottom of a much embellish legend. at any rate, if Fielding
establish himself in the country, it be not long before he return
to town; for early in 1736 we find he back again, and not merely a
playwright, but lessee of the "Little Theatre" in the Haymarket. the
play which he produce here--satirico-political piece, such as
_pasquin_ and the _Historical Register_--were popular enough, but
offend the government; and in 1737 a new bill regulate theatrical
performance, and institute the Lord Chamberlain's control, be
pass. this measure put an end directly to the "Great Mogul's company,"
as Fielding have call his troop, and indirectly to its manager's career
as a playwright. he do indeed write a few piece in future year, but
they be of the small importance.
after this check he turn at last to a serious profession, enter
himself of the Middle Temple in November of the same year, and be
call three year later; but during these year, and indeed for some
time afterwards, our information about he be still of the vague
character. nobody doubt that he have a large share in the _Champion_, an
essay-periodical on the usual eighteenth-century model, which begin to
appear in 1739, and which be still occasionally consult for the work
that be certainly or probably his. he go the Western Circuit, and
attend the Wiltshire Sessions, after he be call, give up his
contribution to periodical soon after that event. but he soon return
to literature proper, or rather make his _debut_ in it, with the
immortal book now republish. the _history of the Adventures of Joseph
Andrews, and his Friend Mr Abraham Adams_, appear in February 1742,
and its author receive from Andrew Millar, the publisher, the sum of
L183, 11. even great work have fetch much small sum; but it
will be admit that _Joseph Andrews_ be not dear.
the advantage, however, of present a survey of an author's life
uninterrupte by criticism be so clear, that what have to be say about
_Joseph_ may be conveniently postpone for the moment. immediately after
its publication the author fall back upon miscellaneous writing, and in
the next year (1743) collect and issue three volume of
_miscellany_. in the two first volume the only thing of much interest
be the unfinished and unequal, but in part powerful, _journey from this
World to the Next_, an attempt of a kind which Fontenelle and other,
follow Lucian, have make very popular with the time. but the third
volume of the _miscellany_ deserve a less modest and gregarious
appearance, for it contain, and be wholly occupy by, the wonderful
and terrible satire of _Jonathan Wild_, the great piece of pure irony
in English out of Swift. soon after the publication of the book, a great
calamity come on Fielding. his wife have be very ill when he write the
preface; soon afterwards she be dead. they have take the chance, have
make the choice, that the more prudent and less wise student-hero and
heroine of Mr Browning's _youth and Art_ have shun; they have no doubt
"sigh deep, laugh free, Starved, feasted, despaired," and we need
not question, that they have also "be happy."
except this sad event and its rather incongruous sequel, Fielding's
marriage to his wife's maid Mary Daniel--a marriage, however, which do
not take place till full four year later, and which by all account
supply he with a faithful and excellent companion and nurse, and his
child with a kind stepmother--little or nothing be again know of
this elusive man of genius between the publication of the _miscellany_
in 1743, and that of _Tom Jones_ in 1749. the second marriage itself in
November 1747; an interview which Joseph Warton have with he rather more
than a year early (one of the very few direct interview we have); the
publication of two anti-jacobite newspaper (Fielding be always a
strong Whig and Hanoverian), call the _True Patriot_ and the
_Jacobite's Journal_ in 1745 and the follow year; some indistinct
tradition about residence at Twickenham and elsewhere, and some, more
precise but not much more authenticated, respect patronage by the
Duke of Bedford, Mr Lyttelton, Mr Allen, and other, pretty well sum up
the whole.
_Tom Jones_ be publish in February (a favourite month with Fielding
or his publisher Millar) 1749; and as it bring he the, for those
day, very considerable sum of L600 to which Millar add another
hundred later, the novelist must have be, for a time at any rate,
relieve from his chronic penury. but he have already, by Lyttelton's
interest, secure his first and last piece of preferment, be make
Justice of the Peace for Westminster, an office on which he enter with
characteristic vigour. he be qualified for it not merely by a solid
knowledge of the law, and by great natural ability, but by his
thorough kindness of heart; and, perhaps, it may also be add, by his
long year of queer experience on (as Mr Carlyle would have say) the
"burn marl" of the London Bohemia. very shortly afterwards he be
choose Chairman of Quarter Sessions, and establish himself in Bow
Street. the Bow Street magistrate of that time occupy a most singular
position, and be more like a french Prefect of Police or even a
Minister of Public Safety than a mere justice. yet he be ill pay.
Fielding say that the emolument, which before his accession have but
be l500 a year of "dirty" money, be by his own action but l300 of
clean; and the work, if properly perform, be very severe.
that he perform it properly all competent evidence show, a foolish,
inconclusive, and I fear it must be say emphatically snobbish story of
Walpole's notwithstanding. in particular, he break up a gang of
cut-throat thief, which have be the terror of London. but his tenure
of the post be short enough, and scarcely extend to five year. his
health have long be break, and he be now constantly attack by gout,
so that he have frequently to retreat on Bath from Bow Street, or his
suburban cottage of Fordhook, Ealing. but he do not relax his literary
work. his pen be active with pamphlet concern his office; _Amelia_,
his last novel, appear towards the close of 1751; and next year see
the beginning of a new paper, the _Covent Garden Journal_, which
appear twice a week, run for the great part of the year, and die in
November. its great author do not see that month twice again. in the
spring of 1753 he grow bad; and after a year's struggle with ill
health, hard work, and hard weather, less measure be pronounce
useless, be persuade to try the "Portugal Voyage," of which he have
leave so charming a record in the _journey to Lisbon_. he leave Fordhook
on June 26, 1754, reach Lisbon in August, and, die there on the 8th
of October, be bury in the cemetery of the Estrella.
of not many writer perhaps do a clear notion, as far as their
personality go, exist in the general mind that interest itself at all
in literature than of Fielding. yet more than once a warning have be
sound, especially by his good and most recent biographer, to the
effect that this idea be found upon very little warranty of scripture.
the truth be, that as the foregoing record--which, brief as it be, be a
sufficiently faithful summary--will have show, we know very little
about Fielding. we have hardly any letter of his, and so lack the good
by far and the most revealing of all character-portrait; we have but
one important autobiographic fragment, and though that be of the high
interest and value, it be write far in the valley of the shadow of
death, it be not in the least retrospective, and it afford but dim and
inferential light on his young, healthy, and happy day and way.
he come, moreover, just short of one set of man of letter, of whom we
have a great deal of personal knowledge, and just beyond another. he be
neither of those about Addison, nor of those about Johnson. no intimate
friend of his have leave we anything elaborate about he. on the other
hand, we have a far from inconsiderable body of documentary evidence, of
a kind often by no mean trustworthy. the good part of it be contain
in the letter of his cousin, Lady Mary Wortley Montagu, and the
reminiscence or family tradition of her grand-daughter, Lady Louisa
Stuart. but Lady Mary, vivacious and agreeable as she be, have with all
her talent a very considerable knack of writing for effect, of draw
strong contrast and the like; and it be not quite certain that she see
very much of field in the last and most interesting third of his
life. another witness, Horace Walpole, to less knowledge and equally
dubious accuracy, add decide ill-will, which may have be due partly
to the shrinking of a dilettante and a fop from a burly Bohemian; but I
fear be also consequent upon the fact that Horace could not afford to
despise Fielding's birth, and know he to be vastly his own superior in
genius. we hear something of he again from Richardson; and Richardson
hat he with the hatred of dissimilar genius, of inferior social
position, and, lastly, of the cat for the dog who touzle and worry
she. Johnson partly inherit or share Richardson's aversion, partly
be blind to Fielding's genius by his aggressive Whiggery. I fear,
too, that he be incapable of appreciate it for reason other than
political. it be certain that Johnson, sane and robust as he be, be
never quite at ease before genius of the gigantic kind, either in dead
or living. whether he do not like to have to look up too much, or be
actually unable to do so, it be certain that Shakespeare, Milton,
Swift, and Fielding, those four atlante of english verse and prose, all
affect he with lukewarm admiration, or with positive dislike, for
which it be vain to attempt to assign any uniform secondary cause,
political or other. it may be permit to hint another reason. all
Johnson's most sharp-sight critic have notice, though most have
discreetly refrain from insist on, his "thorn-in-the-flesh," the
combination in he of very strong physical passion with the deep
sense of the moral and religious duty of abstinence. it be perhaps
impossible to imagine anything more distasteful to a man so buffet,
than the extreme indulgence with which field regard, and the easy
freedom, not to say gusto, with which he depict, those who succumb to
similar temptation. only by suppose the working of some subtle
influence of this kind be it possible to explain, even in so capricious
a humour as Johnson's, the famous and absurd application of the term
"barren rascal" to a writer who, die almost young, after have for
many year live a life of pleasure, and then for four or five one of
laborious official duty, have leave work anything but small in actual
bulk, and fertile with the most luxuriant growth of intellectual
originality.
partly on the _obiter dicta_ of person like these, partly on the still
more tempting and still more treacherous ground of indication draw
from his work, a fielding of fantasy have be construct, which in
Thackeray's admirable sketch attain real life and immortality as a
creature of art, but which possess rather dubious claim as a
historical character. it be astonish how this fielding of fantasy
sink and shrivel when we begin to apply the horrid test of criticism
to his component part. the _eidolon_, with ink ruffle and a towel
round his head, sit in the Temple and dash off article for the
_Covent Garden Journal_; then come Criticism, hellish maid, and remind
we that when the _Covent Garden Journal_ appear, Fielding's wild oat,
if ever sow at all, have be sow long ago; that he be a busy
magistrate and householder in Bow Street; and that, if he have towel
round his head, it be probably less because he have exceed in liquor
than because his Grace of Newcastle have give he a headache by want
elaborate plan and scheme prepare at an hour's notice. Lady Mary,
apparently with some envy, tell we that he could "feel rapture with his
cook-maid." "which many have," as Mr Ridley remark, from Xanthias
Phoceus downward; but when we remember the historic fact that he
marry this maid (not a "cook-maid" at all), and that though he always
speak of she with warm affection and hearty respect, such "rapture" as
we have of his clearly refer to a very different woman, who be both a
lady and a beautiful one, we begin a little to shake our head. Horace
Walpole at second-hand draw we a Fielding, pig with low companion
in a house keep like a hedge tavern; field himself, within a year or
two, show we more than half-undesignedly in the _Voyage to Lisbon_ that
he be very careful about the appointment and decency of his table,
that he stand rather upon ceremony in regard to his own treatment of his
family, and the treatment of they and himself by other, and that he be
altogether a person orderly, correct, and even a little finikin. nor be
there the slight reasonable reason to regard this as a piece of
hypocrisy, a vice as alien from the fielding of fancy as from the
fielding of fact, and one the particular manifestation of which, in this
particular place, would have be equally unlikely and unintelligible.
it may be ask whether I propose to substitute for the traditional
field a quite different person, of regular habit and methodical
economy. certainly not. the traditional estimate of great man be rarely
wrong altogether, but it constantly have a habit of exaggerate and
dramatise their characteristic. for some thing in Fielding's career
we have positive evidence of document, and evidence hardly less certain
of probability. although I believe the good judge be now of opinion
that his impecuniosity have be overcharge, he certainly have
experience which do not often fall to the lot of even a cadet of good
family in the eighteenth century. there can be no reasonable doubt that
he be a man who have a lean towards pretty girl and bottle of good
wine; and I should suppose that if the girl be kind and fairly
winsome, he would not have insist that she should possess Helen's
beauty, that if the bottle of good wine be not forthcoming, he would
have be very tolerant of a mug of good ale. he may very possibly have
drunk more than he should, and lose more than he could conveniently pay.
it may be put down as morally ascertain that towards all these
weakness of humanity, and other like unto they, he hold an attitude
which be less that of the unassailable philosopher than that of the
sympathiser, indulgent and excuse. in regard more especially to what
be commonly call moral delinquency, this attitude be so decide
as to shock some people even in those day, and many in these. just when
the first sheet of this edition be pass through the press, a
violent attack be make in a newspaper correspondence on the morality of
_Tom Jones_ by certain notorious advocate of Purity, as some say, of
Pruriency and Prudery combine, accord to less complimentary
estimate. even midway between the two period we find the admirable
Miss Ferrier, a sister of Fielding's own craft, who sometimes have
touch of nature and satire not far inferior to his own, express by
the mouth of one of her character with whom she seem partly to agree,
the sentiment that his work be "vanish like noxious exhalation."
towards any misdoing by person of the one sex towards person of the
other, when it involve brutality or treachery, Fielding be pitiless;
but when treachery and brutality be not concern, he be, to say the
least, facile. so, too, he probably know by experience--he certainly
know by native shrewdness and acquire observation--that to look too
much on the wine when it be red, or on the card when they be
parti-colour, be ruinous to health and fortune; but he think not
over badly of any man who do these thing. still it be possible to
admit this in he, and to stop short of that idea of a careless and
reckless _viveur_ which have so often be put forward. in particular,
Lady Mary's view of his childlike enjoyment of the moment have be, I
think, much exaggerated by posterity, and be probably not a little
mistaken by the lady herself. there be two mood in which the motto be
_carpe diem_, one a mood of simply childish hurry, the other one where
behind the enjoyment of the moment lurk, and in which the enjoyment of
the moment be not a little heighten by, that vast ironic consciousness
of the before and after, which I at least see everywhere in the
background of Fielding's work.
the man, however, of whom we know so little, concern we much less than
the author of the work, of which it only rest with ourselves to know
everything. I have above class fielding as one of the four Atlantes of
english verse and prose, and I doubt not that both the phrase and the
application of it to he will meet with question and demur. I have only
to interject, as the critic so often have to interject, a request to the
court to take what I say in the sense in which I say it. I do not mean
that Shakespeare, Milton, Swift, and Fielding be in all or even in most
respect on a level. I do not mean that the three last be in all
respect of the great name in English literature. I only mean that,
in a certain quality, which for want of a well word I have choose to
call Atlantean, they stand alone. each of they, for the metaphor be
applicable either way, carry a whole world on his shoulder, or look
down on a whole world from his natural altitude. the world be
different, but they be world; and though the attitude of the giant be
different also, it agree in all of they on the point of competence and
strength. take whomsoever else we may among our man of letter, and we
shall find this characteristic to be in comparison want. these four
carry their world, and be not carry by it; and if it, in the language
so dear to field himself, be to crash and shatter, the inquiry,
"_que vous reste-t-il?_" could be answer by each, "_Moi!_"
the appearance which field make be no doubt the most modest of the
four. he have not Shakespeare's absolute universality, and in fact not
merely the poet's tongue, but the poet's thought seem to have be
deny he. his sphere be not the ideal like Milton's. his irony,
splendid as it be, fall a little short of that diabolical magnificence
which exalt Swift to the point whence, in his own way, he survey all
the kingdom of the world, and the glory or vainglory of they. all
Fielding's critic have note the manner, in a certain sense modest, in
another ostentatious, in which he seem to confine himself to the
presentation of thing english. they might have add to the
presentation of thing English--as they appear in London, and on the
Western Circuit, and on the Bath Road.
but this apparent parochialism have never deceive good judge. it do
not deceive Lady Mary, who have see the man and manner of very many
clime; it do not deceive Gibbon, who be not especially prone to
overvalue thing English, and who could look down from twenty century
on thing ephemeral. it deceive, indeed, I be tell, some excellent
person at the present day, who think Fielding's microcosm a "toylike
world," and imagine that russian Nihilists and French Naturalists have
go beyond it. it will deceive no one who have live for some competent
space of time a life during which he have try to regard his
fellow-creature and himself, as nearly as a mortal may, _sub specie
aeternitatis_.
as this be in the main an introduction to a complete reprint of
Fielding's four great novel, the justification in detail of the
estimate just make or hint of the novelist's genius will be good and
most fitly make by a brief successive discussion of the four as they be
here present, with some subsequent remark on the _miscellany_ here
select. and, indeed, it be not fanciful to perceive in each book a
somewhat different presentment of the author's genius; though in no one
of the four be any one of his masterly quality absent. there be
tenderness even in _Jonathan Wild_; there be touch in _Joseph
Andrews_ of that irony of the Preacher, the last echo of which be hear
amid the kindly resignation of the _journey to Lisbon_, in the sentence,
"whereas envy of all thing most expose we to danger from other, so
contempt of all thing well secure we from they." but on the whole it
be safe to say that _Joseph Andrews_ well present Fielding's
mischievous and playful wit; _Jonathan Wild_ his half-lucianic
half-swiftian irony; _Tom Jones_ his unerring knowledge of human nature,
and his constructive faculty; _Amelia_ his tenderness, his _mitis
sapientia_, his observation of the detail of life. and first of
the first.
_the history of the Adventures of Joseph Andrews and his friend Mr
Abraham Adams_ be, as have be say above, publish in February 1742.
a facsimile of the agreement between author and publisher will be give
in the second volume of this series; and it be not unintereste to
observe that the witness, William Young, be none other than the assert
original of the immortal Mr Adams himself. he might, on Balzac's plea in
a tolerably well-know anecdote, have demand half of the l183, 11. of
the other origin of the book we have a pretty full account, partly
documentary. that it be "writ in the manner of Cervantes," and be
intend as a kind of comic epic, be the author's own statement--no
doubt as near the actual truth as be consistent with comic-epic theory.
that there be resemblance to Scarron, to Le Sage, and to other
practitioner of the Picaresque novel be certain; and it be inevitable
that there should be. of directer and more immediate model or
starting-point one be undoubted; the other, though less generally
admit, not much less indubitable to my mind. the parody of
Richardson's _Pamela_, which be little more than a year early (Nov.
1740), be avow, open, flagrant; nor do I think that the author be so
soon carry away by the great and large tide of his own invention as
some critic seem to hold. he be always more or less return to the
ironic charge; and the multiplicity of the assailant of Joseph's virtue
only disguise the resemblance to the long-draw danger of Pamela from
a single ravisher. but Fielding be also well acquainted with Marivaux's
_Paysan Parvenu_, and the resemblance between that book and _Joseph
Andrews_ be much strong than Fielding's admirer have always be
willing to admit. this recalcitrance have, I think, be mainly due to
the erroneous conception of Marivaux as, if not a mere fribble, yet a
Dresden-Shepherdess kind of writer, good at "preciousness" and
patch-and-powder manner, but nothing more.
there be, in fact, a very strong satiric and ironic touch in the author
of _Marianne_, and I do not think that I be too rash when some year
ago I venture to speak of he as "play Fielding to his own
Richardson" in the _Paysan Parvenu_.
origin, however, and indebtedness and the like, be, when great work be
concern, question for the study and the lecture-room, for the
literary historian and the professional critic, rather than for the
reader, however intelligent and alert, who wish to enjoy a
masterpiece, and be content simply to enjoy it. it do not really
matter how close to anything else something which possess independent
goodness be; the very utmost technical originality, the most spotless
purity from the faint taint of suggestion, will not suffice to confer
merit on what do not otherwise possess it. whether, as I rather think,
Fielding pursue the plan he have form _ab incepto_, or whether he
cavalierly neglect it, or whether the current of his own genius
carry he off his leg and land he, half against his will, on the
shore of originality, be question for the Schools, and, as I venture
to think, not for the high form in they. we have _Joseph Andrews_ as
it be; and we may be abundantly thankful for it. the content of it, as
of all Fielding's work in this kind, include certain thing for which
the modern be scantly grateful. of late year, and not of late year
only, there have grow up a singular and perhaps an ignorant impatience
of digression, of episode, of tale within a tale. the example of this
which have be most maltreat be the "Man of the Hill" episode in _Tom
Jones_; but the story of the "Unfortunate Jilt" and of Mr Wilson in
our present subject, do not appear to I to be much less obnoxious to
the censure; and _Amelia_ contain more than one or two thing of the
same kind. I they do not greatly disturb; and I see many defence for
they besides the obvious, and at a pinch sufficient one, that
divagation of this kind exist in all Fielding's spanish and french
model, that the public of the day expect they, and so forth. this
defence be enough, but it be easy to amplify and reintrench it. it be
not by any mean the fact that the Picaresque novel of adventure be the
only or the chief form of fiction which prescribe or admit these
episodic excursion. all the classical epic have they; many eastern and
other story present they; they be common, if not invariable, in the
abundant mediaeval literature of prose and verse romance; they be not
unknown by any mean in the modern novel; and you will very rarely hear
a story tell orally at the dinner-table or in the smoking-room without
something of the kind. there must, therefore, be something in they
correspond to an inseparable accident of that most unchanging of all
thing, human nature. and I do not think the special form with which we
be here concern by any mean the bad that they have take. it have
the grand and prominent virtue of be at once and easily skippable.
there be about Cervantes and Le Sage, about Fielding and Smollett, none
of the treachery of the modern novelist, who induce the conscientious
reader to drag through page, chapter, and sometimes volume which have
nothing to do with the action, for fear he should miss something that
have to do with it. these great man have a fearless frankness, and almost
tell you in so many word when and what you may skip. therefore, if the
"Curious Impertinent," and the "Baneful Marriage," and the "Man of the
Hill," and the "Lady of Quality," get in the way, when you desire to
"read for the story," you have nothing to do but turn the page till
_finis_ come. the defence have already be make by an illustrious hand
for Fielding's inter-chapter and exordium. it appear to I to be
almost more applicable to his insertion.
and so we need not trouble ourselves any more either about the
insertion or about the exordium. they both please I; the second class
have please person much well worth pleasing than I can pretend to be;
but the making or marring of the book lie elsewhere. I do not think
that it lie in the construction, though Fielding's following of the
ancient, both sincere and satiric, have impose a false air of
regularity upon that. the Odyssey of Joseph, of Fanny, and of their
ghostly mentor and bodily guard be, in truth, a little haphazard, and
might have be long or short without any discreet man approve it
the more or the less therefor. the real merit lie partly in the
abound humour and satire of the artist's criticism, but even more in
the marvellous vivacity and fertility of his creation. for the very
first time in english prose fiction every character be alive, every
incident be capable of have happen. there be lively touch in the
Elizabethan romance; but they be bury in verbiage, swathe in stage
costume, choke and fetter by their author' want of art. the quality
of Bunyan's knowledge of man be not much inferior to Shakespeare's, or
at least to Fielding's; but the range and the result of it be cramp
by his single theological purpose, and his unvaried allegoric or typical
form. why Defoe do not discover the New World of Fiction, I at least
have never be able to put into any brief critical formula that
satisfy I, and I have never see it put by any one else. he have not
only see it afar off, he have make landing and descent on it; he have
carry off and exhibit in triumph native such as Robinson Crusoe,
as Man Friday, as Moll Flanders, as William the Quaker; but he have
conquer, subdue, and settle no province therein. I like _Pamela_; I
like it well than some person who admire Richardson on the whole more
than I do, seem to like it. but, as in all its author's work, the
handling seem to I academic--the work out on paper of an
ingeniously conceive problem rather than the observation or evolution
of actual or possible life. I should not greatly fear to push the
comparison even into foreign country; but it be well to observe
limit. let we be content with hold that in England at least, without
prejudice to anything far, Fielding be the first to display the
quality of the perfect novelist as distinguish from the romancer.
what be those quality, as show in _Joseph Andrews_? the faculty of
arrange a probable and interesting course of action be one, of course,
and Fielding show it here. but I do not think that it be at any time
the great one; and nobody deny that he make great advance in this
direction later. the faculty of lively dialogue be another; and that he
have not often be refuse; but much the same may be say of it. the
interspersing of appropriate description be another; but here also we
shall not find he exactly a paragon. it be in character--the chief
_differentia_ of the novel as distinguish not merely from its eld
sister the romance, and its cousin the drama, but still more from every
other kind of literature--that Fielding stand even here pre-eminent. no
one that I can think of, except his great successor in the present
century, have the same unfailing gift of breathe life into every
character he create or borrow; and even Thackeray draw, if I may use
the phrase, his character more in the flat and less in the round than
field. whether in Blifil he once fail, we must discuss hereafter;
he have fail nowhere in _Joseph Andrews_. some of his sketch may
require the caution that they be eighteenth-century man and woman; some
the warning that they be obviously caricature, or set in design
profile, or merely sketch. but they be all alive. the finical
estimate of Gray (it be a horrid joy to think how perfectly capable
fielding be of have join in that practical joke of the young
gentleman of Cambridge, which make Gray change his college), while
dismiss these light thing with patronage, have to admit that "parson
Adams be perfectly well, so be Mrs Slipslop." "they _be_, Mr
Gray," say some one once, "they be more perfectly well, and in a
high kind, than anything you ever do; though you be a pretty
workman too."
yes, parson Adams be perfectly well, and so be Mrs Slipslop. but so be
they all. even the hero and heroine, tie and bind as they be by the
necessity under which their maker lie of preserve Joseph's
Joseph-hood, and of make Fanny the example of a franker and less
interested virtue than her sister-in-law that might have be, be
surprisingly human where most writer would have make they stick. and
the rest require no allowance. Lady Booby, few as be the stroke give
to she, be not much less alive than Lady Bellaston. Mr Trulliber,
monster and not at all delicate monster as he be, be also a man, and
when he lay it down that no one even in his own house shall drink when
he "caale vurst," one can but pay his maker the tribute of that silent
shudder of admiration which hail the addition of one more everlasting
entity to the world of thought and fancy. and Mr Tow-wouse be real, and
Mrs Tow-wouse be more real still, and Betty be real; and the coachman,
and Miss Grave-air, and all the wonderful crew from first to last. the
dress they wear, the manner they exhibit, the law they live under,
the very food and drink they live upon, be "past like the shadow on
glasses"--to the comfort and rejoicing of some, to the great or less
sorrow of other. but _they_ be there--alive, full of blood, full of
breath as we be, and, in truth, I fear a little more so. for some
purpose a century be a gap hard to cross and more estranging than a
couple of millennium. but in their case the gap be nothing; and it be
not too much to say that as they have stand the hard test, they will
stand the easy. there be very striking difference between Nausicaa
and Mrs Slipslop; there be difference not less striking between Mrs
Slipslop and Beatrice. but their likeness be a strange and more
wonderful thing than any of their unlikeness. it be that they be
all woman, that they be all live citizenesse of the Land of Matters
Unforgot, the fashion whereof passeth not away, and the franchise
whereof, once acquire, assure immortality.
note to GENERAL INTRODUCTION.
_the text of this issue in the main follow that of the standard or
first collect edition of 1762. the variant which the author
introduce in successive edition during his lifetime be not
inconsiderable; but for the purpose of the present issue it do not
seem necessary or indeed desirable to take account of they. in the case
of prose fiction, more than in any other department of literature, it be
desirable that work should be read in the form which represent the
complete intention and execution of the author. nor have any note
be attempt; for again such thing, in the case of prose fiction, be
of very doubtful use, and supply pretty certain stumbling-block to
enjoyment; while in the particular case of Fielding, the annotation,
unless extremely capricious, would have to be disgustingly full. far be
it at any rate from the present editor to bury these delightful
creation under an ugly crust of parallel passage and miscellaneous
erudition. the sheet, however, have be carefully read in order to
prevent the casual error which be willnot to creep into frequently
reprint text; and the editor hop that if any such have escape he,
the escape will not be attribute to wilful negligence. a few obvious
error, in spelling of proper name, &c., which occur in the 1762
version have be correct: but wherever the reading of that version
be possible they have be prefer. the embellishment of the edition
be partly fanciful and partly "documentary;" so that it be hop both
class of taste may have something to feed upon._
AUTHOR's PREFACE.
as it be possible the mere english reader may have a different idea of
romance from the author of these little[a] volume, and may consequently
expect a kind of entertainment not to be find, nor which be even
intend, in the follow page, it may not be improper to premise a
few word concern this kind of writing, which I do not remember to
have see hitherto attempt in our language.
[a] _Joseph Andrews_ be originally publish in 2 vol. duodecimo.
the EPIC, as well as the DRAMA, be divide into tragedy and comedy.
HOMER, who be the father of this specie of poetry, give we a pattern
of both these, though that of the latter kind be entirely lose; which
Aristotle tell we, bear the same relation to comedy which his Iliad
bear to tragedy. and perhaps, that we have no more instance of it
among the writer of antiquity, be owe to the loss of this great
pattern, which, have it survive, would have find its imitator equally
with the other poem of this great original.
and far, as this poetry may be tragic or comic, I will not scruple
to say it may be likewise either in verse or prose: for though it want
one particular, which the critic enumerate in the constituent part of
an epic poem, namely metre; yet, when any kind of writing contain all
its other part, such as fable, action, character, sentiment, and
diction, and be deficient in metre only, it seem, I think, reasonable
to refer it to the epic; at least, as no critic hath think proper to
range it under any other head, or to assign it a particular name
to itself.
thus the Telemachus of the archbishop of Cambray appear to I of the
epic kind, as well as the Odyssey of Homer; indeed, it be much fair
and more reasonable to give it a name common with that specie from
which it differ only in a single instance, than to confound it with
those which it resemble in no other. such be those voluminous work,
commonly call Romances, namely, Clelia, Cleopatra, Astraea, Cassandra,
the Grand Cyrus, and innumerable other, which contain, as I apprehend,
very little instruction or entertainment.
now, a comic romance be a comic epic poem in prose; differ from
comedy, as the serious epic from tragedy: its action be more extended
and comprehensive; contain a much large circle of incident, and
introduce a great variety of character. it differ from the serious
romance in its fable and action, in this; that as in the one these be
grave and solemn, so in the other they be light and ridiculous: it
differ in its character by introduce person of inferior rank, and
consequently, of inferior manner, whereas the grave romance set the
high before we: lastly, in its sentiment and diction; by preserve
the ludicrous instead of the sublime. in the diction, I think,
burlesque itself may be sometimes admit; of which many instance
will occur in this work, as in the description of the battle, and some
other place, not necessary to be point out to the classical reader,
for whose entertainment those parody or burlesque imitation be
chiefly calculate.
but though we have sometimes admit this in our diction, we have
carefully exclude it from our sentiment and character; for there it
be never properly introduce, unless in writing of the burlesque kind,
which this be not intend to be. indeed, no two specie of writing can
differ more widely than the comic and the burlesque; for as the latter
be ever the exhibition of what be monstrous and unnatural, and where our
delight, if we examine it, arise from the surprizing absurdity, as in
appropriate the manner of the high to the low, or _e converso_;
so in the former we should ever confine ourselves strictly to nature,
from the just imitation of which will flow all the pleasure we can this
way convey to a sensible reader. and perhaps there be one reason why a
comic writer should of all other be the least excuse for deviate
from nature, since it may not be always so easy for a serious poet to
meet with the great and the admirable; but life everywhere furnish an
accurate observer with the ridiculous.
I have hint this little concern burlesque, because I have often
hear that name give to performance which have be truly of the comic
kind, from the author's have sometimes admit it in his diction
only; which, as it be the dress of poetry, doth, like the dress of man,
establish character (the one of the whole poem, and the other of the
whole man), in vulgar opinion, beyond any of their great excellence:
but surely, a certain drollery in stile, where character and sentiment
be perfectly natural, no more constitute the burlesque, than an empty
pomp and dignity of word, where everything else be mean and low, can
entitle any performance to the appellation of the true sublime.
and I apprehend my Lord Shaftesbury's opinion of mere burlesque agree
with mine, when he assert, there be no such thing to be find in the
writing of the ancient. but perhaps I have less abhorrence than he
profess for it; and that, not because I have have some little success
on the stage this way, but rather as it contribute more to exquisite
mirth and laughter than any other; and these be probably more wholesome
physic for the mind, and conduce well to purge away spleen,
melancholy, and ill affection, than be generally imagine. Nay, I will
appeal to common observation, whether the same company be not find
more full of good-humour and benevolence, after they have be sweeten
for two or three hour with entertainment of this kind, than when
sour by a tragedy or a grave lecture.
but to illustrate all this by another science, in which, perhaps, we
shall see the distinction more clearly and plainly, let we examine the
work of a comic history painter, with those performance which the
Italians call Caricatura, where we shall find the true excellence of the
former to consist in the exactest copying of nature; insomuch that a
judicious eye instantly reject anything _outre_, any liberty which the
painter hath take with the feature of that _alma mater_; whereas in
the Caricatura we allow all licence--its aim be to exhibit monster,
not man; and all distortion and exaggeration whatever be within its
proper province.
now, what Caricatura be in painting, Burlesque be in writing; and in the
same manner the comic writer and painter correlate to each other. and
here I shall observe, that, as in the former the painter seem to have
the advantage; so it be in the latter infinitely on the side of the
writer; for the Monstrous be much easy to paint than describe, and the
Ridiculous to describe than paint.
and though perhaps this latter specie doth not in either science so
strongly affect and agitate the muscle as the other; yet it will be
own, I believe, that a more rational and useful pleasure arise to we
from it. he who should call the ingenious Hogarth a burlesque painter,
would, in my opinion, do he very little honour; for sure it be much
easy, much less the subject of admiration, to paint a man with a nose,
or any other feature, of a preposterous size, or to expose he in some
absurd or monstrous attitude, than to express the affection of man on
canvas. it hath be think a vast commendation of a painter to say his
figure seem to breathe; but surely it be a much great and nobler
applause, that they appear to think.
but to return. the ridiculous only, as I have before say, fall within
my province in the present work. nor will some explanation of this word
be think impertinent by the reader, if he consider how wonderfully it
hath be mistaken, even by writer who have profess it: for to what
but such a mistake can we attribute the many attempt to ridicule the
black villanie, and, what be yet bad, the most dreadful
calamity? what could exceed the absurdity of an author, who should
write the comedy of Nero, with the merry incident of rip up his
mother's belly? or what would give a great shock to humanity than an
attempt to expose the misery of poverty and distress to ridicule? and
yet the reader will not want much learning to suggest such instance
to himself.
besides, it may seem remarkable, that Aristotle, who be so fond and free
of definition, hath not think proper to define the ridiculous.
indeed, where he tell we it be proper to comedy, he hath remark that
villany be not its object: but he hath not, as I remember, positively
assert what be. nor doth the Abbe Bellegarde, who hath write a
treatise on this subject, though he show we many specie of it, once
trace it to its fountain.
the only source of the true Ridiculous (as it appear to I) be
affectation. but though it arise from one spring only, when we consider
the infinite stream into which this one branch, we shall presently
cease to admire at the copious field it afford to an observer. now,
affectation proceed from one of these two cause, vanity or hypocrisy:
for as vanity put we on affect false character, in order to
purchase applause; so hypocrisy set we on an endeavour to avoid
censure, by conceal our vice under an appearance of their opposite
virtue. and though these two cause be often confound (for there be
some difficulty in distinguish they), yet, as they proceed from very
different motif, so they be as clearly distinct in their operation:
for indeed, the affectation which arise from vanity be near to truth
than the other, as it hath not that violent repugnancy of nature to
struggle with, which that of the hypocrite hath. it may be likewise
note, that affectation doth not imply an absolute negation of those
quality which be affect; and, therefore, though, when it proceed
from hypocrisy, it be nearly ally to deceit; yet when it come from
vanity only, it partake of the nature of ostentation: for instance, the
affectation of liberality in a vain man differ visibly from the same
affectation in the avaricious; for though the vain man be not what he
would appear, or hath not the virtue he affect, to the degree he would
be think to have it; yet it sit less awkwardly on he than on the
avaricious man, who be the very reverse of what he would seem to be.
from the discovery of this affectation arise the Ridiculous, which
always strike the reader with surprize and pleasure; and that in a
high and strong degree when the affectation arise from hypocrisy,
than when from vanity; for to discover any one to be the exact reverse
of what he affect, be more surprizing, and consequently more
ridiculous, than to find he a little deficient in the quality he
desire the reputation of. I might observe that our Ben Jonson, who of
all man understand the Ridiculous the good, hath chiefly use the
hypocritical affectation.
now, from affectation only, the misfortune and calamity of life, or
the imperfection of nature, may become the object of ridicule. surely
he hath a very ill-frame mind who can look on ugliness, infirmity, or
poverty, as ridiculous in themselves: nor do I believe any man live,
who meet a dirty fellow riding through the street in a cart, be
strike with an idea of the Ridiculous from it; but if he should see the
same figure descend from his coach and six, or bolt from his chair with
his hat under his arm, he would then begin to laugh, and with justice.
in the same manner, be we to enter a poor house and behold a wretched
family shiver with cold and languish with hunger, it would not
incline we to laughter (at least we must have very diabolical nature if
it would); but should we discover there a grate, instead of coal,
adorn with flower, empty plate or china dish on the sideboard, or
any other affectation of rich and finery, either on their person or
in their furniture, we might then indeed be excuse for ridicule so
fantastical an appearance. much less be natural imperfection the
object of derision; but when ugliness aim at the applause of beauty, or
lameness endeavour to display agility, it be then that these
unfortunate circumstance, which at first move our compassion, tend
only to raise our mirth.
the poet carry this very far:--
none be for be what they be in fault,
but for not be what they would be think.
where if the metre would suffer the word ridiculous to close the first
line, the thought would be rather more proper. great vice be the
proper object of our detestation, small fault, of our pity; but
affectation appear to I the only true source of the Ridiculous.
but perhaps it may be object to I, that I have against my own rule
introduce vice, and of a very black kind, into this work. to which I
shall answer: first, that it be very difficult to pursue a series of
human action, and keep clear from they. secondly, that the vice to be
find here be rather the accidental consequence of some human frailty
or foible, than cause habitually exist in the mind. thirdly, that
they be never set forth as the object of ridicule, but detestation.
fourthly, that they be never the principal figure at that time on the
scene: and, lastly, they never produce the intend evil.
have thus distinguish Joseph Andrews from the production of romance
writer on the one hand and burlesque writer on the other, and give
some few very short hint (for I intend no more) of this specie of
writing, which I have affirm to be hitherto unattempte in our
language; I shall leave to my good-natured reader to apply my piece to
my observation, and will detain he no long than with a word
concern the character in this work.
and here I solemnly protest I have no intention to vilify or asperse any
one; for though everything be copy from the book of nature, and scarce
a character or action produce which I have not take from my I own
observation and experience; yet I have use the utmost care to obscure
the person by such different circumstance, degree, and colour, that
it will be impossible to guess at they with any degree of certainty; and
if it ever happen otherwise, it be only where the failure characterize
be so minute, that it be a foible only which the party himself may laugh
at as well as any other.
as to the character of Adams, as it be the most glaring in the whole, so
I conceive it be not to be find in any book now extant. it be design
a character of perfect simplicity; and as the goodness of his heart
will recommend he to the good-natured, so I hope it will excuse I to
the gentleman of his cloth; for whom, while they be worthy of their
sacred order, no man can possibly have a great respect. they will
therefore excuse I, notwithstanding the low adventure in which he be
engage, that I have make he a clergyman; since no other office could
have give he so many opportunity of display his worthy
inclination.
