BOOK I.
CHAPTER I.
_of write life in general, and particularly of Pamela; with a word by
the bye of Colley Cibber and other._
it be a trite but true observation, that example work more forcibly on
the mind than precept: and if this be just in what be odious and
blameable, it be more strongly so in what be amiable and praiseworthy.
here emulation most effectually operate upon we, and inspire our
imitation in an irresistible manner. a good man therefore be a standing
lesson to all his acquaintance, and of far great use in that narrow
circle than a good book.
but as it often happen that the good man be but little know, and
consequently cannot extend the usefulness of their example a great way;
the writer may be call in aid to spread their history far, and to
present the amiable picture to those who have not the happiness of
know the original; and so, by communicate such valuable pattern
to the world, he may perhaps do a more extensive service to mankind than
the person whose life originally afford the pattern.
in this light I have always regard those biographer who have record
the action of great and worthy person of both sex. not to mention
those antient writer which of late day be little read, be write
in obsolete, and as they be generally think, unintelligible
language, such as Plutarch, Nepos, and other which I hear of in my
youth; our own language afford many of excellent use and instruction,
finely calculate to sow the seed of virtue in youth, and very easy to
be comprehend by person of moderate capacity. such as the history of
John the Great, who, by his brave and heroic action against man of
large and athletic body, obtain the glorious appellation of the
Giant-killer; that of an Earl of Warwick, whose christian name be Guy;
the life of Argalus and Parthenia; and above all, the history of those
seven worthy personage, the Champions of Christendom. in all these
delight be mix with instruction, and the reader be almost as much
improve as entertain.
but I pass by these and many other to mention two book lately
publish, which represent an admirable pattern of the amiable in either
sex. the former of these, which deal in male virtue, be write by the
great person himself, who live the life he hath record, and be by
many think to have live such a life only in order to write it. the
other be communicate to we by an historian who borrow his light, as
the common method be, from authentic paper and record. the reader, I
believe, already conjecture, I mean the life of Mr Colley Cibber and
of Mrs Pamela Andrews. how artfully doth the former, by insinuate that
he escape be promote to the high station in Church and State,
teach we a contempt of worldly grandeur! how strongly doth he inculcate
an absolute submission to our superior! lastly, how completely doth he
arm we against so uneasy, so wretche a passion as the fear of shame!
how clearly doth he expose the emptiness and vanity of that phantom,
reputation!
what the female reader be teach by the memoir of Mrs Andrews be so
well set forth in the excellent essay or letter prefix to the second
and subsequent edition of that work, that it would be here a needless
repetition. the authentic history with which I now present the public be
an instance of the great good that book be likely to do, and of the
prevalence of example which I have just observe: since it will appear
that it be by keep the excellent pattern of his sister's virtue
before his eye, that Mr Joseph Andrews be chiefly enable to preserve
his purity in the midst of such great temptation. I shall only add that
this character of male chastity, though doubtless as desirable and
become in one part of the human specie as in the other, be almost the
only virtue which the great apologist hath not give himself for the
sake of give the example to his reader.
CHAPTER II.
_of Mr Joseph Andrews, his birth, parentage, education, and great
endowment; with a word or two concern ancestor._
Mr Joseph Andrews, the hero of our ensue history, be esteem to be
the only son of Gaffar and Gammer Andrews, and brother to the
illustrious Pamela, whose virtue be at present so famous. as to his
ancestor, we have search with great diligence, but little success;
be unable to trace they far than his great-grandfather, who, as
an elderly person in the parish remember to have hear his father say,
be an excellent cudgel-player. whether he have any ancestor before
this, we must leave to the opinion of our curious reader, find
nothing of sufficient certainty to rely on. however, we cannot omit
insert an epitaph which an ingenious friend of ours hath
communicated:--
Stay, traveller, for underneath this pew
Lies fast asleep that merry man Andrew:
when the last day's great sun shall gild the sky,
then he shall from his tomb get up and rise.
be merry while thou canst: for surely thou
Shalt shortly be as sad as he be now.
the word be almost out of the stone with antiquity. but it be needless
to observe that Andrew here be writ without an _s_, and be, besides, a
christian name. my friend, moreover, conjecture this to have be the
founder of that sect of laugh philosopher since call
Merry-andrews.
to waive, therefore, a circumstance which, though mention in
conformity to the exact rule of biography, be not greatly material, I
proceed to thing of more consequence. indeed, it be sufficiently
certain that he have as many ancestor as the good man live, and,
perhaps, if we look five or six hundred year backwards, might be
relate to some person of very great figure at present, whose ancestor
within half the last century be bury in as great obscurity. but
suppose, for argument's sake, we should admit that he have no ancestor
at all, but have spring up, accord to the modern phrase, out of a
dunghill, as the Athenians pretend they themselves do from the earth,
would not this autokopros[A] have be justly entitle to all the
praise arise from his own virtue? would it not be hard that a man who
hath no ancestor should therefore be render incapable of acquire
honour; when we see so many who have no virtue enjoy the honour of
their forefather? at ten year old (by which time his education be
advanced to writing and reading) he be bind an apprentice, accord
to the statute, to Sir Thomas Booby, an uncle of Mr Booby's by the
father's side. Sir Thomas have then an estate in his own hand, the
young Andrews be at first employ in what in the country they call
keep bird. his office be to perform the part the ancient assign
to the god Priapus, which deity the modern call by the name of Jack o'
Lent; but his voice be so extremely musical, that it rather allure
the bird than terrify they, he be soon transplant from the field
into the dog-kennel, where he be place under the huntsman, and make
what the sportsman term whipper-in. for this place likewise the
sweetness of his voice disqualify he; the dog prefer the melody
of his chiding to all the alluring note of the huntsman, who soon
become so incensed at it, that he desire Sir Thomas to provide
otherwise for he, and constantly lay every fault the dog be at to
the account of the poor boy, who be now transplant to the stable.
here he soon give proof of strength and agility beyond his year, and
constantly ride the most spirited and vicious horse to water, with an
intrepidity which surprize every one. while he be in this station, he
ride several race for Sir Thomas, and this with such expertness and
success, that the neighbouring gentleman frequently solicit the knight
to permit little Joey (for so he be call) to ride their match. the
good gamester, before they lay their money, always inquire which
horse little Joey be to ride; and the bet be rather proportion by
the rider than by the horse himself; especially after he have scornfully
refuse a considerable bribe to play booty on such an occasion. this
extremely raise his character, and so please the Lady Booby, that she
desire to have he (be now seventeen year of age) for her
own footboy.
[a] in English, spring from a dunghill.
Joey be now prefer from the stable to attend on his lady, to go on
her errand, stand behind her chair, wait at her tea-table, and carry
her prayer-book to church; at which place his voice give he an
opportunity of distinguish himself by sing psalm: he behave
likewise in every other respect so well at Divine service, that it
recommend he to the notice of Mr Abraham Adams, the curate, who take
an opportunity one day, as he be drink a cup of ale in Sir Thomas's
kitchen, to ask the young man several question concern religion;
with his answer to which he be wonderfully pleased.
CHAPTER III.
_of Mr Abraham Adams the curate, Mrs Slipslop the chambermaid, and
other._
Mr Abraham Adams be an excellent scholar. he be a perfect master of
the greek and latin language; to which he add a great share of
knowledge in the oriental tongue; and could read and translate French,
italian, and Spanish. he have apply many year to the most severe
study, and have treasure up a fund of learn rarely to be meet with in
a university. he be, besides, a man of good sense, good part, and good
nature; but be at the same time as entirely ignorant of the way of
this world as an infant just enter into it could possibly be. as he
have never any intention to deceive, so he never suspect such a design
in other. he be generous, friendly, and brave to an excess; but
simplicity be his characteristick: he do, no more than Mr Colley
Cibber, apprehend any such passion as malice and envy to exist in
mankind; which be indeed less remarkable in a country parson than in a
gentleman who hath pass his life behind the scenes,--a place which
hath be seldom think the school of innocence, and where a very
little observation would have convince the great apologist that those
passion have a real existence in the human mind.
his virtue, and his other qualification, as they render he equal to
his office, so they make he an agreeable and valuable companion, and
have so much endear and well recommend he to a bishop, that at the
age of fifty he be provide with a handsome income of twenty-three
pound a year; which, however, he could not make any great figure with,
because he live in a dear country, and be a little encumbered with a
wife and six child.
it be this gentleman, who have, as I have say, observe the singular
devotion of young Andrews, have find mean to question he concern
several particular; as, how many book there be in the New Testament?
which be they? how many chapter they contain? and such like: to all
which, Mr Adams privately say, he answer much well than Sir Thomas,
or two other neighbouring justice of the peace could probably
have do.
Mr Adams be wonderfully solicitous to know at what time, and by what
opportunity, the youth become acquaint with these matter: Joey tell
he that he have very early learn to read and write by the goodness of
his father, who, though he have not interest enough to get he into a
charity school, because a cousin of his father's landlord do not vote
on the right side for a churchwarden in a borough town, yet have be
himself at the expense of sixpence a week for his learning. he tell he
likewise, that ever since he be in Sir Thomas's family he have employ
all his hour of leisure in read good book; that he have read the
Bible, the Whole Duty of Man, and Thomas a Kempis; and that as often as
he could, without be perceive, he have study a great good book
which lie open in the hall window, where he have read, "as how the devil
carry away half a church in sermon-time, without hurt one of the
congregation; and as how a field of corn run away down a hill with all
the tree upon it, and cover another man's meadow." this sufficiently
assure Mr Adams that the good book mean could be no other than Baker's
Chronicle.
the curate, surprize to find such instance of industry and application
in a young man who have never meet with the least encouragement, ask
he, if he do not extremely regret the want of a liberal education, and
the not have be bear of parent who might have indulge his talent
and desire of knowledge? to which he answer, "he hop he have profit
somewhat well from the book he have read than to lament his condition
in this world. that, for his part, he be perfectly content with the
state to which he be call; that he should endeavour to improve his
talent, which be all require of he; but not repine at his own lot,
nor envy those of his better." "well say, my lad," reply the curate;
"and I wish some who have read many more good book, nay, and some who
have write good book themselves, have profit so much by they."
Adams have no near access to Sir Thomas or my lady than through the
waiting-gentlewoman; for Sir Thomas be too apt to estimate man merely
by their dress or fortune; and my lady be a woman of gaiety, who have
be blest with a town education, and never speak of any of her country
neighbour by any other appellation than that of the brute. they both
regard the curate as a kind of domestic only, belong to the parson
of the parish, who be at this time at variance with the knight; for the
parson have for many year live in a constant state of civil war, or,
which be perhaps as bad, of civil law, with Sir Thomas himself and the
tenant of his manor. the foundation of this quarrel be a modus, by
set which aside an advantage of several shilling _per annum_ would
have accrue to the rector; but he have not yet be able to accomplish
his purpose, and have reap hitherto nothing well from the suit than
the pleasure (which he use indeed frequently to say be no small one)
of reflect that he have utterly undo many of the poor tenant,
though he have at the same time greatly impoverished himself.
Mrs Slipslop, the waiting-gentlewoman, be herself the daughter of a
curate, preserve some respect for Adams: she profess great regard for
his learning, and would frequently dispute with he on point of
theology; but always insist on a deference to be pay to she
understanding, as she have be frequently at London, and know more of
the world than a country parson could pretend to.
she have in these dispute a particular advantage over Adams: for she be
a mighty affecter of hard word, which she use in such a manner that
the parson, who durst not offend she by call her word in question,
be frequently at some loss to guess her meaning, and would have be
much less puzzled by an arabian manuscript.
Adams therefore take an opportunity one day, after a pretty long
discourse with she on the essence (or, as she please to term it, the
incence) of matter, to mention the case of young Andrews; desire she
to recommend he to her lady as a youth very susceptible of learning,
and one whose instruction in Latin he would himself undertake; by which
mean he might be qualified for a high station than that of a footman;
and add, she know it be in his master's power easily to provide for
he in a well manner. he therefore desire that the boy might be leave
behind under his care.
"La! Mr Adams," say Mrs Slipslop, "do you think my lady will suffer any
preamble about any such matter? she be go to London very concisely,
and I be confidous would not leave Joey behind she on any account; for
he be one of the genteelest young fellow you may see in a summer's day;
and I be confidous she would as soon think of part with a pair of her
grey mare, for she value herself as much on one as the other." Adams
would have interrupt, but she proceed: "and why be latin more
necessitous for a footman than a gentleman? it be very proper that you
clergyman must learn it, because you cannot preach without it: but I have
hear gentleman say in London, that it be fit for nobody else. I be
confidous my lady would be angry with I for mention it; and I shall
draw myself into no such delemy." at which word her lady's bell rung,
and Mr Adams be force to retire; nor could he gain a second
opportunity with she before their London journey, which happen a few
day afterwards. however, Andrews behave very thankfully and gratefully
to he for his intend kindness, which he tell he he never would
forget, and at the same time receive from the good man many admonition
concern the regulation of his future conduct, and his perseverance in
innocence and industry.
CHAPTER IV.
_what happen after their journey to London._
no soon be young Andrews arrive at London than he begin to scrape an
acquaintance with his party-colour brother, who endeavour to make
he despise his former course of life. his hair be cut after the new
fashion, and become his chief care; he go abroad with it all the
morning in paper, and drest it out in the afternoon. they could not,
however, teach he to game, swear, drink, nor any other genteel vice the
town abound with. he apply most of his leisure hour to music, in
which he greatly improve himself; and become so perfect a connoisseur
in that art, that he lead the opinion of all the other footman at an
opera, and they never condemn or applaud a single song contrary to
his approbation or dislike. he be a little too forward in riot at the
play-house and assembly; and when he attend his lady at church
(which be but seldom) he behave with less seeming devotion than
formerly: however, if he be outwardly a pretty fellow, his moral
remain entirely uncorrupted, though he be at the same time smart
and genteel than any of the beaus in town, either in or out of livery.
his lady, who have often say of he that Joey be the handsome and
genteelest footman in the kingdom, but that it be pity he want
spirit, begin now to find that fault no long; on the contrary, she be
frequently hear to cry out, "ay, there be some life in this fellow."
she plainly see the effect which the town air hath on the sober
constitution. she would now walk out with he into Hyde Park in a
morning, and when tire, which happen almost every minute, would lean
on his arm, and converse with he in great familiarity. whenever she
stept out of her coach, she would take he by the hand, and sometimes,
for fear of stumbling, press it very hard; she admit he to deliver
message at her bedside in a morning, leer at he at table, and
indulge he in all those innocent freedom which woman of figure may
permit without the least sully of their virtue.
but though their virtue remain unsullied, yet now and then some small
arrow will glance on the shadow of it, their reputation; and so it fall
out to Lady Booby, who happen to be walk arm-in-arm with Joey one
morning in Hyde Park, when Lady Tittle and Lady Tattle come accidentally
by in their coach. "bless I," say Lady Tittle, "can I believe my eye?
be that Lady booby?"--"surely," say Tattle. "but what make you
surprized?"--"why, be not that her footman?" reply Tittle. at which
Tattle laugh, and cry, "an old business, I assure you: be it
possible you should not have hear it? the whole town hath know it this
half-year." the consequence of this interview be a whisper through a
hundred visit, which be separately perform by the two ladies[A] the
same afternoon, and might have have a mischievous effect, have it not be
stopt by two fresh reputation which be publish the day afterwards,
and engross the whole talk of the town.
[a] it may seem an absurdity that tattle should visit, as she actually
do, to spread a know scandal: but the reader may reconcile this by
supposing, with I, that, notwithstanding what she say, this be
her first acquaintance with it.
but, whatever opinion or suspicion the scandalous inclination of
defamer might entertain of Lady Booby's innocent freedom, it be
certain they make no impression on young Andrews, who never offer to
encroach beyond the liberty which his lady allow him,--a behaviour
which she impute to the violent respect he preserve for she, and which
serve only to heighten a something she begin to conceive, and which
the next chapter will open a little far.
CHAPTER V.
_the death of Sir Thomas Booby, with the affectionate and mournful
behaviour of his widow, and the great purity of Joseph Andrews._
at this time an accident happen which put a stop to those agreeable
walk, which probably would have soon puff up the cheek of Fame, and
cause she to blow her brazen trumpet through the town; and this be no
other than the death of Sir Thomas Booby, who, depart this life, leave
his disconsolate lady confine to her house, as closely as if she
herself have be attack by some violent disease. during the first six
day the poor lady admit none but Mrs. Slipslop, and three female
friend, who make a party at card: but on the seventh she order Joey,
whom, for a good reason, we shall hereafter call JOSEPH, to bring up her
tea-kettle. the lady be in bed, call Joseph to she, bade he sit
down, and, have accidentally lay her hand on his, she ask he if he
have ever be in love. Joseph answer, with some confusion, it be time
enough for one so young as himself to think on such thing. "as young as
you be," reply the lady, "I be convinced you be no strange to that
passion. come, Joey," say she, "tell I truly, who be the happy girl
whose eye have make a conquest of you?" Joseph return, that all the
woman he have ever see be equally indifferent to he. "oh then," say
the lady, "you be a general lover. indeed, you handsome fellow, like
handsome woman, be very long and difficult in fixing; but yet you
shall never persuade I that your heart be so insusceptible of
affection; I rather impute what you say to your secrecy, a very
commendable quality, and what I be far from be angry with you for.
nothing can be more unworthy in a young man, than to betray any
intimacy with the lady." "Ladies! madam," say Joseph, "I be sure I
never have the impudence to think of any that deserve that name." "donot
pretend to too much modesty," say she, "for that sometimes may be
impertinent: but pray answer I this question. suppose a lady should
happen to like you; suppose she should prefer you to all your sex, and
admit you to the same familiarity as you might have hop for if you
have be bear she equal, be you certain that no vanity could tempt you
to discover she? answer I honestly, Joseph; have you so much more sense
and so much more virtue than you handsome young fellow generally have,
who make no scruple of sacrifice our dear reputation to your pride,
without consider the great obligation we lay on you by our
condescension and confidence? can you keep a secret, my Joey?" "Madam,"
say he, "I hope your ladyship cannot tax I with ever betray the
secret of the family; and I hope, if you be to turn I away, I might
have that character of you." "I donot intend to turn you away, Joey,"
say she, and sigh; "I be afraid it be not in my power." she then
raise herself a little in her bed, and discover one of the white
neck that ever be see; at which Joseph blush. "La!" say she, in an
affect surprize, "what be I do? I have trust myself with a man
alone, naked in bed; suppose you should have any wicked intention upon
my honour, how should I defend myself?" Joseph protest that he never
have the least evil design against she. "no," say she, "perhaps you may
not call your design wicked; and perhaps they be not so."--He swore
they be not. "you misunderstand I," say she; "I mean if they be
against my honour, they may not be wicked; but the world call they so.
but then, say you, the world will never know anything of the matter; yet
would not that be trust to your secrecy? must not my reputation be
then in your power? would you not then be my master?" Joseph beg she
ladyship to be comfort; for that he would never imagine the least
wicked thing against she, and that he have rather die a thousand death
than give she any reason to suspect he. "yes," say she, "I must have
reason to suspect you. be you not a man? and, without vanity, I may
pretend to some charm. but perhaps you may fear I should prosecute you;
indeed I hope you do; and yet Heaven know I should never have the
confidence to appear before a court of justice; and you know, Joey, I be
of a forgiving temper. tell I, Joey, donot you think I should forgive
you?"--"indeed, madam," say Joseph, "I will never do anything to
disoblige your ladyship."--"how," say she, "do you think it would not
disoblige I then? do you think I would willingly suffer you?"--"i donot
understand you, madam," say joseph.--"don't you?" say she, "then you
be either a fool, or pretend to be so; I find I be mistaken in you. so
get you downstairs, and never let I see your face again; your pretended
innocence cannot impose on I."--"Madam," say Joseph, "I would not have
your ladyship think any evil of I. I have always endeavour to be a
dutiful servant both to you and my master."--"O thou villain!" answer
my lady; "why didst thou mention the name of that dear man, unless to
torment I, to bring his precious memory to my mind?" (and then she
burst into a fit of tear.) "get thee from my sight! I shall never
endure thee more." at which word she turn away from he; and Joseph
retreat from the room in a most disconsolate condition, and writ that
letter which the reader will find in the next chapter.
CHAPTER VI.
_how Joseph Andrews writ a letter to his sister Pamela._
"to MRS PAMELA ANDREWS, live with SQUIRE BOOBY.
"DEAR sister,--since I receive your letter of your good lady's death,
we have have a misfortune of the same kind in our family. my worthy
master Sir Thomas die about four day ago; and, what be bad, my poor
lady be certainly go distract. none of the servant expect she to
take it so to heart, because they quarrel almost every day of their
life: but no more of that, because you know, Pamela, I never love to
tell the secret of my master's family; but to be sure you must have
know they never love one another; and I have hear her ladyship wish
his honour dead above a thousand time; but nobody know what it be to
lose a friend till they have lose he.
"donot tell anybody what I write, because I should not care to have
folk say I discover what pass in our family; but if it have not be
so great a lady, I should have think she have have a mind to I. dear
Pamela, donot tell anybody; but she order I to sit down by she
bedside, when she be in naked bed; and she hold my hand, and talk
exactly as a lady do to her sweetheart in a stage-play, which I have
see in Covent Garden, while she want he to be no well than he
should be.
"if madam be mad, I shall not care for stay long in the family; so I
heartily wish you could get I a place, either at the squire's, or some
other neighbouring gentleman's, unless it be true that you be go to
be marry to parson Williams, as folk talk, and then I should be very
willing to be his clerk; for which you know I be qualified, be able
to read and to set a psalm.
"I fancy I shall be discharge very soon; and the moment I be, unless I
hear from you, I shall return to my old master's country-seat, if it be
only to see parson Adams, who be the good man in the world. London be a
bad place, and there be so little good fellowship, that the next-door
neighbour donot know one another. pray give my service to all friend
that inquire for I. so I rest
"your love brother,
"JOSEPH ANDREWS."
as soon as Joseph have seal and direct this letter he walk
downstair, where he meet Mrs. Slipslop, with whom we shall take this
opportunity to bring the reader a little well acquainted. she be a
maiden gentlewoman of about forty-five year of age, who, have make a
small slip in her youth, have continue a good maid ever since. she be
not at this time remarkably handsome; be very short, and rather too
corpulent in body, and somewhat red, with the addition of pimple in the
face. her nose be likewise rather too large, and her eye too little;
nor do she resemble a cow so much in her breath as in two brown globe
which she carry before she; one of her leg be also a little short
than the other, which occasion she to limp as she walk. this fair
creature have long cast the eye of affection on Joseph, in which she have
not meet with quite so good success as she probably wish, though,
besides the allurement of her native charm, she have give he tea,
sweetmeat, wine, and many other delicacy, of which, by keep the
key, she have the absolute command. Joseph, however, have not return
the least gratitude to all these favour, not even so much as a kiss;
though I would not insinuate she be so easily to be satisfy; for
surely then he would have be highly blameable. the truth be, she be
arrive at an age when she think she might indulge herself in any
liberty with a man, without the danger of bring a third person into
the world to betray they. she imagine that by so long a self-denial she
have not only make amend for the small slip of her youth above hint
at, but have likewise lay up a quantity of merit to excuse any future
failing. in a word, she resolve to give a loose to her amorous
inclination, and to pay off the debt of pleasure which she find she
owe herself, as fast as possible.
with these charm of person, and in this disposition of mind, she
encounter poor Joseph at the bottom of the stair, and ask he if he
would drink a glass of something good this morning. Joseph, whose
spirit be not a little cast down, very readily and thankfully
accept the offer; and together they go into a closet, where, have
deliver he a full glass of ratafia, and desire he to sit down, Mrs.
Slipslop thus began:--
"sure nothing can be a more simple contract in a woman than to place her
affection on a boy. if I have ever think it would have be my fate, I
should have wish to die a thousand death rather than live to see that
day. if we like a man, the light hint sophisticate. whereas a boy
propose upon we to break through all the regulation of modesty, before
we can make any oppression upon he." Joseph, who do not understand a
word she say, answer, "yes, madam."--"Yes, madam!" reply Mrs.
Slipslop with some warmth, "do you intend to result my passion? be it
not enough, ungrateful as you be, to make no return to all the favour
I have do you; but you must treat I with ironing? barbarous monster!
how have I deserve that my passion should be result and treat with
iron?" "Madam," answer Joseph, "I donot understand your hard word;
but I be certain you have no occasion to call I ungrateful, for, so far
from intend you any wrong, I have always love you as well as if you
have be my own mother." "how, sirrah!" say Mrs. Slipslop in a rage;
"your own mother? do you assinuate that I be old enough to be your
mother? I donot know what a stripling may think, but I believe a man
would refer I to any green-sickness silly girl whatsomdever: but I
ought to despise you rather than be angry with you, for refer the
conversation of girl to that of a woman of sense."--"Madam," say
Joseph, "I be sure I have always value the honour you do I by your
conversation, for I know you be a woman of learn."--"Yes, but,
Joseph," say she, a little soften by the compliment to her learning,
"if you have a value for I, you certainly would have find some method
of show it I; for I be convict you must see the value I have for
you. yes, Joseph, my eye, whether I would or no, must have declare a
passion I cannot conquer.--oh! Joseph!"
as when a hungry tigress, who long have traverse the wood in fruitless
search, see within the reach of her claws a lamb, she prepare to leap
on her prey; or as a voracious pike, of immense size, survey through
the liquid element a roach or gudgeon, which cannot escape her jaw,
open they wide to swallow the little fish; so do Mrs. Slipslop prepare
to lay her violent amorous hand on the poor Joseph, when luckily her
mistress's bell rung, and deliver the intended martyr from her
clutch. she be oblige to leave he abruptly, and to defer the
execution of her purpose till some other time. we shall therefore return
to the Lady Booby, and give our reader some account of her behaviour,
after she be leave by Joseph in a temper of mind not greatly different
from that of the inflame Slipslop.
CHAPTER VII.
_saying of wise man. a dialogue between the lady and she maid; and a
panegyric, or rather satire, on the passion of love, in the
sublime style._
it be the observation of some antient sage, whose name I have forget,
that passion operate differently on the human mind, as disease on the
body, in proportion to the strength or weakness, soundness or
rottenness, of the one and the other.
we hope, therefore, a judicious reader will give himself some pain to
observe, what we have so greatly labour to describe, the different
operation of this passion of love in the gentle and cultivate mind of
the Lady Booby, from those which it effect in the less polished and
coarser disposition of Mrs Slipslop.
another philosopher, whose name also at present escape my memory, hath
somewhere say, that resolution take in the absence of the beloved
object be very apt to vanish in its presence; on both which wise
saying the follow chapter may serve as a comment.
no soon have Joseph leave the room in the manner we have before relate
than the lady, enrage at her disappointment, begin to reflect with
severity on her conduct. her love be now change to disdain, which
pride assist to torment she. she despise herself for the meanness of
her passion, and Joseph for its ill success. however, she have now get
the well of it in her own opinion, and determine immediately to
dismiss the object. after much tossing and turn in her bed, and many
soliloquy, which if we have no well matter for our reader we would
give he, she at last rung the bell as above mention, and be
presently attend by Mrs Slipslop, who be not much well pleased with
Joseph than the lady herself.
"Slipslop," say Lady Booby, "when do you see Joseph?" the poor woman
be so surprized at the unexpected sound of his name at so critical a
time, that she have the great difficulty to conceal the confusion she
be under from her mistress; whom she answer, nevertheless, with
pretty good confidence, though not entirely void of fear of suspicion,
that she have not see he that morning. "I be afraid," say Lady Booby,
"he be a wild young fellow."--"That he be," say Slipslop, "and a
wicked one too. to my knowledge he game, drink, swear, and fight
eternally; besides, he be horribly indict to wenching."--"Ay!" say
the lady, "I never hear that of he."--"O madam!" answer the other,
"he be so lewd a rascal, that if your ladyship keep he much long,
you will not have one virgin in your house except myself. and yet I
cannot conceive what the wench see in he, to be so foolishly fond as
they be; in my eye, he be as ugly a scarecrow as I ever
uphold."--"Nay," say the lady, "the boy be well enough."--"La! ma'am,"
cry Slipslop, "I think he the ragmaticall fellow in the
family."--"Sure, Slipslop," say she, "you be mistake: but which of
the woman do you most suspect?"--"madam," say Slipslop, "there be Betty
the chambermaid, I be almost convict, be with child by he."--"Ay!"
say the lady, "then pray pay she her wage instantly. I will keep no
such slut in my family. and as for Joseph, you may discard he
too."--"Would your ladyship have he pay off immediately?" cry
Slipslop, "for perhaps, when Betty be go he may mend: and really the
boy be a good servant, and a strong healthy luscious boy enough."--
"this morning," answer the lady with some vehemence. "I wish, madam,"
cry Slipslop, "your ladyship would be so good as to try he a little
long."--"I will not have my command dispute," say the lady; "sure
you be not fond of he yourself?"--"I, madam!" cry Slipslop,
redden, if not blush, "I should be sorry to think your ladyship
have any reason to respect I of fondness for a fellow; and if it be your
pleasure, I shall fulfil it with as much reluctance as possible."--"a
little, I suppose you mean," say the lady; "and so about it instantly."
Mrs. Slipslop go out, and the lady have scarce take two turn before
she fall to knock and ring with great violence. Slipslop, who do
not travel post haste, soon return, and be countermand as to
Joseph, but order to send Betty about her business without delay. she
go out a second time with much great alacrity than before; when the
lady begin immediately to accuse herself of want of resolution, and to
apprehend the return of her affection, with its pernicious consequence;
she therefore apply herself again to the bell, and re-summon Mrs.
Slipslop into her presence; who again return, and be tell by her
mistress that she have consider well of the matter, and be
absolutely resolve to turn away Joseph; which she order she to do
immediately. Slipslop, who know the violence of her lady's temper, and
would not venture her place for any Adonis or Hercules in the universe,
leave she a third time; which she have no soon do, than the little god
Cupid, fear he have not yet do the lady's business, take a fresh
arrow with the sharp point out of his quiver, and shoot it directly
into her heart; in other and plainer language, the lady's passion get
the well of her reason. she call back Slipslop once more, and tell
she she have resolve to see the boy, and examine he herself; therefore
bid she send he up. this waver in her mistress's temper probably put
something into the waiting-gentlewoman's head not necessary to mention
to the sagacious reader.
Lady Booby be go to call she back again, but could not prevail with
herself. the next consideration therefore be, how she should behave to
Joseph when he come in. she resolve to preserve all the dignity of the
woman of fashion to her servant, and to indulge herself in this last
view of Joseph (for that she be most certainly resolve it should be)
at his own expense, by first insulting and then discard he.
o love, what monstrous trick dost thou play with thy votary of both
sex! how dost thou deceive they, and make they deceive themselves!
their folly be thy delight! their sigh make thee laugh, and their
pang be thy merriment!
not the great Rich, who turn man into monkey, wheel-barrow, and
whatever else well humour his fancy, hath so strangely metamorphose
the human shape; nor the great Cibber, who confound all number, gender,
and break through every rule of grammar at his will, hath so distort
the english language as thou dost metamorphose and distort the
human sens.
Thou putt out our eye, stopp up our ear, and takest away the
power of our nostril; so that we can neither see the large object,
hear the loud noise, nor smell the most poignant perfume. again, when
thou pleasest, thou canst make a molehill appear as a mountain, a
Jew's-harp sound like a trumpet, and a daisy smell like a violet. Thou
canst make cowardice brave, avarice generous, pride humble, and cruelty
tender-hearted. in short, thou turn the heart of man inside out, as a
juggler doth a petticoat, and bringest whatsoever pleaseth thee out
from it. if there be any one who doubt all this, let he read the
next chapter.
CHAPTER VIII.
_in which, after some very fine writing, the history go on, and
relate the interview between the lady and Joseph; where the latter hath
set an example which we despair of seeing follow by his sex in this
vicious age._
now the rake Hesperus have call for his breech, and, have well
rub his drowsy eye, prepare to dress himself for all night; by
whose example his brother rake on earth likewise leave those bed in
which they have sleep away the day. now Thetis, the good housewife, begin
to put on the pot, in order to regale the good man Phoebus after his
daily labour be over. in vulgar language, it be in the evening when
Joseph attend his lady's order.
but as it become we to preserve the character of this lady, who be the
heroine of our tale; and as we have naturally a wonderful tenderness for
that beautiful part of the human specie call the fair sex; before we
discover too much of her frailty to our reader, it will be proper to
give he a lively idea of the vast temptation, which overcome all the
effort of a modest and virtuous mind; and then we humbly hope his good
nature will rather pity than condemn the imperfection of human virtue.
[illustration]
Nay, the lady themselves will, we hope, be induce, by consider the
uncommon variety of charm which unite in this young man's person, to
bridle their rampant passion for chastity, and be at least as mild as
their violent modesty and virtue will permit they, in censure the
conduct of a woman who, perhaps, be in her own disposition as chaste
as those pure and sanctify virgin who, after a life innocently spend
in the gaiety of the town, begin about fifty to attend twice _per
diem_ at the polite church and chapel, to return thank for the grace
which preserve they formerly amongst beaus from temptation perhaps
less powerful than what now attack the Lady Booby.
Mr Joseph Andrews be now in the one-and-twentieth year of his age. he
be of the high degree of middle stature; his limb be put together
with great elegance, and no less strength; his leg and thigh be
form in the exactest proportion; his shoulder be broad and brawny,
but yet his arm hang so easily, that he have all the symptom of strength
without the least clumsiness. his hair be of a nut-brown colour, and
be display in wanton ringlet down his back; his forehead be high,
his eye dark, and as full of sweetness as of fire; his nose a little
inclined to the Roman; his tooth white and even; his lip full, red, and
soft; his beard be only rough on his chin and upper lip; but his
cheek, in which his blood glow, be overspread with a thick down;
his countenance have a tenderness join with a sensibility
inexpressible. add to this the most perfect neatness in his dress, and
an air which, to those who have not see many nobleman, would give an
idea of nobility.
such be the person who now appear before the lady. she view he
some time in silence, and twice or thrice before she spake change she
mind as to the manner in which she should begin. at length she say to
he, "Joseph, I be sorry to hear such complaint against you: I be tell
you behave so rudely to the maid, that they cannot do their business in
quiet; I mean those who be not wicked enough to hearken to your
solicitation. as to other, they may, perhaps, not call you rude; for
there be wicked slut who make one ashamed of one's own sex, and be as
ready to admit any nauseous familiarity as fellow to offer it: nay,
there be such in my family, but they shall not stay in it; that
impudent trollop who be with child by you be discharge by this time."
as a person who be strike through the heart with a thunderbolt look
extremely surprised, nay, and perhaps be so too--thus the poor Joseph
receive the false accusation of his mistress; he blush and look
confound, which she misinterpret to be symptom of his guilt, and
thus go on:--
"come hither, Joseph: another mistress might discard you for these
offence; but I have a compassion for your youth, and if I could be
certain you would be no more guilty--consider, child," lay her hand
carelessly upon his, "you be a handsome young fellow, and might do
well; you might make your fortune." "Madam," say Joseph, "I do assure
your ladyship I donot know whether any maid in the house be man or
woman." "oh fie! Joseph," answer the lady, "donot commit another crime
in deny the truth. I could pardon the first; but I hate a lyar."
"Madam," cry Joseph, "I hope your ladyship will not be offend at my
assert my innocence; for, by all that be sacred, I have never offer
more than kiss." "kiss!" say the lady, with great discomposure of
countenance, and more redness in her cheek than anger in her eye; "do
you call that no crime? Kissing, Joseph, be as a prologue to a play. can
I believe a young fellow of your age and complexion will be content with
kissing? no, Joseph, there be no woman who grant that but will grant
more; and I be deceive greatly in you if you would not put she closely
to it. what would you think, Joseph, if I admit you to kiss I?"
Joseph reply he would soon die than have any such thought. "and
yet, Joseph," return she, "lady have admit their footman to such
familiarity; and footman, I confess to you, much less deserving they;
fellow without half your charm--for such might almost excuse the
crime. tell I therefore, Joseph, if I should admit you to such freedom,
what would you think of me?--tell I freely." "Madam," say Joseph, "I
should think your ladyship condescend a great deal below yourself."
"Pugh!" say she; "that I be to answer to myself: but would not you
insist on more? would you be content with a kiss? would not your
inclination be all on fire rather by such a favour?" "Madam," say
Joseph, "if they be, I hope I should be able to controul they, without
suffer they to get the well of my virtue." you have hear, reader,
poet talk of the statue of Surprize; you have hear likewise, or else
you have hear very little, how Surprize make one of the son of Croesus
speak, though he be dumb. you have see the face, in the
eighteen-penny gallery, when, through the trap-door, to soft or no
music, Mr. Bridgewater, Mr. William Mills, or some other of ghostly
appearance, hath ascend, with a face all pale with powder, and a shirt
all bloody with ribbons;--but from none of these, nor from Phidias or
Praxiteles, if they should return to life--no, not from the inimitable
pencil of my friend Hogarth, could you receive such an idea of surprize
as would have enter in at your eye have they beheld the Lady Booby
when those last word issue out from the lip of Joseph. "your virtue!"
say the lady, recover after a silence of two minute; "I shall never
survive it. your virtue!--intolerable confidence! have you the assurance
to pretend, that when a lady demean herself to throw aside the rule of
decency, in order to honour you with the high favour in her power,
your virtue should resist her inclination? that, when she have conquer
her own virtue, she should find an obstruction in your?" "Madam," say
Joseph, "I cannot see why she have no virtue should be a reason against
my have any; or why, because I be a man, or because I be poor, my
virtue must be subservient to her pleasure." "I be out of patience,"
cry the lady: "do ever mortal hear of a man's virtue? do ever the
great or the grave man pretend to any of this kind? will
magistrate who punish lewdness, or parson who preach against it, make
any scruple of commit it? and can a boy, a stripling, have the
confidence to talk of his virtue?" "Madam," say Joseph, "that boy be
the brother of Pamela, and would be ashamed that the chastity of his
family, which be preserve in she, should be stain in he. if there
be such man as your ladyship mention, I be sorry for it; and I wish
they have an opportunity of read over those letter which my father
hath send I of my sister Pamela's; nor do I doubt but such an example
would amend they." "you impudent villain!" cry the lady in a rage; "do
you insult I with the folly of my relation, who hath expose himself

have always wonder my late Lady Booby ever keep in her house. Sirrah!
get out of my sight, and prepare to set out this night; for I will order
you your wage immediately, and you shall be strip and turn away."
"Madam," say Joseph, "I be sorry I have offend your ladyship, I be
sure I never intend it." "yes, sirrah," cry she, "you have have the
vanity to misconstrue the little innocent freedom I take, in order to
try whether what I have hear be true. o' my conscience, you have have
the assurance to imagine I be fond of you myself." Joseph answer, he
have only speak out of tenderness for his virtue; at which word she
fly into a violent passion, and refuse to hear more, order he
instantly to leave the room.
he be no soon go than she burst forth into the follow
exclamation:--"whither doth this violent passion hurry we? what
meanness do we submit to from its impulse! wisely we resist its first
and least approach; for it be then only we can assure ourselves the
victory. no woman could ever safely say, so far only will I go. have I
not expose myself to the refusal of my footman? I cannot bear the
reflection." upon which she apply herself to the bell, and ring it
with infinite more violence than be necessary--the faithful Slipslop
attend near at hand: to say the truth, she have conceive a suspicion
at her last interview with her mistress, and have wait ever since in
the antechamber, have carefully apply her ear to the keyhole during
the whole time that the precede conversation pass between Joseph
and the lady.
CHAPTER IX.
_what pass between the lady and Mrs Slipslop; in which we prophesy
there be some stroke which every one will not truly comprehend at the
first reading._
"Slipslop," say the lady, "I find too much reason to believe all thou
hast tell I of this wicked Joseph; I have determine to part with he
instantly; so go you to the steward, and bid he pay his wage."
Slipslop, who have preserve hitherto a distance to her lady--rather out
of necessity than inclination--and who think the knowledge of this
secret have throw down all distinction between they, answer she
mistress very pertly--"she wish she know her own mind; and that she
be certain she would call she back again before she be get half-way
downstair." the lady reply, she have take a resolution, and be
resolve to keep it. "I be sorry for it," cry Slipslop, "and, if I have
know you would have punish the poor lad so severely, you should never
have hear a particle of the matter. herebe a fuss indeed about
nothing!" "nothing!" return my lady; "do you think I will countenance
lewdness in my house?" "if you will turn away every footman," say
Slipslop, "that be a lover of the sport, you must soon open the coach
door yourself, or get a set of mophrodite to wait upon you; and I be
sure I hat the sight of they even sing in an opera." "do as I bid
you," say my lady, "and donot shock my ear with your beastly
language." "Marry-come-up," cry Slipslop, "people's ear be sometimes
the nice part about they."
the lady, who begin to admire the new style in which she
waiting-gentlewoman deliver herself, and by the conclusion of her
speech suspect somewhat of the truth, call she back, and desire to
know what she mean by the extraordinary degree of freedom in which she
think proper to indulge her tongue. "Freedom!" say Slipslop; "I donot
know what you call freedom, madam; servant have tongue as well as
their mistress." "yes, and saucy one too," answer the lady; "but I
assure you I shall bear no such impertinence." "Impertinence! I donot
know that I be impertinent," say Slipslop. "yes, indeed you be," cry
my lady, "and, unless you mend your manner, this house be no place for
you." "manner!" cry Slipslop; "I never be think to want manner
nor modesty neither; and for place, there be more place than one; and
I know what I know." "what do you know, mistress?" answer the lady. "I
be not oblige to tell that to everybody," say Slipslop, "any more than
I be oblige to keep it a secret." "I desire you would provide
yourself," answer the lady. "with all my heart," reply the
waiting-gentlewoman; and so depart in a passion, and slap the door
after she.
the lady too plainly perceive that her waiting-gentlewoman know more
than she would willingly have have she acquainted with; and this she
impute to Joseph's have discover to she what pass at the first
interview. this, therefore, blow up her rage against he, and confirm
she in a resolution of part with he.
but the dismiss Mrs Slipslop be a point not so easily to be resolve
upon. she have the utmost tenderness for her reputation, as she know on
that depend many of the most valuable blessing of life; particularly
card, make curtsy in public place, and, above all, the pleasure of
demolish the reputation of other, in which innocent amusement she
have an extraordinary delight. she therefore determine to submit to any
insult from a servant, rather than run a risque of lose the title to
so many great privilege.
she therefore send for her steward, Mr Peter Pounce, and order he to
pay Joseph his wage, to strip off his livery, and to turn he out of
the house that evening.
she then call Slipslop up, and, after refresh her spirit with a
small cordial, which she keep in her corset, she begin in the
follow manner:--
"Slipslop, why will you, who know my passionate temper, attempt to
provoke I by your answer? I be convinced you be an honest servant,
and should be very unwilling to part with you. I believe, likewise, you
have find I an indulgent mistress on many occasion, and have as
little reason on your side to desire a change. I cannot help be
surprize, therefore, that you will take the sure method to offend
I--I mean, repeat my word, which you know I have always detest."
the prudent waiting-gentlewoman have duly weigh the whole matter, and
find, on mature deliberation, that a good place in possession be
well than one in expectation. as she find her mistress, therefore,
inclined to relent, she think proper also to put on some small
condescension, which be as readily accept; and so the affair be
reconcile, all offence forgive, and a present of a gown and petticoat
make she, as an instance of her lady's future favour.
she offer once or twice to speak in favour of Joseph; but find she
lady's heart so obdurate, that she prudently dropt all such effort. she
consider there be more footman in the house, and some as stout
fellow, though not quite so handsome, as Joseph; besides, the reader
hath already see her tender advance have not meet with the encouragement
she might have reasonable expect. she think she have throw away a
great deal of sack and sweetmeat on an ungrateful rascal; and, be a
little inclined to the opinion of that female sect, who hold one lusty
young fellow to be nearly as good as another lusty young fellow, she at
last give up Joseph and his cause, and, with a triumph over her passion
highly commendable, walk off with her present, and with great
tranquillity pay a visit to a stone-bottle, which be of sovereign use
to a philosophical temper.
she leave not her mistress so easy. the poor lady could not reflect
without agony that her dear reputation be in the power of her servant.
all her comfort as to Joseph be, that she hop he do not understand
her meaning; at least she could say for herself, she have not plainly
express anything to he; and as to Mrs Slipslop, she imagine she
could bribe she to secrecy.
but what hurt she most be, that in reality she have not so entirely
conquer her passion; the little god lie lurk in her heart, though
anger and distain so hood-wink she, that she could not see he. she
be a thousand time on the very brink of revoke the sentence she have
pass against the poor youth. love become his advocate, and whisper
many thing in his favour. Honour likewise endeavour to vindicate his
crime, and Pity to mitigate his punishment. on the other side, Pride and
Revenge speak as loudly against he. and thus the poor lady be torture
with perplexity, opposite passion distract and tear her mind
different way.
so have I see, in the hall of Westminster, where Serjeant bramble hath
be retain on the right side, and Serjeant Puzzle on the left, the
balance of opinion (so equal be their fee) alternately incline to
either scale. now bramble throw in an argument, and Puzzle's scale
strike the beam; again bramble share the like fate, overpower by the
weight of Puzzle. here bramble hit, there Puzzle strike; here one have
you, there t'other have you; till at last all become one scene of
confusion in the tortured mind of the hearer; equal wager be lay on
the success, and neither judge nor jury can possibly make anything of
the matter; all thing be so envelop by the careful serjeant in
doubt and obscurity.
or, as it happen in the conscience, where honour and honesty pull one
way, and a bribe and necessity another.--if it be our present
business only to make simile, we could produce many more to this
purpose; but a simile (as well as a word) to the wise.--We shall
therefore see a little after our hero, for whom the reader be doubtless
in some pain.
CHAPTER X.
_Joseph write another letter: his transaction with Mr Peter Pounce,
&c., with his departure from Lady Booby._
the disconsolate Joseph would not have have an understanding sufficient
for the principal subject of such a book as this, if he have any long
misunderstood the drift of his mistress; and indeed, that he do not
discern it soon, the reader will be pleased to impute to an
unwillingness in he to discover what he must condemn in she as a fault.
have therefore quit her presence, he retire into his own garret,
and enter himself into an ejaculation on the numberless calamitie
which attend beauty, and the misfortune it be to be handsomer than
one's neighbour.
he then sit down, and address himself to his sister Pamela in the
follow words:--
"Dear Sister pamela,--hope you be well, what news have I to tell you!
o Pamela! my mistress be fall in love with I-that be, what great
folk call fall in love-she have a mind to ruin I; but I hope I shall
have more resolution and more grace than to part with my virtue to any
lady upon earth.
"Mr Adams hath often tell I, that chastity be as great a virtue in a
man as in a woman. he say he never know any more than his wife, and I
shall endeavour to follow his example. indeed, it be owe entirely to
his excellent sermon and advice, together with your letter, that I
have be able to resist a temptation, which, he say, no man comply
with, but he repent in this world, or be damn for it in the next; and
why should I trust to repentance on my deathbed, since I may die in my
sleep? what fine thing be good advice and good example! but I be
glad she turn I out of the chamber as she do: for I have once almost
forget every word parson Adams have ever say to I.
"I donot doubt, dear sister, but you will have grace to preserve your
virtue against all trial; and I beg you earnestly to pray I may be
enable to preserve mine; for truly it be very severely attack by more
than one; but I hope I shall copy your example, and that of Joseph my
namesake, and maintain my virtue against all temptation."
Joseph have not finish his letter, when he be summon downstairs by
Mr Peter Pounce, to receive his wage; for, besides that out of eight
pound a year he allow his father and mother four, he have be
oblige, in order to furnish himself with musical instrument, to apply
to the generosity of the aforesaid Peter, who, on urgent occasion, use
to advance the servant their wage: not before they be due, but
before they be payable; that be, perhaps, half a year after they be
due; and this at the moderate premium of fifty per cent, or a little
more: by which charitable method, together with lend money to other
people, and even to his own master and mistress, the honest man have,
from nothing, in a few year amass a small sum of twenty thousand
pound or thereabout.
Joseph have receive his little remainder of wage, and have stript
off his livery, be force to borrow a frock and breech of one of the
servant (for he be so beloved in the family, that they would all have
lend he anything): and, be tell by Peter that he must not stay a
moment long in the house than be necessary to pack up his linen,
which he easily do in a very narrow compass, he take a melancholy leave
of his fellow-servant, and set out at seven in the evening.
he have proceed the length of two or three street, before he
absolutely determined with himself whether he should leave the town that
night, or, procure a lodging, wait till the morning. at last, the moon
shin very bright help he to come to a resolution of begin his
journey immediately, to which likewise he have some other inducement;
which the reader, without be a conjurer, cannot possibly guess, till
we have give he those hint which it may be now proper to open.
CHAPTER XI.
_of several new matter not expect._
it be an observation sometimes make, that to indicate our idea of a
simple fellow, we say, he be easily to be see through: nor do I believe
it a more improper denotation of a simple book. instead of apply this
to any particular performance, we chuse rather to remark the contrary in
this history, where the scene open itself by small degree; and he be a
sagacious reader who can see two chapter before he.
for this reason, we have not hitherto hint a matter which now seem
necessary to be explain; since it may be wonder at, first, that
Joseph make such extraordinary haste out of town, which hath be
already shewn; and secondly, which will be now shewn, that, instead of
proceed to the habitation of his father and mother, or to his beloved
sister Pamela, he choose rather to set out full speed to the Lady Booby's
country-seat, which he have leave on his journey to London.
be it know, then, that in the same parish where this seat stand there
live a young girl whom Joseph (though the good of son and brother)
long more impatiently to see than his parent or his sister. she be a
poor girl, who have formerly be breed up in Sir John's family; whence, a
little before the journey to London, she have be discard by Mrs
Slipslop, on account of her extraordinary beauty: for I never could find
any other reason.
this young creature (who now live with a farmer in the parish) have be
always belove by Joseph, and return his affection. she be two year
only young than our hero. they have be acquaint from their infancy,
and have conceive a very early like for each other; which have grow to
such a degree of affection, that Mr Adams have with much ado prevent
they from marry, and persuade they to wait till a few year' service
and thrift have a little improved their experience, and enable they to
live comfortably together.
they follow this good man's advice, as indeed his word be little less
than a law in his parish; for as he have show his parishioner, by an
uniform behaviour of thirty-five year' duration, that he have their good
entirely at heart, so they consult he on every occasion, and very
seldom act contrary to his opinion.
nothing can be imagine more tender than be the parting between these
two lover. a thousand sigh heave the bosom of Joseph, a thousand
tear distil from the lovely eye of Fanny (for that be her name).
though her modesty would only suffer she to admit his eager kiss, her
violent love make she more than passive in his embrace; and she often
pull he to her breast with a soft pressure, which though perhaps it
would not have squeeze an insect to death, cause more emotion in the
heart of Joseph than the close cornish hug could have do.
the reader may perhaps wonder that so fond a pair should, during a
twelvemonth's absence, never converse with one another: indeed, there
be but one reason which do or could have prevent they; and this be,
that poor Fanny could neither write nor read: nor could she be prevail
upon to transmit the delicacy of her tender and chaste passion by the
hand of an amanuensis.
they content themselves therefore with frequent inquiry after each
other's health, with a mutual confidence in each other's fidelity, and
the prospect of their future happiness.
have explain these matter to our reader, and, as far as possible,
satisfy all his doubt, we return to honest Joseph, whom we leave just
set out on his travel by the light of the moon.
those who have read any romance or poetry, antient or modern, must have
be inform that love hath wing: by which they be not to understand,
as some young lady by mistake have do, that a lover can fly; the
writer, by this ingenious allegory, intend to insinuate no more than
that lover do not march like horse-guard; in short, that they put the
good leg foremost; which our lusty youth, who could walk with any man,
do so heartily on this occasion, that within four hour he reach a
famous house of hospitality well know to the western traveller. it
present you a lion on the sign-post: and the master, who be christen
Timotheus, be commonly call plain Tim. some have conceive that he
hath particularly choose the lion for his sign, as he doth in
countenance greatly resemble that magnanimous beast, though his
disposition savour more of the sweetness of the lamb. he be a person
well receive among all sort of man, be qualified to render himself
agreeable to any; as he be well verse in history and politic, hath a
smattering in law and divinity, crack a good jest, and play
wonderfully well on the french horn.
a violent storm of hail force Joseph to take shelter in this inn, where
he remember Sir Thomas have din in his way to town. Joseph have no
soon seat himself by the kitchen fire than Timotheus, observe his
livery, begin to condole the loss of his late master; who be, he say,
his very particular and intimate acquaintance, with whom he have crack
many a merry bottle, ay many a dozen, in his time. he then remark,
that all these thing be over now, all pass, and just as if they have
never be; and conclude with an excellent observation on the certainty
of death, which his wife say be indeed very true. a fellow now arrive
at the same inn with two horse, one of which he be lead far
down into the country to meet his master; these he put into the stable,
and come and take his place by Joseph's side, who immediately know he
to be the servant of a neighbouring gentleman, who use to visit at
their house.
this fellow be likewise force in by the storm; for he have order to go
twenty mile far that evening, and luckily on the same road which
Joseph himself intend to take. he, therefore, embrace this
opportunity of compliment his friend with his master's horse
(notwithstanding he have receive express command to the contrary),
which be readily accept; and so, after they have drink a loving pot,
and the storm be over, they set out together.
CHAPTER XII.
_contain many surprizing adventure which Joseph Andrews meet with on
the road, scarce credible to those who have never travel in a
stage-coach._
nothing remarkable happen on the road till their arrival at the inn to
which the horse be order; whither they come about two in the
morning. the moon then shine very bright; and Joseph, make his friend
a present of a pint of wine, and thank he for the favour of his
horse, notwithstanding all entreaty to the contrary, proceed on his
journey on foot.
he have not go above two mile, charm with the hope of shortly see
his beloved Fanny, when he be meet by two fellow in a narrow lane, and
order to stand and deliver. he readily give they all the money he have,
which be somewhat less than two pound; and tell they he hop they
would be so generous as to return he a few shilling, to defray his
charge on his way home.
one of the ruffian answer with an oath, "yes, wewill give you
something presently: but first strip and be d---n'd to you."--"strip,"
cry the other, "or Iwill blow your brain to the devil." Joseph,
remember that he have borrow his coat and breech of a friend, and
that he should be ashamed of make any excuse for not return they,
reply, he hop they would not insist on his clothe, which be not
worth much, but consider the coldness of the night. "you be cold, be
you, you rascal?" say one of the robber: "Iwill warm you with a
vengeance;" and, damn his eye, snap a pistol at his head; which
he have no soon do than the other level a blow at he with his
stick, which Joseph, who be expert at cudgel-playing, catch with his,
and return the favour so successfully on his adversary, that he lay
he sprawl at his foot, and at the same instant receive a blow from
behind, with the butt end of a pistol, from the other villain, which
fell he to the ground, and totally deprive he of his sens.
the thief who have be knock down have now recover himself; and both
together fall to belabour poor Joseph with their stick, till they
be convince they have put an end to his miserable be: they then
strip he entirely naked, throw he into a ditch, and depart with
their booty.
the poor wretch, who lie motionless a long time, just begin to recover
his sens as a stage-coach come by. the postillion, hear a man's
groan, stopt his horse, and tell the coachman he be certain there be
a dead man lie in the ditch, for he hear he groan. "go on, sirrah,"
say the coachman; "we be confound late, and have no time to look
after dead man." a lady, who hear what the postillion say, and
likewise hear the groan, call eagerly to the coachman to stop and see
what be the matter. upon which he bid the postillion alight, and look
into the ditch. he do so, and return, "that there be a man sit
upright, as naked as ever he be bear."--"O J--sus!" cry the lady; "a
naked man! dear coachman, drive on and leave he." upon this the
gentleman get out of the coach; and Joseph beg they to have mercy
upon he: for that he have be rob and almost beat to death.
"rob!" cry an old gentleman: "let we make all the haste imaginable,
or we shall be rob too." a young man who belong to the law
answer, "he wish they have pass by without take any notice; but
that now they might be prove to have be last in his company; if he
should die they might be call to some account for his murder. he
therefore think it advisable to save the poor creature's life, for
their own sake, if possible; at least, if he die, to prevent the
jury's finding that they flee for it. he be therefore of opinion to
take the man into the coach, and carry he to the next inn." the lady
insist, "that he should not come into the coach. that if they lift
he in, she would herself alight: for she have rather stay in that place
to all eternity than ride with a naked man." the coachman object,
"that he could not suffer he to be take in unless somebody would pay a
shilling for his carriage the four mile." which the two gentleman
refuse to do. but the lawyer, who be afraid of some mischief happen
to himself, if the wretch be leave behind in that condition, say no
man could be too cautious in these matter, and that he remember very
extraordinary case in the book, threaten the coachman, and bid he
deny take he up at his peril; for that, if he die, he should be
indict for his murder; and if he live, and bring an action against
he, he would willingly take a brief in it. these word have a sensible
effect on the coachman, who be well acquainted with the person who
speak they; and the old gentleman above mention, think the naked
man would afford he frequent opportunity of show his wit to the
lady, offer to join with the company in give a mug of beer for his
fare; till, partly alarm by the threat of the one, and partly by the
promise of the other, and be perhaps a little move with compassion
at the poor creature's condition, who stand bleed and shiver with
the cold, he at length agree; and Joseph be now advance to the
coach, where, see the lady, who hold the stick of her fan before her
eye, he absolutely refuse, miserable as he be, to enter, unless he
be furnish with sufficient covering to prevent give the least
offence to decency--so perfectly modest be this young man; such mighty
effect have the spotless example of the amiable Pamela, and the
excellent sermon of Mr Adams, work upon he.
though there be several greatcoat about the coach, it be not easy to
get over this difficulty which Joseph have start. the two gentleman
complain they be cold, and could not spare a rag; the man of wit
say, with a laugh, that charity begin at home; and the coachman, who
have two greatcoat spread under he, refuse to lend either, lest they
should be make bloody: the lady's footman desire to be excuse for the
same reason, which the lady herself, notwithstanding her abhorrence of a
naked man, approve: and it be more than probable poor Joseph, who
obstinately adhere to his modest resolution, must have perish, unless
the postillion (a lad who hath be since transport for rob a
hen-roost) have voluntarily stript off a greatcoat, his only garment, at
the same time swear a great oath (for which he be rebuke by the
passenger), "that he would rather ride in his shirt all his life than
suffer a fellow-creature to lie in so miserable a condition."
Joseph, have put on the greatcoat, be lift into the coach, which
now proceed on its journey. he declare himself almost dead with the
cold, which give the man of wit an occasion to ask the lady if she could
not accommodate he with a dram. she answer, with some resentment,
"she wonder at his ask she such a question; but assure he she
never taste any such thing."
the lawyer be inquire into the circumstance of the robbery, when the
coach stopt, and one of the ruffian, put a pistol in, demand
their money of the passenger, who readily give it they; and the lady,
in her fright, deliver up a little silver bottle, of about a
half-pint size, which the rogue, clap it to his mouth, and drink
her health, declare, hold some of the good nante he have ever taste:
this the lady afterwards assure the company be the mistake of she
maid, for that she have order she to fill the bottle with
Hungary-water.
as soon as the fellow be depart, the lawyer, who have, it seem, a
case of pistol in the seat of the coach, inform the company, that if
it have be daylight, and he could have come at his pistol, he would
not have submit to the robbery: he likewise set forth that he have
often meet highwayman when he travel on horseback, but none ever durst
attack he; conclude that, if he have not be more afraid for the lady
than for himself, he should not have now part with his money
so easily.
as wit be generally observe to love to reside in empty pocket, so the
gentleman whose ingenuity we have above remark, as soon as he have
part with his money, begin to grow wonderfully facetious. he make
frequent allusion to Adam and Eve, and say many excellent thing on
fig and fig-leaf; which perhaps give more offence to Joseph than to
any other in the company.
the lawyer likewise make several very pretty jest without depart
from his profession. he say, "if Joseph and the lady be alone, he
would be more capable of make a conveyance to she, as his affair be
not fetter with any incumbrance; hewould warrant he soon suffer a
recovery by a writ of entry, which be the proper way to create heir in
tail; that, for his own part, he would engage to make so firm a
settlement in a coach, that there should be no danger of an ejectment,"
with an inundation of the like gibberish, which he continue to vent
till the coach arrive at an inn, where one servant-maid only be up, in
readiness to attend the coachman, and furnish he with cold meat and a
dram. Joseph desire to alight, and that he might have a bed prepare
for he, which the maid readily promise to perform; and, be a
good-natured wench, and not so squeamish as the lady have be, she clapt
a large fagot on the fire, and, furnish Joseph with a greatcoat
belong to one of the hostler, desire he to sit down and warm
himself whilst she make his bed. the coachman, in the meantime, take an
opportunity to call up a surgeon, who live within a few door; after
which, he remind his passenger how late they be, and, after they
have take leave of Joseph, hurry they off as fast as he could.
the wench soon get Joseph to bed, and promise to use her interest to
borrow he a shirt; but imagine, as she afterwards say, by his being
so bloody, that he must be a dead man, she run with all speed to hasten
the surgeon, who be more than half drest, apprehend that the coach
have be overturn, and some gentleman or lady hurt. as soon as the
wench have inform he at his window that it be a poor foot-passenger
who have be strip of all he have, and almost murder, he chide she
for disturb he so early, slip off his clothe again, and very
quietly return to bed and to sleep.
Aurora now begin to shew her bloom cheek over the hill, whilst ten
million of feather songster, in jocund chorus, repeat ode a
thousand time sweet than those of our laureat, and sing both the day
and the song; when the master of the inn, Mr Tow-wouse, arise, and
learn from his maid an account of the robbery, and the situation of
his poor naked guest, he shake his head, and cry, "good-lack-a-day!"
and then order the girl to carry he one of his own shirt.
Mrs Tow-wouse be just awake, and have stretch out her arm in vain to
fold her depart husband, when the maid enter the room. "whobe there?
Betty?"--"Yes, madam."--"Wherebe your master?"--"he's without, madam;
he hath send I for a shirt to lend a poor naked man, who hath be
rob and murder."--"touch one if you dare, you slut," say Mrs
Tow-wouse: "your master be a pretty sort of a man, to take in naked
vagabond, and clothe they with his own clothe. I shall have no such
doing. if you offer to touch anything, Iwill throw the chamber-pot at
your head. go, send your master to I."--"Yes, madam," answer Betty.
as soon as he come in, she thus begin: "what the devil do you mean by
this, Mr Tow-wouse? be I to buy shirt to lend to a set of scabby
rascals?"--"my dear," say Mr Tow-wouse, "this be a poor
wretch."--"Yes," say she, "I know it be a poor wretch; but what the
devil have we to do with poor wretch? the law make we provide for too
many already. we shall have thirty or forty poor wretch in red coat
shortly."--"My dear," cry Tow-wouse, "this man hath be rob of all
he hath."--"Well then," say she, "wherebe his money to pay his
reckoning? why doth not such a fellow go to an alehouse? I shall send
he pack as soon as I be up, I assure you."--"My dear," say he,
"common charity willnot suffer you to do that."--"Common charity, a f--t!"
say she, "common charity teach we to provide for ourselves and our
family; and I and mine willnot be ruin by your charity, I assure
you."--"Well," say he, "my dear, do as you will, when you be up; you
know I never contradict you."--"No," say she; "if the devil be to
contradict I, I would make the house too hot to hold he."
with such like discourse they consume near half-an-hour, whilst Betty
provide a shirt from the hostler, who be one of her sweetheart, and
put it on poor Joseph. the surgeon have likewise at last visit he, and
wash and drest his wound, and be now come to acquaint Mr Tow-wouse
that his guest be in such extreme danger of his life, that he scarce
see any hope of his recovery. "herebe a pretty kettle of fish," cry
Mrs Tow-wouse, "you have bring upon we! we be like to have a funeral
at our own expense." Tow-wouse (who, notwithstanding his charity, would
have give his vote as freely as ever he do at an election, that any
other house in the kingdom should have quiet possession of his guest)
answer, "my dear, I be not to blame; he be bring hither by the
stage-coach, and Betty have put he to bed before I be stir."--"I'll
Betty she," say she.--At which, with half her garment on, the other
half under her arm, she sally out in quest of the unfortunate Betty,
whilst Tow-wouse and the surgeon go to pay a visit to poor Joseph, and
inquire into the circumstance of this melancholy affair.
CHAPTER XIII.
_what happen to Joseph during his sickness at the inn, with the
curious discourse between he and Mr Barnabas, the parson of
the parish._
as soon as Joseph have communicate a particular history of the robbery,
together with a short account of himself, and his intended journey, he
ask the surgeon if he apprehend he to be in any danger: to which
the surgeon very honestly answer, "he fear he be; for that his
pulse be very exalted and feverish, and, if his fever should prove more
than symptomatic, it would be impossible to save he." Joseph, fetch
a deep sigh, cry, "Poor Fanny, I would I could have live to see thee!
but God's will be do."
the surgeon then advise he, if he have any worldly affair to settle,
that he would do it as soon as possible; for, though he hop he might
recover, yet he think himself oblige to acquaint he he be in great
danger; and if the malign concoction of his humour should cause a
suscitation of his fever, he might soon grow delirious and incapable to
make his will. Joseph answer, "that it be impossible for any creature
in the universe to be in a poor condition than himself; for since the
robbery he have not one thing of any kind whatever which he could call
his own." "I have," say he, "a poor little piece of gold, which they
take away, that would have be a comfort to I in all my affliction;
but surely, Fanny, I want nothing to remind I of thee. I have thy dear
image in my heart, and no villain can ever tear it thence."
Joseph desire paper and pen, to write a letter, but they be refuse
