he; and he be advise to use all his endeavour to compose himself.
they then leave he; and Mr Tow-wouse send to a clergyman to come and
administer his good office to the soul of poor Joseph, since the
surgeon despair of make any successful application to his body.
Mr Barnabas (for that be the clergyman's name) come as soon as send
for; and, have first drink a dish of tea with the landlady, and
afterwards a bowl of punch with the landlord, he walk up to the room
where Joseph lay; but, find he asleep, return to take the other
sneaker; which when he have finish, he again creep softly up to the
chamber-door, and, have open it, hear the sick man talk to
himself in the follow manner:--
"o most adorable Pamela! most virtuous sister! whose example could alone
enable I to withstand all the temptation of rich and beauty, and to
preserve my virtue pure and chaste for the arm of my dear Fanny, if it
have please Heaven that I should ever have come unto they. what rich,
or honours, or pleasure, can make we amend for the loss of innocence?
Doth not that alone afford we more consolation than all worldly
acquisition? what but innocence and virtue could give any comfort to
such a miserable wretch as I be? yet these can make I prefer this sick
and painful bed to all the pleasure I should have find in my lady's.
these can make I face death without fear; and though I love my fanny
more than ever man love a woman, these can teach I to resign myself to
the Divine will without repine. o thou delightful charming creature!
if Heaven have indulge thee to my arm, the poor, humblest state
would have be a paradise; I could have live with thee in the low
cottage without envy the palace, the dainty, or the rich of any
man breathing. but I must leave thee, leave thee for ever, my dearest
angel! I must think of another world; and I heartily pray thou may'st
meet comfort in this."--Barnabas think he have hear enough, so
downstairs he go, and tell Tow-wouse he could do his guest no service;
for that he be very light-head, and have utter nothing but a
rhapsody of nonsense all the time he stay in the room.
the surgeon return in the afternoon, and find his patient in a high
fever, as he say, than when he leave he, though not delirious; for,
notwithstanding Mr Barnabas's opinion, he have not be once out of his
sens since his arrival at the inn.
Mr Barnabas be again send for, and with much difficulty prevail on to
make another visit. as soon as he enter the room he tell Joseph "he
be come to pray by he, and to prepare he for another world: in the
first place, therefore, he hop he have repent of all his sin."
Joseph answer, "he hop he have; but there be one thing which he know
not whether he should call a sin; if it be, he fear he should die in
the commission of it; and that be, the regret of part with a young
woman whom he love as tenderly as he do his heart-string." Barnabas
bad he be assure "that any repining at the Divine will be one of the
great sin he could commit; that he ought to forget all carnal
affection, and think of well thing." Joseph say, "that neither in
this world nor the next he could forget his fanny; and that the thought,
however grievous, of part from she for ever, be not half so
torment as the fear of what she would suffer when she know his
misfortune." Barnabas say, "that such fear argue a diffidence and
despondence very criminal; that he must divest himself of all human
passion, and fix his heart above." Joseph answer, "that be what he
desire to do, and should be oblige to he if he would enable he to
accomplish it." Barnabas reply, "that must be do by grace." Joseph
beseech he to discover how he might attain it. Barnabas answer, "by
prayer and faith." he then question he concern his forgiveness of
the thief. Joseph answer, "he fear that be more than he could do;
for nothing would give he more pleasure than to hear they be
take."--"That," cry Barnabas, "be for the sake of justice."--"Yes,"
say Joseph, "but if I be to meet they again, I be afraid I should
attack they, and kill they too, if I could."--"doubtles," answer
Barnabas, "it be lawful to kill a thief; but can you say you forgive
they as a Christian ought?" Joseph desire to know what that forgiveness
be. "that be," answer Barnabas, "to forgive they as--as--it be to
forgive they as--in short, it be to forgive they as a Christian."--
Joseph reply, "he forgive they as much as he could."--"Well, well,"
say Barnabas, "that will do." he then demand of he, "if he
remember any more sin unrepente of; and if he do, he desire he to
make haste and repent of they as fast as he could, that they might
repeat over a few prayer together." Joseph answer, "he could not
recollect any great crime he have be guilty of, and that those he have
commit he be sincerely sorry for." Barnabas say that be enough,
and then proceed to prayer with all the expedition he be master of,
some company then wait for he below in the parlour, where the
ingredient for punch be all in readiness; but no one would squeeze
the orange till he come.
Joseph complain he be dry, and desire a little tea; which Barnabas
report to Mrs Tow-wouse, who answer, "she have just do drink it,
and could not be slop all day;" but order Betty to carry he up
some small beer.
Betty obey her mistress's command; but Joseph, as soon as he have
taste it, say, he fear it would increase his fever, and that he
long very much for tea; to which the good-natured Betty answer, he
should have tea, if there be any in the land; she accordingly go and
buy he some herself, and attend he with it; where we will leave
she and Joseph together for some time, to entertain the reader with
other matter.
CHAPTER XIV.
_be very full of adventure which succeed each other at the inn._
it be now the dusk of the evening, when a grave person ride into the
inn, and, commit his horse to the hostler, go directly into the
kitchen, and, have call for a pipe of tobacco, take his place by the
fireside, where several other person be likewise assemble.
the discourse run altogether on the robbery which be commit the
night before, and on the poor wretch who lie above in the dreadful
condition in which we have already see he. Mrs Tow-wouse say, "she
wonder what the devil Tom Whipwell mean by bring such guest to
her house, when there be so many alehouse on the road proper for
their reception. but she assure he, if he die, the parish should be
at the expense of the funeral." she add, "nothing would serve the
fellow's turn but tea, she would assure he." Betty, who be just
return from her charitable office, answer, she believe he be a
gentleman, for she never see a fine skin in her life. "pox on his
skin!" reply Mrs Tow-wouse, "I suppose that be all we be like to have
for the reckoning. I desire no such gentleman should ever call at the
Dragon" (which it seem be the sign of the inn).
the gentleman lately arrive discover a great deal of emotion at the
distress of this poor creature, whom he observe to be fall not into
the most compassionate hand. and indeed, if Mrs Tow-wouse have give no
utterance to the sweetness of her temper, nature have take such pain in
her countenance, that Hogarth himself never give more expression to
a picture.
her person be short, thin, and crooked. her forehead project in the
middle, and thence descend in a declivity to the top of her nose,
which be sharp and red, and would have hang over her lip, have not
nature turn up the end of it. her lip be two bit of skin, which,
whenever she speak, she draw together in a purse. her chin be peak;
and at the upper end of that skin which compose her cheek, stand two
bone, that almost hide a pair of small red eye. add to this a voice
most wonderfully adapt to the sentiment it be to convey, be both
loud and hoarse.
it be not easy to say whether the gentleman have conceive a great
dislike for his landlady or compassion for her unhappy guest. he
inquire very earnestly of the surgeon, who be now come into the
kitchen, whether he have any hope of his recovery? he beg he to use
all possible mean towards it, tell he, "it be I the duty of man of
all profession to apply their skill gratis for the relief of the poor
and necessitous." the surgeon answer, "he should take proper care; but
he defy all the surgeon in London to do he any good."--"Pray, sir,"
say the gentleman, "what be his wounds?"--"why, do you know anything
of wound?" say the surgeon (wink upon Mrs Tow-wouse).--"Sir, I have
a small smattering in surgery," answer the gentleman.--"a
smattering--ho, ho, ho!" say the surgeon; "I believe it be a
smattering indeed."
the company be all attentive, expect to hear the doctor, who be
what they call a dry fellow, expose the gentleman.
he begin therefore with an air of triumph: "I I suppose, sir, you have
travelled?"--"No, really, sir," say the gentleman.--"Ho! then you have
practise in the hospital perhaps?"--"No, sir."--"Hum! not that
neither? whence, sir, then, if I may be so bold to inquire, have you get
your knowledge in surgery?"--"sir," answer the gentleman, "I do not
pretend to much; but the little I know I have from book."--"book!"
cry the doctor. "what, I suppose you have read Galen and
Hippocrates!"--"No, sir," say the gentleman.--"How! you understand
surgery," answer the doctor, "and not read Galen and Hippocrates?"--
"Sir," cry the other, "I believe there be many surgeon who have
never read these author."--"i believe so too," say the doctor, "more
shame for they; but, thank to my education, I have they by heart, and
very seldom go without they both in my pocket."--"they be pretty large
book," say the gentleman.--"Aye," say the doctor, "I believe I know
how large they be well than you." (at which he fall a winking, and
the whole company burst into a laugh.)
the doctor pursue his triumph, ask the gentleman, "if he do not
understand physic as well as surgery." "rather well," answer the
gentleman.--"Aye, like enough," cry the doctor, with a wink. "why, I
know a little of physic too."--"I wish I know half so much," say
Tow-wouse, "Iwould never wear an apron again."--"why, I believe, landlord,"
cry the doctor, "there be few man, though I say it, within twelve
mile of the place, that handle a fever well. _Veniente accurrite
morbo_: that be my method. I suppose, brother, you understand
_latin_?"--"a little," say the gentleman.--"Aye, and greek now, Iwill
warrant you: _ton dapomibominos poluflosboio Thalasses_. but I have
almost forget these thing: I could have repeat Homer by heart
once."--"Ifags! the gentleman have catch a traytor," say Mrs Tow-wouse;
at which they all fall a laughing.
the gentleman, who have not the least affection for joking, very
contentedly suffer the doctor to enjoy his victory, which he do with
no small satisfaction; and, have sufficiently sound his depth, tell
he, "he be thoroughly convinced of his great learning and ability;
and that he would be oblige to he if he would let he know his opinion
of his patient's case above-stair."--"sir," say the doctor, "his case
be that of a dead man--the contusion on his head have perforate the
internal membrane of the occiput, and divelicate that radical small
minute invisible nerve which cohere to the pericranium; and this be
attend with a fever at first symptomatic, then pneumatic; and he be at
length grown deliriuus, or delirious, as the vulgar express it."
he be proceed in this learn manner, when a mighty noise
interrupt he. some young fellow in the neighbourhood have take one
of the thief, and be bring he into the inn. Betty run upstairs
with this news to Joseph, who beg they might search for a little
piece of broken gold, which have a ribband tie to it, and which he could
swear to amongst all the hoard of the rich man in the universe.
notwithstanding the fellow's persisting in his innocence, the mob be
very busy in search he, and presently, among other thing, pull
out the piece of gold just mention; which Betty no soon see than she
lay violent hand on it, and convey it up to Joseph, who receive it
with rapture of joy, and, hug it in his bosom, declare he could
now die content.
within a few minute afterwards come in some other fellow, with a
bundle which they have find in a ditch, and which be indeed the cloath
which have be strip off from Joseph, and the other thing they have
take from he.
the gentleman no soon see the coat than he declare he know the
livery; and, if it have be take from the poor creature above-stair,
desire he might see he; for that he be very well acquainted with the
family to whom that livery belong.
he be accordingly conduct up by Betty; but what, reader, be the
surprize on both side, when he see Joseph be the person in bed, and
when Joseph discover the face of his good friend Mr Abraham Adams!
it would be impertinent to insert a discourse which chiefly turn on
the relation of matter already well known to the reader; for, as soon
as the curate have satisfy Joseph concern the perfect health of his
Fanny, he be on his side very inquisitive into all the particular
which have produce this unfortunate accident.
to return therefore to the kitchen, where a great variety of company
be now assemble from all the room of the house, as well as the
neighbourhood: so much delight do man take in contemplate the
countenance of a thief.
Mr Tow-wouse begin to rub his hand with pleasure at see so large an
assembly; who would, he hop, shortly adjourn into several apartment,
in order to discourse over the robbery, and drink a health to all honest
man. but Mrs Tow-wouse, whose misfortune it be commonly to see thing a
little perversely, begin to rail at those who bring the fellow into
her house; tell her husband, "they be very likely to thrive who
keep a house of entertainment for beggar and thief."
the mob have now finish their search, and could find nothing about the
captive likely to prove any evidence; for as to the cloath, though the
mob be very well satisfied with that proof, yet, as the surgeon
observe, they could not convict he, because they be not find in his
custody; to which Barnabas agree, and add that these be _bona
waviata_, and belong to the lord of the manor.
"how," say the surgeon, "do you say these good belong to the lord of
the manor?"--"i do," cry Barnabas.--"Then I deny it," say the
surgeon: "what can the lord of the manor have to do in the case? will
any one attempt to persuade I that what a man find be not his
own?"--"i have hear," say an old fellow in the corner, "justice
Wise-one say, that, if every man have his right, whatever be find
belong to the king of London."--"That may be true," say Barnabas, "in
some sense; for the law make a difference between thing steal and
thing find; for a thing may be steal that never be find, and a thing
may be find that never be steal: now, good that be both steal and
find be _waviata_; and they belong to the lord of the manor."--"So the
lord of the manor be the receiver of steal good," say the doctor; at
which there be an universal laugh, be first begin by himself.
while the prisoner, by persist in his innocence, have almost (as there
be no evidence against he) bring over Barnabas, the surgeon,
Tow-wouse, and several other to his side, Betty inform they that they
have overlook a little piece of gold, which she have carry up to the
man in bed, and which he offer to swear to amongst a million, aye,
amongst ten thousand. this immediately turn the scale against the
prisoner, and every one now conclude he guilty. it be resolve,
therefore, to keep he secure that night, and early in the morning to
carry he before a justice.
CHAPTER XV.
_show how Mrs Tow-wouse be a little mollify; and how officious Mr
Barnabas and the surgeon be to prosecute the thief: with a
dissertation account for their zeal, and that of many other person
not mention in this history._
Betty tell her mistress she believe the man in bed be a great man
than they take he for; for, besides the extreme whiteness of his skin,
and the softness of his hand, she observe a very great familiarity
between the gentleman and he; and add, she be certain they be
intimate acquaintance, if not relation.
this somewhat abate the severity of Mrs Tow-wouse's countenance. she
say, "God forbid she should not discharge the duty of a Christian,
since the poor gentleman be bring to her house. she have a natural
antipathy to vagabond; but could pity the misfortune of a Christian
as soon as another." Tow-wouse say, "if the traveller be a gentleman,
though he hath no money about he now, we shall most likely be pay
hereafter; so you may begin to score whenever you will." Mrs Tow-wouse
answer, "hold your simple tongue, and donot instruct I in my
business. I be sure I be sorry for the gentleman's misfortune with all
my heart; and I hope the villain who hath use he so barbarously will
be hang. Betty, go see what he want. God forbid he should want
anything in my house."
Barnabas and the surgeon go up to Joseph to satisfy themselves
concern the piece of gold; Joseph be with difficulty prevail upon
to show it they, but would by no entreaty be bring to deliver it out
of his own possession. he however attest this to be the same which have
be take from he, and Betty be ready to swear to the finding it on
the thief.
the only difficulty that remain be, how to produce this gold before
the justice; for as to carry Joseph himself, it seem impossible;
nor be there any great likelihood of obtain it from he, for he have
fasten it with a ribband to his arm, and solemnly vow that nothing
but irresistible force should ever separate they; in which resolution,
Mr Adams, clench a fist rather less than the knuckle of an ox,
declare he would support he.
a dispute arise on this occasion concern evidence not very necessary
to be relate here; after which the surgeon dress Mr Joseph's head,
still persist in the imminent danger in which his patient lay, but
concluding, with a very important look, "that he begin to have some
hope; that he should send he a sanative soporiferous draught, and
would see he in the morning." after which Barnabas and he depart, and
leave Mr Joseph and Mr Adams together.
Adams inform Joseph of the occasion of this journey which he be
make to London, namely, to publish three volume of sermon; be
encourage, as he say, by an advertisement lately set forth by the
society of bookseller, who propose to purchase any copy offer to
they, at a price to be settle by two person; but though he imagine he
should get a considerable sum of money on this occasion, which his
family be in urgent need of, he protest he would not leave Joseph in
his present condition: finally, he tell he, "he have nine shilling and
threepence halfpenny in his pocket, which he be welcome to use as
he please."
this goodness of parson Adams bring tear into Joseph's eye; he
declare, "he have now a second reason to desire life, that he might show
his gratitude to such a friend." Adams bid he "be cheerful; for that
he plainly see the surgeon, besides his ignorance, desire to make a
merit of cure he, though the wound in his head, he perceive, be
by no mean dangerous; that he be convince he have no fever, and
doubt not but he would be able to travel in a day or two."
these word infuse a spirit into Joseph; he say, "he find himself
very sore from the bruise, but have no reason to think any of his bone
injure, or that he have receive any harm in his inside, unless that he
feel something very odd in his stomach; but he know not whether that
might not arise from not have eat one morsel for above twenty-four
hour." be then ask if he have any inclination to eat, he answer
in the affirmative. then parson Adams desire he to "name what he have
the great fancy for; whether a poached egg, or chicken-broth." he
answer, "he could eat both very well; but that he seem to have the
great appetite for a piece of boil beef and cabbage."
Adams be pleased with so perfect a confirmation that he have not the
least fever, but advise he to a light diet for that evening. he
accordingly eat either a rabbit or a fowl, I never could with any
tolerable certainty discover which; after this he be, by Mrs
Tow-wouse's order, convey into a well bed and equip with one of
her husband's shirt.
in the morning early, Barnabas and the surgeon come to the inn, in order
to see the thief convey before the justice. they have consume the
whole night in debate what measure they should take to produce the
piece of gold in evidence against he; for they be both extremely
zealous in the business, though neither of they be in the least
interested in the prosecution; neither of they have ever receive any
private injury from the fellow, nor have either of they ever be
suspect of love the publick well enough to give they a sermon or a
dose of physic for nothing.
to help our reader, therefore, as much as possible to account for this
zeal, we must inform he that, as this parish be so unfortunate as to
have no lawyer in it, there have be a constant contention between the
two doctor, spiritual and physical, concern their ability in a
science, in which, as neither of they profess it, they have equal
pretension to dispute each other's opinion. these dispute be
carry on with great contempt on both side, and have almost divide the
parish; Mr Tow-wouse and one half of the neighbour incline to the
surgeon, and Mrs Tow-wouse with the other half to the parson. the
surgeon draw his knowledge from those inestimable fountain, call the
Attorney's Pocket Companion, and Mr Jacob's Law-Tables; Barnabas trust
entirely to Wood's Institutes. it happen on this occasion, as be
pretty frequently the case, that these two learn man differ about
the sufficiency of evidence; the doctor be of opinion that the maid's
oath would convict the prisoner without produce the gold; the parson,
_e contra, totis viribus._ to display their part, therefore, before
the justice and the parish, be the sole motive which we can discover to
this zeal which both of they pretend to have for public justice.
o vanity! how little be thy force acknowledge, or thy operation
discern! how wantonly dost thou deceive mankind under different
disguise! sometimes thou dost wear the face of pity, sometimes of
generosity: nay, thou hast the assurance even to put on those glorious
ornament which belong only to heroic virtue. Thou odious, deform
monster! whom priest have rail at, philosopher despise, and poet
ridicule; be there a wretch so abandon as to own thee for an
acquaintance in public?--yet, how few will refuse to enjoy thee in
private? nay, thou art the pursuit of most man through their life. the
great villainy be daily practise to please thee; nor be the
mean thief below, or the great hero above, thy notice. Thy
embrace be often the sole aim and sole reward of the private robbery
and the plunder province. it be to pamper up thee, thou harlot, that
we attempt to withdraw from other what we do not want, or to withhold
from they what they do. all our passion be thy slave. Avarice itself
be often no more than thy handmaid, and even Lust thy pimp. the bully
fear, like a coward, fly before thee, and Joy and Grief hide their
head in thy presence.
I know thou wilt think that whilst I abuse thee I court thee, and that
thy love hath inspire I to write this sarcastical panegyric on thee;
but thou art deceive: I value thee not of a farthing; nor will it give
I any pain if thou shouldst prevail on the reader to censure this
digression as arrant nonsense; for know, to thy confusion, that I have
introduce thee for no other purpose than to lengthen out a short
chapter, and so I return to my history.
CHAPTER XVI.
_the escape of the thief. Mr Adams's disappointment. the arrival of
two very extraordinary personage, and the introduction of parson Adams
to parson Barnabas._
Barnabas and the surgeon, be return, as we have say, to the inn,
in order to convey the thief before the justice, be greatly concern
to find a small accident have happen, which somewhat disconcert they;
and this be no other than the thief's escape, who have modestly
withdraw himself by night, decline all ostentation, and not chuse,
in imitation of some great man, to distinguish himself at the expense of
be point at.
when the company have retire the evening before, the thief be detain
in a room where the constable, and one of the young fellow who take
he, be plant as his guard. about the second watch a general
complaint of drought be make, both by the prisoner and his keeper.
among whom it be at last agree that the constable should remain on
duty, and the young fellow call up the tapster; in which disposition the
latter apprehend not the least danger, as the constable be well
armed, and could besides easily summon he back to his assistance, if
the prisoner make the least attempt to gain his liberty.
the young fellow have not long leave the room before it come into the
constable's head that the prisoner might leap on he by surprize, and,
thereby prevent he of the use of his weapon, especially the long
staff in which he chiefly confide, might reduce the success of a
struggle to a equal chance. he wisely, therefore, to prevent this
inconvenience, slipt out of the room himself, and lock the door,
wait without with his staff in his hand, ready lift to fall the
unhappy prisoner, if by ill fortune he should attempt to break out.
but human life, as hath be discover by some great man or other (for
I would by no mean be understand to affect the honour of make any
such discovery), very much resemble a game at chess; for as in the
latter, while a gamester be too attentive to secure himself very
strongly on one side the board, he be apt to leave an unguarded opening
on the other; so doth it often happen in life, and so do it happen on
this occasion; for whilst the cautious constable with such wonderful
sagacity have possess himself of the door, he most unhappily forget
the window.
the thief, who play on the other side, no soon perceive this
opening than he begin to move that way; and, find the passage easy,
he take with he the young fellow's hat, and without any ceremony
step into the street and make the good of his way.
the young fellow, return with a double mug of strong beer, be a
little surprized to find the constable at the door; but much more so
when, the door be open, he perceive the prisoner have make his
escape, and which way. he throw down the beer, and, without utter
anything to the constable except a hearty curse or two, he nimbly leapt
out of the window, and go again in pursuit of his prey, be very
unwilling to lose the reward which he have assure himself of.
the constable hath not be discharge of suspicion on this account; it
hath be say that, not be concern in the take the thief, he
could not have be entitle to any part of the reward if he have be
convict; that the thief have several guineas in his pocket; that it be
very unlikely he should have be guilty of such an oversight; that his
pretence for leave the room be absurd; that it be his constant
maxim, that a wise man never refuse money on any condition; that at
every election he always have sell his vote to both party, &c.
but, notwithstanding these and many other such allegation, I be
sufficiently convinced of his innocence; have be positively assure
of it by those who receive their information from his own mouth;
which, in the opinion of some modern, be the good and indeed
only evidence.
all the family be now up, and with many other assemble in the
kitchen, where Mr Tow-wouse be in some tribulation; the surgeon have
declare that by law he be liable to be indict for the thief's
escape, as it be out of his house; he be a little comfort, however,
by Mr Barnabas's opinion, that as the escape be by night the indictment
would not lie.
Mrs Tow-wouse deliver herself in the following word: "sure never be
such a fool as my husband; would any other person live have leave a man
in the custody of such a drunken drowsy blockhead as Tom Suckbribe?"
(which be the constable's name); "and if he could be indict without
any harm to his wife and child, I should be glad of it." (then the
bell rung in Joseph's room.) "why Betty, John, Chamberlain, where the
devil be you all? have you no ear, or no conscience, not to tend the
sick well? see what the gentleman want. why donot you go yourself, Mr
Tow-wouse? but any one may die for you; you have no more feeling than a
deal board. if a man live a fortnight in your house without spend a
penny, you would never put he in mind of it. see whether he drink tea
or coffee for breakfast." "yes, my dear," cry Tow-wouse. she then
ask the doctor and Mr Barnabas what morning's draught they choose, who
answer, they have a pot of cyder-and at the fire; which we will leave
they merry over, and return to Joseph.
he have rise pretty early this morning; but, though his wound be far
from threaten any danger, he be so sore with the bruise, that it
be impossible for he to think of undertake a journey yet; Mr Adams,
therefore, whose stock be visibly decrease with the expense of supper
and breakfast, and which could not survive that day's scoring, begin to
consider how it be possible to recruit it. at last he cry, "he have
luckily hit on a sure method, and, though it would oblige he to return
himself home together with Joseph, it matter not much." he then send
for Tow-wouse, and, take he into another room, tell he "he want to
borrow three guineas, for which he would put ample security into his
hand." Tow-wouse, who expect a watch, or ring, or something of double
the value, answer, "he believe he could furnish he." upon which
Adams, point to his saddle-bag, tell he, with a face and voice full
of solemnity, "that there be in that bag no less than nine volume of
manuscript sermon, as well worth a hundred pound as a shilling be
worth twelve penny, and that he would deposit one of the volume in his
hand by way of pledge; not doubt but that he would have the honesty
to return it on his repayment of the money; for otherwise he must be a
very great loser, see that every volume would at least bring he ten
pound, as he have be inform by a neighbouring clergyman in the
country; for," say he, "as to my own part, have never yet deal in
printing, I do not pretend to ascertain the exact value of such thing."
Tow-wouse, who be a little surprized at the pawn, say (and not without
some truth), "that he be no judge of the price of such kind of good;
and as for money, he really be very short." Adams answer, "certainly
he would not scruple to lend he three guineas on what be undoubtedly
worth at least ten." the landlord reply, "he do not believe he have
so much money in the house, and besides, he be to make up a sum. he be
very confident the book be of much high value, and heartily sorry
it do not suit he." he then cry out, "come sir!" though nobody
call; and run downstairs without any fear of break his neck.
Poor Adams be extremely deject at this disappointment, nor know he
what further stratagem to try. he immediately apply to his pipe, his
constant friend and comfort in his affliction; and, lean over the
rail, he devote himself to meditation, assist by the inspire fume
of tobacco.
he have on a nightcap draw over his wig, and a short greatcoat, which
half cover his cassock--a dress which, add to something comical
enough in his countenance, compose a figure likely to attract the eye
of those who be not over give to observation.
whilst he be smoake his pipe in this posture, a coach and six, with a
numerous attendance, drive into the inn. there alight from the coach a
young fellow and a brace of pointer, after which another young fellow
leapt from the box, and shake the former by the hand; and both, together
with the dog, be instantly conduct by Mr Tow-wouse into an
apartment; whither as they pass, they entertain themselves with the
follow short facetious dialogue:--
"you be a pretty fellow for a coachman, Jack!" say he from the coach;
"you have almost overturn we just now."--"Pox take you!" say the
coachman; "if I have only break your neck, it would have be save
somebody else the trouble; but I should have be sorry for the
pointer."--"why, you son of a b--," answer the other, "if nobody
could shoot well than you, the pointer would be of no use."--"D--n
I," say the coachman, "I will shoot with you five guineas a
shot."--"you be hang," say the other; "for five guineas you shall
shoot at my a--."--"done," say the coachman; "Iwill pepper you well
than ever you be pepper by Jenny Bouncer."--"pepper your
grandmother," say the other: "here's Tow-wouse will let you shoot at
he for a shilling a time."--"i know his honour well," cry
Tow-wouse; "I never see a surer shoot at a partridge. every man miss
now and then; but if I could shoot half as well as his honour, I would
desire no well livelihood than I could get by my gun."--"Pox on you,"
say the coachman, "you demolish more game now than your head's worth.
therebe a bitch, Tow-wouse: by g-- she never blinked[a] a bird in her
life."--"i have a puppy, not a year old, shall hunt with she for a
hundred," cry the other gentleman.--"done," say the coachman: "but
you will be pox'd before you make the bett."--"if you have a mind for a
bett," cry the coachman, "I will match my spot dog with your white
bitch for a hundred, play or pay."--"Done," say the other: "and Iwill
run Baldface against Slouch with you for another."--"No," cry he from
the box; "but Iwill venture Miss Jenny against Baldface, or Hannibal
either."--"Go to the devil," cry he from the coach: "I will make every
bett your own way, to be sure! I will match Hannibal with Slouch for a
thousand, if you dare; and I say do first."
[Footnote a:
to blink be a term use to signify the dog's passing by a bird without
pointing at it.]
they be now arrive; and the reader will be very contented to leave
they, and repair to the kitchen; where Barnabas, the surgeon, and an
exciseman be smoake their pipe over some cyder-and; and where the
servant, who attend the two noble gentleman we have just see alight,
be now arrive.
"Tom," cry one of the footman, "therebe parson Adams smoake his
pipe in the gallery."--"Yes," say Tom; "I pull off my hat to he, and
the parson speak to I."
"be the gentleman a clergyman, then?" say Barnabas (for his cassock have
be tie up when he arrive). "yes, sir," answer the footman; "and
one there be but few like."--"Aye," say Barnabas; "if I have know it
soon, I should have desire his company; I would always shew a proper
respect for the cloth: but what say you, doctor, shall we adjourn into a
room, and invite he to take part of a bowl of punch?"
this proposal be immediately agree to and execute; and parson Adams
accept the invitation, much civility pass between the two
clergyman, who both declare the great honour they have for the cloth.
they have not be long together before they enter into a discourse on
small tithe, which continue a full hour, without the doctor or
exciseman's have one opportunity to offer a word.
it be then propose to begin a general conversation, and the exciseman
open on foreign affair; but a word unluckily drop from one of
they introduce a dissertation on the hardship suffer by the inferior
clergy; which, after a long duration, conclude with bring the nine
volume of sermon on the carpet.
Barnabas greatly discourage poor Adams; he say, "the age be so
wicked, that nobody read sermon: would you think it, Mr Adams?" say
he, "I once intend to print a volume of sermon myself, and they have
the approbation of two or three bishop; but what do you think a
bookseller offer me?"--"Twelve guineas perhaps," cry Adams.--"Not
twelve penny, I assure you," answer Barnabas: "nay, the dog refuse I
a Concordance in exchange. at last I offer to give he the printing
they, for the sake of dedicate they to that very gentleman who just
now drive his own coach into the inn; and, I assure you, he have the
impudence to refuse my offer; by which mean I lose a good living, that
be afterwards give away in exchange for a pointer, to one who--but I
will not say anything against the cloth. so you may guess, Mr Adams,
what you be to expect; for if sermon would have go down, I
believe--I will not be vain; but to be concise with you, three bishop
say they be the good that ever be writ: but indeed there be a
pretty moderate number print already, and not all sell yet."--"Pray,
sir," say Adams, "to what do you think the number may amount?"--"sir,"
answer Barnabas, "a bookseller tell I, he believe five thousand
volume at least."--"five thousand?" quoth the surgeon: "what can they
be writ upon? I remember when I be a boy, I use to read one
Tillotson's sermon; and, I be sure, if a man practise half so much as
be in one of those sermon, he will go to heaven."--"doctor," cry
Barnabas, "you have a prophane way of talk, for which I must reprove
you. a man can never have his duty too frequently inculcate into he.
and as for Tillotson, to be sure he be a good writer, and say thing
very well; but comparison be odious; another man may write as well as
he--I believe there be some of my sermons,"--and then he apply the
candle to his pipe.--"And I believe there be some of my discourse,"
cry Adams, "which the bishop would not think totally unworthy of
be print; and I have be inform I might procure a very large sum
(indeed an immense one) on they."--"I doubt that," answer Barnabas:
"however, if you desire to make some money of they, perhaps you may sell
they by advertise the manuscript sermon of a clergyman lately
deceased, all warrant original, and never print. and now I think of
it, I should be oblige to you, if there be ever a funeral one among
they, to lend it I; for I be this very day to preach a funeral sermon,
for which I have not pen a line, though I be to have a double
price."--adam answer, "he have but one, which he fear would not
serve his purpose, be sacred to the memory of a magistrate, who have
exert himself very singularly in the preservation of the morality of
his neighbour, insomuch that he have neither alehouse nor lewd woman in
the parish where he live."--"No," reply Barnabas, "that will not do
quite so well; for the deceased, upon whose virtue I be to harangue,
be a little too much addict to liquor, and publickly keep a
mistress.--i believe I must take a common sermon, and trust to my memory
to introduce something handsome on he."--"To your invention rather,"
say the doctor: "your memory will be apter to put you out; for no man
living remember anything good of he."
with such kind of spiritual discourse, they empty the bowl of punch,
pay their reckoning, and separate: Adams and the doctor go up to
Joseph, parson Barnabas depart to celebrate the aforesaid deceased,
and the exciseman descend into the cellar to gauge the vessel.
Joseph be now ready to sit down to a loin of mutton, and wait for Mr
Adams, when he and the doctor come in. the doctor, have feel his pulse
and examine his wound, declare he much well, which he impute to
that sanative soporiferous draught, a medicine "whose virtue," he say,
"be never to be sufficiently extoll." and great indeed they must be,
if Joseph be so much indebted to they as the doctor imagine; since
nothing more than those effluvia which escape the cork could have
contribute to his recovery; for the medicine have stand untouched in the
window ever since its arrival.
Joseph pass that day, and the three following, with his friend Adams,
in which nothing so remarkable happen as the swift progress of his
recovery. as he have an excellent habit of body, his wound be now
almost heal; and his bruise give he so little uneasiness, that he
press Mr Adams to let he depart; tell he he should never be able to
return sufficient thank for all his favour, but beg that he might
no long delay his journey to London.
Adams, notwithstanding the ignorance, as he conceive it, of Mr
Tow-wouse, and the envy (for such he think it) of Mr Barnabas, have
great expectation from his sermon: see therefore Joseph in so good
a way, he tell he he would agree to his set out the next morning in
the stage-coach, that he believe he should have sufficient, after the
reckoning pay, to procure he one day's conveyance in it, and
afterwards he would be able to get on on foot, or might be favour with
a lift in some neighbour's waggon, especially as there be then to be a
fair in the town whither the coach would carry he, to which number
from his parish resort--and as to himself, he agree to proceed to the
great city.
they be now walk in the inn-yard, when a fat, fair, short person
ride in, and, alight from his horse, go directly up to Barnabas,
who be smoake his pipe on a bench. the parson and the stranger shake
one another very lovingly by the hand, and go into a room together.
the evening now come on, Joseph retire to his chamber, whither the
good Adams accompany he, and take this opportunity to expatiate on
the great mercy God have lately show he, of which he ought not only
to have the deep inward sense, but likewise to express outward
thankfulness for they. they therefore fall both on their knee, and
spend a considerable time in prayer and thanksgiving.
they have just finish when Betty come in and tell Mr Adams Mr Barnabas
desire to speak to he on some business of consequence below-stair.
Joseph desire, if it be likely to detain he long, he would let he
know it, that he might go to bed, which Adams promise, and in that case
they wish one another good-night.
CHAPTER XVII.
_a pleasant discourse between the two parson and the bookseller, 'which
be break off by an unlucky accident happen in the inn, which
produce a dialogue between Mrs Tow-wouse and her maid of no
gentle kind._
as soon as Adams come into the room, Mr Barnabas introduce he to the
stranger, who be, he tell he, a bookseller, and would be as likely to
deal with he for his sermon as any man whatever. Adams, salute the
stranger, answer Barnabas, that he be very much oblige to he; that
nothing could be more convenient, for he have no other business to the
great city, and be heartily desirous of return with the young man,
who be just recover of his misfortune. he then snapt his finger (as
be usual with he), and take two or three turn about the room in an
extasy. and to induce the bookseller to be as expeditious as possible,
as likewise to offer he a well price for his commodity, he assure
they their meeting be extremely lucky to himself; for that he have the
most pressing occasion for money at that time, his own be almost
spend, and have a friend then in the same inn, who be just recover
from some wound he have receive from robber, and be in a most
indigent condition. "so that nothing," say he, "could be so opportune
for the supply both our necessity as my make an immediate bargain
with you."
as soon as he have seat himself, the stranger begin in these word:
"Sir, I do not care absolutely to deny engage in what my friend Mr
Barnabas recommend; but sermon be mere drug. the trade be so vastly
stock with they, that really, unless they come out with the name of
Whitefield or Wesley, or some other such great man, as a bishop, or
those sort of people, I donot care to touch; unless now it be a sermon
preach on the 30th of January; or we could say in the title-page,
publish at the earnest request of the congregation, or the
inhabitant; but, truly, for a dry piece of sermon, I have rather be
excuse; especially as my hand be so full at present. however, sir, as
Mr Barnabas mention they to I, I will, if you please, take the
manuscript with I to town, and send you my opinion of it in a very
short time."
"oh!" say Adams, "if you desire it, I will read two or three discourse
as a speciman." this Barnabas, who love sermon no well than a grocer
doth fig, immediately object to, and advise Adams to let the
bookseller have his sermon: tell he, "if he give he a direction,
he might be certain of a speedy answer;" add, he need not scruple
trust they in his possession. "no," say the bookseller, "if it be a
play that have be act twenty night together, I believe it would
be safe."
Adams do not at all relish the last expression; he say "he be sorry
to hear sermon compare to play." "not by I, I assure you," cry the
bookseller, "though I donot know whether the licensing act may not
shortly bring they to the same footing; but I have formerly know a
hundred guineas give for a play."--"more shame for those who give it,"
cry Barnabas.--"Why so?" say the bookseller, "for they get hundred
by it."--"But be there no difference between convey good or ill
instruction to mankind?" say Adams: "would not an honest mind rather
lose money by the one, than gain it by the other?"--"if you can find any
such, I will not be their hindrance," answer the bookseller; "but I
think those person who get by preach sermon be the proper to
lose by print they: for my part, the copy that sell good will be
always the good copy in my opinion; I be no enemy to sermon, but
because they donot sell: for I would as soon print one of Whitefield's
as any farce whatever."
"whoever print such heterodox stuff ought to be hang," say Barnabas.
"Sir," say he, turn to Adams, "this fellow's writing (I know not
whether you have see they) be level at the clergy. he would reduce
we to the example of the primitive age, forsooth! and would insinuate
to the people that a clergyman ought to be always preach and pray.
he pretend to understand the scripture literally; and would make
mankind believe that the poverty and low estate which be recommend to
the Church in its infancy, and be only temporary doctrine adapt to
she under persecution, be to be preserve in her flourishing and
establish state. Sir, the principle of Toland, Woolston, and all the
freethinker, be not calculate to do half the mischief, as those
profess by this fellow and his follower."
"Sir," answer Adams, "if Mr Whitefield have carry his doctrine no
far than you mention, I should have remain, as I once be, his
well-wisher. I be, myself, as great an enemy to the luxury and splendour
of the clergy as he can be. I do not, more than he, by the flourish
estate of the Church, understand the palace, equipage, dress,
furniture, rich dainty, and vast fortune, of her minister. surely
those thing, which savour so strongly of this world, become not the
servant of one who profess his kingdom be not of it. but when he
begin to call nonsense and enthusiasm to his aid, and set up the
detestable doctrine of faith against good work, I be his friend no
long; for surely that doctrine be coin in hell; and one would think
none but the devil himself could have the confidence to preach it. for
can anything be more derogatory to the honour of God than for man to
imagine that the all-wise being will hereafter say to the good and
virtuous, 'notwithstanding the purity of thy life, notwithstanding that
constant rule of virtue and goodness in which you walk upon earth,
still, as thou didst not believe everything in the true orthodox manner,
thy want of faith shall condemn thee?' or, on the other side, can any
doctrine have a more pernicious influence on society, than a persuasion
that it will be a good plea for the villain at the last day--'lord, it
be true I never obey one of thy commandment, yet punish I not, for I
believe they all?'"--"i suppose, sir," say the bookseller, "your
sermon be of a different kind."--"Aye, sir," say Adams; "the
contrary, I thank Heaven, be inculcate in almost every page, or I
should belye my own opinion, which hath always be, that a virtuous and
good Turk, or heathen, be more acceptable in the sight of their creator
than a vicious and wicked Christian, though his faith be as perfectly
orthodox as St Paul's himself."--"i wish you success," say the
bookseller, "but must beg to be excuse, as my hand be so very full at
present; and, indeed, I be afraid you will find a backwardness in the
trade to engage in a book which the clergy would be certain to cry
down."--"God forbid," say Adams, "any book should be propagate which
the clergy would cry down; but if you mean by the clergy, some few
design factious man, who have it at heart to establish some favourite
scheme at the price of the liberty of mankind, and the very essence of
religion, it be not in the power of such person to decry any book they
please; witness that excellent book call, 'a Plain Account of the
Nature and end of the sacrament;' a book write (if I may venture on
the expression) with the pen of an angel, and calculate to restore the
true use of Christianity, and of that sacred institution; for what could
tend more to the noble purpose of religion than frequent chearful
meeting among the member of a society, in which they should, in the
presence of one another, and in the service of the Supreme Being, make
promise of be good, friendly, and benevolent to each other? now,
this excellent book be attack by a party, but unsuccessfully." at
these word Barnabas fall a-ring with all the violence imaginable;
upon which a servant attend, he bid he "bring a bill immediately;
for that he be in company, for aught he know, with the devil himself;
and he expect to hear the Alcoran, the Leviathan, or Woolston
commend, if he staid a few minute long." Adams desire, "as he be
so much move at his mention a book which he do without apprehend
any possibility of offence, that he would be so kind to propose any
objection he have to it, which he would endeavour to answer."--"I
propose objection!" say Barnabas, "I never read a syllable in any such
wicked book; I never see it in my life, I assure you."--adam be go
to answer, when a most hideous uproar begin in the inn. Mrs Tow-wouse,
Mr Tow-wouse, and Betty, all lift up their voice together; but Mrs
Tow-wouse's voice, like a bass viol in a concert, be clearly and
distinctly distinguished among the rest, and be hear to articulate the
following sounds:--"O you damn'd villain! be this the return to all the
care I have take of your family? this the reward of my virtue? be this
the manner in which you behave to one who bring you a fortune, and
prefer you to so many match, all your better? to abuse my bed, my
own bed, with my own servant! but Iwill maul the slut, Iwill tear her
nasty eye out! be ever such a pitiful dog, to take up with such a mean
trollop? if she have be a gentlewoman, like myself, it have be some
excuse; but a beggarly, saucy, dirty servant-maid. get you out of my
house, you whore." to which she add another name, which we do not care
to stain our paper with. it be a monosyllable beginning with a b--, and
indeed be the same as if she have pronounce the word, she-dog. which
term we shall, to avoid offence, use on this occasion, though indeed
both the mistress and maid utter the above-mention b--, a word
extremely disgustful to female of the low sort. Betty have bear all
hitherto with patience, and have utter only lamentation; but the last
appellation sting she to the quick. "I be a woman as well as yourself,"
she roar out, "and no she-dog; and if I have be a little naughty, I
be not the first; if I have be no well than I should be," cry she,
sobbing, "thatbe no reason you should call I out of my name; my
be-better be wo-rse than I."--"Huzzy, huzzy," say Mrs Tow-wouse,
"have you the impudence to answer I? do I not catch you, you
saucy"--and then again repeat the terrible word so odious to female
ear. "I cannot bear that name," answer Betty: "if I have be wicked,
I be to answer for it myself in the other world; but I have do nothing
thatbe unnatural; and I will go out of your house this moment, for I
will never be call she-dog by any mistress in England." Mrs Tow-wouse
then arm herself with the spit, but be prevent from execute any
dreadful purpose by Mr Adams, who confine her arm with the strength
of a wrist which Hercules would not have be ashamed of. Mr Tow-wouse,
be catch, as our lawyer express it, with the manner, and have no
defence to make, very prudently withdraw himself; and Betty commit
herself to the protection of the hostler, who, though she could not
conceive he pleased with what have happen, be, in her opinion, rather
a gentle beast than her mistress.
Mrs Tow-wouse, at the intercession of Mr Adams, and find the enemy
vanish, begin to compose herself, and at length recover the usual
serenity of her temper, in which we will leave she, to open to the
reader the step which lead to a catastrophe, common enough, and comical
enough too perhaps, in modern history, yet often fatal to the repose and
well-being of family, and the subject of many tragedy, both in life
and on the stage.
CHAPTER XVIII.
_the history of Betty the chambermaid, and an account of what occasion
the violent scene in the precede chapter._
Betty, who be the occasion of all this hurry, have some good quality.
she have good-nature, generosity, and compassion, but unfortunately, her
constitution be compose of those warm ingredient which, though the
purity of court or nunnery might have happily controule they, be
by no mean able to endure the ticklish situation of a chambermaid at an
inn; who be daily liable to the solicitation of lover of all
complexion; to the dangerous addresse of fine gentleman of the army,
who sometimes be oblige to reside with they a whole year together;
and, above all, be expose to the caress of footman, stage-coachman,
and drawer; all of whom employ the whole artillery of kissing,
flattering, bribe, and every other weapon which be to be find in the
whole armoury of love, against they.
Betty, who be but one-and-twenty, have now live three year in this
dangerous situation, during which she have escape pretty well. an ensign
of foot be the first person who make an impression on her heart; he do
indeed raise a flame in she which require the care of a surgeon
to cool.
while she burn for he, several other burn for she. officer of the
army, young gentleman travel the western circuit, inoffensive
squire, and some of graver character, be set a-fire by her charm!
at length, have perfectly recover the effect of her first unhappy
passion, she seem to have vow a state of perpetual chastity. she be
long deaf to all the suffering of her lover, till one day, at a
neighbouring fair, the rhetoric of John the hostler, with a new straw
hat and a pint of wine, make a second conquest over she.
she do not, however, feel any of those flame on this occasion which
have be the consequence of her former amour; nor, indeed, those other
ill effect which prudent young woman very justly apprehend from too
absolute an indulgence to the press endearment of their lover. this
latter, perhaps, be a little owe to she not be entirely constant
to John, with whom she permit Tom Whipwell the stage-coachman, and
now and then a handsome young traveller, to share she favour.
Mr Tow-wouse have for some time cast the languish eye of affection on
this young maiden. he have lay hold on every opportunity of say
tender thing to she, squeeze she by the hand, and sometimes kiss
her lip; for, as the violence of his passion have considerably abate to
Mrs Tow-wouse, so, like water, which be stopt from its usual current in
one place, it naturally seek a vent in another. Mrs Tow-wouse be
think to have perceive this abatement, and, probably, it add very
little to the natural sweetness of her temper; for though she be as
true to her husband as the dial to the sun, she be rather more desirous
of be shine on, as be more capable of feel his warmth.
ever since Joseph's arrival, Betty have conceive an extraordinary liking
to he, which discover itself more and more as he grow well and
well; till that fatal evening, when, as she be warm his bed, her
passion grow to such a height, and so perfectly master both her
modesty and her reason, that, after many fruitless hint and sly
insinuation, she at last throw down the warming-pan, and, embrace he
with great eagerness, swear he be the handsome creature she have
ever see.
Joseph, in great confusion, leap from she, and tell she he be sorry to
see a young woman cast off all regard to modesty; but she have go too
far to recede, and grow so very indecent, that Joseph be oblige,
contrary to his inclination, to use some violence to she; and, take
she in his arm, he shut she out of the room, and lock the door.
how ought man to rejoice that his chastity be always in his own power;
that, if he hath sufficient strength of mind, he hath always a competent
strength of body to defend himself, and cannot, like a poor weak woman,
be ravish against his will!
Betty be in the most violent agitation at this disappointment. rage and
lust pull her heart, as with two string, two different way; one
moment she think of stab Joseph; the next, of take he in her
arm, and devour he with kiss; but the latter passion be far more
prevalent. then she think of revenge his refusal on herself; but,
whilst she be engage in this meditation, happily death present
himself to she in so many shape, of drown, hanging, poisoning, &c.,
that her distracted mind could resolve on none. in this perturbation of
spirit, it accidentally occur to her memory that her master's bed be
not make; she therefore go directly to his room, where he happen at
that time to be engage at his bureau. as soon as she see he, she
attempt to retire; but he call she back, and, take she by the
hand, squeeze she so tenderly, at the same time whisper so many soft
thing into her ear, and then press she so closely with his kiss,
that the vanquish fair one, whose passion be already raise, and
which be not so whimsically capricious that one man only could lay
they, though, perhaps, she would have rather prefer that one--the
vanquish fair one quietly submit, I say, to her master's will, who
have just attain the accomplishment of his bliss when Mrs Tow-wouse
unexpectedly enter the room, and cause all that confusion which we
have before see, and which it be not necessary, at present, to take any
farther notice of; since, without the assistance of a single hint from
we, every reader of any speculation or experience, though not marry
himself, may easily conjecture that it conclude with the discharge of
Betty, the submission of Mr Tow-wouse, with some thing to be perform
on his side by way of gratitude for his wife's goodness in be
reconcile to he, with many hearty promise never to offend any more in
the like manner; and, lastly, his quietly and contentedly bear to be
remind of his transgression, as a kind of penance, once or twice a
day during the residue of his life.
BOOK II.
CHAPTER I.
_of division in Authors_.
there be certain mystery or secret in all trade, from the high
to the low, from that of prime-ministering to this of authoring,
which be seldom discover unless to member of the same calling. among
those use by we gentleman of the latter occupation, I take this of
divide our work into book and chapter to be none of the least
considerable. now, for want of be truly acquaint with this secret,
common reader imagine, that by this art of divide we mean only to
swell our work to a much large bulk than they would otherwise be
extend to. these several place therefore in our paper, which be
fill with our book and chapter, be understand as so much buckram,
stay, and stay-tape in a taylor's bill, serve only to make up the sum
total, commonly find at the bottom of our first page and of his last.
but in reality the case be otherwise, and in this as well as all other
instance we consult the advantage of our reader, not our own; and
indeed, many notable use arise to he from this method; for, first,
those little space between our chapter may be look upon as an inn or
resting-place where he may stop and take a glass or any other
refreshment as it please he. Nay, our fine reader will, perhaps, be
scarce able to travel far than through one of they in a day. as to
those vacant page which be place between our book, they be to be
regard as those stage where in long journie the traveller stay some
time to repose himself, and consider of what he hath see in the part
he hath already pass through; a consideration which I take the liberty
to recommend a little to the reader; for, however swift his capacity may
be, I would not advise he to travel through these page too fast; for
if he doth, he may probably miss the seeing some curious production of
nature, which will be observe by the slow and more accurate reader. a
volume without any such place of rest resemble the opening of wild or
sea, which tire the eye and fatigue the spirit when enter upon.
secondly, what be the content prefix to every chapter but so many
inscription over the gate of inn (to continue the same metaphor),
inform the reader what entertainment he be to expect, which if he
like not, he may travel on to the next; for, in biography, as we be
not tie down to an exact concatenation equally with other historian,
so a chapter or two (for instance, this I be now write) may be often
pass over without any injury to the whole. and in these inscription I
have be as faithful as possible, not imitate the celebrated
Montaigne, who promise you one thing and give you another; nor some
title-page author, who promise a great deal and produce nothing at all.
there be, besides these more obvious benefit, several other which our
reader enjoy from this art of dividing; though perhaps most of they too
mysterious to be presently understand by any who be not initiate into
the science of author. to mention, therefore, but one which be most
obvious, it prevent spoil the beauty of a book by turn down its
leaf, a method otherwise necessary to those reader who (though they
read with great improvement and advantage) be apt, when they return to
their study after half-an-hour's absence, to forget where they leave off.
these division have the sanction of great antiquity. Homer not only
divide his great work into twenty-four book (in compliment perhaps to
the twenty-four letter to which he have very particular obligation),
but, accord to the opinion of some very sagacious critic, hawk
they all separately, deliver only one book at a time (probably by
subscription). he be the first inventor of the art which hath so long
lain dormant, of publishing by number; an art now bring to such
perfection, that even dictionary be divide and exhibit piecemeal
to the public; nay, one bookseller hath (to encourage learn and ease
the public) contrive to give they a dictionary in this divide manner
for only fifteen shilling more than it would have cost entire.
virgil hath give we his poem in twelve book, an argument of his
modesty; for by that, doubtless, he would insinuate that he pretend to
no more than half the merit of the Greek; for the same reason, our
Milton go originally no far than ten; till, be puff up by the
praise of his friend, he put himself on the same footing with the
roman poet.
I shall not, however, enter so deep into this matter as some very
learn critick have do; who have with infinite labour and acute
discernment discover what book be proper for embellishment, and what
require simplicity only, particularly with regard to simile, which I
think be now generally agree to become any book but the first.
I will dismiss this chapter with the following observation: that it
become an author generally to divide a book, as it do a butcher to
joint his meat, for such assistance be of great help to both the reader
and the carver. and now, have indulge myself a little, I will
endeavour to indulge the curiosity of my reader, who be no doubt
impatient to know what he will find in the subsequent chapter of
this book.
CHAPTER II.
_a surprize instance of Mr Adams's short memory, with the unfortunate
consequence which it bring on Joseph._
Mr Adams and Joseph be now ready to depart different way, when an
accident determine the former to return with his friend, which
Tow-wouse, Barnabas, and the bookseller have not be able to do. this
accident be, that those sermon, which the parson be travel to
London to publish, be, o my good reader! leave behind; what he have
mistake for they in the saddlebag be no other than three shirt, a
pair of shoe, and some other necessary, which Mrs Adams, who think
her husband would want shirt more than sermon on his journey, have
carefully provide he.
this discovery be now luckily owe to the presence of Joseph at the
opening the saddlebag; who, have hear his friend say he carry with
he nine volume of sermon, and not be of that sect of philosopher
who can reduce all the matter of the world into a nutshell, see there
be no room for they in the bag, where the parson have say they be
deposit, have the curiosity to cry out, "bless I, sir, where be your
sermon?" the parson answer, "there, there, child; there they be,
under my shirt." now it happen that he have take forth his last
shirt, and the vehicle remain visibly empty. "sure, sir," say
Joseph, "there be nothing in the bag." upon which Adams, starting, and
testify some surprize, cry, "hey! fie, fie upon it! they be not
here sure enough. ay, they be certainly leave behind."
Joseph be greatly concerned at the uneasiness which he apprehend his
friend must feel from this disappointment; he beg he to pursue his
journey, and promise he would himself return with the book to he with
the utmost expedition. "no, thank you, child," answer Adams; "it shall
not be so. what would it avail I, to tarry in the great city, unless I
have my discourse with I, which be _ut ita dicam_, the sole cause, the
_aitia monotate_ of my peregrination? no, child, as this accident hath
happen, I be resolve to return back to my cure, together with you;
which indeed my inclination sufficiently lead I to. this
disappointment may perhaps be intend for my good." he conclude with a
verse out of Theocritus, which signify no more than that sometimes it
rain, and sometimes the sun shin.
Joseph bow with obedience and thankfulness for the inclination which
the parson express of return with he; and now the bill be call
for, which, on examination, amount within a shilling to the sum Mr
Adams have in his pocket. perhaps the reader may wonder how he be able
to produce a sufficient sum for so many day: that he may not be
surprize, therefore, it cannot be unnecessary to acquaint he that he
have borrow a guinea of a servant belong to the coach and six, who
have be formerly one of his parishioner, and whose master, the owner
of the coach, then live within three mile of he; for so good be the
credit of Mr Adams, that even Mr Peter, the Lady Booby's steward, would
have lend he a guinea with very little security.
[illustration]
Mr Adams discharge the bill, and they be both set out, have
agree to ride and tie; a method of travel much use by person who
have but one horse between they, and be thus perform. the two
traveller set out together, one on horseback, the other on foot: now,
as it generally happen that he on horseback outgoes he on foot, the
custom be, that, when he arrive at the distance agree on, he be to
dismount, tie the horse to some gate, tree, post, or other thing, and
then proceed on foot; when the other come up to the horse he untie
he, mount, and gallop on, till, have pass by his
fellow-traveller, he likewise arrive at the place of tie. and this be
that method of travel so much in use among our prudent ancestor,
who know that horse have mouth as well as leg, and that they could not
use the latter without be at the expense of suffer the beast
themselves to use the former. this be the method in use in those day
when, instead of a coach and six, a member of parliament's lady use to
mount a pillion behind her husband; and a grave serjeant at law
condescend to amble to Westminster on an easy pad, with his clerk
kick his heel behind he.
Adams be now go some minute, have insist on Joseph's beginning
the journey on horseback, and Joseph have his foot in the stirrup, when
the hostler present he a bill for the horse's board during his
residence at the inn. Joseph say Mr Adams have pay all; but this
matter, be refer to Mr Tow-wouse, be by he decide in favour of
the hostler, and indeed with truth and justice; for this be a fresh
instance of that shortness of memory which do not arise from want of
part, but that continual hurry in which parson Adams be
always involve.
Joseph be now reduce to a dilemma which extremely puzzle he. the sum
due for horse-meat be twelve shilling (for Adams, who have borrow the
beast of his clerk, have order he to be feed as well as they could
feed he), and the cash in his pocket amount to sixpence (for Adams
have divide the last shilling with he). now, though there have be
some ingenious person who have contrive to pay twelve shilling with
sixpence, Joseph be not one of they. he have never contract a debt in
his life, and be consequently the less ready at an expedient to
extricate himself. Tow-wouse be willing to give he credit till next
time, to which Mrs Tow-wouse would probably have consent (for such be
Joseph's beauty, that it have make some impression even on that piece of
flint which that good woman wear in her bosom by way of heart). Joseph
would have find, therefore, very likely the passage free, have he not,
when he honestly discover the nakedness of his pocket, pull out
that little piece of gold which we have mention before. this cause
Mrs Tow-wouse's eye to water; she tell Joseph she do not conceive a
man could want money whilst he have gold in his pocket. Joseph answer
he have such a value for that little piece of gold, that he would not
part with it for a hundred time the rich which the great esquire
in the county be worth. "a pretty way, indeed," say Mrs Tow-wouse, "to
run in debt, and then refuse to part with your money, because you have a
value for it! I never know any piece of gold of more value than as many
shilling as it would change for."--"Not to preserve my life from
starving, nor to redeem it from a robber, would I part with this dear
piece!" answer Joseph. "what," say Mrs Tow-wouse, "I suppose it be
give you by some vile trollop, some miss or other; if it have be the
present of a virtuous woman, you would not have have such a value for it.
my husband be a fool if he part with the horse without be pay for
he."--"No, no, I cannot part with the horse, indeed, till I have the
money," cry Tow-wouse. a resolution highly commend by a lawyer then
in the yard, who declare Mr Tow-wouse might justify the detainer.
as we cannot therefore at present get Mr Joseph out of the inn, we shall
leave he in it, and carry our reader on after parson Adams, who, his
mind be perfectly at ease, fall into a contemplation on a passage in
Aeschylus, which entertain he for three mile together, without
suffer he once to reflect on his fellow-traveller.
at length, have spin out his thread, and be now at the summit of a
hill, he cast his eye backwards, and wonder that he could not see any
sign of Joseph. as he leave he ready to mount the horse, he could not
apprehend any mischief have happen, neither could he suspect that he
miss his way, it be so broad and plain; the only reason which
present itself to he be, that he have meet with an acquaintance who
have prevail with he to delay some time in discourse.
he therefore resolve to proceed slowly forwards, not doubt but that
he should be shortly overtake; and soon come to a large water, which,
fill the whole road, he see no method of pass unless by wad
through, which he accordingly do up to his middle; but be no soon
get to the other side than he perceive, if he have look over the
hedge, he would have find a footpath capable of conduct he without
wet his shoe.
his surprize at Joseph's not come up grow now very troublesome: he
begin to fear he know not what; and as he determine to move no far,
and, if he do not shortly overtake he, to return back, he wish to
find a house of public entertainment where he might dry his clothe and
refresh himself with a pint; but, see no such (for no other reason
than because he do not cast his eye a hundred yard forwards), he sit
himself down on a stile, and pull out his Aeschylus.
a fellow pass presently by, Adams ask he if he could direct he
to an alehouse. the fellow, who have just leave it, and perceive the
house and sign to be within sight, think he have jeer he, and be
of a morose temper, bid he follow his nose and be d---n'd. Adams tell
he he be a saucy jackanape; upon which the fellow turn about
angrily; but, perceive Adams clench his fist, he think proper to go
on without take any farth notice.
a horseman, follow immediately after, and be ask the same
question, answer, "Friend, there be one within a stone's throw; I
believe you may see it before you." Adams, lift up his eye, cry,
"I protest, and so there be;" and, thank his informer, proceed
directly to it.
CHAPTER III.
_the opinion of two lawyer concern the same gentleman, with Mr
Adams's inquiry into the religion of his host._
he have just enter the house, and call for his pint, and seat
himself, when two horseman come to the door, and, fasten their horse
to the rail, alight. they say there be a violent shower of rain
come on, which they intend to weather there, and go into a little
room by themselves, not perceive Mr Adams.
one of these immediately ask the other, "if he have see a more comical
adventure a great while?" upon which the other say, "he doubt
whether, by law, the landlord could justify detain the horse for his
corn and hay." but the former answer, "undoubtedly he can; it be an
adjudge case, and I have know it try."
Adams, who, though he be, as the reader may suspect, a little inclined

the poor parson resolve to return presently to the inn, though he know

now very violent, be over.
the three traveller then sit down together over a mug of good beer;

devil may carry any one before he for I; I would rather be try

in the neighbourhood, I would sell it for half the value rather than
live near he."
Adams shake his head, and say, "he be sorry such man be suffer to

Adams, who be in the utmost anxiety at those different character of

"no, no, master," answer the host (a shrewd, cunning fellow); "I know

"and how doth he behave as a justice, pray?"--"Faith, friend," answer

it be not my business to contradict gentleman while they be drink in

CHAPTER IV.
_the history of Leonora, or the unfortunate jilt._
Leonora be the daughter of a gentleman of fortune; she be tall and
well-shape, with a sprightliness in her countenance which often
attract beyond more regular feature join with an insipid air: nor be
this kind of beauty less apt to deceive than allure; the good humour
which it indicate be often mistake for good nature, and the
vivacity for true understanding.
Leonora, who be now at the age of eighteen, live with an aunt of her
in a town in the north of England. she be an extreme lover of gaiety,
and very rarely miss a ball or any other public assembly; where she
have frequent opportunity of satisfy a greedy appetite of vanity,
with the preference which be give she by the man to almost every other
woman present.
among many young fellow who be particular in their gallantry
towards she, Horatio soon distinguish himself in her eye beyond all
his competitor; she dance with more than ordinary gaiety when he
happen to be her partner; neither the fairness of the evening, nor the
musick of the nightingale, could lengthen her walk like his company. she
affect no long to understand the civility of other; whilst she
incline so attentive an ear to every compliment of Horatio, that she
often smile even when it be too delicate for her comprehension.
"pray, madam," say Adams, "who be this squire Horatio?"
Horatio, say the lady, be a young gentleman of a good family, breed to
the law, and have be some few year call to the degree of a
barrister. his face and person be such as the generality allow
handsome; but he have a dignity in his air very rarely to be see. his
temper be of the saturnine complexion, and without the least taint of
moroseness. he have wit and humour, with an inclination to satire, which
he indulge rather too much.
this gentleman, who have contract the most violent passion for Leonora,
be the last person who perceive the probability of its success. the
whole town have make the match for he before he himself have draw a
confidence from her action sufficient to mention his passion to she;
for it be his opinion (and perhaps he be there in the right) that it
be highly impolitick to talk seriously of love to a woman before you
have make such a progress in her affection, that she herself expect
and desire to hear it.
but whatever diffidence the fear of a lover may create, which be apt
to magnify every favour confer on a rival, and to see the little
advance towards themselves through the other end of the perspective, it
be impossible that Horatio's passion should so blind his discernment as
to prevent his conceiving hope from the behaviour of Leonora, whose
fondness for he be now as visible to an indifferent person in their
company as his for she.
"I never know any of these forward slut come to good" (say the lady
who refuse Joseph's entrance into the coach), "nor shall I wonder at
anything she doth in the sequel."
the lady proceed in her story thus: it be in the midst of a gay
conversation in the walk one evening, when Horatio whisper Leonora,
that he be desirous to take a turn or two with she in private, for that
he have something to communicate to she of great consequence. "be you
sure it be of consequence?" say she, smile. "I hope," answer he,
"you will think so too, since the whole future happiness of my life must
depend on the event."
Leonora, who very much suspect what be come, would have defer it
till another time; but Horatio, who have more than half conquer the
difficulty of speak by the first motion, be so very importunate,
that she at last yield, and, leave the rest of the company, they
turn aside into an unfrequented walk.
they have retire far out of the sight of the company, both maintain a
strict silence. at last Horatio make a full stop, and take Leonora,
who stand pale and trembling, gently by the hand, he fetch a deep
sigh, and then, look on her eye with all the tenderness imaginable,
he cry out in a faltering accent, "O Leonora! be it necessary for I
to declare to you on what the future happiness of my life must be
found? must I say there be something belong to you which be a bar
to my happiness, and which unless you will part with, I must be
miserable!"--"what can that be?" reply Leonora. "no wonder," say he,
"you be surprize that I should make an objection to anything which be
your: yet sure you may guess, since it be the only one which the rich
of the world, if they be mine, should purchase for I. oh, it be that
which you must part with to bestow all the rest! can Leonora, or rather
will she, doubt long? let I then whisper it in her ear--it be your
name, madam. it be by part with that, by your condescension to be for
ever mine, which must at once prevent I from be the most miserable,
and will render I the happy of mankind."
Leonora, cover with blush, and with as angry a look as she could
possibly put on, tell he, "that have she suspect what his declaration
would have be, he should not have decoy she from her company, that
he have so surprize and fright she, that she beg he to convey she
back as quick as possible;" which he, tremble very near as much as
herself, do.
"More fool he," cry Slipslop; "it be a sign he know very little of our
sect."--"Truly, madam," say Adams, "I think you be in the right: I
should have insist to know a piece of her mind, when I have carry
matter so far." but Mrs Grave-airs desire the lady to omit all such
fulsome stuff in her story, for that it make she sick.
well then, madam, to be as concise as possible, say the lady, many

Mrs Grave-airs object to hear these letter; but be put to the
