HORATIO to LEONORA.
"how vain, most adorable creature, be the pursuit of pleasure in the
absence of an object to which the mind be entirely devoted, unless it
have some relation to that object! I be last night condemn to the
society of man of wit and learning, which, however agreeable it might
have formerly be to I, now only give I a suspicion that they impute
my absence in conversation to the true cause. for which reason, when
your engagement forbid I the ecstatic happiness of see you, I be
always desirous to be alone; since my sentiment for Leonora be so
delicate, that I cannot bear the apprehension of another's prying into
those delightful endearment with which the warm imagination of a lover
will sometimes indulge he, and which I suspect my eye then betray. to
fear this discovery of our thought may perhaps appear too ridiculous a
nicety to mind not susceptible of all the tenderness of this delicate
passion. and surely we shall suspect there be few such, when we
consider that it require every human virtue to exert itself in its full
extent; since the beloved, whose happiness it ultimately respect, may
give we charming opportunity of be brave in her defence, generous
to her want, compassionate to her affliction, grateful to her
kindness; and in the same manner, of exercise every other virtue,
which he who would not do to any degree, and that with the utmost
rapture, can never deserve the name of a lover. it be, therefore, with a
view to the delicate modesty of your mind that I cultivate it so purely
in my own; and it be that which will sufficiently suggest to you the
uneasiness I bear from those liberty, which man to whom the world
allow politeness will sometimes give themselves on these occasion.
"can I tell you with what eagerness I expect the arrival of that blest
day, when I shall experience the falsehood of a common assertion, that
the great human happiness consist in hope? a doctrine which no
person have ever strong reason to believe than myself at present, since
none ever taste such bliss as fire my bosom with the thought of
spend my future day with such a companion, and that every action of
my life will have the glorious satisfaction of conducing to your
happiness."
LEONORA to HORATIO.[A]
[a] this letter be write by a young lady on read the former.
"the refinement of your mind have be so evidently prove by every word
and action ever since I have the first pleasure of know you, that I
think it impossible my good opinion of Horatio could have be
heighten to any additional proof of merit. this very thought be my
amusement when I receive your last letter, which, when I open, I
confess I be surprize to find the delicate sentiment express there
so far exceed what I think could come even from you (although I
know all the generous principle human nature be capable of be centre
in your breast), that word cannot paint what I feel on the reflection
that my happiness shall be the ultimate end of all your action.
"oh, Horatio! what a life must that be, where the mean domestic care
be sweeten by the pleasing consideration that the man on earth who
good deserve, and to whom you be most inclined to give your
affection, be to reap either profit or pleasure from all you do! in
such a case toil must be turn into diversion, and nothing but the
unavoidable inconvenience of life can make we remember that we
be mortal.
"if the solitary turn of your thought, and the desire of keep they
undiscovered, make even the conversation of man of wit and learn
tedious to you, what anxious hour must I spend, who be condemn by
custom to the conversation of woman, whose natural curiosity lead they
to pry into all my thought, and whose envy can never suffer Horatio's
heart to be possess by any one, without force they into malicious
design against the person who be so happy as to possess it! but,
indeed, if ever envy can possibly have any excuse, or even alleviation,
it be in this case, where the good be so great, and it must be equally
natural to all to wish it for themselves; nor be I ashamed to own it:
and to your merit, Horatio, I be oblige, that prevent my being in that
most uneasy of all the situation I can figure in my imagination, of
be lead by inclination to love the person whom my own judgment force
I to condemn."
matter be in so great forwardness between this fond couple, that the
day be fix for their marriage, and be now within a fortnight, when
the session chance to be hold for that county in a town about twenty
mile' distance from that which be the scene of our story. it seem, it
be usual for the young gentleman of the bar to repair to these session,
not so much for the sake of profit as to show their part and learn the
law of the justice of peace; for which purpose one of the wise and
gravest of all the justice be appoint speaker, or chairman, as they
modestly call it, and he read they a lecture, and instruct they in the
true knowledge of the law.
"you be here guilty of a little mistake," say Adams, "which, if you
please, I will correct: I have attend at one of these
quarter-session, where I observe the counsel teach the justice,
instead of learn anything of they."
it be not very material, say the lady. Hither repair Horatio, who, as

oh, why have not woman as good resolution to maintain their vow as they
have often good inclination in make they!
the gentleman who own the coach and six come to the assembly. his

Sir, answer the lady, I have be tell he have on a cut velvet coat of

this fine figure do not more entirely engage the eye of every lady in

Leonora see herself admire by the fine stranger, and envy by every

in this temper of mind, Bellarmine, have inquire who she be,

be capable of feeling.
at these word, Adams fetch a deep groan, which fright the lady,

Leonora retire (continue the lady) about six in the morning, but not

in the afternoon, Bellarmine, in the dear coach and six, come to wait on

thus what Horatio have by sigh and tear, love and tenderness, be so

from the opening of the assembly till the end of Bellarmine's visit,

Aye, but Bellarmine be the genteeler, and the fine man; yes, that he

Bellarmine may be as miserable for I too." she be thus argue with

relieve her anxiety for the present.
the next morning Bellarmine breakfast with she in presence of her

break they. will any woman hesitate a moment whether she shall ride in a
coach or walk on foot all the day of her life? but Bellarmine drive
six, and Horatio not even a pair."--"Yes, but, madam, what will the
world say?" answer Leonora: "will not they condemn me?"--"The world be
always on the side of prudence," cry the aunt, "and would surely
condemn you if you sacrifice your interest to any motive whatever. oh!
I know the world very well; and you shew your ignorance, my dear, by
your objection. o' my conscience! the world be wise. I have live
long in it than you; and I assure you there be not anything worth our
regard besides money; nor do I ever know one person who marry from
other consideration, who do not afterwards heartily repent it.
besides, if we examine the two man, can you prefer a sneak fellow,
who hath be breed at the university, to a fine gentleman just come from
his travel. all the world must allow Bellarmine to be a fine gentleman,
positively a fine gentleman, and a handsome man."--"Perhaps, madam, I
should not doubt, if I know how to be handsomely off with the
other."--"Oh! leave that to I," say the aunt. "you know your father
hath not be acquaint with the affair. indeed, for my part I think
it might do well enough, not dream of such an offer; but Iwill
disengage you: leave I to give the fellow an answer. I warrant you
shall have no farth trouble."
Leonora be at length satisfied with her aunt's reasoning; and
Bellarmine sup with she that evening, it be agree he should the
next morning go to her father and propose the match, which she consent
should be consummate at his return.
the aunt retire soon after supper; and, the lover be leave together,
Bellarmine begin in the follow manner: "yes, madam; this coat, I
assure you, be make at Paris, and I defy the good English taylor even
to imitate it. there be not one of they can cut, madam; they cannot cut.
if you observe how this skirt be turn, and this sleeve: a clumsy
english rascal can do nothing like it. pray, how do you like my
livery?" Leonora answer, "she think they very pretty."--"All

anything more than a greatcoat to an Englishman. you know one must
encourage our own people what one can, especially as, before I have a
place, I be in the country interest, he, he, he! but for myself, I
would see the dirty island at the bottom of the sea, rather than wear a
single rag of english work about I: and I be sure, after you have make
one tour to Paris, you will be of the same opinion with regard to your
own clothe. you cannot conceive what an addition a french dress would be
to your beauty; I positively assure you, at the first opera I see since
I come over, I mistook the english lady for chambermaid, he, he, he!"
with such sort of polite discourse do the gay Bellarmine entertain his

"poor woman!" say Mrs Slipslop, "what a terrible quandary she must be
in!"--"not at all," say Mrs Grave-airs; "such slut can never be
confound."--"She must have then more than corinthian assurance," say
Adams; "aye, more than Lais herself."
a long silence, continue the lady, prevail in the whole company. if

"she could not guess the meaning of his question." Horatio tell she

which need be any secret to you."
"youwill pardon I," say Horatio, "if I desire to know who this

before I, d--n I?" "Hark'ee, sir," say Horatio, "I would advise you
to lay aside that fierce air; for I be mightily deceive if this lady
have not a violent desire to get your worship a good drubbing." "Sir,"

if I understand your meaning." "Sir," answer Horatio, "she be rather
your protectress; but give yourself no more air, for you see I be
prepared for you" (shake his whip at he). "oh! _serviteur tre
humble_," say Bellarmine: "_Je vous entend parfaitment bien_." at which
time the aunt, who have hear of Horatio's visit, enter the room, and
soon satisfy all his doubt. she convince he that he be never more

Leonora; who now burst into tear, and wonder what reason she have
give he to use she in so barbarous a manner. Horatio desire

without any great ceremony, and depart, leave the lady with his
rival to consult for his safety, which Leonora fear her indiscretion
might have endanger; but the aunt comfort she with assurance that
Horatio would not venture his person against so accomplish a cavalier
as Bellarmine, and that, be a lawyer, he would seek revenge in his
own way, and the most they have to apprehend from he be an action.
they at length therefore agree to permit Bellarmine to retire to his
lodging, have first settle all matter relate to the journey which
he be to undertake in the morning, and their preparation for the
nuptial at his return.
but, alas! as wise man have observe, the seat of valour be not the
countenance; and many a grave and plain man will, on a just provocation,
betake himself to that mischievous metal, cold iron; while man of a
fiercer brow, and sometimes with that emblem of courage, a cockade, will
more prudently decline it.
Leonora be wake in the morning, from a visionary coach and six, with

that he lie languish at an inn, and the surgeon have declare the

but that if he should die her affliction would be of no service to
Bellarmine, and would only expose herself, which might, probably, keep
she some time without any future offer; that, as matter have happen,
her wise way would be to think no more of Bellarmine, but to endeavour
to regain the affection of Horatio." "speak not to I," cry the
disconsolate Leonora; "be it not owe to I that poor Bellarmine have
lose his life? have not these curse charm (at which word she look
steadfastly in the glass) be the ruin of the most charming man of this
age? can I ever bear to contemplate my own face again (with her eye
still fix on the glass)? be I not the murderess of the fine
gentleman? no other woman in the town could have make any impression on
he." "never think of thing past," cry the aunt: "think of regain
the affection of Horatio." "what reason," say the niece, "have I to
hope he would forgive I? no, I have lose he as well as the other, and
it be your wicked advice which be the occasion of all; you seduce I,
contrary to my inclination, to abandon poor horatio (at which word she
burst into tear); you prevail upon I, whether I would or no, to give
up my affection for he; have it not be for you, Bellarmine never
would have enter into my thought; have not his address be back
by your persuasion, they never would have make any impression on I; I
should have defy all the fortune and equipage in the world; but it be
you, it be you, who get the well of my youth and simplicity, and
force I to lose my dear horatio for ever."
the aunt be almost bear down with this torrent of word; she, however,
rally all the strength she could, and, draw her mouth up in a
purse, begin: "I be not surprize, niece, at this ingratitude. those who
advise young woman for their interest, must always expect such a return:
I be convince my brother will thank I for break off your match with
Horatio, at any rate."--"That may not be in your power yet," answer

that many present, and some pretty valuable one, have pass from
horatio to the old lady; but as true it be, that Bellarmine, when he
breakfast with she and her niece, have compliment she with a
brilliant from his finger, of much great value than all she have
touch of the other.)
the aunt's gall be on float to reply, when a servant bring a letter
into the room, which Leonora, hear it come from Bellarmine, with
great eagerness open, and read as follows:--
"MOST DIVINE CREATURE,--The wound which I fear you have hear I
receive from my rival be not like to be so fatal as those shot into my
heart which have be fire from your eye, _tout brilliant_. those be
the only cannon by which I be to fall; for my surgeon give I hope of
be soon able to attend your _ruelle_; till when, unless you would do
I an honour which I have scarce the _hardiesse_ to think of, your
absence will be the great anguish which can be feel by,
"Madam,
"_Avec toute le respecte_ in the world,
"your most obedient, most absolute _devote_,
"BELLARMINE."
as soon as Leonora perceive such hope of Bellarmine's recovery, and
that the gossip Fame have, accord to custom, so enlarge his danger,
she presently abandon all further thought of Horatio, and be soon
reconcile to her aunt, who receive she again into favour, with a more
christian forgiveness than we generally meet with. indeed, it be

give she concern the present. she might apprehend such rumour,

her countenance and behaviour for many year, she have establish.
Leonora's passion return now for Bellarmine with great force, after
its small relaxation, than ever. she propose to her aunt to make he a

intervene to prevent your intend match, too forward a behaviour with

be marry, ought to consider of, and provide against, the possibility
of the affair's break off." Leonora say, "she should be indifferent

of mankind." she, therefore, resolve to visit he, notwithstanding all

execute her resolution.
the lady be proceed in her story, when the coach drive into the inn
where the company be to dine, sorely to the dissatisfaction of Mr
Adams, whose ear be the most hungry part about he; he be, as the
reader may perhaps guess, of an insatiable curiosity, and heartily
desirous of hear the end of this amour, though he profess he could
scarce wish success to a lady of so inconstant a disposition.
CHAPTER V.
_a dreadful quarrel which happen at the Inn where the company din,
with its bloody consequence to Mr Adams._
as soon as the passenger have alight from the coach, Mr Adams, as be
his custom, make directly to the kitchen, where he find Joseph sit
by the fire, and the hostess anoint his leg; for the horse which Mr
Adams have borrow of his clerk have so violent a propensity to kneeling,
that one would have think it have be his trade, as well as his
master's; nor would he always give any notice of such his intention; he
be often find on his knee when the rider least expect it. this
foible, however, be of no great inconvenience to the parson, who be
accustomed to it; and, as his leg almost touch the ground when he
bestrode the beast, have but a little way to fall, and throw himself
forward on such occasion with so much dexterity that he never receive
any mischief; the horse and he frequently roll many pace' distance,
and afterwards both get up and meet as good friend as ever.
Poor Joseph, who have not be use to such kind of cattle, though an
excellent horseman, do not so happily disengage himself; but, fall
with his leg under the beast, receive a violent contusion, to which the
good woman be, as we have say, apply a warm hand, with some
camphorate spirit, just at the time when the parson enter
the kitchen.
he have scarce express his concern for Joseph's misfortune before the
host likewise enter. he be by no mean of Mr Tow-wouse's gentle
disposition; and be, indeed, perfect master of his house, and
everything in it but his guest.
this surly fellow, who always proportion his respect to the appearance
of a traveller, from "God bless your honour," down to plain "come
presently," observe his wife on her knee to a footman, cry out,
without consider his circumstance, "what a pox be the woman about?
why donot you mind the company in the coach? go and ask they what they
will have for dinner." "my dear," say she, "you know they can have
nothing but what be at the fire, which will be ready presently; and
really the poor young man's leg be very much bruised." at which word
she fall to chafe more violently than before: the bell then happen
to ring, he damn'd his wife, and bid she go in to the company, and not
stand rub there all day, for he do not believe the young fellow's
leg be so bad as he pretend; and if it be, within twenty mile he
would find a surgeon to cut it off. upon these word, Adams fetch two
stride across the room; and snap his finger over his head,
mutter aloud, he would excommunicate such a wretch for a farthing, for
he believe the devil have more humanity. these word occasion a
dialogue between Adams and the host, in which there be two or three
sharp reply, till Joseph bad the latter know how to behave himself to
his better. at which the host (have first strictly survey Adams)
scornfully repeat the word "better," fly into a rage, and, tell
Joseph he be as able to walk out of his house as he have be to walk
into it, offer to lay violent hand on he; which perceive, Adams
deal he so sound a compliment over his face with his fist, that the
blood immediately gush out of his nose in a stream. the host, be
unwilling to be outdo in courtesy, especially by a person of Adams's
figure, return the favour with so much gratitude, that the parson's
nostril begin to look a little red than usual. upon which he again
assail his antagonist, and with another stroke lay he sprawl on
the floor.
the hostess, who be a well wife than so surly a husband deserve,
see her husband all bloody and stretch along, hasten presently to
his assistance, or rather to revenge the blow, which, to all appearance,
be the last he would ever receive; when, lo! a pan full of hog's blood,
which unluckily stand on the dresser, present itself first to her
hand. she seize it in her fury, and without any reflection, discharge
it into the parson's face; and with so good an aim, that much the
great part first salute his countenance, and trickle thence in so
large a current down to his beard, and over his garment, that a more
horrible spectacle be hardly to be see, or even imagine. all which
be perceive by Mrs Slipslop, who enter the kitchen at that instant.
this good gentlewoman, not be of a temper so extremely cool and
patient as perhaps be require to ask many question on this occasion,
fly with great impetuosity at the hostess's cap, which, together with
some of her hair, she pluck from her head in a moment, give she, at
the same time, several hearty cuff in the face; which by frequent
practice on the inferior servant, she have learn an excellent knack of
deliver with a good grace. poor Joseph could hardly rise from his
chair; the parson be employ in wipe the blood from his eye, which
have entirely blind he; and the landlord be but just begin to
stir; whilst Mrs Slipslop, hold down the landlady's face with she
leave hand, make so dexterous an use of her right, that the poor woman
begin to roar, in a key which alarm all the company in the inn.
there happen to be in the inn, at this time, besides the lady who
arrive in the stage-coach, the two gentleman who be present at Mr
Tow-wouse's when Joseph be detain for his horse's meat, and whom we
have before mention to have stopt at the alehouse with Adams. there
be likewise a gentleman just return from his travel to Italy; all
whom the horrid outcry of murder presently bring into the kitchen,
where the several combatant be find in the posture already
describe.
it be now no difficulty to put an end to the fray, the conqueror be
satisfied with the vengeance they have take, and the conquered have no
appetite to renew the fight. the principal figure, and which engage the
eye of all, be Adams, who be all over cover with blood, which the
whole company conclude to be his own, and consequently imagine he no
long for this world. but the host, who have now recover from his
blow, and be rise from the ground, soon deliver they from this
apprehension, by damn his wife for waste the hog's pudding, and
tell she all would have be very well if she have not intermeddle,
like a b--as she be; add, he be very glad the gentlewoman have pay
she, though not half what she deserve. the poor woman have indeed fare
much the bad; have, besides the unmerciful cuff receive, lose a
quantity of hair, which Mrs Slipslop in triumph hold in her left hand.
the traveller, address himself to Mrs Grave-airs, desire she not to
be frighten; for here have be only a little boxing, which he say, to
their _disgracia_, the English be _accustomata_ to: add, it must
be, however, a sight somewhat strange to he, who be just come from
Italy; the Italians not be addict to the _cuffardo_ but _bastonza_,

accuse you." he then return to the lady, and cry, "I find the

I have see such a _spectaculo_ in my way from Viterbo."
one of the gentleman have learn from the host the occasion of this

say the gentleman, "I mean you will recover damage in that action
which, undoubtedly, you intend to bring, as soon as a writ can be

but you have a right to my evidence; and if I be swear, I must speak the
truth. I see you sprawl on the floor, and blood gush from your

circumstance, every drop of my blood should convey an ounce of gold
into my pocket: remember I donot advise you to go to law; but if your
jury be Christians, they must give swing damage. thatbe
all."--"master," cry the host, scratch his head, "I have no stomach

of my neighbour have be at law about a house, till they have both

be a sufficient excuse for his wife, that she spill they in his

Whilst one of the above-mention gentleman be employ, as we have

the side of Mr Adams, whom he advise to bring his action immediately.
he say the assault of the wife be in law the assault of the husband,
for they be but one person; and he be liable to pay damage, which he

and my order, I should think you affront both." at the word order, the
gentleman star (for he be too bloody to be of any modern order of
knight); and, turn hastily about, say, "every man know his own
business."
matter be now compose, the company retire to their several
apartment; the two gentleman congratulate each other on the success
of their good office in procure a perfect reconciliation between the
contending party; and the traveller go to his repast, cry, "as
the italian poet says--
'_Je voi_ very well _que tutta e pace_,
so send up dinner, good Boniface.'"
the coachman begin now to grow importunate with his passenger, whose

this smart dialogue between some people and some folk be go on at

Adams make haste to acquaint Mrs Slipslop with this good news, as he

the prudent gentlewoman, who despise the anger of Miss Grave-airs

everything be now adjust, the company enter the coach, which be

as soon as the coach have leave the inn, the woman all together fall to
