fellow must have warm it. indeed, there be some wretched, miserable
old object, that turn one's stomach; I should not wonder if she have
refuse such a one; I be as nice as herself, and should have care no
more than herself for the company of stink old fellow; but, hold up
thy head, Joseph, thou art none of those; and she who hath not
compulsion for thee be a Myhummetman, and I will maintain it." this
conversation make Joseph uneasy as well as the lady; who, perceive
the spirit which Mrs Slipslop be in (for indeed she be not a cup too
low), begin to fear the consequence; one of they therefore desire the
lady to conclude the story. "Aye, madam," say Slipslop, "I beg your
ladyship to give we that story you commensate in the morning;" which
request that well-breed woman immediately comply with.
CHAPTER VI.
_conclusion of the unfortunate jilt._
Leonora, have once break through the bound which custom and modesty
impose on her sex, soon give an unbridled indulgence to her passion. her
visit to Bellarmine be more constant, as well as long, than his
surgeon's: in a word, she become absolutely his nurse; make his
water-gruel, administer he his medicine; and, notwithstanding the
prudent advice of her aunt to the contrary, almost intirely reside in
her wound lover's apartment.
the lady of the town begin to take her conduct under consideration: it
be the chief topic of discourse at their tea-table, and be very
severely censure by the most part; especially by Lindamira, a lady
whose discreet and starch carriage, together with a constant attendance
at church three time a day, have utterly defeat many malicious attack
on her own reputation; for such be the envy that Lindamira's virtue have
attract, that, notwithstanding her own strict behaviour and strict
enquiry into the life of other, she have not be able to escape be
the mark of some arrow herself, which, however, do she no injury; a
blessing, perhaps, owe by she to the clergy, who be her chief male
companion, and with two or three of whom she have be barbarously and
unjustly calumniate.
"not so unjustly neither, perhaps," say Slipslop; "for the clergy be
man, as well as other folk."
the extreme delicacy of Lindamira's virtue be cruelly hurt by those
freedom which Leonora allow herself: she say, "it be an affront to
her sex; that she do not imagine it consistent with any woman's honour
to speak to the creature, or to be see in her company; and that, for
her part, she should always refuse to dance at an assembly with she,
for fear of contamination by take she by the hand."
but to return to my story: as soon as Bellarmine be recover, which
be somewhat within a month from his receive the wound, he set out,
accord to agreement, for Leonora's father's, in order to propose the
match, and settle all matter with he touching settlement, and
the like.
a little before his arrival the old gentleman have receive an intimation
of the affair by the follow letter, which I can repeat verbatim, and
which, they say, be write neither by Leonora nor her aunt, though it
be in a woman's hand. the letter be in these words:--
"SIR,--I be sorry to acquaint you that your daughter, Leonora, hath
act one of the basest as well as most simple part with a young
gentleman to whom she have engage herself, and whom she hath (pardon the
word) jilt for another of inferior fortune, notwithstanding his
superior figure. you may take what measure you please on this occasion;
I have perform what I think my duty; as I have, though unknown to
you, a very great respect for your family."
the old gentleman do not give himself the trouble to answer this kind
epistle; nor do he take any notice of it, after he have read it, till he
see Bellarmine. he be, to say the truth, one of those father who look
on child as an unhappy consequence of their youthful pleasure;
which, as he would have be delighted not to have have attend they, so
be he no less pleased with any opportunity to rid himself of the
incumbrance. he pass, in the world's language, as an exceeding good
father; be not only so rapacious as to rob and plunder all mankind to
the utmost of his power, but even to deny himself the conveniencie, and
almost necessary, of life; which his neighbour attribute to a desire
of raise immense fortune for his child: but in fact it be not
so; he heap up money for its own sake only, and look on his child
as his rival, who be to enjoy his beloved mistress when he be
incapable of possess she, and which he would have be much more
charm with the power of carry along with he; nor have his child
any other security of be his heir than that the law would constitute
they such without a will, and that he have not affection enough for any
one live to take the trouble of write one.
to this gentleman come Bellarmine, on the errand I have mention. his
person, his equipage, his family, and his estate, seem to the father
to make he an advantageous match for his daughter: he therefore very
readily accept his proposal: but when Bellarmine imagine the
principal affair conclude, and begin to open the incidental matter of
fortune, the old gentleman presently change his countenance, say,
"he resolve never to marry his daughter on a Smithfield match; that
whoever have love for she to take she would, when he die, find her share
of his fortune in his coffer; but he have see such example of
undutifulness happen from the too early generosity of parent, that he
have make a vow never to part with a shilling whilst he live." he
commend the saying of Solomon, "he that spareth the rod spoileth the
child;" but add, "he might have likewise assert, that he that
spareth the purse saveth the child." he then run into a discourse on the
extravagance of the youth of the age; whence he launch into a
dissertation on horse; and come at length to commend those Bellarmine
drive. that fine gentleman, who at another season would have be well
enough please to dwell a little on that subject, be now very eager to
resume the circumstance of fortune. he say, "he have a very high value
for the young lady, and would receive she with less than he would any
other whatever; but that even his love to she make some regard to
worldly matter necessary; for it would be a most distract sight for
he to see she, when he have the honour to be her husband, in less than a
coach and six." the old gentleman answer, "Four will do, four will
do;" and then take a turn from horse to extravagance and from
extravagance to horse, till he come round to the equipage again;
whither he be no soon arrive than Bellarmine bring he back to the
point; but all to no purpose; he make his escape from that subject in a
minute; till at last the lover declare, "that in the present situation
of his affair it be impossible for he, though he love Leonora more
than _tout le monde_, to marry she without any fortune." to which the
father answer, "he be sorry that his daughter must lose so valuable a
match; that, if he have an inclination, at present it be not in his
power to advance a shilling: that he have have great loss, and be at
great expense on project; which, though he have great expectation from
they, have yet produce he nothing: that he do not know what might
happen hereafter, as on the birth of a son, or such accident; but he
would make no promise, or enter into any article, for he would not break
his vow for all the daughter in the world."
in short, lady, to keep you no long in suspense, Bellarmine, have
try every argument and persuasion which he could invent, and find
they all ineffectual, at length take his leave, but not in order to
return to Leonora; he proceed directly to his own seat, whence, after
a few day' stay, he return to Paris, to the great delight of the
French and the honour of the english nation.
but as soon as he arrive at his home he presently despatch a
messenger with the following epistle to Leonora:--
"ADORABLE and charmante,--i be sorry to have the honour to tell you I
be not the _heureux_ person destine for your divine arm. your papa
hath tell I so with a _politesse_ not often see on this side Paris.
you may perhaps guess his manner of refuse I. _ah, mon Dieu!_ you
will certainly believe I, madam, incapable myself of deliver this
_triste_ message, which I intend to try the french air to cure the
consequence of. _a jamais! Coeur! ange! Au diable!_ if your papa
oblige you to a marriage, I hope we shall see you at Paris; till when,
the wind that flow from thence will be the warm _dans le monde_, for
it will consist almost entirely of my sigh. _Adieu, ma princesse!
ah, l'amour!_
"BELLARMINE."
I shall not attempt, lady, to describe Leonora's condition when she
receive this letter. it be a picture of horror, which I should have as
little pleasure in draw as you in behold. she immediately leave the
place where she be the subject of conversation and ridicule, and
retire to that house I show you when I begin the story; where she
hath ever since lead a disconsolate life, and deserve, perhaps, pity for
her misfortune, more than our censure for a behaviour to which the
artifice of her aunt very probably contribute, and to which very young
woman be often render too liable by that blameable levity in the
education of our sex.
"if I be inclined to pity she," say a young lady in the coach, "it
would be for the loss of Horatio; for I cannot discern any misfortune in
she miss such a husband as Bellarmine."
"why, I must own," say Slipslop, "the gentleman be a little
false-hearted; but howsumever, it be hard to have two lover, and get
never a husband at all. but pray, madam, what become of _our-asho_?"
he remain, say the lady, still unmarried, and hath apply himself so
strictly to his business, that he hath raise, I hear, a very
considerable fortune. and what be remarkable, they say he never hear
the name of Leonora without a sigh, nor hath ever utter one syllable
to charge she with her ill-conduct towards he.
CHAPTER VII.
_a very short chapter, in which parson Adams go a great way._
the lady, have finish her story, receive the thank of the company;
and now Joseph, put his head out of the coach, cry out, "never
believe I if yonder be not our parson Adams walk along without his
horse!"--"On my word, and so he be," say Slipslop: "and as sure as
twopence he hath leave he behind at the inn." indeed, true it be, the
parson have exhibit a fresh instance of his absence of mind; for he be
so pleased with have get Joseph into the coach, that he never once
thought of the beast in the stable; and, find his leg as nimble as
he desire, he sally out, brandish a crabstick, and have keep on
before the coach, mend and slacken his pace occasionally, so that
he have never be much more or less than a quarter of a mile
distant from it.
Mrs Slipslop desire the coachman to overtake he, which he attempt,
but in vain; for the fast he drive the fast run the parson, often
cry out, "aye, aye, catch I if you can;" till at length the coachman
swear he would as soon attempt to drive after a greyhound, and, give
the parson two or three hearty curse, he cry'd, "softly, softly, boy,"
to his horse, which the civil beast immediately obey.
but we will be more courteous to our reader than he be to Mrs
Slipslop; and, leave the coach and its company to pursue their
journey, we will carry our reader on after parson Adams, who stretch
forward without once look behind he, till, have leave the coach
full three mile in his rear, he come to a place where, by keep the
extreme track to the right, it be just barely possible for a human
creature to miss his way. this track, however, do he keep, as indeed he
have a wonderful capacity at these kind of bare possibility, and,
travel in it about three mile over the plain, he arrive at the
summit of a hill, whence look a great way backwards, and perceive
no coach in sight, he sit himself down on the turf, and, pull out his
Aeschylus, determine to wait here for its arrival.
he have not sit long here before a gun go off very near, a little
startle he; he look up and see a gentleman within a hundred pace
take up a partridge which he have just shoot.
Adams stand up and present a figure to the gentleman which would have
move laughter in many; for his cassock have just again fall down below
his greatcoat, that be to say, it reach his knee, whereas the skirt
of his greatcoat descend no low than half-way down his thigh; but
the gentleman's mirth give way to his surprize at behold such a
personage in such a place.
Adams, advance to the gentleman, tell he he hop he have good sport,
to which the other answer, "very little."--"I see, sir," say Adams,
"you have smite one partridge;" to which the sportsman make no reply,
but proceed to charge his piece.
whilst the gun be charge, Adams remain in silence, which he at last
break by observe that it be a delightful evening. the gentleman, who
have at first sight conceive a very distasteful opinion of the parson,
begin, on perceive a book in his hand and smoake likewise the
information of the cassock, to change his thought, and make a small
advance to conversation on his side by say, "Sir, I suppose you be
not one of these part?"
Adams immediately tell he, "No; that he be a traveller, and invite by
the beauty of the evening and the place to repose a little and amuse
himself with reading."--"I may as well repose myself too," say the
sportsman, "for I have be out this whole afternoon, and the devil a
bird have I see till I come hither."
"perhaps then the game be not very plenty hereabouts?" cry Adams. "no,
sir," say the gentleman: "the soldier, who be quarter in the
neighbourhood, have kill it all."--"It be very probable," cry Adams,
"for shooting be their profession."--"Ay, shoot the game," answer
the other; "but I donot see they be so forward to shoot our enemy. I
donot like that affair of Carthagena; if I have be there, I believe I
should have do other-guess thing, d--n I: whatbe a man's life when
his country demand it? a man who willnot sacrifice his life for his
country deserve to be hang, d--n I." which word he speak with so
violent a gesture, so loud a voice, so strong an accent, and so fierce a
countenance, that he might have frighten a captain of train band at
the head of his company; but Mr Adams be not greatly subject to fear;
he tell he intrepidly that he very much approve his virtue, but
dislike his swearing, and beg he not to addict himself to so bad a
custom, without which he say he might fight as bravely as Achilles do.
indeed he be charm with this discourse; he tell the gentleman he
would willingly have go many mile to have meet a man of his generous
way of thinking; that, if he please to sit down, he should be greatly
delighted to commune with he; for, though he be a clergyman, he would
himself be ready, if thereto call, to lay down his life for
his country.
the gentleman sit down, and Adams by he; and then the latter begin, as
in the follow chapter, a discourse which we have place by itself, as
it be not only the most curious in this but perhaps in any other book.
CHAPTER VIII.
_a notable dissertation by Mr Abraham Adams; wherein that gentleman
appear in a political light._
"I do assure you, sir" (say he, take the gentleman by the hand), "I
be heartily glad to meet with a man of your kidney; for, though I be a
poor parson, I will be bold to say I be an honest man, and would not do
an ill thing to be make a bishop; nay, though it hath not fall in my
way to offer so noble a sacrifice, I have not be without opportunity
of suffer for the sake of my conscience, I thank Heaven for they; for
I have have relation, though I say it, who make some figure in the
world; particularly a nephew, who be a shopkeeper and an alderman of a
corporation. he be a good lad, and be under my care when a boy; and I
believe would do what I bid he to his die day. indeed, it look like
extreme vanity in I to affect be a man of such consequence as to
have so great an interest in an alderman; but other have think so
too, as manifestly appear by the rector, whose curate I formerly be,
send for I on the approach of an election, and tell I, if I
expect to continue in his cure, that I must bring my nephew to vote
for one Colonel Courtly, a gentleman whom I have never hear tiding of
till that instant. I tell the rector I have no power over my nephew's
vote (God forgive I for such prevarication!); that I suppose he would
give it accord to his conscience; that I would by no means endeavour
to influence he to give it otherwise. he tell I it be in vain to
equivocate; that he know I have already speak to he in favour of esquire
Fickle, my neighbour; and, indeed, it be true I have; for it be at a
season when the church be in danger, and when all good man expect
they know not what would happen to we all. I then answer boldly, if he
think I have give my promise, he affront I in propose any breach
of it. not to be too prolix; I persevere, and so do my nephew, in the
esquire's interest, who be choose chiefly through his mean; and so I
lose my curacy, well, sir, but do you think the esquire ever mention a
word of the church? _Ne verbum quidem, ut ita dicam_: within two year
he get a place, and hath ever since live in London; where I have be
inform (but God forbid I should believe that,) that he never so much
as goeth to church. I remain, sir, a considerable time without any
cure, and live a full month on one funeral sermon, which I preach on
the indisposition of a clergyman; but this by the bye. at last, when Mr
Fickle get his place, Colonel courtly stand again; and who should make
interest for he but Mr Fickle himself! that very identical Mr Fickle,
who have formerly tell I the colonel be an enemy to both the church and
state, have the confidence to sollicit my nephew for he; and the colonel
himself offer I to make I chaplain to his regiment, which I refuse
in favour of Sir Oliver Hearty, who tell we he would sacrifice
everything to his country; and I believe he would, except his hunting,
which he stick so close to, that in five year together he go but
twice up to parliament; and one of those time, I have be tell, never
be within sight of the House. however, he be a worthy man, and the
good friend I ever have; for, by his interest with a bishop, he get I
replace into my curacy, and give I eight pound out of his own pocket
to buy I a gown and cassock, and furnish my house. he have our interest
while he live, which be not many year. on his death I have fresh
application make to I; for all the world know the interest I have with
my good nephew, who now be a lead man in the corporation; and Sir
Thomas Booby, buy the estate which have be Sir Oliver's, propose
himself a candidate. he be then a young gentleman just come from his
travel; and it do I good to hear he discourse on affair which, for
my part, I know nothing of. if I have be master of a thousand vote he
should have have they all. I engage my nephew in his interest, and he
be elect; and a very fine parliament-man he be. they tell I he make
speech of an hour long, and, I have be tell, very fine one; but he
could never persuade the parliament to be of his opinion. _Non omnia
possumus omne_. he promise I a living, poor man! and I believe I
should have have it, but an accident happen, which be, that my lady
have promise it before, unknown to he. this, indeed, I never hear till
afterwards; for my nephew, who die about a month before the incumbent,
always tell I I might be assure of it. since that time, Sir Thomas,
poor man, have always so much business, that he never could find leisure
to see I. I believe it be partly my lady's fault too, who do not
think my dress good enough for the gentry at her table. however, I must
do he the justice to say he never be ungrateful; and I have always
find his kitchen, and his cellar too, open to I: many a time, after
service on a Sunday--for I preach at four church--have I recruit my
spirit with a glass of his ale. since my nephew's death, the
corporation be in other hand; and I be not a man of that consequence I
be formerly. I have now no long any talent to lay out in the service
of my country; and to whom nothing be give, of he can nothing be
require. however, on all proper season, such as the approach of an
election, I throw a suitable dash or two into my sermon; which I have
the pleasure to hear be not disagreeable to Sir Thomas and the other
honest gentleman my neighbour, who have all promise I these five
year to procure an ordination for a son of mine, who be now near
thirty, hath an infinite stock of learning, and be, I thank Heaven, of
an unexceptionable life; though, as he be never at an university, the
bishop refuse to ordain he. too much care cannot indeed be take in
admit any to the sacred office; though I hope he will never act so
as to be a disgrace to any order, but will serve his God and his country
to the utmost of his power, as I have endeavour to do before he; nay,
and will lay down his life whenever call to that purpose. I be sure I
have educate he in those principle; so that I have acquit my duty,
and shall have nothing to answer for on that account. but I do not
distrust he, for he be a good boy; and if Providence should throw it in
his way to be of as much consequence in a public light as his father
once be, I can answer for he he will use his talent as honestly as I
have do."
CHAPTER IX.
_in which the gentleman discant on bravery and heroic virtue, till an
unlucky accident put an end to the discourse._
the gentleman highly commend Mr Adams for his good resolution, and
tell he, "he hop his son would tread in his step;" add, "that if
he would not die for his country, he would not be worthy to live in it.
Iwould make no more of shoot a man that would not die for his
country, than--
"Sir," say he, "I have disinherit a nephew, who be in the army,
because he would not exchange his commission and go to the West Indies.
I believe the rascal be a coward, though he pretend to be in love
forsooth. I would have all such fellow hang, sir; I would have they
hang." Adams answer, "that would be too severe; that man do not
make themselves; and if fear have too much ascendance in the mind, the
man be rather to be pity than abhor; that reason and time might
teach he to subdue it." he say, "a man might be a coward at one time,
and brave at another. Homer," say he, "who so well understand and
copy Nature, hath teach we this lesson; for Paris fight and Hector
run away. Nay, we have a mighty instance of this in the history of
later age, no long ago than the 705th year of Rome, when the great
Pompey, who have win so many battle and be honour with so many
triumph, and of whose valour several author, especially Cicero and
Paterculus, have form such elogium; this very Pompey leave the battle
of Pharsalia before he have lose it, and retreat to his tent, where he
sit like the most pusillanimous rascal in a fit of despair, and yield
a victory, which be to determine the empire of the world, to Caesar. I
be not much travel in the history of modern time, that be to say,
these last thousand year; but those who be can, I make no question,
furnish you with parallel instance." he conclude, therefore, that, have
he take any such hasty resolution against his nephew, he hop he
would consider well, and retract they. the gentleman answer with
great warmth, and talk much of courage and his country, till,
perceive it grow late, he ask Adams, "what place he intend for
that night?" he tell he, "he wait there for the stage-coach."--"The
stage-coach, sir!" say the gentleman; "they be all pass by long ago.
you may see the last yourself almost three mile before we."--"I protest
and so they be," cry Adams; "then I must make haste and follow they."
the gentleman tell he, "he would hardly be able to overtake they; and
that, if he do not know his way, he would be in danger of lose
himself on the down, for it would be presently dark; and he might
ramble about all night, and perhaps find himself far from his
journey's end in the morning than he be now." he advise he,
therefore, "to accompany he to his house, which be very little out of
his way," assure he "that he would find some country fellow in his
parish who would conduct he for sixpence to the city where he be
go." Adams accept this proposal, and on they travel, the
gentleman renew his discourse on courage, and the infamy of not be
ready, at all time, to sacrifice our life to our country. night
overtake they much about the same time as they arrive near some bush;
whence, on a sudden, they hear the most violent shriek imaginable in a
female voice. Adams offer to snatch the gun out of his companion's
hand. "what be you do?" say he. "do!" say Adams; "I be
hasten to the assistance of the poor creature whom some villain be
murder." "you be not mad enough, I hope," say the gentleman,
tremble: "do you consider this gun be only charge with shot, and that
the robber be most probably furnish with pistol load with
bullet? this be no business of our; let we make as much haste as
possible out of the way, or we may fall into their hand ourselves." the
shriek now increase, Adams make no answer, but snapt his finger,
and, brandish his crabstick, make directly to the place whence the
voice issue; and the man of courage make as much expedition towards his
own home, whither he escape in a very short time without once look
behind he; where we will leave he, to contemplate his own bravery, and
to censure the want of it in other, and return to the good Adams, who,
on come up to the place whence the noise proceed, find a woman
struggle with a man, who have throw she on the ground, and have almost
overpower she. the great ability of Mr Adams be not necessary to
have form a right judgment of this affair on the first sight. he do
not, therefore, want the entreaty of the poor wretch to assist she;
but, lift up his crabstick, he immediately level a blow at that
part of the ravisher's head where, accord to the opinion of the
ancient, the brain of some person be deposit, and which he have
undoubtedly let forth, have not nature (who, as wise man have observe,
equip all creature with what be most expedient for they) take a
provident care (as she always doth with those she intend for
encounter) to make this part of the head three time as thick as those
of ordinary man who be design to exercise talent which be vulgarly
call rational, and for whom, as brain be necessary, she be oblige
to leave some room for they in the cavity of the skull; whereas, those
ingredient be entirely useless to person of the heroic calling, she
hath an opportunity of thicken the bone, so as to make it less
subject to any impression, or liable to be crack or break: and
indeed, in some who be predestine to the command of army and
empire, she be suppose sometimes to make that part perfectly solid.
as a game cock, when engage in amorous toy with a hen, if perchance
he espy another cock at hand, immediately quit his female, and
oppose himself to his rival, so do the ravisher, on the information of
the crabstick, immediately leap from the woman and hasten to assail the
man. he have no weapon but what Nature have furnish he with. however,
he clench his fist, and presently dart it at that part of Adams's
breast where the heart be lodge. Adams stagger at the violence of the
blow, when, throw away his staff, he likewise clench that fist
which we have before commemorate, and would have discharge it full in
the breast of his antagonist, have he not dexterously catch it with his
leave hand, at the same time dart his head (which some modern hero
of the low class use, like the battering-ram of the ancient, for a
weapon of offence; another reason to admire the cunningness of Nature,
in compose it of those impenetrable material); dash his head, I
say, into the stomach of Adams, he tumble he on his back; and, not
have any regard to the law of heroism, which would have restrain
he from any farth attack on his enemy till he be again on his leg,
he throw himself upon he, and, lay hold on the ground with his left
hand, he with his right belabour the body of Adams till he be weary,
and indeed till he conclude (to use the language of fighting) "that he
have do his business;" or, in the language of poetry, "that he have send
he to the shade below;" in plain English, "that he be dead."
but Adams, who be no chicken, and could bear a drubbing as well as any
boxing champion in the universe, lie still only to watch his
opportunity; and now, perceive his antagonist to pant with his
labour, he exert his utmost force at once, and with such success that
he overturn he, and become his superior; when, fix one of his
knee in his breast, he cry out in an exulting voice, "it be my turn
now;" and, after a few minute' constant application, he give he so
dexterous a blow just under his chin that the fellow no long retain
any motion, and Adams begin to fear he have strike he once too often;
for he often assert "he should be concern to have the blood of even
the wicked upon he."
Adams get up and call aloud to the young woman. "be of good cheer,
damsel," say he, "you be no long in danger of your ravisher, who, I
be terribly afraid, lie dead at my foot; but God forgive I what I have
do in defence of innocence!" the poor wretch, who have be some time
in recover strength enough to rise, and have afterwards, during the
engagement, stand tremble, be disable by fear even from run
away, hear her champion be victorious, come up to he, but not
without apprehension even of her deliverer; which, however, she be
soon relieve from by his courteous behaviour and gentle word. they
be both stand by the body, which lie motionless on the ground, and
which Adams wish to see stir much more than the woman do, when he
earnestly beg she to tell he "by what misfortune she come, at such a
time of night, into so lonely a place." she acquaint he, "she be
travel towards London, and have accidentally meet with the person from
whom he have deliver she, who tell she he be likewise on his journey
to the same place, and would keep her company; an offer which,
suspect no harm, she have accept; that he tell she they be at a
small distance from an inn where she might take up she lodge that
evening, and he would show she a near way to it than by follow the
road; that if she have suspect he (which she do not, he speak so
kindly to she), be alone on these down in the dark, she have no human
mean to avoid he; that, therefore, she put her whole trust in
Providence, and walk on, expect every moment to arrive at the inn;
when on a sudden, be come to those bush, he desire she to stop,
and after some rude kiss, which she resist, and some entreaty,
which she reject, he lay violent hand on she, and be attempt to
execute his wicked will, when, she thank G--, he timely come up and
prevent he." Adams encourage she for say she have put her whole
trust in Providence, and tell she, "he doubt not but Providence have
send he to her deliverance, as a reward for that trust. he wish
indeed he have not deprive the wicked wretch of life, but G--'s will be
do;" say, "he hop the goodness of his intention would excuse he in
the next world, and he trust in her evidence to acquit he in this."
he be then silent, and begin to consider with himself whether it would
be properer to make his escape, or to deliver himself into the hand of
justice; which meditation end as the reader will see in the
next chapter.
CHAPTER X.
_give an account of the strange catastrophe of the precede
adventure, which draw poor Adams into fresh calamity; and who the
woman be who owe the preservation of her chastity to his
victorious arm._
the silence of Adams, add to the darkness of the night and loneliness
of the place, strike dreadful apprehension into the poor woman's mind;
she begin to fear as great an enemy in her deliverer as he have
deliver she from; and as she have not light enough to discover the age
of Adams, and the benevolence visible in his countenance, she suspect
he have use she as some very honest man have use their country; and have
rescue she out of the hand of one rifler in order to rifle she
himself. such be the suspicion she draw from his silence; but indeed
they be ill-ground. he stand over his vanquished enemy, wisely
weigh in his mind the objection which might be make to either of the
two method of proceeding mention in the last chapter, his judgment
sometimes incline to the one, and sometimes to the other; for both
seem to he so equally advisable and so equally dangerous, that
probably he would have end his day, at least two or three of they, on
that very spot, before he have take any resolution; at length he lift
up his eye, and spy a light at a distance, to which he instantly
address himself with _Heus tu, traveller, heus tu!_ he presently hear
several voice, and perceive the light approach toward he. the
person who attend the light begin some to laugh, other to sing, and
other to hollow, at which the woman testify some fear (for she have
conceal her suspicion of the parson himself); but Adams say, "be of
good cheer, damsel, and repose thy trust in the same providence which
hath hitherto protect thee, and never will forsake the innocent."
these people, who now approach, be no other, reader, than a set of
young fellow, who come to these bush in pursuit of a diversion which
they call bird-batting. this, if you be ignorant of it (as perhaps if
thou hast never travel beyond Kensington, Islington, Hackney, or the
Borough, thou mayst be), I will inform thee, be perform by hold a
large clap-net before a lanthorn, and at the same time beat the
bush; for the bird, when they be disturb from their place of
rest, or roost, immediately make to the light, and so be intice
within the net. Adams immediately tell they what happen, and desire
they to hold the lanthorn to the face of the man on the ground, for he
fear he have smite he fatally. but indeed his fear be frivolous;
for the fellow, though he have be stun by the last blow he receive,
have long since recover his sens, and, find himself quit of Adams,
have listen attentively to the discourse between he and the young
woman; for whose departure he have patiently wait, that he might
likewise withdraw himself, have no long hope of succeed in his
desire, which be moreover almost as well cool by Mr Adams as they
could have be by the young woman herself have he obtain his utmost
wish. this fellow, who have a readiness at improve any accident,
think he might now play a well part than that of a dead man; and,
accordingly, the moment the candle be hold to his face he leapt up,
and, lay hold on Adams, cry out, "no, villain, I be not dead,
though you and your wicked whore might well think I so, after the
barbarous cruelty you have exercise on I. gentleman," say he, "you
be luckily come to the assistance of a poor traveller, who would
otherwise have be rob and murder by this vile man and woman, who
lead I hither out of my way from the high-road, and both fall on I
have use I as you see." Adams be go to answer, when one of the
young fellow cry, "D--n they, letus carry they both before the
justice." the poor woman begin to tremble, and Adams lift up his
voice, but in vain. three or four of they lay hand on he; and one
hold the lanthorn to his face, they all agree he have the most
villainous countenance they ever beheld; and an attorney's clerk, who
be of the company, declare he be sure he have remember he at the
bar. as to the woman, her hair be dishevel in the struggle, and her
nose have bleed; so that they could not perceive whether she be handsome
or ugly, but they say her fright plainly discover her guilt. and
search her pocket, as they do those of Adams, for money, which the
fellow say he have lose, they find in her pocket a purse with some gold
in it, which abundantly convince they, especially as the fellow offer
to swear to it. Mr Adams be find to have no more than one halfpenny
about he. this the clerk say "be a great presumption that he be an
old offender, by cunningly give all the booty to the woman." to which
all the rest readily assent.
this accident promise they well sport than what they have propose,
they quit their intention of catch bird, and unanimously resolve
to proceed to the justice with the offender. be inform what a
desperate fellow Adams be, they tie his hand behind he; and, have
hid their net among the bush, and the lanthorn be carry before
they, they place the two prisoner in their front, and then begin their
march; Adams not only submit patiently to his own fate, but
comfort and encourage his companion under her suffering.
whilst they be on their way the clerk inform the rest that this
adventure would prove a very beneficial one; for that they would all be
entitle to their proportion of L80 for apprehend the robber. this
occasion a contention concern the part which they have severally
bear in take they; one insist he ought to have the great share,
for he have first lay his hand on Adams; another claim a superior
part for have first hold the lanthorn to the man's face on the ground,
by which, he say, "the whole be discover." the clerk claim
four-fifth of the reward for have propose to search the prisoner,
and likewise the carry they before the justice: he say, "indeed, in
strict justice, he ought to have the whole." these claim, however,
they at last consent to refer to a future decision, but seem all to
agree that the clerk be entitle to a moiety. they then debate what
money should be allot to the young fellow who have be employ only
in hold the net. he very modestly say, "that he do not apprehend
any large proportion would fall to his share, but hop they would allow
he something; he desire they to consider that they have assign their
net to his care, which prevent he from be as forward as any in
lay hold of the robber" (for so those innocent people be call);
"that if he have not occupy the net, some other must;" conclude,
however, "that he should be content with the small share
imaginable, and should think that rather their bounty than his merit."
but they be all unanimous in exclude he from any part whatever, the
clerk particularly swear, "if they give he a shilling they might do
what they please with the rest; for he would not concern himself with
the affair." this contention be so hot, and so totally engage the
attention of all the party, that a dexterous nimble thief, have he be
in Mr Adams's situation, would have take care to have give the justice
no trouble that evening. indeed, it require not the art of a sheppard
to escape, especially as the darkness of the night would have so much
befriend he; but Adams trust rather to his innocence than his
heel, and, without thinking of flight, which be easy, or resistance
(which be impossible, as there be six lusty young fellow, besides
the villain himself, present), he walk with perfect resignation the
way they think proper to conduct he.
Adams frequently vent himself in ejaculation during their journey; at
last, poor Joseph Andrews occur to his mind, he could not refrain
sigh forth his name, which be hear by his companion in
affliction, she cry with some vehemence, "sure I should know that
voice; you cannot certainly, sir, be Mr Abraham adams?"--"indeed,
damsel," say he, "that be my name; there be something also in your
voice which persuade I I have hear it before."--"La! sir," say she,
"donot you remember poor fanny?"--"how, Fanny!" answer Adams: "indeed
I very well remember you; what can have bring you hither?"--"I have
tell you, sir," reply she, "I be travel towards London; but I
think you mention Joseph Andrews; pray what be become of him?"--"i
leave he, child, this afternoon," say Adams, "in the stage-coach, in
his way towards our parish, whither he be go to see you."--"To see
I! La, sir," answer Fanny, "sure you jeer I; what should he be go
to see I for?"--"can you ask that?" reply Adams. "I hope, Fanny, you
be not inconstant; I assure you he deserve much well of you."--"La!
Mr Adams," say she, "what be Mr Joseph to I? I be sure I never have
anything to say to he, but as one fellow-servant might to another."--"I
be sorry to hear this," say Adams; "a virtuous passion for a young man
be what no woman need be ashamed of. you either do not tell I truth, or
you be false to a very worthy man." Adams then tell she what have
happen at the inn, to which she listen very attentively; and a sigh
often escape from she, notwithstanding her utmost endeavour to the
contrary; nor could she prevent herself from ask a thousand
question, which would have assure any one but Adams, who never see
far into people than they desire to let he, of the truth of a
passion she endeavour to conceal. indeed, the fact be, that this poor
girl, have hear of Joseph's misfortune, by some of the servant
belong to the coach which we have formerly mention to have stopt at
the inn while the poor youth be confine to his bed, that instant
abandon the cow she be milk, and, take with she a little bundle
of clothe under her arm, and all the money she be worth in her own
purse, without consult any one, immediately set forward in pursuit of
one whom, notwithstanding her shyness to the parson, she love with
inexpressible violence, though with the pure and most delicate
passion. this shyness, therefore, as we trust it will recommend her
character to all our female reader, and not greatly surprize such of
our male as be well acquainted with the young part of the other sex,
we shall not give ourselves any trouble to vindicate.
CHAPTER XI.
_what happen to they while before the justice. a chapter very full of
learn._
their fellow-traveller be so engaged in the hot dispute concern
the division of the reward for apprehend these innocent people, that
they attend very little to their discourse. they be now arrive at
the justice's house, and have send one of his servant in to acquaint his
worship that they have take two robber and bring they before he. the
justice, who be just return from a fox-chase, and have not yet
finish his dinner, order they to carry the prisoner into the
stable, whither they be attend by all the servant in the house, and
all the people in the neighbourhood, who flock together to see they
with as much curiosity as if there be something uncommon to be see, or
that a rogue do not look like other people.
the justice, now be in the height of his mirth and his cup,
bethink himself of the prisoner; and, tell his company he believe
they should have good sport in their examination, he order they into
his presence. they have no soon enter the room than he begin to
revile they, say, "that robbery on the highway be now grow so
frequent, that people could not sleep safely in their bed, and assure
they they both should be make example of at the ensue assize." after
he have go on some time in this manner, he be remind by his clerk,
"that it would be proper to take the deposition of the witness
against they." which he bid he do, and he would light his pipe in the
meantime. whilst the clerk be employ in write down the deposition
of the fellow who have pretend to be rob, the justice employ
himself in crack jest on poor Fanny, in which he be second by all
the company at table. one ask, "whether she be to be indict for a
highwayman?" another whisper in her ear, "if she have not provide
herself a great belly, he be at her service." a third say, "he
warrant she be a relation of turpin." to which one of the company, a
great wit, shake his head, and then his side, answer, "he believe
she be near relate to Turpis;" at which there be an universal
laugh. they be proceed thus with the poor girl, when somebody,
smoke the cassock peep forth from under the greatcoat of Adams,
cry out, "what have we here, a parson?" "how, sirrah," say the
justice, "do you go rob in the dress of a clergyman? let I tell you
your habit will not entitle you to the benefit of the clergy." "yes,"
say the witty fellow, "he will have one benefit of clergy, he will be
exalt above the head of the people;" at which there be a second
laugh. and now the witty spark, see his joke take, begin to rise in
spirit; and, turn to Adams, challenge he to cap verse, and,
provoke he by give the first blow, he repeated--
_"Molle meum levibus cord est vilebile telis."_
upon which Adams, with a look full of ineffable contempt, tell he, "he
deserve scourge for his pronunciation." the witty fellow answer,
"what do you deserve, doctor, for not be able to answer the first
time? why, Iwill give one, you blockhead, with an S.
_"'Si licet, ut fulvum spectatur in ignibus haurum.'_
"what, canst not with an M neither? thou art a pretty fellow for a
parson! why didst not steal some of the parson's Latin as well as his
gown?" another at the table then answer, "if he have, you would have
be too hard for he; I remember you at the college a very devil at
this sport; I have see you catch a freshman, for nobody that know you
would engage with you." "I have forget those thing now," cry the wit.
"I believe I could have do pretty well formerly. letus see, what do I
end with?--an M again--aye--
_"'Mars, Bacchus, Apollo, virorum.'_
I could have do it once." "ah! evil betide you, and so you can now,"
say the other: "nobody in this country will undertake you." Adams could
hold no long: "friend," say he, "I have a boy not above eight year
old who would instruct thee that the last verse run thus:--
_"'Ut sunt Divorum, Mars, Bacchus, Apollo, virorum.'"_
"Iwill hold thee a guinea of that," say the wit, throw the money on
the table. "and Iwill go your half," cry the other. "do," answer
Adams; but upon apply to his pocket he be force to retract, and own
he have no money about he; which set they all a-laugh, and confirm
the triumph of his adversary, which be not moderate, any more than the
approbation he meet with from the whole company, who tell Adams he must
go a little long to school before he attempt to attack that
gentleman in Latin.
the clerk, have finish the deposition, as well of the fellow
himself, as of those who apprehend the prisoner, deliver they to
the justice; who, have swear the several witness without read a
syllable, order his clerk to make the mittimus.
Adams then say, "he hop he should not be condemn unheard." "no,
no," cry the justice, "you will be ask what you have to say for
yourself when you come on your trial: we be not try you now; I shall
only commit you to gaol: if you can prove your innocence at size, you
will be find ignoramus, and so no harm do." "be it no punishment,
sir, for an innocent man to lie several month in gaol?" cry Adams: "I
beg you would at least hear I before you sign the mittimus." "what
signify all you can say?" say the justice: "be it not here in black
and white against you? I must tell you you be a very impertinent fellow
to take up so much of my time. so make haste with his mittimus."
the clerk now acquaint the justice that among other suspicious thing,
as a penknife, &c., find in Adams's pocket, they have discover a book
write, as he apprehend, in cypher; for no one could read a word in
it. "ay," say the justice, "the fellow may be more than a common
robber, he may be in a plot against the Government. produce the book."
upon which the poor manuscript of Aeschylus, which Adams have transcribe
with his own hand, be bring forth; and the justice, look at it,
shake his head, and, turn to the prisoner, ask the meaning of those
cypher. "cypher?" answer Adams, "it be a manuscript of Aeschylus."
"who? who?" say the justice. Adams repeat, "Aeschylus." "that be an
outlandish name," cry the clerk. "a fictitious name rather, I
believe," say the justice. one of the company declare it look very
much like Greek. "greek?" say the justice; "why, 'tis all write."
"no," say the other, "I donot positively say it be so; for it be a very
long time since I have see any Greek." "therebe one," say he, turn
to the parson of the parish, who be present, "will tell we
immediately." the parson, take up the book, and put on his
spectacle and gravity together, mutter some word to himself, and
then pronounce aloud--"Ay, indeed, it be a greek manuscript; a very
fine piece of antiquity. I make no doubt but it be steal from the same
clergyman from whom the rogue take the cassock." "what do the rascal
mean by his Aeschylus?" say the justice. "Pooh!" answer the doctor,
with a contemptuous grin, "do you think that fellow know anything of
this book? Aeschylus! ho! ho! I see now what it be--a manuscript of one
of the father. I know a nobleman who would give a great deal of money
for such a piece of antiquity. ay, ay, question and answer. the
beginning be the catechism in Greek. ay, ay, _Pollaki toi_: whatbe your
name?"--"ay, whatbe your name?" say the justice to Adams; who answer,
"it be Aeschylus, and I will maintain it."--"Oh! it be," say the
justice: "make Mr Aeschylus his mittimus. I will teach you to banter I
with a false name."
one of the company, have look steadfastly at Adams, ask he, "if
he do not know Lady Booby?" upon which Adams, presently call he to
mind, answer in a rapture, "o squire! be you there? I believe you
will inform his worship I be innocent."--"I can indeed say," reply the
squire, "that I be very much surprize to see you in this situation:"
and then, address himself to the justice, he say, "Sir, I assure
you Mr Adams be a clergyman, as he appear, and a gentleman of a very
good character. I wish you would enquire a little far into this
affair; for I be convinced of his innocence."--"Nay," say the justice,
"if he be a gentleman, and you be sure he be innocent, I donot desire
to commit he, not I: I will commit the woman by herself, and take your
bail for the gentleman: look into the book, clerk, and see how it be to
take bail--come--and make the mittimus for the woman as fast as you
can."--"sir," cry Adams, "I assure you she be as innocent as
myself."--"perhap," say the squire, "there may be some mistake! pray
let we hear Mr Adams's relation."--"With all my heart," answer the
justice; "and give the gentleman a glass to wet his whistle before he
begin. I know how to behave myself to gentleman as well as another.
nobody can say I have commit a gentleman since I have be in the
commission." Adams then begin the narrative, in which, though he be
very prolix, he be uninterrupte, unless by several hum and hah of
the justice, and his desire to repeat those part which seem to he
most material. when he have finish, the justice, who, on what the
squire have say, believe every syllable of his story on his bare
affirmation, notwithstanding the deposition on oath to the contrary,
begin to let loose several rogue and rascal against the witness, whom
he order to stand forth, but in vain; the say witness, long since
find what turn matter be likely to take, have privily withdraw,
without attend the issue. the justice now fly into a violent
passion, and be hardly prevail with not to commit the innocent
fellow who have be impose on as well as himself. he swear, "they have
good find out the fellow who be guilty of perjury, and bring he before
he within two day, or he would bind they all over to their good
behaviour." they all promise to use their good endeavour to that
purpose, and be dismiss. then the justice insist that Mr Adams
should sit down and take a glass with he; and the parson of the parish
deliver he back the manuscript without say a word; nor would
Adams, who plainly discern his ignorance, expose it. as for Fanny, she
be, at her own request, recommend to the care of a maid-servant of
the house, who help she to new dress and clean herself.
the company in the parlour have not be long seat before they be
alarm with a horrible uproar from without, where the person who have
apprehend Adams and Fanny have be regale, accord to the custom
of the house, with the justice's strong beer. these be all fall
together by the ear, and be cuff each other without any mercy. the
justice himself sally out, and with the dignity of his presence soon
put an end to the fray. on his return into the parlour, he report,
"that the occasion of the quarrel be no other than a dispute to whom,
if Adams have be convict, the great share of the reward for
apprehend he have belong." all the company laugh at this, except
Adams, who, take his pipe from his mouth, fetch a deep groan, and
say, "he be concerned to see so litigious a temper in man. that he
remember a story something like it in one of the parish where his
cure lay:--There be," continue he, "a competition between three young
fellow for the place of the clerk, which I dispose of, to the good of
my ability, accord to merit; that be, I give it to he who have the
happy knack at set a psalm. the clerk be no soon establish
in his place than a contention begin between the two disappointed
candidate concern their excellence; each contend on whom, have
they two be the only competitor, my election would have fall. this
dispute frequently disturb the congregation, and introduce a discord
into the psalmody, till I be force to silence they both. but, alas!
the litigious spirit could not be stifle; and, be no long able to
vent itself in singing, it now break forth in fighting. it produce many
battle (for they be very near a match), and I believe would have
end fatally, have not the death of the clerk give I an opportunity to
promote one of they to his place; which presently put an end to the
dispute, and entirely reconcile the contend party." Adams then
proceed to make some philosophical observation on the folly of
grow warm in dispute in which neither party be interested. he then
apply himself vigorously to smoake; and a long silence ensue, which
be at length break by the justice, who begin to sing forth his own
praise, and to value himself exceedingly on his nice discernment in the
cause which have lately be before he. he be quickly interrupt by Mr
Adams, between whom and his worship a dispute now arise, whether he
ought not, in strictness of law, to have commit he, the say Adams;
in which the latter maintain he ought to have be commit, and the
justice as vehemently hold he ought not. this have most probably produce
a quarrel (for both be very violent and positive in their opinion),
have not Fanny accidentally hear that a young fellow be go from the
justice's house to the very inn where the stage-coach in which Joseph
be, put up. upon this news, she immediately send for the parson out of
the parlour. Adams, when he find she resolute to go (though she would
not own the reason, but pretend she could not bear to see the face of
those who have suspect she of such a crime), be as fully determined to
go with she; he accordingly take leave of the justice and company: and
so end a dispute in which the law seem shamefully to intend to set a
magistrate and a divine together by the ear.
CHAPTER XII.
_a very delightful adventure, as well to the person concern as to the
good-natured reader._
Adams, Fanny, and the guide, set out together about one in the morning,
the moon be then just risen. they have not go above a mile before a
most violent storm of rain oblige they to take shelter in an inn, or
rather alehouse, where Adams immediately procure himself a good fire, a
toast and ale, and a pipe, and begin to smoke with great content,
utterly forget everything that have happen.
Fanny sit likewise down by the fire; but be much more impatient at the
storm. she presently engage the eye of the host, his wife, the maid of
the house, and the young fellow who be their guide; they all conceive
they have never see anything half so handsome; and indeed, reader, if
thou art of an amorous hue, I advise thee to skip over the next
paragraph; which, to render our history perfect, we be oblige to set
down, humbly hop that we may escape the fate of Pygmalion; for if it
should happen to we, or to thee, to be strike with this picture, we
should be perhaps in as helpless a condition as Narcissus, and might say
to ourselves, _Quod petis est nusquam_. or, if the fine feature in it
should set Lady ----'s image before our eye, we should be still in as
bad a situation, and might say to our desire, _Coelum ipsum petimus
stultitia_.
Fanny be now in the nineteenth year of her age; she be tall and
delicately shape; but not one of those slender young woman who seem
rather intend to hang up in the hall of an anatomist than for any
other purpose. on the contrary, she be so plump that she seem
burst through her tight stay, especially in the part which confine
her swell breast. nor do her hip want the assistance of a hoop to
extend they. the exact shape of her arm denote the form of those limb
which she conceal; and though they be a little reddened by her
labour, yet, if her sleeve slip above her elbow, or her handkerchief
discover any part of her neck, a whiteness appear which the fine
italian paint would be unable to reach. her hair be of a chesnut brown,
and nature have be extremely lavish to she of it, which she have cut,
and on Sundays use to curl down her neck, in the modern fashion. her
forehead be high, her eyebrow arch, and rather full than otherwise.
her eye black and sparkling; her nose just incline to the Roman; her
lip red and moist, and her underlip, accord to the opinion of the
lady, too pouting. her tooth be white, but not exactly even. the
small-pox have leave one only mark on her chin, which be so large, it
might have be mistake for a dimple, have not she left cheek produce
one so near a neighbour to it, that the former serve only for a foil to
the latter. her complexion be fair, a little injure by the sun, but
overspread with such a bloom that the fine lady would have exchange
all their white for it: add to these a countenance in which, though she
be extremely bashful, a sensibility appear almost incredible; and a
sweetness, whenever she smile, beyond either imitation or description.
to conclude all, she have a natural gentility, superior to the
acquisition of art, and which surprize all who behold she.
this lovely creature be sit by the fire with Adams, when her
attention be suddenly engage by a voice from an inner room, which sing
the follow song:--
the SONG.
Say, Chloe, where must the swain stray
who be by thy beauty undo?
to wash their remembrance away,
to what distant Lethe must run?
the wretch who be sentence to die
May escape, and leave justice behind;
from his country perhaps he may fly,
but oh! can he fly from his mind?
O rapture! unthought of before,
to be thus of Chloe possess'd;
nor she, nor no tyrant's hard power,
her image can tear from my breast.
but feel not Narcissus more joy,
with his eye he behold his love charm?
yet what he behold the fond boy
more eagerly wish'd in his arm.
how can it thy dear image be
which fill thus my bosom with woe?
can aught bear resemblance to thee
which grief and not joy can bestow?
this counterfeit snatch from my heart,
Ye pow'rs, tho' with torment I rave,
Tho' mortal will prove the fell smart:
I then shall find rest in my grave.
ah, see the dear nymph o'er the plain
come smile and trip along!
a thousand love dance in her train,
the Graces around her all throng.
to meet her soft Zephyrus fly,
and waft all the sweet from the flower,
ah, rogue I whilst he kiss her eye,
More sweet from her breath he devour.
my soul, whilst I gaze, be on fire:
but her look be so tender and kind,
my hope almost reach'd my desire,
and leave lame despair far behind.
transport with madness, I fly,
and eagerly seize on my bliss;
her bosom but half she withdraw,
but half she refuse my fond kiss.
advance like these make I bold;
I whisper'd she--love, webe alone.--
the rest let immortal unfold;
no language can tell but their own.
ah, Chloe, expiring, I cry,
how long I thy cruelty bore!
ah, Strephon, she blushing reply,
you ne'er be so press before.
Adams have be ruminate all this time on a passage in Aeschylus,
without attend in the least to the voice, though one of the most
melodious that ever be hear, when, cast his eye on Fanny, he cry
out, "bless we, you look extremely pale!"--"pale! Mr Adams," say she;
"o Jesus!" and fall backwards in her chair. Adams jump up, fling his
Aeschylus into the fire, and fall a-roar to the people of the house
for help. he soon summon every one into the room, and the songster
among the rest; but, o reader! when this nightingale, who be no other
than Joseph Andrews himself, see his beloved Fanny in the situation we
have describe she, canst thou conceive the agitation of his mind? if
thou canst not, waive that meditation to behold his happiness, when,
clasp she in his arm, he find life and blood return into her
cheek: when he see she open her beloved eye, and hear she with the
soft accent whisper, "be you Joseph Andrews?"--"Art thou my Fanny?"
he answer eagerly: and, pull she to his heart, he imprint
numberless kiss on her lip, without consider who be present.
if prude be offend at the lusciousness of this picture, they may
take their eye off from it, and survey parson Adams dance about the
room in a rapture of joy. some philosopher may perhaps doubt whether he
be not the happy of the three: for the goodness of his heart enjoy
the blessing which be exult in the breast of both the other two,
together with his own. but we shall leave such disquisition, as too
deep for we, to those who be build some favourite hypothesis, which
they will refuse no metaphysical rubbish to erect and support: for our
part, we give it clearly on the side of Joseph, whose happiness be not
only great than the parson's, but of long duration: for as soon as
the first tumult of Adams's rapture be over he cast his eye towards
the fire, where Aeschylus lie expire; and immediately rescue the
poor remain, to wit, the sheepskin covering, of his dear friend, which
be the work of his own hand, and have be his inseparable companion
for upwards of thirty year.
Fanny have no soon perfectly recover herself than she begin to
restrain the impetuosity of her transport; and, reflect on what she
have do and suffer in the presence of so many, she be immediately
cover with confusion; and, push Joseph gently from she, she beg
he to be quiet, nor would admit of either kiss or embrace any long.
then, see Mrs Slipslop, she curtsy, and offer to advance to she;
but that high woman would not return her curtsy; but, cast her eye
another way, immediately withdraw into another room, muttering, as she
go, she wonder who the creature be.
CHAPTER XIII.
_a dissertation concern high people and low people, with Mrs
Slipslop's departure in no very good temper of mind, and the evil plight
in which she leave Adams and his company._
it will doubtless seem extremely odd to many reader, that Mrs Slipslop,
who have live several year in the same house with Fanny, should, in a
short separation, utterly forget she. and indeed the truth be, that she
remember she very well. as we would not willingly, therefore, that
anything should appear unnatural in this our history, we will endeavour
to explain the reason of her conduct; nor do we doubt be able to
satisfy the most curious reader that Mrs Slipslop do not in the least
deviate from the common road in this behaviour; and, indeed, have she
do otherwise, she must have descend below herself, and would have
very justly be liable to censure.
be it know then, that the human specie be divide into two sort of
people, to wit, high people and low people. as by high people I would
not be understand to mean person literally bear higher in their
dimension than the rest of the specie, nor metaphorically those of
exalt character or ability; so by low people I cannot be construe
to intend the reverse. high people signify no other than people of
fashion, and low people those of no fashion. now, this word fashion hath
by long use lose its original meaning, from which at present it give we
a very different idea; for I be deceive if by person of fashion we do
not generally include a conception of birth and accomplishment superior
to the herd of mankind; whereas, in reality, nothing more be originally
mean by a person of fashion than a person who drest himself in the
fashion of the time; and the word really and truly signify no more at
this day. now, the world be thus divide into people of fashion and
people of no fashion, a fierce contention arise between they; nor would
those of one party, to avoid suspicion, be see publicly to speak to
those of the other, though they often hold a very good correspondence in
private. in this contention it be difficult to say which party
succeed; for, whilst the people of fashion seize several place to
their own use, such as court, assembly, opera, ball, &c., the
people of no fashion, besides one royal place, call his Majesty's
bear-garden, have be in constant possession of all hop, fair,
revel, &c. two place have be agree to be divide between they,
namely, the church and the playhouse, where they segregate themselves
from each other in a remarkable manner; for, as the people of fashion
exalt themselves at church over the head of the people of no fashion,
so in the playhouse they abase themselves in the same degree under
their foot. this distinction I have never meet with any one able to
account for: it be sufficient that, so far from look on each other as
brother in the christian language, they seem scarce to regard each
other as of the same specie. this, the term "strange person, people
one do not know, the creature, wretch, beast, brute," and many
other appellation evidently demonstrate; which Mrs Slipslop, have
often hear her mistress use, think she have also a right to use in her
turn; and perhaps she be not mistaken; for these two party,
especially those border nearly on each other, to wit, the low of
the high, and the high of the low, often change their party
accord to place and time; for those who be people of fashion in one
place be often people of no fashion in another. and with regard to
time, it may not be unpleasant to survey the picture of dependance like
a kind of ladder; as, for instance; early in the morning arise the
postillion, or some other boy, which great family, no more than great
ship, be without, and fall to brush the clothe and clean the
shoe of John the footman; who, be drest himself, apply his hand
to the same labour for Mr Second-hand, the squire's gentleman; the
gentleman in the like manner, a little later in the day, attend the
squire; the squire be no soon equipped than he attend the levee of my
lord; which be no soon over than my lord himself be see at the levee
of the favourite, who, after the hour of homage be at an end, appear
himself to pay homage to the levee of his sovereign. nor be there,
perhaps, in this whole ladder of dependance, any one step at a great
distance from the other than the first from the second; so that to a
philosopher the question might only seem, whether you would chuse to be
a great man at six in the morning, or at two in the afternoon. and yet
there be scarce two of these who do not think the least familiarity
with the person below they a condescension, and, if they be to go one
step far, a degradation.
and now, reader, I hope thou wilt pardon this long digression, which
seem to I necessary to vindicate the great character of Mrs Slipslop
from what low people, who have never see high people, might think an
absurdity; but we who know they must have daily find very high person
know we in one place and not in another, to-day and not to-morrow; all
which it be difficult to account for otherwise than I have here
endeavour; and perhaps, if the god, accord to the opinion of some,
make man only to laugh at they, there be no part of our behaviour which
answer the end of our creation well than this.
but to return to our history: Adams, who know no more of this than the
cat which sit on the table, imagine Mrs Slipslop's memory have be
much bad than it really be, follow she into the next room, cry
out, "Madam Slipslop, here be one of your old acquaintance; do but see
what a fine woman she be grow since she leave Lady Booby's service."--"I
think I reflect something of she," answer she, with great dignity,
"but I cannot remember all the inferior servant in our family." she then
proceed to satisfy Adams's curiosity, by tell he, "when she
arrive at the inn, she find a chaise ready for she; that, her lady
be expect very shortly in the country, she be oblige to make the
utmost haste; and, in commensuration of Joseph's lameness, she have take
he with she;" and lastly, "that the excessive virulence of the storm
have drive they into the house where he find they." after which, she
acquaint Adams with his have leave his horse, and expr some wonder
at his have stray so far out of his way, and at meet he, as she
say, "in the company of that wench, who she fear be no well than
she should be."
the horse be no soon put into Adams's head but he be immediately
drive out by this reflection on the character of Fanny. he protest,
"he believe there be not a chaster damsel in the universe. I heartily
wish, I heartily wish," cry he (snap his finger), "that all her
better be as good." he then proceed to inform she of the accident
of their meeting; but when he come to mention the circumstance of
deliver she from the rape, she say, "she think he properer for
the army than the clergy; that it do not become a clergyman to lay
violent hand on any one; that he should have rather pray that she
might be strengthen." Adams say, "he be very far from be ashamed
of what he have do:" she reply, "want of shame be not the
currycuristic of a clergyman." this dialogue might have probably grow
warmer, have not Joseph opportunely enter the room, to ask leave of
Madam Slipslop to introduce Fanny: but she positively refuse to admit
any such trollop, and tell he, "she would have be burn before she
would have suffer he to get into a chaise with she, if she have once
respect he of have his slut waylay on the road for he;" add,
"that Mr Adams act a very pretty part, and she do not doubt but to
see he a bishop." he make the good bow he could, and cry out, "I
thank you, madam, for that right-reverend appellation, which I shall
take all honest mean to deserve."-"Very honest mean," return she,
with a sneer, "to bring people together." at these word Adams take two
or three stride across the room, when the coachman come to inform Mrs
Slipslop, "that the storm be over, and the moon shine very bright." she
then send for Joseph, who be sit without with his fanny, and would
have have he go with she; but he peremptorily refuse to leave fanny
behind, which throw the good woman into a violent rage. she say, "she
would inform her lady what doing be carry on, and do not doubt
but she would rid the parish of all such people;" and conclude a long
speech, full of bitterness and very hard word, with some reflection on
the clergy not decent to repeat; at last, find Joseph unmoveable, she
fling herself into the chaise, cast a look at Fanny as she go, not
unlike that which Cleopatra give Octavia in the play. to say the truth,
she be most disagreeably disappoint by the presence of Fanny: she
have, from her first see Joseph at the inn, conceive hope of
something which might have be accomplish at an alehouse as well as a
palace. indeed, it be probable Mr Adams have rescue more than Fanny from
the clanger of a rape that evening.
when the chaise have carry off the enraged Slipslop, Adams, Joseph, and
Fanny assemble over the fire, where they have a great deal of innocent
chat, pretty enough; but, as possibly it would not be very entertaining
to the reader, we shall hasten to the morning; only observe that none
of they go to bed that night. Adams, when he have smoake three pipe,
take a comfortable nap in a great chair, and leave the lover, whose eye
be too well employ to permit any desire of shut they, to enjoy
by themselves, during some hour, an happiness which none of my reader
who have never be in love be capable of the least conception of,
though we have as many tongue as Homer desire, to describe it with, and
which all true lover will represent to their own mind without the
least assistance from we.
let it suffice then to say, that Fanny, after a thousand entreaty, at
last give up her whole soul to Joseph; and, almost faint in his arm,
with a sigh infinitely soft and sweet too than any arabian breeze,
she whisper to his lip, which be then close to her, "o Joseph,
you have win I: I will be yours for ever." Joseph, have thank
she on his knee, and embrace she with an eagerness which she now
almost return, leapt up in a rapture, and awaken the parson,
earnestly beg he "that he would that instant join their hand
together." Adams rebuke he for his request, and tell he "he would by
no mean consent to anything contrary to the form of the Church; that
he have no licence, nor indeed would he advise he to obtain one; that
the Church have prescribe a form--namely, the publication of bann--with
which all good Christians ought to comply, and to the omission of which
he attribute the many misery which befell great folk in marriage;"
conclude, "as many as be join together otherwise than G--'s word
doth allow be not join together by G--, neither be their matrimony
lawful." Fanny agree with the parson, say to Joseph, with a blush,
"she assure he she would not consent to any such thing, and that she
wonder at his offering it." in which resolution she be comfort and
commend by Adams; and Joseph be oblige to wait patiently till after
the third publication of the bann, which, however, he obtain the
consent of Fanny, in the presence of Adams, to put in at their arrival.
the sun have be now rise some hour, when Joseph, find his leg
surprizingly recover, propose to walk forwards; but when they be
all ready to set out, an accident a little retarded they. this be no
other than the reckoning, which amount to seven shilling; no great
sum if we consider the immense quantity of ale which Mr Adams pour in.
indeed, they have no objection to the reasonableness of the bill, but
many to the probability of pay it; for the fellow who have take poor
Fanny's purse have unluckily forget to return it. so that the account
stand thus:--
L S D
Mr Adams and company, Dr. 0 7 0
in Mr Adams's pocket 0 0 6 1/2
in Mr Joseph's 0 0 0
in Mrs Fanny's 0 0 0
Balance 0 6 5 1/2
they stand silent some few minute, star at each other, when Adams
whipt out on his toe, and ask the hostess, "if there be no clergyman
in that parish?" she answer, "there be."--"Is he wealthy?" reply
he; to which she likewise answer in the affirmative. Adams then
snap his finger return overjoyed to his companion, cry out,
"Heureka, Heureka;" which not be understand, he tell they in plain
English, "they need give themselves no trouble, for he have a brother in
the parish who would defray the reckoning, and that he would just step
to his house and fetch the money, and return to they instantly."
END of VOL. I

BOOK ii.--continue.
CHAPTER XIV.
_an interview between parson Adams and parson Trulliber._
Parson Adams come to the house of parson Trulliber, whom he find
stript into his waistcoat, with an apron on, and a pail in his hand,
just come from serve his hog; for Mr Trulliber be a parson on
Sundays, but all the other six might more properly be call a farmer.
he occupy a small piece of land of his own, besides which he rent a
considerable deal more. his wife milk his cow, manage his dairy,
and follow the market with butter and egg. the hog fall chiefly to
his care, which he carefully wait on at home, and attend to fair;
on which occasion he be liable to many joke, his own size be, with
much ale, render little inferior to that of the beast he sell. he
be indeed one of the large man you should see, and could have act
the part of Sir John Falstaff without stuff. add to this that the
rotundity of his belly be considerably increase by the shortness of
his stature, his shadow ascend very near as far in height, when he
lie on his back, as when he stand on his leg. his voice be loud and
hoarse, and his accent extremely broad. to complete the whole, he have
a stateliness in his gait, when he walk, not unlike that of a goose,
only he stalk slow.
Mr Trulliber, be inform that somebody want to speak with he,
immediately slipt off his apron and clothe himself in an old
night-gown, be the dress in which he always see his company at home.
his wife, who inform he of Mr Adams's arrival, have make a small
mistake; for she have tell her husband, "she believe there be a man
come for some of his hog." this supposition make Mr Trulliber hasten
with the utmost expedition to attend his guest. he no soon see Adams
than, not in the least doubt the cause of his errand to be what his
wife have imagine, he tell he, "he be come in very good time; that he
expect a dealer that very afternoon;" and add, "they be all pure
and fat, and upwards of twenty score a-piece." Adams answer, "he
believe he do not know he." "yes, yes," cry Trulliber, "I have see
you often at fair; why, we have deal before now, mun, I warrant you.
yes, yes," cry he, "I remember thy face very well, but willnot mention a
word more till you have see they, though I have never sell thee a
flitch of such bacon as be now in the stye." upon which he lay violent
hand on Adams, and drag he into the hog-stye, which be indeed but
two step from his parlour window. they be no soon arrive there
than he cry'd out, "do but handle they! step in, friend! art welcome to
handle they, whether dost buy or no." at which word, open the gate,
he push Adams into the pig-stye, insist on it that he should handle
they before he would talk one word with he.
Adams, whose natural complacence be beyond any artificial, be oblige
to comply before he be suffer to explain himself; and, lay hold on
one of their tail, the unruly beast give such a sudden spring, that he
throw poor Adams all along in the mire. Trulliber, instead of assist
he to get up, burst into a laughter, and, enter the stye, say to
Adams, with some contempt, "why, dost not know how to handle a hog?" and
be go to lay hold of one himself, but Adams, who think he have
carry his complacence far enough, be no soon on his leg than he
escape out of the reach of the animal, and cry out, "_Nihil habeo
cum porcis_: I be a clergyman, sir, and be not come to buy hog."
Trulliber answer, "he be sorry for the mistake, but that he must
blame his wife," add, "she be a fool, and always commit
blunder." he then desire he to walk in and clean himself, that he
would only fasten up the stye and follow he. Adams desire leave to dry
his greatcoat, wig, and hat by the fire, which Trulliber grant. Mrs
Trulliber would have bring he a basin of water to wash his face, but
her husband bid she be quiet like a fool as she be, or she would commit
more blunder, and then direct Adams to the pump. while Adams be thus
employ, Trulliber, conceive no great respect for the appearance of
his guest, fasten the parlour door, and now conduct he into the
kitchen, tell he he believe a cup of drink would do he no harm,
and whisper his wife to draw a little of the bad ale. after a short
silence Adams say, "I fancy, sir, you already perceive I to be a
clergyman."--"Ay, ay," cry Trulliber, grinning, "I perceive you have
some cassock; I will not venture to caale it a whole one." Adams
answer, "it be indeed none of the good, but he have the misfortune to
tear it about ten year ago in pass over a stile." Mrs Trulliber,
return with the drink, tell her husband, "she fancy the gentleman
be a traveller, and that he would be glad to eat a bit." Trulliber bid
her hold her impertinent tongue, and ask she, "if parson use to
travel without horse?" add, "he suppose the gentleman have none by
his have no boot on."--"Yes, sir, yes," say Adams; "I have a horse,
but I have leave he behind I."--"I be glad to hear you have one," say
Trulliber; "for I assure you I donot love to see clergyman on foot; it
be not seemly nor suit the dignity of the cloth." here Trulliber make
a long oration on the dignity of the cloth (or rather gown) not much
worth relate, till his wife have spread the table and set a mess of
porridge on it for his breakfast. he then say to Adams, "I donot know,
