friend, how you come to caale on I; however, as you be here, if you
think proper to eat a morsel, you may." Adams accept the invitation,
and the two parson sit down together; Mrs Trulliber wait behind her
husband's chair, as be, it seem, her custom. trulliber eat heartily,
but scarce put anything in his mouth without find fault with his
wife's cookery. all which the poor woman bear patiently. indeed, she be
so absolute an admirer of her husband's greatness and importance, of
which she have frequent hint from his own mouth, that she almost carry
her adoration to an opinion of his infallibility. to say the truth, the
parson have exercise she more way than one; and the pious woman have so
well edify by her husband's sermon, that she have resolve to receive
the bad thing of this world together with the good. she have indeed be
at first a little contentious; but he have long since get the well;
partly by her love for this, partly by her fear of that, partly by her
religion, partly by the respect he pay himself, and partly by that
which he receive from the parish. she have, in short, absolutely
submit, and now worship her husband, as Sarah do Abraham, call
he (not lord, but) master. whilst they be at table her husband give
she a fresh example of his greatness; for, as she have just deliver a
cup of ale to Adams, he snatch it out of his hand, and, cry out, "I
caal'd vurst," swallow down the ale. Adams deny it; it be refer
to the wife, who, though her conscience be on the side of Adams, durst
not give it against her husband; upon which he say, "no, sir, no; I
should not have be so rude to have take it from you if you have caal'd
vurst, but Iwould have you know Ibe a well man than to suffer the good he
in the kingdom to drink before I in my own house when I caale vurst."
as soon as their breakfast be end, Adams begin in the following
manner: "I think, sir, it be high time to inform you of the business of
my embassy. I be a traveller, and be pass this way in company with
two young people, a lad and a damsel, my parishioner, towards my own
cure; we stopt at a house of hospitality in the parish, where they
direct I to you as have the cure."--"Though I be but a curate,"
say Trulliber, "I believe I be as warm as the vicar himself, or perhaps
the rector of the next parish too; I believe I could buy they
both."--"sir," cry Adams, "I rejoice thereat. now, sir, my business
be, that we be by various accident stript of our money, and be not
able to pay our reckoning, be seven shilling. I therefore request
you to assist I with the loan of those seven shilling, and also seven
shilling more, which, peradventure, I shall return to you; but if not,
I be convinced you will joyfully embrace such an opportunity of lay
up a treasure in a well place than any this world afford."
suppose a stranger, who enter the chamber of a lawyer, be imagine
a client, when the lawyer be prepare his palm for the fee, should
pull out a writ against he. suppose an apothecary, at the door of a
chariot contain some great doctor of eminent skill, should, instead
of direction to a patient, present he with a potion for himself.
suppose a minister should, instead of a good round sum, treat my lord
----, or sir ----, or esq. ---- with a good broomstick. suppose a civil
companion, or a lead captain, should, instead of virtue, and honour, and
beauty, and part, and admiration, thunder vice, and infamy, and
ugliness, and folly, and contempt, in his patron's ear. suppose, when a
tradesman first carry in his bill, the man of fashion should pay it;
or suppose, if he do so, the tradesman should abate what he have
overcharge, on the supposition of wait. in short--suppose what you
will, you never can nor will suppose anything equal to the astonishment
which seize on Trulliber, as soon as Adams have end his speech. a
while he roll his eye in silence; sometimes survey Adams, then his
wife; then cast they on the ground, then lift they up to heaven.
at last he burst forth in the following accent: "Sir, I believe I know
where to lay up my little treasure as well as another. I thank g--, if I
be not so warm as some, I be content; that be a blessing great than
rich; and he to whom that be give need ask no more. to be content
with a little be great than to possess the world; which a man may
possess without be so. lay up my treasure! what matter where a man's
treasure be whose heart be in the scripture? there be the treasure of a
Christian." at these word the water run from Adams's eye; and,
catch Trulliber by the hand in a rapture, "Brother," say he,
"heaven bless the accident by which I come to see you! I would have
walk many a mile to have commune with you; and, believe I, I will
shortly pay you a second visit; but my friend, I fancy, by this time,
wonder at my stay; so let I have the money immediately." Trulliber then
put on a stern look, and cry out, "Thou dost not intend to rob I?" at
which the wife, burst into tear, fall on her knee and roar out,
"o dear sir! for Heaven's sake donot rob my master; we be but poor
people." "get up, for a fool as thou art, and go about thy business,"
say trulliber; "dost think the man will venture his life? he be a
beggar, and no robber." "very true, indeed," answer Adams. "I wish,
with all my heart, the tithing-man be here," cry Trulliber; "I would
have thee punish as a vagabond for thy impudence. fourteen shilling
indeed! I willnot give thee a farthing. I believe thou art no more a
clergyman than the woman there" (point to his wife); "but if thou
art, dost deserve to have thy gown stript over thy shoulder for run
about the country in such a manner." "I forgive your suspicion," say
Adams; "but suppose I be not a clergyman, I be nevertheless thy brother;
and thou, as a Christian, much more as a clergyman, art oblige to
relieve my distress." "dost preach to I?" reply Trulliber; "dost
pretend to instruct I in my duty?" "ifack, a good story," cry Mrs
Trulliber, "to preach to my master." "silence, woman," cry Trulliber.
"I would have thee know, friend" (address himself to Adams), "I shall
not learn my duty from such as thee. I know what charity be, well than
to give to vagabond." "besides, if we be incline, the poor's rate
oblige we to give so much charity," cry the wife. "Pugh! thou art a
fool. Poor's reate! hold thy nonsense," answer Trulliber; and then,
turn to Adams, he tell he, "he would give he nothing." "I be
sorry," answer Adams, "that you do know what charity be, since you
practise it no well: I must tell you, if you trust to your knowledge
for your justification, you will find yourself deceive, though you
should add faith to it, without good work." "fellow," cry Trulliber,
"dost thou speak against faith in my house? get out of my door: I will
no long remain under the same roof with a wretch who speak wantonly
of faith and the scripture." "name not the scripture," say Adams.
"how! not name the scripture! do you disbelieve the scripture?" cry
Trulliber. "no; but you do," answer Adams, "if I may reason from your
practice; for their command be so explicit, and their reward and
punishment so immense, that it be impossible a man should stedfastly
believe without obey. now, there be no command more express, no duty
more frequently enjoin, than charity. whoever, therefore, be void of
charity, I make no scruple of pronounce that he be no Christian." "I
would not advise thee," say Trulliber, "to say that I be no Christian:
I willnot take it of you; for I believe I be as good a man as thyself"
(and indeed, though he be now rather too corpulent for athletic
exercise, he have, in his youth, be one of the good boxer and
cudgel-player in the county). his wife, see he clench his fist,
interpose, and beg he not to fight, but show himself a true
Christian, and take the law of he. as nothing could provoke Adams to
strike, but an absolute assault on himself or his friend, he smile at
the angry look and gesture of Trulliber; and, tell he he be sorry
to see such man in order, depart without further ceremony.
CHAPTER XV.
_an adventure, the consequence of a new instance which parson Adams give
of his forgetfulness._
when he come back to the inn he find Joseph and Fanny sit together.
they be so far from think his absence long, as he have fear they
would, that they never once miss or think of he. indeed, I have
be often assure by both, that they spend these hour in a most
delightful conversation; but, as I never could prevail on either to
relate it, so I cannot communicate it to the reader.
Adams acquaint the lover with the ill success of his enterprize. they
be all greatly confound, none be able to propose any method of
depart, till Joseph at last advise call in the hostess, and
desire she to trust they; which Fanny say she despair of her doing,
as she be one of the sourest-face woman she have ever behold.
but she be agreeably disappoint; for the hostess be no soon ask
the question than she readily agree; and, with a curtsy and smile,
wish they a good journey. however, lest Fanny's skill in physiognomy
should be call in question, we will venture to assign one reason
which might probably incline she to this confidence and good-humour.
when Adams say he be go to visit his brother, he have unwittingly
impose on Joseph and Fanny, who both believe he have mean his natural
brother, and not his brother in divinity, and have so inform the
hostess, on her enquiry after he. now Mr Trulliber have, by his
profession of piety, by his gravity, austerity, reserve, and the
opinion of his great wealth, so great an authority in his parish, that
they all live in the utmost fear and apprehension of he. it be
therefore no wonder that the hostess, who know it be in his option
whether she should ever sell another mug of drink, do not dare to
affront his supposed brother by deny he credit.
they be now just on their departure when Adams recollect he have leave
his greatcoat and hat at Mr Trulliber's. as he be not desirous of
renew his visit, the hostess herself, have no servant at home,
offer to fetch it.
this be an unfortunate expedient; for the hostess be soon undeceived
in the opinion she have entertain of Adams, whom Trulliber abuse in
the gross term, especially when he hear he have have the assurance to
pretend to be his near relation.
at her return, therefore, she entirely change her note. she say,
"folk might be ashamed of travel about, and pretend to be what
they be not. that taxis be high, and for her part she be oblige to
pay for what she have; she could not therefore possibly, nor would she,
trust anybody; no, not her own father. that money be never scarcer, and
she want to make up a sum. that she expect, therefore, they should
pay their reckoning before they leave the house."
Adams be now greatly perplexed; but, as he know that he could easily
have borrow such a sum in his own parish, and as he know he would have
lend it himself to any mortal in distress, so he take fresh courage, and
sally out all round the parish, but to no purpose; he return as
pennyless as he go, groan and lament that it be possible, in a
country profess Christianity, for a wretch to starve in the midst of
his fellow-creature who abound.
whilst he be go, the hostess, who stay as a sort of guard with
Joseph and Fanny, entertain they with the goodness of parson
Trulliber. and, indeed, he have not only a very good character as to
other quality in the neighbourhood, but be repute a man of great
charity; for, though he never give a farthing, he have always that word
in his mouth.
Adams be no soon return the second time than the storm grow
exceedingly high, the hostess declare, among other thing, that, if
they offer to stir without pay she, she would soon overtake they
with a warrant.
Plato and Aristotle, or somebody else, hath say, _that when the most
exquisite cunning fail, chance often hit the mark, and that by mean
the least expect_. Virgil express this very boldly:--
_Turne, quod optanti divum promittere nemo
Auderet, volvenda die, en! attulit ultro._
I would quote more great man if I could; but my memory not permit
I, I will proceed to exemplify these observation by the follow
instance:--
there chance (for Adams have not cunne enough to contrive it) to be at
that time in the alehouse a fellow who have be formerly a drummer in an
irish regiment, and now travel the country as a pedlar. this man,
have attentively listen to the discourse of the hostess, at last
take Adams aside, and ask he what the sum be for which they be
detain. as soon as he be inform, he sigh, and say, "he be sorry
it be so much; for that he have no more than six shilling and sixpence
in his pocket, which he would lend they with all his heart." Adams give
a caper, and cry'd out, "it would do; for that he have sixpence himself."
and thus these poor people, who could not engage the compassion of
rich and piety, be at length deliver out of their distress by the
charity of a poor pedlar.
I shall refer it to my reader to make what observation he please on
this incident: it be sufficient for I to inform he that, after Adams
and his companion have return he a thousand thank, and tell he
where he might call to be repay, they all sally out of the house
without any compliment from their hostess, or indeed without pay she
any; Adams declare he would take particular care never to call there
again; and she on her side assure they she want no such guest.
CHAPTER XVI.
_a very curious adventure, in which Mr Adams give a much great
instance of the honest simplicity of his heart, than of his experience
in the way of this world._
our traveller have walk about two mile from that inn, which they have
more reason to have mistake for a castle than Don Quixote ever have any
of those in which he sojourn, see they have meet with such difficulty
in escape out of its wall, when they come to a parish, and beheld a
sign of invitation hang out. a gentleman sit smoake a pipe at the
door, of whom Adams inquire the road, and receive so courteous and
oblige an answer, accompany with so smile a countenance, that the
good parson, whose heart be naturally disposed to love and affection,
begin to ask several other question; particularly the name of the
parish, and who be the owner of a large house whose front they then have
in prospect. the gentleman answer as obligingly as before; and as to
the house, acquaint he it be his own. he then proceed in the
following manner: "Sir, I presume by your habit you be a clergyman; and
as you be travel on foot I suppose a glass of good beer will not be
disagreeable to you; and I can recommend my landlord's within as some of
the good in all this country. what say you, will you halt a little and
let we take a pipe together? there be no well tobacco in the kingdom."
this proposal be not displease to Adams, who have allay his thirst
that day with no well liquor than what Mrs Trulliber's cellar have
produce; and which be indeed little superior, either in richness or
flavour, to that which distil from those grain her generous husband
bestow on his hog. have, therefore, abundantly thank the
gentleman for his kind invitation, and bid Joseph and Fanny follow he,
he enter the alehouse, where a large loaf and cheese and a pitcher of
beer, which truly answer the character give of it, be set before
they, the three traveller fall to eating, with appetite infinitely
more voracious than be to be find at the most exquisite eating-house
in the parish of St. James's.
the gentleman express great delight in the hearty and cheerful
behaviour of Adams; and particularly in the familiarity with which he
converse with Joseph and Fanny, whom he often call his child; a
term he explain to mean no more than his parishioner; say, "he
look on all those whom God have intrust to his care to stand to he
in that relation." the gentleman, shake he by the hand, highly
applaud those sentiment. "they be, indeed," say he, "the true
principle of a christian divine; and I heartily wish they be
universal; but, on the contrary, I be sorry to say the parson of our
parish, instead of esteem his poor parishioner as a part of his
family, seem rather to consider they as not of the same specie with
himself. he seldom speak to any, unless some few of the rich of
we; nay, indeed, he will not move his hat to the other. I often laugh
when I behold he on Sundays strut along the churchyard like a
turkey-cock through row of his parishioner, who bow to he with as
much submission, and be as unregarde, as a set of servile courtier
by the proud prince in Christendom. but if such temporal pride be
ridiculous, surely the spiritual be odious and detestable; if such a
puff--up empty human bladder, strut in princely robe, justly
move one's derision, surely in the habit of a priest it must raise
our scorn."
"Doubtless," answer Adams, "your opinion be right; but I hope such
example be rare. the clergy whom I have the honour to know maintain a
different behaviour; and you will allow I, sir, that the readiness
which too many of the laity show to contemn the order may be one reason
of their avoid too much humility." "very true, indeed," say the
gentleman; "I find, sir, you be a man of excellent sense, and be happy
in this opportunity of know you; perhaps our accidental meeting may
not be disadvantageous to you neither. at present I shall only say to
you that the incumbent of this living be old and infirm, and that it be
in my gift. doctor, give I your hand; and assure yourself of it at his
decease." Adams tell he, "he be never more confound in his life than
at his utter incapacity to make any return to such noble and unmerited
generosity." "a mere trifle, sir," cry the gentleman, "scarce worth
your acceptance; a little more than three hundred a year. I wish it be
double the value for your sake." Adams bow, and cry from the
emotion of his gratitude; when the other ask he, "if he be marry,
or have any child, besides those in the spiritual sense he have
mention." "Sir," reply the parson, "I have a wife and six at your
service." "that be unlucky," say the gentleman; "for I would otherwise
have take you into my own house as my chaplain; however, I have another
in the parish (for the parsonage-house be not good enough), which I will
furnish for you. pray, do your wife understand a dairy?" "I cannot
profess she do," say Adams. "I be sorry for it," quoth the gentleman;
"I would have give you half-a-dozen cow, and very good ground to have
maintain they." "Sir," say Adams, in an ecstasy, "you be too
liberal; indeed you be." "not at all," cry the gentleman: "I esteem
rich only as they give I an opportunity of do good; and I never
see one whom I have a great inclination to serve." at which word he
shake he heartily by the hand, and tell he he have sufficient room in
his house to entertain he and his friend. Adams beg he might give
he no such trouble; that they could be very well accommodate in the
house where they be; forget they have not a sixpenny piece among
they. the gentleman would not be deny; and, inform himself how far
they be travel, he say it be too long a journey to take on foot,
and beg that they would favour he by suffer he to lend they a
servant and horse; add, withal, that, if they would do he the
pleasure of their company only two day, he would furnish they with his
coach and six. Adams, turn to Joseph, say, "how lucky be this
gentleman's goodness to you, who I be afraid would be scarce able to
hold out on your lame leg!" and then, address the person who make he
these liberal promise, after much bowing, he cry out, "blessed be the
hour which first introduce I to a man of your charity! you be indeed
a Christian of the true primitive kind, and an honour to the country
wherein you live. I would willingly have take a pilgrimage to the Holy
Land to have behold you; for the advantage which we draw from your
goodness give I little pleasure, in comparison of what I enjoy for your
own sake when I consider the treasure you be by these mean lay up
for yourself in a country that passeth not away. we will therefore, most
generous sir, accept your goodness, as well the entertainment you have
so kindly offer we at your house this evening, as the accommodation of
your horse to-morrow morning." he then begin to search for his hat, as
do Joseph for his; and both they and Fanny be in order of departure,
when the gentleman, stop short, and seem to meditate by himself
for the space of about a minute, exclaim thus: "sure never anything
be so unlucky; I have forget that my house-keeper be go abroad, and
hath lock up all my room; indeed, I would break they open for you,
but shall not be able to furnish you with a bed; for she have likewise
put away all my linen. I be glad it enter into my head before I have
give you the trouble of walk there; besides, I believe you will find
well accommodation here than you expected.--landlord, you can provide
good bed for these people, cannot you?" "yes, and please your worship,"
cry the host, "and such as no lord or justice of the peace in the
kingdom need be ashamed to lie in." "I be heartily sorry," say the
gentleman, "for this disappointment. I be resolve I will never suffer
she to carry away the key again." "pray, sir, let it not make you
uneasy," cry Adams; "we shall do very well here; and the loan of your
horse be a favour we shall be incapable of make any return to." "ay!"
say the squire, "the horse shall attend you here at what hour in the
morning you please;" and now, after many civility too tedious to
enumerate, many squeeze by the hand, with most affectionate look and
smile at each other, and after appoint the horse at seven the next
morning, the gentleman take his leave of they, and depart to his own
house. Adams and his companion return to the table, where the parson
smoake another pipe, and then they all retire to rest.
Mr Adams rise very early, and call Joseph out of his bed, between whom
a very fierce dispute ensue, whether Fanny should ride behind Joseph,
or behind the gentleman's servant; Joseph insist on it that he be
perfectly recover, and be as capable of take care of Fanny as any
other person could be. but Adams would not agree to it, and declare he
would not trust she behind he; for that he be weak than he imagine
himself to be.
this dispute continue a long time, and have begin to be very hot, when a
servant arrive from their good friend, to acquaint they that he be
unfortunately prevent from lend they any horse; for that his groom
have, unknown to he, put his whole stable under a course of physic.
this advice presently strike the two disputant dumb: Adams cry out,
"be ever anything so unlucky as this poor gentleman? I protest I be
more sorry on his account than my own. you see, Joseph, how this
good-natured man be treat by his servant; one lock up his linen,
another physics his horse, and I suppose, by his be at this house
last night, the butler have lock up his cellar. bless we! how
good-nature be use in this world! I protest I be more concerned on his
account than my own." "so be not I," cry Joseph; "not that I be much
trouble about walk on foot; all my concern be, how we shall get out
of the house, unless God send another pedlar to redeem we. but
certainly this gentleman have such an affection for you, that he would
lend you a large sum than we owe here, which be not above four or five
shilling." "very true, child," answer Adams; "I will write a letter
to he, and will even venture to solicit he for three half-crown;
there will be no harm in have two or three shilling in our pocket;
as we have full forty mile to travel, we may possibly have occasion for
they."
Fanny be now risen, Joseph pay she a visit, and leave Adams to
write his letter, which have finish, he despatch a boy with it to
the gentleman, and then seat himself by the door, light his pipe,
and betook himself to meditation.
the boy stay long than seem to be necessary, Joseph, who with
Fanny be now return to the parson, express some apprehension that
the gentleman's steward have lock up his purse too. to which Adams
answer, "it might very possibly be, and he should wonder at no
liberty which the devil might put into the head of a wicked servant
to take with so worthy a master;" but add, "that, as the sum be so
small, so noble a gentleman would be easily able to procure it in the
parish, though he have it not in his own pocket. indeed," say he, "if
it be four or five guineas, or any such large quantity of money, it
might be a different matter."
they be now sit down to breakfast over some toast and ale, when the
boy return and inform they that the gentleman be not at home. "very
well!" cry Adams; "but why, child, do you not stay till his return?
go back again, my good boy, and wait for his come home; he cannot be
go far, as his horse be all sick; and besides, he have no intention
to go abroad, for he invite we to spend this day and tomorrow at his
house. therefore go back, child, and tarry till his return home." the
messenger depart, and be back again with great expedition, bring
an account that the gentleman be go a long journey, and would not be
at home again this month. at these word Adams seem greatly
confound, say, "this must be a sudden accident, as the sickness or
death of a relation or some such unforeseen misfortune;" and then,
turn to Joseph, cry, "I wish you have remind I to have borrow
this money last night." Joseph, smile, answer, "he be very much
deceive if the gentleman would not have find some excuse to avoid
lending it.--i own," say he, "I be never much pleased with his
professing so much kindness for you at first sight; for I have hear the
gentleman of our cloth in London tell many such story of their
master. but when the boy bring the message back of his not be at
home, I presently know what would follow; for, whenever a man of fashion
doth not care to fulfil his promise, the custom be to order his
servant that he will never be at home to the person so promise. in
London they call it deny he. I have myself deny Sir Thomas Booby
above a hundred time, and when the man hath dance attendance for about
a month or sometimes long, he be acquaint in the end that the
gentleman be go out of town and could do nothing in the
business."--"Good Lord!" say Adams, "what wickedness be there in the
christian world! I profess almost equal to what I have read of the
heathen. but surely, Joseph, your suspicion of this gentleman must be
unjust, for what a silly fellow must he be who would do the devil's work
for nothing! and canst thou tell I any interest he could possibly
propose to himself by deceive we in his professions?"--"It be not for
I," answer Joseph, "to give reason for what man do, to a gentleman
of your learning."--"you say right," quoth Adams; "knowledge of man be
only to be learn from book; Plato and Seneca for that; and those be
author, I be afraid, child, you never read."--"Not I, sir, truly,"
answer Joseph; "all I know be, it be a maxim among the gentleman of
our cloth, that those master who promise the most perform the least;
and I have often hear they say they have find the large vail in
those family where they be not promise any. but, sir, instead of
consider any far these matter, it would be our wise way to
contrive some method of get out of this house; for the generous
gentleman, instead of do we any service, hath leave we the whole
reckon to pay." Adams be go to answer, when their host come in,
and, with a kind of jeer smile, say, "well, master! the squire hath
not send his horse for you yet. laud help I! how easily some folk
make promises!"--"How!" say Adams; "have you ever know he do anything
of this kind before?"--"Ay! marry have I," answer the host: "it be no
business of mine, you know, sir, to say anything to a gentleman to his
face; but now he be not here, I will assure you, he hath not his fellow
within the three next market-town. I own I could not help laugh when
I hear he offer you the living, for thereby hang a good jest. I
think he would have offer you my house next, for one be no more his
to dispose of than the other." at these word Adams, blessing himself,
declare, "he have never read of such a monster. but what vex I most,"
say he, "be, that he hath decoy we into run up a long debt with
you, which we be not able to pay, for we have no money about we, and,
what be bad, live at such a distance, that if you should trust we, I
be afraid you would lose your money for want of our find any
conveniency of send it."--"trust you, master!" say the host, "that I
will with all my heart. I honour the clergy too much to deny trust
one of they for such a trifle; besides, I like your fear of never pay
I. I have lose many a debt in my lifetime, but be promise to be pay
they all in a very short time. I will score this reckoning for the
novelty of it. it be the first, I do assure you, of its kind. but what
say you, master, shall we have t'other pot before we part? it will waste
but a little chalk more, and if you never pay I a shill the loss
will not ruin I." Adams like the invitation very well, especially as
it be deliver with so hearty an accent. he shake his host by the
hand, and thank he, say, "he would tarry another pot rather for the
pleasure of such worthy company than for the liquor;" add, "he be
glad to find some Christians leave in the kingdom, for that he almost
begin to suspect that he be sojourn in a country inhabit only by
Jews and Turks."
the kind host produce the liquor, and Joseph with Fanny retire into
the garden, where, while they solace themselves with amorous discourse,
Adams sit down with his host; and, both fill their glass, and
light their pipe, they begin that dialogue which the reader will
find in the next chapter.
CHAPTER XVII.
_a dialogue between Mr Abraham Adams and his host, which, by the
disagreement in their opinion, seem to threaten an unlucky
catastrophe, have it not be timely prevent by the return of
the lover._
"Sir," say the host, "I assure you you be not the first to whom our
squire hath promise more than he hath perform. he be so famous for
this practice, that his word will not be take for much by those who
know he. I remember a young fellow whom he promise his parent to make
an exciseman. the poor people, who could ill afford it, breed their son
to writing and account, and other learning to qualify he for the
place; and the boy hold up his head above his condition with these
hope; nor would he go to plough, nor to any other kind of work, and
go constantly drest as fine as could be, with two clean Holland shirt
a week, and this for several year; till at last he follow the squire
up to London, think there to mind he of his promise; but he could
never get sight of he. so that, be out of money and business, he
fall into evil company and wicked course; and in the end come to a
sentence of transportation, the news of which break the mother's
heart.--I will tell you another true story of he. there be a neighbour
of mine, a farmer, who have two son whom he breed up to the business.
pretty lad they be. nothing would serve the squire but that the
young must be make a parson. upon which he persuade the father to
send he to school, promise that he would afterwards maintain he at
the university, and, when he be of a proper age, give he a living. but
after the lad have be seven year at school, and his father bring he
to the squire, with a letter from his master that he be fit for the
university, the squire, instead of mind his promise, or send he
thither at his expense, only tell his father that the young man be a
fine scholar, and it be pity he could not afford to keep he at Oxford
for four or five year more, by which time, if he could get he a
curacy, he might have he ordain. the farmer say, 'he be not a man
sufficient to do any such thing.'--'Why, then,' answer the squire, 'I
be very sorry you have give he so much learning; for, if he cannot get
his living by that, it will rather spoil he for anything else; and your
other son, who can hardly write his name, will do more at plough and
sowing, and be in a well condition, than he.' and indeed so it prove;
for the poor lad, not find friend to maintain he in his learning,
as he have expect, and be unwilling to work, fall to drinking,
though he be a very sober lad before; and in a short time, partly with
grief, and partly with good liquor, fall into a consumption, and
died.--Nay, I can tell you more still: there be another, a young woman,
and the handsome in all this neighbourhood, whom he entice up to
London, promise to make she a gentlewoman to one of your woman of
quality; but, instead of keep his word, we have since hear, after
have a child by she himself, she become a common whore; then keep a
coffeehouse in Covent Garden; and a little after die of the french
distemper in a gaol.--I could tell you many more story; but how do you
imagine he serve I myself? you must know, sir, I be breed a seafaring
man, and have be many voyage; till at last I come to be master of a
ship myself, and be in a fair way of make a fortune, when I be
attack by one of those curse guarda-costa who take our ship before
the beginning of the war; and after a fight, wherein I lose the great
part of my crew, my rigging be all demolish, and two shot receive
between wind and water, I be force to strike. the villain carry off
my ship, a brigantine of 150 ton--a pretty creature she be--and put I,
a man, and a boy, into a little bad pink, in which, with much ado, we at
last make Falmouth; though I believe the Spaniards do not imagine she
could possibly live a day at sea. upon my return hither, where my wife,
who be of this country, then live, the squire tell I he be so
pleased with the defence I have make against the enemy, that he do not
fear get I promote to a lieutenancy of a man-of-war, if I would
accept of it; which I thankfully assure he I would. well, sir, two or
three year pass, during which I have many repeat promise, not only
from the squire, but (as he tell I) from the lord of the admiralty. he
never return from London but I be assure I might be satisfied now,
for I be certain of the first vacancy; and, what surprize I still,
when I reflect on it, these assurance be give I with no less
confidence, after so many disappointment, than at first. at last, sir,
grow weary, and somewhat suspicious, after so much delay, I write to
a friend in London, who I know have some acquaintance at the good house
in the admiralty, and desire he to back the squire's interest; for
indeed I fear he have solicit the affair with more coldness than he
pretend. and what answer do you think my friend send I? truly, sir,
he acquaint I that the squire have never mention my name at the
admiralty in his life; and, unless I have much faithfuller interest,
advise I to give over my pretension; which I immediately do, and,
with the concurrence of my wife, resolve to set up an alehouse, where
you be heartily welcome; and so my service to you; and may the squire,
and all such sneak rascal, go to the devil together."--"O fie!" say
Adams, "o fie! he be indeed a wicked man; but g-- will, I hope, turn his
heart to repentance. Nay, if he could but once see the meanness of this
detestable vice; would he but once reflect that he be one of the most
scandalous as well as pernicious lyar; sure he must despise himself to
so intolerable a degree, that it would be impossible for he to continue
a moment in such a course. and to confess the truth, notwithstanding the
baseness of this character, which he hath too well deserve, he hath in
his countenance sufficient symptom of that _bona indole_, that
sweetness of disposition, which furnish out a good Christian."--"Ah,
master! master!" say the host, "if you have travel as far as I have,
and converse with the many nation where I have trade, you would not
give any credit to a man's countenance. symptom in his countenance,
quotha! I would look there, perhaps, to see whether a man have the
small-pox, but for nothing else." he speak this with so little regard to
the parson's observation, that it a good deal nettle he; and, take
the pipe hastily from his mouth, he thus answer: "Master of mine,
perhaps I have travel a great deal far than you without the
assistance of a ship. do you imagine sailing by different city or
country be travel? no.
"Caelum non animum mutant qui trans mare currunt.
"I can go far in an afternoon than you in a twelvemonth. what, I
suppose you have see the Pillars of Hercules, and perhaps the wall of
Carthage. Nay, you may have hear Scylla, and see Charybdis; you may
have enter the closet where Archimedes be find at the taking of
Syracuse. I suppose you have sail among the cyclade, and pass the
famous strait which take their name from the unfortunate Helle, whose
fate be sweetly describe by Apollonius Rhodius; you have pass the
very spot, I conceive, where Daedalus fall into that sea, his waxen
wing be melt by the sun; you have traverse the Euxine sea, I make
no doubt; nay, you may have be on the bank of the Caspian, and call
at Colchis, to see if there be ever another golden fleece." "not I,
truly, master," answer the host: "I never touch at any of these
place."--"but I have be at all these," reply Adams. "then, I
suppose," cry the host, "you have be at the East Indies; for there
be no such, I will be swear, either in the West or the Levant."--"Pray
wherebe the Levant?" quoth Adams; "that should be in the East Indies by
right." "Oho! you be a pretty traveller," cry the host, "and not know
the Levant! my service to you, master; you must not talk of these thing
with I! you must not tip we the traveller; it willnot go here." "since
thou art so dull to misunderstand I still," quoth Adams, "I will inform
thee; the travel I mean be in book, the only way of travel by
which any knowledge be to be acquire. from they I learn what I assert
just now, that nature generally imprint such a portraiture of the mind
in the countenance, that a skilful physiognomist will rarely be
deceive. I presume you have never read the story of Socrates to this
purpose, and therefore I will tell it you. a certain physiognomist
assert of Socrates, that he plainly discover by his feature that he
be a rogue in his nature. a character so contrary to the tenour of all
this great man's action, and the generally receive opinion concern
he, incense the boy of Athens so that they throw stone at the
physiognomist, and would have demolish he for his ignorance, have not
Socrates himself prevent they by confess the truth of his
observation, and acknowledge that, though he correct his
disposition by philosophy, he be indeed naturally as incline to vice
as have be predicate of he. now, pray resolve I--how should a man
know this story if he have not read it?" "well, master," say the host,
"and what signify it whether a man know it or no? he who go abroad,
as I have do, will always have opportunity enough of know the
world without trouble his head with Socrates, or any such fellow."
"friend," cry Adams, "if a man should sail round the world, and anchor
in every harbour of it, without learn, he would return home as
ignorant as he go out." "Lord help you!" answer the host; "there be
my boatswain, poor fellow! he could scarce either write or read, and yet
he would navigate a ship with any master of a man-of-war; and a very
pretty knowledge of trade he have too." "trade," answer Adams, "as
Aristotle prove in his first chapter of Politics, be below a
philosopher, and unnatural as it be manage now." the host look
stedfastly at Adams, and after a minute's silence ask he, "if he be
one of the writer of the Gazetteers? for I have hear," say he, "they
be writ by parson." "gazetteer!" answer Adams, "what be that?" "it
be a dirty newspaper," reply the host, "which hath be give away all
over the nation for these many year, to abuse trade and honest man,
which I would not suffer to lye on my table, though it hath be offer
I for nothing." "not I truly," say Adams; "I never write anything but
sermon; and I assure you I be no enemy to trade, whilst it be
consistent with honesty; nay, I have always look on the tradesman as a
very valuable member of society, and, perhaps, inferior to none but the
man of learn." "no, I believe he be not, nor to he neither,"
answer the host. "of what use would learn be in a country without
trade? what would all you parson do to clothe your back and feed your
belly? who fetch you your silk, and your linen, and your wine,
and all the other necessary of life? I speak chiefly with regard to
the sailor." "you should say the extravagancy of life," reply the
parson; "but admit they be the necessary, there be something more
necessary than life itself, which be provide by learn; I mean the
learning of the clergy. who clothe you with piety, meekness, humility,
charity, patience, and all the other christian virtue? who feed your
soul with the milk of brotherly love, and diet they with all the
dainty food of holiness, which at once cleanse they of all impure
carnal affection, and fatten they with the truly rich spirit of grace?
who doth this?" "ay, who, indeed?" cry the host; "for I do not
remember ever to have see any such clothing or such feeding. and so, in
the mean time, master, my service to you." Adams be go to answer
with some severity, when Joseph and Fanny return and press his
departure so eagerly that he would not refuse they; and so, grasp his
crabstick, he take leave of his host (neither of they be so well
pleased with each other as they have be at their first sit down
together), and with Joseph and Fanny, who both express much
impatience, depart, and now all together renew their journey.
BOOK III.
CHAPTER I.
_Matter prefatory in praise of biography._
notwithstanding the preference which may be vulgarly give to the
authority of those romance writer who entitle their book "the history
of England, the history of France, of Spain, &c.," it be most certain
that truth be to be find only in the work of those who celebrate the
life of great man, and be commonly call biographer, as the other
should indeed be term topographer, or chorographer; word which
might well mark the distinction between they; it be the business of
the latter chiefly to describe country and city, which, with the
assistance of map, they do pretty justly, and may be depend upon; but
as to the action and character of man, their writing be not quite so
authentic, of which there need no other proof than those eternal
contradiction occur between two topographer who undertake the
history of the same country: for instance, between my Lord Clarendon and
Mr Whitelocke, between Mr Echard and Rapin, and many other; where,
fact be set forth in a different light, every reader believe as he
please; and, indeed, the more judicious and suspicious very justly
esteem the whole as no other than a romance, in which the writer hath
indulge a happy and fertile invention. but though these widely differ
in the narrative of fact; some ascribe victory to the one, and other
to the other party; some represent the same man as a rogue, while
other give he a great and honest character; yet all agree in the scene
where the fact be suppose to have happen, and where the person, who
be both a rogue and an honest man, live. now with we biographer the
case be different; the fact we deliver may be rely on, though we
often mistake the age and country wherein they happen: for, though it
may be worth the examination of critic, whether the shepherd
Chrysostom, who, as Cervantes inform we, die for love of the fair
Marcella, who hat he, be ever in Spain, will any one doubt but that
such a silly fellow hath really exist? be there in the world such a
sceptic as to disbelieve the madness of Cardenio, the perfidy of
Ferdinand, the impertinent curiosity of Anselmo, the weakness of
Camilla, the irresolute friendship of Lothario? though perhaps, as to
the time and place where those several person live, that good
historian may be deplorably deficient. but the most known instance of
this kind be in the true history of Gil Blas, where the inimitable
biographer hath make a notorious blunder in the country of Dr Sangrado,
who use his patient as a vintner doth his wine-vessel, by let out
their blood, and fill they up with water. Doth not every one, who be
the least verse in physical history, know that Spain be not the
country in which this doctor live? the same writer hath likewise err
in the country of his archbishop, as well as that of those great
personage whose understanding be too sublime to taste anything but
tragedy, and in many other. the same mistake may likewise be observe
in Scarron, the Arabian Nights, the history of Marianne and le Paisan
Parvenu, and perhaps some few other writer of this class, whom I have
not read, or do not at present recollect; for I would by no mean be
think to comprehend those person of surprize genius, the author of
immense romance, or the modern novel and Atalantis writer; who,
without any assistance from nature or history, record person who never
be, or will be, and fact which never do, nor possibly can, happen;
whose hero be of their own creation, and their brain the chaos
whence all their material be select. not that such writer deserve
no honour; so far otherwise, that perhaps they merit the high; for
what can be noble than to be as an example of the wonderful extent of
human genius? one may apply to they what Balzac say of Aristotle, that
they be a second nature (for they have no communication with the first;
by which, author of an inferior class, who cannot stand alone, be
oblige to support themselves as with crutch); but these of whom I be
now speak seem to be possess of those stilt, which the excellent
Voltaire tell we, in his letter, "carry the genius far off, but with
an regular pace." indeed, far out of the sight of the reader,
beyond the realm of Chaos and old Night.
but to return to the former class, who be content to copy nature,
instead of form original from the confused heap of matter in their
own brain, be not such a book as that which record the achievement of
the renowned Don Quixote more worthy the name of a history than even
Mariana's: for, whereas the latter be confine to a particular period of
time, and to a particular nation, the former be the history of the world
in general, at least that part which be polish by law, art, and
science; and of that from the time it be first polished to this day;
nay, and forwards as long as it shall so remain?
I shall now proceed to apply these observation to the work before we;
for indeed I have set they down principally to obviate some
construction which the good nature of mankind, who be always forward
to see their friend' virtue record, may put to particular part. I
question not but several of my reader will know the lawyer in the
stage-coach the moment they hear his voice. it be likewise odd but the
wit and the prude meet with some of their acquaintance, as well as all
the rest of my character. to prevent, therefore, any such malicious
application, I declare here, once for all, I describe not man, but
manner; not an individual, but a species. perhaps it will be answer,
be not the character then take from life? to which I answer in the
affirmative; nay, I believe I might aver that I have writ little more
than I have see. the lawyer be not only alive, but hath be so these
four thousand year; and I hope G-- will indulge his life as many yet to
come. he hath not indeed confine himself to one profession, one
religion, or one country; but when the first mean selfish creature
appear on the human stage, who make self the centre of the whole
creation, would give himself no pain, incur no danger, advance no money,
to assist or preserve his fellow-creature; then be our lawyer bear;
and, whilst such a person as I have describe exist on earth, so long
shall he remain upon it. it be, therefore, do he little honour to
imagine he endeavour to mimick some little obscure fellow, because he
happen to resemble he in one particular feature, or perhaps in his
profession; whereas his appearance in the world be calculate for much
more general and noble purpose; not to expose one pitiful wretch to the
small and contemptible circle of his acquaintance; but to hold the glass
to thousand in their closet, that they may contemplate their
deformity, and endeavour to reduce it, and thus by suffer private
mortification may avoid public shame. this place the boundary between,
and distinguish the satirist from the libeller: for the former
privately correct the fault for the benefit of the person, like a
parent; the latter publickly expose the person himself, as an example
to other, like an executioner.
there be besides little circumstance to be consider; as the drapery
of a picture, which though fashion vary at different time, the
resemblance of the countenance be not by those mean diminish. thus I
believe we may venture to say Mrs Tow-wouse be coeval with our lawyer:
and, though perhaps, during the change which so long an existence must
have pass through, she may in her turn have stand behind the bar at an
inn, I will not scruple to affirm she hath likewise in the revolution of
age sit on a throne. in short, where extreme turbulency of temper,
avarice, and an insensibility of human misery, with a degree of
hypocrisy, have unite in a female composition, Mrs Tow-wouse be that
woman; and where a good inclination, eclipse by a poverty of spirit and
understanding, hath glimmer forth in a man, that man hath be no
other than her sneak husband.
I shall detain my reader no long than to give he one caution more of
an opposite kind: for, as in most of our particular character we mean
not to lash individual, but all of the like sort, so, in our general
description, we mean not universal, but would be understand with many
exception: for instance, in our description of high people, we cannot
be intend to include such as, whilst they be an honour to their high
rank, by a well-guide condescension make their superiority as easy as
possible to those whom fortune chiefly hath place below they. of this
number I could name a peer no less elevate by nature than by fortune;
who, whilst he wear the noble ensign of honour on his person, bear
the true stamp of dignity on his mind, adorn with greatness,
enrich with knowledge, and embellish with genius. I have see this
man relieve with generosity, while he hath converse with freedom, and
be to the same person a patron and a companion. I could name a commoner,
raise higher above the multitude by superior talent than be in the
power of his prince to exalt he, whose behaviour to those he hath
oblige be more amiable than the obligation itself; and who be so great
a master of affability, that, if he could divest himself of an inherent
greatness in his manner, would often make the low of his acquaintance
forget who be the master of that palace in which they be so
courteously entertain. these be picture which must be, I believe,
know: I declare they be take from the life, and not intend to
exceed it. by those high people, therefore, whom I have describe, I
mean a set of wretch, who, while they be a disgrace to their
ancestor, whose honour and fortune they inherit (or perhaps a great
to their mother, for such degeneracy be scarce credible), have the
insolence to treat those with disregard who be at least equal to the
founder of their own splendor. it be, I fancy, impossible to conceive a
spectacle more worthy of our indignation, than that of a fellow, who be
not only a blot in the escutcheon of a great family, but a scandal to
the human specie, maintain a supercilious behaviour to man who be
an honour to their nature and a disgrace to their fortune.
and now, reader, take these hint along with you, you may, if you
please, proceed to the sequel of this our true history.
CHAPTER II.
_a night scene, wherein several wonderful adventure befel Adams and his
fellow-traveller._
it be so late when our traveller leave the inn or alehouse (for it
might be call either), that they have not travel many mile before
night overtake they, or meet they, which you please. the reader must
excuse I if I be not particular as to the way they take; for, as we be
now draw near the seat of the Boobies, and as that be a ticklish
name, which malicious person may apply, accord to their evil
inclination, to several worthy country squire, a race of man whom we
look upon as entirely inoffensive, and for whom we have an adequate
regard, we shall lend no assistance to any such malicious purpose.
Darkness have now overspread the hemisphere, when Fanny whisper Joseph
"that she beg to rest herself a little; for that she be so tired
she could walk no far." Joseph immediately prevail with parson
Adams, who be as brisk as a bee, to stop. he have no soon seat
himself than he lament the loss of his dear Aeschylus; but be a
little comfort when remind that, if he have it in his possession, he
could not see to read.
the sky be so clouded, that not a star appear. it be indeed,
accord to Milton, darkness visible. this be a circumstance, however,
very favourable to Joseph; for Fanny, not suspicious of be oversee
by Adams, give a loose to her passion which she have never do before,
and, recline her head on his bosom, throw her arm carelessly round
he, and suffer he to lay his cheek close to her. all this infuse
such happiness into Joseph, that he would not have change his turf for
the fine down in the fine palace in the universe.
Adams sit at some distance from the lover, and, be unwilling to
disturb they, apply himself to meditation; in which he have not
spend much time before he discover a light at some distance that
seem approach towards he. he immediately hail it; but, to his
sorrow and surprize, it stop for a moment, and then disappear.
he then call to Joseph, ask he, "if he have not see the light?"
Joseph answer, "he have."--"And do you not mark how it vanish?"
return he: "though I be not afraid of ghost, I do not absolutely
disbelieve they."
he then enter into a meditation on those unsubstantial being; which
be soon interrupt by several voice, which he think almost at his
elbow, though in fact they be not so extremely near. however, he could
distinctly hear they agree on the murder of any one they meet; and a
little after hear one of they say, "he have kill a dozen since that
day fortnight."
Adams now fall on his knee, and commit himself to the care of
Providence; and poor Fanny, who likewise hear those terrible word,
embrace Joseph so closely, that have not he, whose ear be also open,
be apprehensive on her account, he would have think no danger which
threaten only himself too dear a price for such embrace.
Joseph now draw forth his penknife, and Adams, have finish his
ejaculation, grasp his crab-stick, his only weapon, and, come up to
Joseph, would have have he quit Fanny, and place she in the rear; but
his advice be fruitless; she cling close to he, not at all regard
the presence of Adams, and in a soothe voice declare, "she would die
in his arm." Joseph, clasp she with inexpressible eagerness,
whisper she, "that he prefer death in her to life out of they."
Adams, brandish his crabstick, say, "he despise death as much as
any man," and then repeat aloud--
"Est hic, est animus lucis contemptor et illum,
Qui vita bene credat emi quo tendis, honorem."
upon this the voice cease for a moment, and then one of they call
out, "D--n you, who be there?" to which Adams be prudent enough to make
no reply; and of a sudden he observe half-a-dozen light, which seem
to rise all at once from the ground and advance briskly towards he.
this he immediately conclude to be an apparition; and now, begin to
conceive that the voice be of the same kind, he call out, "in the
name of the L--d, what wouldst thou have?" he have no soon speak than
he hear one of the voice cry out, "D--n they, here they come;" and
soon after hear several hearty blow, as if a number of man have be
engage at quarterstaff. he be just advance towards the place of
combat, when Joseph, catch he by the skirt, beg he that they
might take the opportunity of the dark to convey away Fanny from the
danger which threaten she. he presently comply, and, Joseph lifting
up Fanny, they all three make the good of their way; and without look
behind they, or be overtake, they have travel full two mile, poor
Fanny not once complain of be tired, when they see afar off
several light scatter at a small distance from each other, and at the
same time find themselves on the descent of a very steep hill. Adams's
foot slip, he instantly disappear, which greatly frighten both
Joseph and Fanny: indeed, if the light have permit they to see it,
they would scarce have refrain laugh to see the parson roll down
the hill; which he do from top to bottom, without receive any harm.
he then hollow as loud as he could, to inform they of his safety, and
relieve they from the fear which they have conceive for he. Joseph and
Fanny halt some time, consider what to do; at last they advance a
few pace, where the declivity seem least steep; and then Joseph,
take his fanny in his arm, walk firmly down the hill, without
make a false step, and at length land she at the bottom, where Adams
soon come to they.
learn hence, my fair countrywoman, to consider your own weakness, and
the many occasion on which the strength of a man may be useful to you;
and, duly weigh this, take care that you match not yourself with
the spindle-shank beaus and _petit-maitre_ of the age, who, instead
of be able, like Joseph Andrews, to carry you in lusty arm through
the rugged way and downhill steep of life, will rather want to support
their feeble limb with your strength and assistance.
our traveller now move forwards where the near light present
itself; and, have cross a common field, they come to a meadow, where
they seem to be at a very little distance from the light, when, to
their grief, they arrive at the bank of a river. Adams here make a
full stop, and declare he could swim, but doubt how it be possible
to get Fanny over: to which Joseph answer, "if they walk along its
bank, they might be certain of soon find a bridge, especially as by
the number of light they might be assure a parish be near." "Odso,
thatbe true indeed," say Adams; "I do not think of that."
accordingly, Joseph's advice be take, they pass over two meadow,
and come to a little orchard, which lead they to a house. Fanny beg of
Joseph to knock at the door, assure he "she be so weary that she
could hardly stand on her foot." Adams, who be foremost, perform this
ceremony; and, the door be immediately open, a plain kind of man
appear at it: Adams acquaint he "that they have a young woman with
they who be so tired with her journey that he should be much oblige to
he if he would suffer she to come in and rest herself." the man, who
see Fanny by the light of the candle which he hold in his hand,
perceive her innocent and modest look, and have no apprehension
from the civil behaviour of Adams, presently answer, "that the young
woman be very welcome to rest herself in his house, and so be her
company." he then usher they into a very decent room, where his wife
be sit at a table: she immediately rise up, and assist they in
set forth chair, and desire they to sit down; which they have no
soon do than the man of the house ask they if they would have
anything to refresh themselves with? Adams thank he, and answer he
should be oblige to he for a cup of his ale, which be likewise choose
by Joseph and Fanny. whilst he be go to fill a very large jug with
this liquor, his wife tell Fanny she seem greatly fatigued, and
desire she to take something strong than ale; but she refuse with
many thank, say it be true she be very much tired, but a little
rest she hop would restore she. as soon as the company be all
seat, Mr Adams, who have fill himself with ale, and by public
permission have light his pipe, turn to the master of the house,
ask he, "if evil spirit do not use to walk in that neighbourhood?"
to which receive no answer, he begin to inform he of the adventure
which they meet with on the down; nor have he proceed far in the story
when somebody knock very hard at the door. the company express some
amazement, and Fanny and the good woman turn pale: her husband go
forth, and whilst he be absent, which be some time, they all remain
silent, look at one another, and hear several voice discourse
pretty loudly. Adams be fully persuade that spirit be abroad, and
begin to meditate some exorcism; Joseph a little inclined to the same
opinion; Fanny be more afraid of man; and the good woman herself begin
to suspect her guest, and imagine those without be rogue belong
to their gang. at length the master of the house return, and,
laughing, tell Adams he have discover his apparition; that the
murderer be sheep-stealer, and the twelve person murder be no
other than twelve sheep; add, that the shepherd have get the well
of they, have secure two, and be proceed with they to a justice of
peace. this account greatly relieve the fear of the whole company; but
Adams mutter to himself, "he be convince of the truth of apparition
for all that."
they now sit chearfully round the fire, till the master of the house,
have survey his guest, and conceive that the cassock, which,
have fall down, appear under Adams's greatcoat, and the shabby
livery on Joseph Andrews, do not well suit with the familiarity
between they, begin to entertain some suspicion not much to their
advantage: address himself therefore to Adams, he say, "he
perceive he be a clergyman by his dress, and suppose that honest man
be his footman." "Sir," answer Adams, "I be a clergyman at your
service; but as to that young man, whom you have rightly term honest,
he be at present in nobody's service; he never live in any other
family than that of Lady Booby, from whence he be discharge, I assure
you, for no crime." Joseph say, "he do not wonder the gentleman be
surprize to see one of Mr Adams's character condescend to so much
goodness with a poor man."--"Child," say Adams, "I should be ashamed
of my cloth if I think a poor man, who be honest, below my notice or
my familiarity. I know not how those who think otherwise can profess
themselves follower and servant of he who make no distinction,
unless, peradventure, by prefer the poor to the rich.--Sir," say
he, address himself to the gentleman, "these two poor young people
be my parishioner, and I look on they and love they as my child.
there be something singular enough in their history, but I have not now
time to recount it." the master of the house, notwithstanding the
simplicity which discover itself in Adams, know too much of the world
to give a hasty belief to profession. he be not yet quite certain
that Adams have any more of the clergyman in he than his cassock. to
try he therefore far, he ask he, "if Mr Pope have lately
publish anything new?" Adams answer, "he have hear great
commendation of that poet, but that he have never read nor know any of
his work."--"Ho! ho!" say the gentleman to himself, "have I catch
you? what!" say he, "have you never see his Homer?" Adams answer,
"he have never read any translation of the classick." "why, truly,"
reply'd the gentleman, "there be a dignity in the greek language which
I think no modern tongue can reach."--"Do you understand Greek, sir?"
say Adams hastily. "a little, sir," answer the gentleman. "do you
know, sir," cry'd Adams, "where I can buy an Aeschylus? an unlucky
misfortune lately happen to mine." Aeschylus be beyond the
gentleman, though he know he very well by name; he therefore,
return back to Homer, ask Adams, "what part of the Iliad he
think most excellent?" Adams return, "his question would be
properer, what kind of beauty be the chief in poetry? for that Homer
be equally excellent in they all. and, indeed," continue he, "what
Cicero say of a complete orator may well be apply to a great poet:
'he ought to comprehend all perfection.' Homer do this in the most
excellent degree; it be not without reason, therefore, that the
philosopher, in the twenty-second chapter of his Poeticks, mention he
by no other appellation than that of the Poet. he be the father of the
drama as well as the epic; not of tragedy only, but of comedy also; for
his Margites, which be deplorably lose, bore, say Aristotle, the same
analogy to comedy as his Odyssey and Iliad to tragedy. to he,
therefore, we owe Aristophanes as well as euripide, Sophocles, and my
poor Aeschylus. but if you please we will confine ourselves (at least
for the present) to the Iliad, his noble work; though neither
Aristotle nor Horace give it the preference, as I remember, to the
Odyssey. first, then, as to his subject, can anything be more simple,
and at the same time more noble? he be rightly praise by the first of
those judicious critic for not chuse the whole war, which, though he
say it hath a complete beginning and end, would have be too great
for the understanding to comprehend at one view. I have, therefore,
often wonder why so correct a writer as Horace should, in his epistle
to Lollius, call he the Trojani Belli Scriptorem. secondly, his
action, term by Aristotle, Pragmaton Systasis; be it possible for the
mind of man to conceive an idea of such perfect unity, and at the same
time so replete with greatness? and here I must observe, what I do not
remember to have see note by any, the Harmotton, that agreement of
his action to his subject: for, as the subject be anger, how agreeable
be his action, which be war; from which every incident arise and to
which every episode immediately relate. thirdly, his manner, which
Aristotle place second in his description of the several part of
tragedy, and which he say be include in the action; I be at a loss
whether I should rather admire the exactness of his judgment in the
nice distinction or the immensity of his imagination in their variety.
for, as to the former of these, how accurately be the sedate, injure
resentment of Achilles, distinguish from the hot, insult passion
of Agamemnon! how widely doth the brutal courage of Ajax differ from
the amiable bravery of Diomedes; and the wisdom of Nestor, which be the
result of long reflection and experience, from the cunning of Ulysses,
the effect of art and subtlety only! if we consider their variety, we
may cry out, with Aristotle in his 24th chapter, that no part of this
divine poem be destitute of manner. indeed, I might affirm that there
be scarce a character in human nature untouched in some part or other.
and, as there be no passion which he be not able to describe, so be
there none in his reader which he cannot raise. if he hath any superior
excellence to the rest, I have be inclined to fancy it be in the
pathetic. I be sure I never read with dry eye the two episode where
Andromache be introduce in the former lament the danger, and in the
latter the death, of Hector. the image be so extremely tender in
these, that I be convince the poet have the worthy and good heart
imaginable. nor can I help observe how Sophocles fall short of the
beauty of the original, in that imitation of the dissuasive speech of
Andromache which he hath put into the mouth of Tecmessa. and yet
Sophocles be the great genius who ever write tragedy; nor have any
of his successor in that art, that be to say, neither euripide nor
Seneca the tragedian, be able to come near he. as to his sentiment
and diction, I need say nothing; the former be particularly remarkable
for the utmost perfection on that head, namely, propriety; and as to
the latter, Aristotle, whom doubtless you have read over and over, be
very diffuse. I shall mention but one thing more, which that great
critic in his division of tragedy call Opsis, or the scenery; and
which be as proper to the epic as to the drama, with this difference,
that in the former it fall to the share of the poet, and in the latter
to that of the painter. but do ever painter imagine a scene like that
in the 13th and 14th iliad? where the reader see at one view the
prospect of Troy, with the army draw up before it; the grecian army,
camp, and fleet; Jupiter sit on Mount Ida, with his head wrapt in a
cloud, and a thunderbolt in his hand, look towards Thrace; Neptune
drive through the sea, which divide on each side to permit his
passage, and then seat himself on Mount Samos; the heaven open,
and the deity all seat on their throne. this be sublime! this be
poetry!" Adams then rapt out a hundred greek verse, and with such a
voice, emphasis, and action, that he almost frighten the woman; and
as for the gentleman, he be so far from entertain any further
suspicion of Adams, that he now doubt whether he have not a bishop in
his house. he run into the most extravagant encomium on his learning;
and the goodness of his heart begin to dilate to all the stranger. he
say he have great compassion for the poor young woman, who look pale
and faint with her journey; and in truth he conceive a much high
opinion of her quality than it deserve. he say he be sorry he could
not accommodate they all; but if they be content with his fireside,
he would sit up with the man; and the young woman might, if she
please, partake his wife's bed, which he advise she to; for that they
must walk upwards of a mile to any house of entertainment, and that not
very good neither. Adams, who like his seat, his ale, his tobacco, and
his company, persuade Fanny to accept this kind proposal, in which
sollicitation he be second by Joseph. nor be she very difficultly
prevail on; for she have sleep little the last night and not at all
the precede; so that love itself be scarce able to keep her eye
open any long. the offer, therefore, be kindly accept, the good
woman produce everything eatable in her house on the table, and the
guest, be heartily invite, as heartily regale themselves,
especially parson Adams. as to the other two, they be example of the
truth of that physical observation, that love, like other sweet thing,
be no whetter of the stomach.
Supper be no soon end, than Fanny at her own request retire, and
the good woman bear her company. the man of the house, Adams, and
Joseph, who would modestly have withdraw, have not the gentleman
insist on the contrary, draw round the fireside, where Adams (to use
his own word) replenish his pipe, and the gentleman produce a bottle
of excellent beer, be the good liquor in his house.
the modest behaviour of Joseph, with the gracefulness of his person, the
character which Adams give of he, and the friendship he seem to
entertain for he, begin to work on the gentleman's affection, and
raise in he a curiosity to know the singularity which Adams have
mention in his history. this curiosity Adams be no soon informed of
than, with Joseph's consent, he agree to gratify it; and accordingly
relate all he know, with as much tenderness as be possible for the
character of Lady Booby; and conclude with the long, faithful, and
mutual passion between he and Fanny, not conceal the meanness of her
birth and education. these latter circumstance entirely cure a
jealousy which have lately rise in the gentleman's mind, that Fanny be
the daughter of some person of fashion, and that Joseph have run away
with she, and Adams be concern in the plot. he be now enamoured of
his guest, drink their health with great chearfulness, and return
many thank to Adams, who have spend much breath, for he be a
circumstantial teller of a story.
Adams tell he it be now in his power to return that favour; for his
extraordinary goodness, as well as that fund of literature he be master
of,[a] which he do not expect to find under such a roof, have raise in
he more curiosity than he have ever know. "therefore," say he, "if it
be not too troublesome, sir, your history, if you please."
[a] the author hath by some be represent to have make a blunder
here: for Adams have indeed show some learning (say they), perhaps
all the author have; but the gentleman hath show none, unless his
approbation of Mr Adams be such: but surely it would be preposterous
in he to call it so. I have, however, notwithstanding this
criticism, which I be tell come from the mouth of a great orator in
a public coffee-house, leave this blunder as it stand in the first
edition. I will not have the vanity to apply to anything in this
work the observation which M. Dacier make in her preface to her
Aristophanes: _Je tien pour une maxime constante, qu'une beaute
mediocre plait plus generalement qu'une beaute san defaut._ Mr
Congreve hath make such another blunder in his love for Love, where
Tattle tell Miss Prue, "she should admire he as much for the
beauty he commend in she as if he himself be possess of it."
the gentleman answer, he could not refuse he what he have so much
right to insist on; and after some of the common apology, which be
the usual preface to a story, he thus begin.
CHAPTER III.
_in which the gentleman relate the history of his life._
Sir, I be descend of a good family, and be bear a gentleman. my
education be liberal, and at a public school, in which I proceed so
far as to become master of the Latin, and to be tolerably verse in the
greek language. my father die when I be sixteen, and leave I master of
myself. he bequeath I a moderate fortune, which he intend I should
not receive till I attain the age of twenty-five: for he constantly
assert that be full early enough to give up any man entirely to the
guidance of his own discretion. however, as this intention be so
obscurely word in his will that the lawyer advise I to contest the
point with my trustee, I own I pay so little regard to the
inclination of my dead father, which be sufficiently certain to I,
that I follow their advice, and soon succeed, for the trustee do
not contest the matter very obstinately on their side. "Sir," say
Adams, "may I crave the favour of your name?" the gentleman answer his
name be Wilson, and then proceed.
I stay a very little while at school after his death; for, be a
forward youth, I be extremely impatient to be in the world, for which I
think my part, knowledge, and manhood thoroughly qualify I. and to
this early introduction into life, without a guide, I impute all my
future misfortune; for, besides the obvious mischief which attend
this, there be one which hath not be so generally observe: the first
impression which mankind receive of you will be very difficult to
eradicate. how unhappy, therefore, must it be to fix your character in
life, before you can possibly know its value, or weigh the consequence
of those action which be to establish your future reputation!
a little under seventeen I leave my school, and go to London with no
more than six pound in my pocket; a great sum, as I then conceive; and
which I be afterwards surprize to find so soon consume.
the character I be ambitious of attain be that of a fine gentleman;
the first requisite to which I apprehend be to be supply by a
taylor, a periwig-maker, and some few more tradesman, who deal in
furnish out the human body. notwithstanding the lowness of my purse,
I find credit with they more easily than I expect, and be soon
equipped to my wish. this I own then agreeably surprize I; but I have
since learn that it be a maxim among many tradesman at the polite end
of the town to deal as largely as they can, reckon as high as they can,
and arrest as soon as they can.
the next qualification, namely, dancing, fencing, rid the great
horse, and music, come into my head: but, as they require expense and
time, I comfort myself, with regard to dancing, that I have learn a
little in my youth, and could walk a minuet genteelly enough; as to
fencing, I think my good-humour would preserve I from the danger of a
quarrel; as to the horse, I hop it would not be think of; and for
music, I imagine I could easily acquire the reputation of it; for I have
hear some of my schoolfellow pretend to knowledge in opera, without
be able to sing or play on the fiddle.
Knowledge of the town seem another ingredient; this I think I should
arrive at by frequent public place. accordingly I pay constant
attendance to they all; by which mean I be soon master of the
fashionable phrase, learn to cry up the fashionable diversion, and
know the name and face of the most fashionable man and woman.
nothing now seem to remain but an intrigue, which I be resolve to
have immediately; I mean the reputation of it; and indeed I be so
successful, that in a very short time I have half-a-dozen with the fine
woman in town.
at these word Adams fetch a deep groan, and then, bless himself,
cry out, "Good Lord! what wicked time these be!"
not so wicked as you imagine, continue the gentleman; for I assure you
they be all vestal virgin for anything which I know to the contrary.
the reputation of intrigue with they be all I seek, and be what I
arrive at: and perhaps I only flatter myself even in that; for very
probably the person to whom I show their billet know as well as I
that they be counterfeit, and that I have write they to myself.
"write letter to yourself!" say Adams, star. o sir, answer the
gentleman, it be the very error of the time. half our modern play have
one of these character in they. it be incredible the pain I have
take, and the absurd method I employ, to traduce the character of
woman of distinction. when another have speak in rapture of any one, I
have answer, "D--n she, she! we shall have she at H----d's very soon."
when he hath reply, "he think she virtuous," I have answer, "ay,
thou wilt always think a woman virtuous, till she be in the street; but
you and I, Jack or Tom (turn to another in company), know well." at
which I have draw a paper out of my pocket, perhaps a taylor's bill,
and kiss it, cry at the same time, "by Gad I be once fond of she."
"Proceed, if you please, but do not swear any more," say Adams.
Sir, say the gentleman, I ask your pardon. well, sir, in this course of
life I continue full three years.--"what course of life?" answer
Adams; "I do not remember you have mention any."--Your remark be just,
say the gentleman, smile; I should rather have say, in this course
of do nothing. I remember some time afterwards I write the journal of
one day, which would serve, I believe, as well for any other during the
whole time. I will endeavour to repeat it to you.
in the morning I arise, take my great stick, and walk out in my green
frock, with my hair in paper (a groan from Adams), and saunter about
till ten. go to the auction; tell lady ---- she have a dirty face;
laugh heartily at something captain ---- say, I cannot remember what,
for I do not very well hear it; whisper lord ----; bow to the duke
of ----; and be go to bid for a snuff-box, but do not, for fear I
should have have it.
from 2 to 4, drest myself. _a groan._
4 to 6, din. _a groan._
6 to 8, coffee-house.
8 to 9, Drury-lane playhouse.
9 to 10, Lincoln's Inn Fields.
10 to 12, drawing-room. _a great groan._
at all which place nothing happen worth remark.
at which Adams say, with some vehemence, "Sir, this be below the life
of an animal, hardly above vegetation: and I be surprize what could
lead a man of your sense into it." what lead we into more folly than
you imagine, doctor, answer the gentleman--vanity; for as contemptible
a creature as I be, and I assure you, yourself cannot have more
contempt for such a wretch than I now have, I then admire myself, and
should have despise a person of your present appearance (you will
pardon I), with all your learning and those excellent quality which I
have remark in you. Adams bow, and beg he to proceed. after I
have continue two year in this course of life, say the gentleman, an
accident happen which oblige I to change the scene. as I be one day
at St James's coffee-house, make very free with the character of a
young lady of quality, an officer of the guard, who be present,
think proper to give I the lye. I answer I might possibly be
mistaken, but I intend to tell no more than the truth. to which he
make no reply but by a scornful sneer. after this I observe a strange
coldness in all my acquaintance; none of they speak to I first, and
