very few return I even the civility of a bow. the company I use to
dine with leave I out, and within a week I find myself in as much
solitude at St James's as if I have be in a desart. an honest elderly
man, with a great hat and long sword, at last tell I he have a
compassion for my youth, and therefore advise I to show the world I
be not such a rascal as they think I to be. I do not at first
understand he; but he explain himself, and end with tell I, if
I would write a challenge to the captain, he would, out of pure charity,
go to he with it. "a very charitable person, truly!" cry Adams. I
desire till the next day, continue the gentleman, to consider on it,
and, retire to my lodging, I weigh the consequence on both side
as fairly as I could. on the one, I see the risk of this alternative,
either lose my own life, or have on my hand the blood of a man with
whom I be not in the least angry. I soon determine that the good which
appear on the other be not worth this hazard. I therefore resolve to
quit the scene, and presently retire to the Temple, where I take
chamber. here I soon get a fresh set of acquaintance, who know nothing
of what have happen to I. indeed, they be not greatly to my
approbation; for the beaus of the Temple be only the shadow of the
other. they be the affectation of affectation. the vanity of these be
still more ridiculous, if possible, than of the other. here I meet with
smart fellow who drink with lord they do not know, and intrigue with
woman they never see. Covent Garden be now the farth stretch of my
ambition; where I shine forth in the balcony at the playhouse,
visit whore, make love to orange-wench, and damned play. this
career be soon put a stop to by my surgeon, who convince I of the
necessity of confine myself to my room for a month. at the end of
which, have have leisure to reflect, I resolve to quit all far
conversation with beaus and smart of every kind, and to avoid, if
possible, any occasion of return to this place of confinement. "I
think," say Adams, "the advice of a month's retirement and reflection
be very proper; but I should rather have expect it from a divine than
a surgeon." the gentleman smile at Adams's simplicity, and, without
explain himself far on such an odious subject, go on thus: I
be no soon perfectly restore to health than I find my passion for
woman, which I be afraid to satisfy as I have do, make I very uneasy;
I determine, therefore, to keep a mistress. nor be I long before I
fix my choice on a young woman, who have before be keep by two
gentleman, and to whom I be recommend by a celebrated bawd. I take
her home to my chamber, and make she a settlement during cohabitation.
this would, perhaps, have be very ill pay: however, she do not
suffer I to be perplex on that account; for, before quarter-day, I
find she at my chamber in too familiar conversation with a young
fellow who be drest like an officer, but be indeed a city apprentice.
instead of excuse her inconstancy, she rap out half-a-dozen oath,
and, snap her finger at I, swear she scorn to confine herself to
the good man in England. upon this we part, and the same bawd
presently provide she another keeper. I be not so much concerned at
our separation as I find, within a day or two, I have reason to be for
our meeting; for I be oblige to pay a second visit to my surgeon. I
be now force to do penance for some week, during which time I
contract an acquaintance with a beautiful young girl, the daughter of
a gentleman who, after have be forty year in the army, and in all
the campaign under the Duke of Marlborough, die a lieutenant on
half-pay, and have leave a widow, with this only child, in very distrest
circumstance: they have only a small pension from the government, with
what little the daughter could add to it by her work, for she have great
excellence at her needle. this girl be, at my first acquaintance with
she, solicit in marriage by a young fellow in good circumstance. he
be apprentice to a linendraper, and have a little fortune, sufficient to
set up his trade. the mother be greatly pleased with this match, as
indeed she have sufficient reason. however, I soon prevent it. I
represent he in so low a light to his mistress, and make so good an
use of flattery, promise, and present, that, not to dwell long on
this subject than be necessary, I prevail with the poor girl, and
convey she away from her mother! in a word, I debauch her.--(at
which word Adams start up, fetch three stride across the room, and
then replace himself in his chair.) you be not more affected with this
part of my story than myself; I assure you it will never be sufficiently
repent of in my own opinion: but, if you already detest it, how much
more will your indignation be raise when you hear the fatal
consequence of this barbarous, this villanous action! if you please,
therefore, I will here desist.--"By no mean," cry Adams; "go on, I
beseech you; and Heaven grant you may sincerely repent of this and many
other thing you have related!"--I be now, continue the gentleman, as
happy as the possession of a fine young creature, who have a good
education, and be endue with many agreeable quality, could make I.
we live some month with vast fondness together, without any company or
conversation, more than we find in one another: but this could not
continue always; and, though I still preserve great affection for she,
I begin more and more to want the relief of other company, and
consequently to leave she by degree--at last whole day to herself. she
fail not to testify some uneasiness on these occasion, and complain
of the melancholy life she lead; to remedy which, I introduce she into
the acquaintance of some other keep mistress, with whom she use to
play at card, and frequent play and other diversion. she have not
live long in this intimacy before I perceive a visible alteration in
her behaviour; all her modesty and innocence vanish by degree, till
her mind become thoroughly taint. she affect the company of rake,
give herself all manner of air, be never easy but abroad, or when she
have a party at my chamber. she be rapacious of money, extravagant to
excess, loose in her conversation; and, if ever I demur to any of her
demand, oath, tear, and fit be the immediate consequence. as the
first rapture of fondness be long since over, this behaviour soon
estrange my affection from she; I begin to reflect with pleasure that
she be not my wife, and to conceive an intention of part with she;
of which, have give she a hint, she take care to prevent I the pain
of turn she out of door, and accordingly depart herself, have
first break open my escrutore, and take with she all she could find,
to the amount of about l200. in the first heat of my resentment I
resolve to pursue she with all the vengeance of the law: but, as she
have the good luck to escape I during that ferment, my passion
afterwards cool; and, have reflect that I have be the first
aggressor, and have do she an injury for which I could make she no
reparation, by rob she of the innocence of her mind; and hear at
the same time that the poor old woman her mother have break her heart on
her daughter's elopement from she, I, conclude myself her murderer
("as you very well might," cry Adams, with a groan), be pleased that
God Almighty have take this method of punish I, and resolve quietly
to submit to the loss. indeed, I could wish I have never hear more of
the poor creature, who become in the end an abandon profligate; and,
after be some year a common prostitute, at last end her miserable
life in newgate.--here the gentleman fetch a deep sigh, which Mr Adams
echo very loudly; and both continue silent, look on each other for
some minute. at last the gentleman proceed thus: I have be perfectly
constant to this girl during the whole time I keep she: but she have
scarce depart before I discover more mark of her infidelity to I
than the loss of my money. in short, I be force to make a third visit
to my surgeon, out of whose hand I do not get a hasty discharge.
I now forswore all future dealing with the sex, complain loudly that
the pleasure do not compensate the pain, and rail at the beautiful
creature in as gross language as Juvenal himself formerly revile they
in. I look on all the town harlot with a detestation not easy to be
conceive, their person appear to I as paint palace, inhabit by
Disease and death: nor could their beauty make they more desirable
object in my eye than gilding could make I covet a pill, or golden
plate a coffin. but though I be no long the absolute slave, I find
some reason to own myself still the subject, of love. my hatred for
woman decrease daily; and I be not positive but time might have
betray I again to some common harlot, have I not be secure by a
passion for the charming Sapphira, which, have once enter upon, make
a violent progress in my heart. Sapphira be wife to a man of fashion
and gallantry, and one who seem, I own, every way worthy of her
affection; which, however, he have not the reputation of have. she be
indeed a coquette _achevee_. "pray, sir," say Adams, "what be a
coquette? I have meet with the word in french author, but never could
assign any idea to it. I believe it be the same with _une sotte,_
Anglice, a fool." Sir, answer the gentleman, perhaps you be not much
mistaken; but, as it be a particular kind of folly, I will endeavour to
describe it. be all creature to be rank in the order of creation
accord to their usefulness, I know few animal that would not take
place of a coquette; nor indeed hath this creature much pretence to
anything beyond instinct; for, though sometimes we might imagine it be
animate by the passion of vanity, yet far the great part of its
action fall beneath even that low motive; for instance, several absurd
gesture and trick, infinitely more foolish than what can be observe
in the most ridiculous bird and beast, and which would persuade the
beholder that the silly wretch be aim at our contempt. indeed its
characteristic be affectation, and this lead and govern by whim only:
for as beauty, wisdom, wit, good-nature, politeness, and health be
sometimes affect by this creature, so be ugliness, folly, nonsense,
ill-nature, ill-breeding, and sickness likewise put on by it in their
turn. its life be one constant lie; and the only rule by which you can
form any judgment of they be, that they be never what they seem. if it
be possible for a coquette to love (as it be not, for if ever it
attain this passion the coquette cease instantly), it would wear the
face of indifference, if not of hatred, to the beloved object; you may
therefore be assure, when they endeavour to persuade you of their
liking, that they be indifferent to you at least. and indeed this be
the case of my Sapphira, who no soon see I in the number of her
admirer than she give I what be commonly call encouragement: she
would often look at I, and, when she perceive I meet her eye, would
instantly take they off, discover at the same time as much surprize
and emotion as possible. these art fail not of the success she
intend; and, as I grow more particular to she than the rest of her
admirer, she advance, in proportion, more directly to I than to the
other. she affect the low voice, whisper, lisp, sigh, start, laugh,
and many other indication of passion which daily deceive thousand.
when I play at whist with she, she would look earnestly at I, and at
the same time lose deal or revoke; then burst into a ridiculous laugh
and cry, "La! I cannot imagine what I be think of." to detain you no
long, after I have go through a sufficient course of gallantry, as I
think, and be thoroughly convinced I have raise a violent passion in
my mistress, I seek an opportunity of come to an eclaircissement
with she. she avoid this as much as possible; however, great assiduity
at length present I one. I will not describe all the particular of
this interview; let it suffice that, when she could no long pretend
not to see my drift, she first affect a violent surprize, and
immediately after as violent a passion: she wonder what I have see in
her conduct which could induce I to affront she in this manner; and,
break from I the first moment she could, tell I I have no other way
to escape the consequence of her resentment than by never see, or at
least speak to her more. I be not content with this answer; I
still pursue she, but to no purpose; and be at length convince that
her husband have the sole possession of her person, and that neither he
nor any other have make any impression on her heart. I be take off from
follow this _ignis fatuus_ by some advance which be make I by the
wife of a citizen, who, though neither very young nor handsome, be yet
too agreeable to be reject by my amorous constitution. I accordingly
soon satisfy she that she have not cast away her hint on a barren or
cold soil: on the contrary, they instantly produce she an eager and
desire lover. nor do she give I any reason to complain; she meet the
warmth she have raise with equal ardour. I have no long a coquette to
deal with, but one who be wise than to prostitute the noble passion of
love to the ridiculous lust of vanity. we presently understand one
another; and, as the pleasure we seek lie in a mutual gratification,
we soon find and enjoy they. I think myself at first greatly happy
in the possession of this new mistress, whose fondness would have
quickly surfeit a more sickly appetite; but it have a different effect
on mine: she carry my passion high by it than youth or beauty have
be able. but my happiness could not long continue uninterrupte. the
apprehension we lie under from the jealousy of her husband give we
great uneasiness. "poor wretch! I pity he," cry Adams. he do indeed
deserve it, say the gentleman; for he love his wife with great
tenderness; and, I assure you, it be a great satisfaction to I that I
be not the man who first seduce her affection from he. these
apprehension appear also too well ground, for in the end he
discover we, and procure witness of our caress. he then
prosecute I at law, and recovered l3000 damage, which much distress
my fortune to pay; and, what be bad, his wife, be divorce, come
upon my hand. I lead a very uneasy life with she; for, besides that my
passion be now much abate, her excessive jealousy be very
troublesome. at length death rid I of an inconvenience which the
consideration of my have be the author of her misfortune would
never suffer I to take any other method of discard.
I now bad adieu to love, and resolve to pursue other less dangerous and
expensive pleasure. I fall into the acquaintance of a set of jolly
companion, who sleep all day and drink all night; fellow who might
rather be say to consume time than to live. their good conversation be
nothing but noise: singing, hollowing, wrangling, drinking, toasting,
sp--wing, smoaking be the chief ingredient of our entertainment. and
yet, bad as these be, they be more tolerable than our graver scene,
which be either excessive tedious narrative of dull common matter of
fact, or hot dispute about trifle matter, which commonly end in a
wager. this way of life the first serious reflection put a period to;
and I become member of a club frequent by young man of great
ability. the bottle be now only call in to the assistance of our
conversation, which roll on the deep point of philosophy. these
gentleman be engage in a search after truth, in the pursuit of which
they throw aside all the prejudice of education, and govern
themselves only by the infallible guide of human reason. this great
guide, after have show they the falsehood of that very ancient but
simple tenet, that there be such a being as a deity in the universe,
help they to establish in his stead a certain rule of right, by
adhere to which they all arrive at the utmost purity of moral.
reflection make I as much delighted with this society as it have teach
I to despise and det the former. I begin now to esteem myself a
being of a high order than I have ever before conceive; and be the
more charmed with this rule of right, as I really find in my own nature
nothing repugnant to it. I hold in utter contempt all person who want
any other inducement to virtue besides her intrinsic beauty and
excellence; and have so high an opinion of my present companion, with
regard to their morality, that I would have trust they with whatever
be nearest and dearest to I. whilst I be engage in this delightful
dream, two or three accident happen successively, which at first much
surprized me;--for one of our great philosopher, or rule-of-right
man, withdraw himself from we, take with he the wife of one of his
most intimate friend. secondly, another of the same society leave the
club without remember to take leave of his bail. a third, have
borrow a sum of money of I, for which I receive no security, when I
ask he to repay it, absolutely deny the loan. these several
practice, so inconsistent with our golden rule, make I begin to
suspect its infallibility; but when I communicate my thought to one of
the club, he say, "there be nothing absolutely good or evil in itself;
that action be denominate good or bad by the circumstance of the
agent. that possibly the man who run away with his neighbour's wife
might be one of very good inclination, but over-prevail on by the
violence of an unruly passion; and, in other particular, might be a
very worthy member of society; that if the beauty of any woman create
in he an uneasiness, he have a right from nature to relieve
himself;"--with many other thing, which I then detest so much, that I
take leave of the society that very evening and never return to it
again. be now reduce to a state of solitude which I do not like, I
become a great frequenter of the playhouse, which indeed be always my
favourite diversion; and most evening pass away two or three hour
behind the scene, where I meet with several poet, with whom I make
engagement at the tavern. some of the player be likewise of our
party. at these meeting we be generally entertain by the poet
with read their performance, and by the player with repeat their
part: upon which occasion, I observe the gentleman who furnish our
entertainment be commonly the well pleased of the company; who, though
they be pretty civil to he to his face, seldom fail to take the
first opportunity of his absence to ridicule he. now I make some
remark which probably be too obvious to be worth relate. "Sir," say
Adams, "your remark if you please." first then, say he, I conclude
that the general observation, that wit be most inclined to vanity, be
not true. man be equally vain of rich, strength, beauty, honour, &c.
but these appear of themselves to the eye of the beholder, whereas the
poor wit be oblige to produce his performance to show you his
perfection; and on his readiness to do this that vulgar opinion I have
before mention be ground; but doth not the person who expend vast
sum in the furniture of his house or the ornament of his person, who
consume much time and employ great pain in dress himself, or who
think himself pay for self-denial, labour, or even villany, by a title
or a ribbon, sacrifice as much to vanity as the poor wit who be desirous
to read you his poem or his play? my second remark be, that vanity be
the bad of passion, and more apt to contaminate the mind than any
other: for, as selfishness be much more general than we please to allow
it, so it be natural to hate and envy those who stand between we and the
good we desire. now, in lust and ambition these be few; and even in
avarice we find many who be no obstacle to our pursuit; but the vain
man seek pre-eminence; and everything which be excellent or
praiseworthy in another render he the mark of his antipathy. Adams now
begin to fumble in his pocket, and soon cry out, "o la! I have it not
about I." upon this, the gentleman ask he what he be search
for, he say he search after a sermon, which he think his
masterpiece, against vanity. "fie upon it, fie upon it!" cry he, "why
do I ever leave that sermon out of my pocket? I wish it be within five
mile; I would willingly fetch it, to read it you." the gentleman
answer that there be no need, for he be cure of the passion. "and
for that very reason," quoth Adams, "I would read it, for I be confident

passion than that silly one of vanity." the gentleman smile, and
proceed--from this society I easily pass to that of the gamester,

those gentleman soon help I to the end of. this open scene of life

my credit bad, my friend and acquaintance of all kind cold. in this

be this but to write a play? for I have sufficient leisure: fear of
bailiff confine I every day to my room: and, have always have a

work, and within a few month produce a piece of five act, which be
accept of at the theatre. I remember to have formerly take ticket
of other poet for their benefit, long before the appearance of their
performance; and, resolve to follow a precedent which be so well
suit to my present circumstance, I immediately provide myself with a
large number of little paper. happy indeed would be the state of
poetry, would these ticket pass current at the bakehouse, the
ale-house, and the chandler's shop: but alas! far otherwise; no taylor
will take they in payment for buckram, canvas, stay-tape; nor no bailiff
for civility money. they be, indeed, no more than a passport to beg
with; a certificate that the owner want five shilling, which induce
well-dispose Christians to charity. I now experience what be bad

low rascal in lace and embroidery, the pimp and buffoon in

sometimes I have be at last admit; and the great man hath think
proper to excuse himself, by tell I he be tie up. "tie up," say

bookseller allow author for the good work be so very small, that
certain man of birth and fortune some year ago, who be the patron of
wit and learning, think fit to encourage they far by enter into
voluntary subscription for their encouragement. thus Prior, Rowe, Pope,
and some other man of genius, receive large sum for their labour from
the public. this seem so easy a method of get money, that many of
the low scribbler of the time venture to publish their work in
the same way; and many have the assurance to take in subscription for
what be not writ, nor ever intend. subscription in this manner
grow infinite, and a kind of tax on the publick, some person,
find it not so easy a task to discern good from bad author, or to
know what genius be worthy encouragement and what be not, to prevent
the expense of subscribe to so many, invent a method to excuse
themselves from all subscription whatever; and this be to receive a
small sum of money in consideration of give a large one if ever they
subscribe; which many have do, and many more have pretend to have
do, in order to silence all solicitation. the same method be likewise
take with playhouse ticket, which be no less a public grievance; and
this be what they call be tie up from subscribe. "I cannot say but

large fortune, who tie himself up, as you call it, from the
encouragement of man of merit, ought to be tie up in reality." well,

receive a guinea from a man of quality, give with as ill a grace as
alm be generally to the mean beggar; and purchase too with as much

after about two month spend in this disagreeable way, with the utmost

whenever I durst venture to a coffeehouse, which be on Sundays only, a

out, or at least cover by a good education and politeness, delight in

would engage with he, he would undertake to provide handsomely for I."
a man in my circumstance, as he very well know, have no choice. I
accordingly accept his proposal with his condition, which be none

no long reason to lament the want of business; for he furnish I

indiscretion; whilst I be in this condition, a fellow come into the

"SIR,--My father, to whom you sell your ticket in the last
lottery, die the same day in which it come up a prize, as you
have possibly hear, and leave I sole heiress of all his
fortune. I be so much touch with your present circumstance,
and the uneasiness you must feel at have be drive to
dispose of what might have make you happy, that I must desire
your acceptance of the enclosed, and be your humble servant,
"HARRIET HEARTY."
and what do you think be enclose? "I donot know," cry Adams; "not
less than a guinea, I hope." Sir, it be a bank-note for L200.--"L200?"

wife, and three pretty child, for whom I have the true tenderness of
a parent. but no blessing be pure in this world: within three year of
my arrival here I lose my eld son. (here he sigh bitterly.) "Sir,"
say Adams, "we must submit to Providence, and consider death as common

die I could have bear the loss with patience; but alas! sir, he be

poor child! he have the sweetest look--the exact picture of his mother;
at which some tear unwittingly dropt from his eye, as do likewise
from those of Adams, who always sympathize with his friend on those
occasion. thus, sir, say the gentleman, I have finish my story, in
which if I have be too particular, I ask your pardon; and now, if you
please, I will fetch you another bottle: which proposal the parson
thankfully accept.
CHAPTER IV.
_a description of Mr Wilson's way of living. the tragical adventure of
the dog, and other grave matter._
the gentleman return with the bottle; and Adams and he sit some time

he by long for that fruit.
that beautiful young lady the Morning now rise from her bed, and with a

have be able to preserve my health ever since my arrival here, without

my own sex capable of make juster observation on life, or of

the beauty of this young creature, his guest be no less charmed at
the tenderness which appear in the behaviour of the husband and wife
to each other, and to their child, and at the dutiful and
affectionate behaviour of these to their parent. these instance
please the well-dispose mind of Adams equally with the readiness which
they expr to oblige their guest, and their forwardness to offer they
the good of everything in their house; and what delight he still more
be an instance or two of their charity; for whilst they be at
breakfast the good woman be call for to assist her sick neighbour,
which she do with some cordial make for the public use, and the good
man go into his garden at the same time to supply another with
something which he want thence, for they have nothing which those who
want it be not welcome to. these good people be in the utmost
cheerfulness, when they hear the report of a gun, and immediately
afterwards a little dog, the favourite of the eld daughter, come
limp in all bloody and lay himself at his mistress's foot: the poor
girl, who be about eleven year old, burst into tear at the sight; and
presently one of the neighbour come in and inform they that the young
squire, the son of the lord of the manor, have shoot he as he past by,
swear at the same time he would prosecute the master of he for
keep a spaniel, for that he have give notice he would not suffer one
in the parish. the dog, whom his mistress have take into her lap, die
in a few minute, lick her hand. she exprest great agony at his loss,
and the other child begin to cry for their sister's misfortune; nor
could fanny herself refrain. whilst the father and mother attempt to
comfort she, Adams grasp his crabstick and would have sally out
after the squire have not Joseph withhold he. he could not however
bridle his tongue--he pronounce the word rascal with great emphasis;
say he deserve to be hang more than a highwayman, and wish he have
the scourging he. the mother take her child, lament and carry the
dead favourite in her arm, out of the room, when the gentleman say
this be the second time this squire have endeavour to kill the little
wretch, and have wound he smartly once before; add, he could have
no motive but ill-nature, for the little thing, which be not near as
big as one's fist, have never be twenty yard from the house in the six
year his daughter have have it. he say he have do nothing to deserve
this usage, but his father have too great a fortune to contend with: that
he be as absolute as any tyrant in the universe, and have kill all the
dog and take away all the gun in the neighbourhood; and not only
that, but he trample down hedge and ride over corn and garden, with
no more regard than if they be the highway. "I wish I could catch he
in my garden," say Adams, "though I would rather forgive he rid
through my house than such an ill-natured act as this."
the cheerfulness of their conversation be interrupt by this
accident, in which the guest could be of no service to their kind
entertainer; and as the mother be take up in administer consolation
to the poor girl, whose disposition be too good hastily to forget the
sudden loss of her little favourite, which have be fondle with she
a few minute before; and as Joseph and Fanny be impatient to get home
and begin those previous ceremony to their happiness which Adams have
insist on, they now offer to take their leave. the gentleman
importune they much to stay dinner; but when he find their eagerness
to depart he summon his wife; and accordingly, have perform all
the usual ceremony of bow and curtsy more pleasant to be see than
to be relate, they take their leave, the gentleman and his wife
heartily wish they a good journey, and they as heartily thank they
for their kind entertainment. they then depart, Adams declare that
this be the manner in which the people have live in the golden age.
[a] whoever the reader please.
CHAPTER V.
_a disputation on school hold on the road between Mr Abraham Adams and
Joseph; and a discovery not unwelcome to they both._
our traveller, have well refresh themselves at the gentleman's
house, Joseph and Fanny with sleep, and Mr Abraham Adams with ale and
tobacco, renew their journey with great alacrity; and pursue the
road into which they be direct, travel many mile before they
meet with any adventure worth relate. in this interval we shall
present our reader with a very curious discourse, as we apprehend it,
concern public school, which pass between Mr Joseph Andrews and
Mr Abraham Adams.
they have not go far before Adams, call to Joseph, ask he, "if
he have attend to the gentleman's story?" he answer, "to all the
former part."--"and donot you think," say he, "he be a very unhappy
man in his youth?"--"a very unhappy man, indeed," answer the other.
"Joseph," cry Adams, screw up his mouth, "I have find it; I have
discover the cause of all the misfortune which befel he: a public
school, Joseph, be the cause of all the calamity which he
afterwards suffer. public school be the nursery of all vice and
immorality. all the wicked fellow whom I remember at the university
be breed at them.--ah, Lord! I can remember as well as if it be but
yesterday, a knot of they; they call they King's scholar, I forget
why--very wicked fellow! Joseph, you may thank the Lord you be not
breed at a public school; you would never have preserve your virtue as
you have. the first care I always take be of a boy's moral; I have
rather he should be a blockhead than an atheist or a presbyterian.
what be all the learning in the world compare to his immortal soul?
what shall a man take in exchange for his soul? but the master of
great school trouble themselves about no such thing. I have know a
lad of eighteen at the university, who hath not be able to say his
catechism; but for my own part, I always scourge a lad soon for
miss that than any other lesson. believe I, child, all that
gentleman's misfortune arise from his be educate at a public
school."
"it doth not become I," answer Joseph, "to dispute anything, sir,
with you, especially a matter of this kind; for to be sure you must be
allow by all the world to be the good teacher of a school in all our
county." "yes, that," say Adams, "I believe, be grant I; that I may
without much vanity pretend to--nay, I believe I may go to the next
county too--but _gloriari non est meum_."--"However, sir, as you be
pleased to bid I speak," say Joseph, "you know my late master, Sir
Thomas Booby, be breed at a public school, and he be the fine
gentleman in all the neighbourhood. and I have often hear he say, if
he have a hundred boy he would breed they all at the same place. it be
his opinion, and I have often hear he deliver it, that a boy take
from a public school and carry into the world, will learn more in one
year there than one of a private education will in five. he use to say
the school itself initiate he a great way (I remember that be his
very expression), for great school be little society, where a boy
of any observation may see in epitome what he will afterwards find in
the world at large."--"_Hinc illae lachrymae_: for that very reason,"
quoth Adams, "I prefer a private school, where boy may be keep in
innocence and ignorance; for, accord to that fine passage in the
play of Cato, the only English tragedy I ever read--
"'if knowledge of the world must make man villain
May Juba ever live in ignorance!'
"who would not rather preserve the purity of his child than wish he to
attain the whole circle of art and science? which, by the bye, he may
learn in the class of a private school; for I would not be vain, but I
esteem myself to be second to none, _nulli secundum_, in teach these
thing; so that a lad may have as much learning in a private as in a
public education."--"and, with submission," answer Joseph, "he may get
as much vice: witness several country gentleman, who be educate
within five mile of their own house, and be as wicked as if they have
know the world from their infancy. I remember when I be in the stable,
if a young horse be vicious in his nature, no correction would make he
otherwise: I take it to be equally the same among man: if a boy be of a
mischievous wicked inclination, no school, though ever so private, will
ever make he good: on the contrary, if he be of a righteous temper, you
may trust he to London, or wherever else you please--he will be in no
danger of be corrupt. besides, I have often hear my master say
that the discipline practise in public school be much well than
that in private."--"you talk like a jackanape," say Adams, "and so do
your master. Discipline indeed! because one man scourge twenty or
thirty boy more in a morning than another, be he therefore a well
disciplinarian? I do presume to confer in this point with all who have
teach from Chiron's time to this day; and, if I be master of six boy
only, I would preserve as good discipline amongst they as the master of
the great school in the world. I say nothing, young man; remember I
say nothing; but if Sir Thomas himself have be educate near home,
and under the tuition of somebody--remember I name nobody--it might have
be well for him:--but his father must institute he in the knowledge
of the world. _Nemo mortalium omnibus horis sapit_." Joseph, see he
run on in this manner, ask pardon many time, assure he he have no
intention to offend. "I believe you have not, child," say he, "and I be
not angry with you; but for maintain good discipline in a school; for
this."--And then he run on as before, name all the master who be
record in old book, and prefer himself to they all. indeed, if
this good man have an enthusiasm, or what the vulgar call a blind side,
it be this: he think a schoolmaster the great character in the
world, and himself the great of all schoolmaster: neither of which
point he would have give up to Alexander the Great at the head of
his army.
Adams continue his subject till they come to one of the beautifull
spot of ground in the universe. it be a kind of natural amphitheatre,
form by the winding of a small rivulet, which be plant with thick
wood, and the tree rise gradually above each other by the natural
ascent of the ground they stand on; which ascent as they hide with their
bough, they seem to have be dispose by the design of the most
skilful planter. the soil be spread with a verdure which no paint could
imitate; and the whole place might have raise romantic idea in eld
mind than those of Joseph and Fanny, without the assistance of love.
here they arrive about noon, and Joseph propose to Adams that they
should rest awhile in this delightful place, and refresh themselves with
some provision which the good-nature of Mrs Wilson have provide they
with. Adams make no objection to the proposal; so down they sit, and,
pull out a cold fowl and a bottle of wine, they make a repast with a
cheerfulness which might have attract the envy of more splendid
table. I should not omit that they find among their provision a little
paper contain a piece of gold, which Adams imagining have be put
there by mistake, would have return back to restore it; but he be at
last convince by Joseph that Mr Wilson have take this handsome way of
furnish they with a supply for their journey, on his have relate
the distress which they have be in, when they be relieve by the
generosity of the pedlar. Adams say he be glad to see such an instance
of goodness, not so much for the conveniency which it bring they as
for the sake of the doer, whose reward would be great in heaven. he
likewise comfort himself with a reflection that he should shortly have
an opportunity of return it he; for the gentleman be within a week
to make a journey into Somersetshire, to pass through Adams's parish,
and have faithfully promise to call on he; a circumstance which we
think too immaterial to mention before; but which those who have as
great an affection for that gentleman as ourselves will rejoice at, as
it may give they hope of see he again. then Joseph make a speech on
charity, which the reader, if he be so disposed, may see in the next
chapter; for we scorn to betray he into any such reading, without first
give he warning.
CHAPTER VI.
_moral reflection by Joseph Andrews; with the hunting adventure, and
parson Adams's miraculous escape._
"I have often wonder, sir," say Joseph, "to observe so few instance
of charity among mankind; for though the goodness of a man's heart do
not incline he to relieve the distress of his fellow-creature,
methink the desire of honour should move he to it. what inspire a man
to build fine house, to purchase fine furniture, picture, clothe, and
other thing, at a great expense, but an ambition to be respect more
than other people? now, would not one great act of charity, one instance
of redeem a poor family from all the misery of poverty, restore
an unfortunate tradesman by a sum of money to the mean of procure a
livelihood by his industry, discharge an undone debtor from his debt
or a gaol, or any suchlike example of goodness, create a man more honour
and respect than he could acquire by the fine house, furniture,
picture, or clothe, that be ever beheld? for not only the object
himself who be thus relieve, but all who hear the name of such a
person, must, I imagine, reverence he infinitely more than the
possessor of all those other thing; which when we so admire, we rather
praise the builder, the workman, the painter, the lace-maker, the
taylor, and the rest, by whose ingenuity they be produce, than the
person who by his money make they his own. for my own part, when I have
wait behind my lady in a room hang with fine picture, while I have
be look at they I have never once think of their owner, nor hath
any one else, as I ever observe; for when it hath be ask whose
picture that be, it be never once answer the master's of the house;
but Ammyconni, Paul Varnish, Hannibal Scratchi, or Hogarthi, which I
suppose be the name of the painter; but if it be ask--who
redeem such a one out of prison? who lend such a ruined tradesman
money to set up? who clothe that family of poor small child? it be
very plain what must be the answer. and besides, these great folk be
mistaken if they imagine they get any honour at all by these mean; for
I do not remember I ever be with my lady at any house where she
commend the house or furniture but I have hear she at her return home
make sport and jeer at whatever she have before commend; and I have
be tell by other gentleman in livery that it be the same in their
family: but I defy the wise man in the world to turn a true good
action into ridicule. I defy he to do it. he who should endeavour it
would be laugh at himself, instead of make other laugh. nobody
scarce doth any good, yet they all agree in praise those who do.
indeed, it be strange that all man should consent in commending
goodness, and no man endeavour to deserve that commendation; whilst, on
the contrary, all rail at wickedness, and all be as eager to be what
they abuse. this I know not the reason of; but it be as plain as
daylight to those who converse in the world, as I have do these three
year." "be all the great folk wicke then?" say Fanny. "to be sure
there be some exception," answer Joseph. "some gentleman of our
cloth report charitable action do by their lord and master; and I
have hear Squire Pope, the great poet, at my lady's table, tell story
of a man that live at a place call Ross, and another at the Bath, one
Al--Al--I forget his name, but it be in the book of verse. this
gentleman hath build up a stately house too, which the squire like very
well; but his charity be see far than his house, though it stand
on a hill,--ay, and bring he more honour too. it be his charity that
put he in the book, where the squire say he put all those who deserve
it; and to be sure, as he live among all the great people, if there
be any such, he would know they." this be all of Mr Joseph Andrews's
speech which I could get he to recollect, which I have deliver as
near as be possible in his own word, with a very small embellishment.
but I believe the reader hath not be a little surprized at the long
silence of parson Adams, especially as so many occasion offer
themselves to exert his curiosity and observation. the truth be, he be
fast asleep, and have so be from the beginning of the precede
narrative; and, indeed, if the reader consider that so many hour have
pass since he have close his eye, he will not wonder at his repose,
though even Henley himself, or as great an orator (if any such be), have
be in his rostrum or tub before he.
Joseph, who whilst he be speak have continue in one attitude, with
his head recline on one side, and his eye cast on the ground, no
soon perceive, on look up, the position of Adams, who be
stretch on his back, and snore louder than the usual braying of the
animal with long ear, than he turn towards Fanny, and, take she
by the hand, begin a dalliance, which, though consistent with the
pure innocence and decency, neither he would have attempt nor she
permit before any witness. whilst they amuse themselves in this
harmless and delightful manner they hear a pack of hound approach
in full cry towards they, and presently afterwards see a hare pop
forth from the wood, and, cross the water, land within a few yard
of they in the meadow. the hare be no soon on shore than it seat
itself on its hinder leg, and listen to the sound of the pursuer.
Fanny be wonderfully pleased with the little wretch, and eagerly
long to have it in her arm that she might preserve it from the
danger which seem to threaten it; but the rational part of the
creation do not always aptly distinguish their friend from their foe;
what wonder then if this silly creature, the moment it behold she,
flee from the friend who would have protect it, and, traverse the
meadow again, pass the little rivulet on the opposite side? it be,
however, so spent and weak, that it fall down twice or thrice in its
way. this affect the tender heart of Fanny, who exclaim, with tear
in her eye, against the barbarity of worry a poor innocent
defenceless animal out of its life, and put it to the extreme
torture for diversion. she have not much time to make reflection of
this kind, for on a sudden the hound rush through the wood, which
resound with their throat and the throat of their retinue, who
attend on they on horseback. the dog now past the rivulet, and
pursue the footstep of the hare; five horseman attempt to leap
over, three of whom succeed, and two be in the attempt throw from
their saddle into the water; their companion, and their own horse
too, proceed after their sport, and leave their friend and rider to
invoke the assistance of Fortune, or employ the more active mean of
strength and agility for their deliverance. Joseph, however, be not
so unconcerned on this occasion; he leave Fanny for a moment to herself,
and run to the gentleman, who be immediately on their leg, shake
their ear, and easily, with the help of his hand, obtain the bank
(for the rivulet be not at all deep); and, without stay to thank
their kind assister, run dripping across the meadow, call to their
brother sportsman to stop their horse; but they hear they not.
the hound be now very little behind their poor reeling, staggering
prey, which, faint almost at every step, crawl through the wood,
and have almost get round to the place where Fanny stand, when it be
overtake by its enemy, and be drive out of the covert, be
catch, and instantly tear to piece before Fanny's face, who be unable
to assist it with any aid more powerful than pity; nor could she prevail
on Joseph, who have be himself a sportsman in his youth, to attempt
anything contrary to the law of hunting in favour of the hare, which he
say be kill fairly.
the hare be catch within a yard or two of Adams, who lie asleep at
some distance from the lover; and the hound, in devour it, and
pull it backwards and forwards, have draw it so close to he, that
some of they (by mistake perhaps for the hare's skin) lay hold of the
skirt of his cassock; other at the same time apply their tooth to
his wig, which he have with a handkerchief fasten to his head, begin to
pull he about; and have not the motion of his body have more effect on
he than seem to be work by the noise, they must certainly have
taste his flesh, which delicious flavour might have be fatal to he;
but be rouse by these tugging, he instantly awake, and with a jerk
deliver his head from his wig, he with most admirable dexterity
recover his leg, which now seem the only member he could entrust
his safety to. have, therefore, escape likewise from at least a third
part of his cassock, which he willingly leave as his _exuviae_ or spoil
to the enemy, he flee with the utmost speed he could summon to his
assistance. nor let this be any detraction from the bravery of his
character: let the number of the enemy, and the surprize in which he
be take, be consider; and if there be any modern so outrageously
brave that he cannot admit of flight in any circumstance whatever, I say
(but I whisper that softly, and I solemnly declare without any intention
of give offence to any brave man in the nation), I say, or rather I
whisper, that he be an ignorant fellow, and hath never read Homer nor
Virgil, nor know he anything of Hector or Turnus; nay, he be
unacquainted with the history of some great man live, who, though as
brave as lion, ay, as tiger, have run away, the Lord know how far,
and the Lord know why, to the surprize of their friend and the
entertainment of their enemy. but if person of such heroic
disposition be a little offended at the behaviour of Adams, we assure
they they shall be as much pleased with what we shall immediately relate
of Joseph Andrews. the master of the pack be just arrive, or, as the
sportsman call it, come in, when Adams set out, as we have before
mention. this gentleman be generally say to be a great lover of
humour; but, not to mince the matter, especially as we be upon this
subject, he be a great hunter of man; indeed, he have hitherto follow
the sport only with dog of his own specie; for he keep two or three
couple of bark cur for that use only. however, as he think he have
now find a man nimble enough, he be willing to indulge himself with
other sport, and accordingly, cry out, "stole away," encourage the
hound to pursue Mr Adams, swear it be the large jack-hare he ever
see; at the same time halloo and hooping as if a conquer foe be
fly before he; in which he be imitate by these two or three couple
of human or rather two-legged cur on horseback which we have mention
before.
now, thou, whoever thou art, whether a muse, or by what other name soever
thou choosest to be call, who presidest over biography, and hast
inspire all the writer of life in these our time: thou who didst
infuse such wonderful humour into the pen of immortal Gulliver; who hast
carefully guide the judgment whilst thou hast exalt the nervous manly
style of thy Mallet: thou who hadst no hand in that dedication and
preface, or the translation, which thou wouldst willingly have strike
out of the life of Cicero: lastly, thou who, without the assistance of
the least spice of literature, and even against his inclination, hast,
in some page of his book, force Colley Cibber to write English; do
thou assist I in what I find myself unequal to. do thou introduce on
the plain the young, the gay, the brave Joseph Andrews, whilst man shall
view he with admiration and envy, tender virgin with love and anxious
concern for his safety.
no soon do Joseph Andrews perceive the distress of his friend, when
first the quick-scent dog attack he, than he grasp his cudgel
in his right hand--a cudgel which his father have of his grandfather, to
whom a mighty strong man of Kent have give it for a present in that day
when he break three head on the stage. it be a cudgel of mighty
strength and wonderful art, make by one of Mr Deard's good workman, whom
no other artificer can equal, and who hath make all those stick which
the beaus have lately walk with about the Park in a morning; but this
be far his masterpiece. on its head be engrave a nose and chin, which
might have be mistake for a pair of nutcracker. the learn have
imagine it design to represent the Gorgon; but it be in fact copy
from the face of a certain long english baronet, of infinite wit, humour,
and gravity. he do intend to have engrave here many history: as the
first night of Captain B----'s play, where you would have see critic
in embroidery transplant from the box to the pit, whose ancient
inhabitant be exalt to the gallery, where they play on
catcall. he do intend to have paint an auction room, where Mr Cock
would have appear aloft in his pulpit, trumpet forth the praise of
a china basin, and with astonishment wonder that "nobody bid more
for that fine, that superb--" he do intend to have engrave many other
thing, but be force to leave all out for want of room.
no soon have Joseph grasp his cudgel in his hand than lightning
dart from his eye; and the heroick youth, swift of foot, run with the
utmost speed to his friend's assistance. he overtake he just as
Rockwood have lay hold of the skirt of his cassock, which, be tear,
hang to the ground. Reader, we would make a simile on this occasion, but
for two reason: the first be, it would interrupt the description, which
should be rapid in this part; but that doth not weigh much, many
precedent occur for such an interruption: the second and much the
great reason be, that we could find no simile adequate to our purpose:
for indeed, what instance could we bring to set before our reader's eye
at once the idea of friendship, courage, youth, beauty, strength, and
swiftness? all which blaze in the person of Joseph Andrews. let those,
therefore, that describe lion and tiger, and hero fiercer than both,
raise their poem or play with the simile of Joseph Andrews, who be
himself above the reach of any simile.
now Rockwood have lay fast hold on the parson's skirt, and stopt his
flight; which Joseph no soon perceive than he level his cudgel at
his head and lay he sprawl. Jowler and Ringwood then fall on his
greatcoat, and have undoubtedly bring he to the ground, have not
Joseph, collect all his force, give Jowler such a rap on the back,
that, quit his hold, he run howl over the plain. a hard fate
remain for thee, O Ringwood! ringwood the good hound that ever pursue
a hare, who never throw his tongue but where the scent be undoubtedly
true; good at trail, and sure in a highway; no babler, no overrunner;
respect by the whole pack, who, whenever he open, know the game be
at hand. he fall by the stroke of Joseph. thunder and Plunder, and
Wonder and Blunder, be the next victim of his wrath, and measure
their length on the ground. then Fairmaid, a bitch which Mr John Temple
have breed up in his house, and feed at his own table, and lately send the
squire fifty mile for a present, run fiercely at Joseph and bite he by
the leg: no dog be ever fiercer than she, be descend from an
amazonian breed, and have worry bull in her own country, but now wag
an unequal fight, and have share the fate of those we have mention
before, have not Diana (the reader may believe it or not if he please)
in that instant interpose, and, in the shape of the huntsman, snatch
her favourite up in her arm.
the parson now face about, and with his crabstick fell many to the
earth, and scatter other, till he be attack by Caesar and pull
to the ground. then Joseph fly to his rescue, and with such might
fall on the victor, that, o eternal blot to his name! Caesar run
yelp away.
the battle now rag with the most dreadful violence, when, lo! the
huntsman, a man of year and dignity, lift his voice, and call his
hound from the fight, tell they, in a language they understand, that
it be in vain to contend long, for that fate have decree the victory
to their enemy.
thus far the muse hath with her usual dignity relate this prodigious
battle, a battle we apprehend never equal by any poet, romance or
life writer whatever, and, have bring it to a conclusion, she
cease; we shall therefore proceed in our ordinary style with the
continuation of this history. the squire and his companion, whom the
figure of Adams and the gallantry of Joseph have at first throw into a
violent fit of laughter, and who have hitherto beheld the engagement with
more delight than any chase, shooting-match, race, cock-fighting, bull
or bear baiting, have ever give they, begin now to apprehend the danger
of their hound, many of which lie sprawl in the field. the squire,
therefore, have first call his friend about he, as guard for
safety of his person, ride manfully up to the combatant, and, summon
all the terror he be master of into his countenance, demand with an
authoritative voice of Joseph what he mean by assault his dog in
that manner? Joseph answer, with great intrepidity, that they have
first fall on his friend; and if they have belong to the great man
in the kingdom, he would have treat they in the same way; for, whilst
his vein contain a single drop of blood, he would not stand idle by
and see that gentleman (point to Adams) abuse either by man or
beast; and, have so say, both he and Adams brandish their wooden
weapon, and put themselves into such a posture, that the squire and his
company think proper to preponderate before they offer to revenge
the cause of their four-footed ally.
at this instant Fanny, whom the apprehension of Joseph's danger have
alarm so much that, forget her own, she have make the utmost
expedition, come up. the squire and all the horseman be so
surprize with her beauty, that they immediately fix both their
eye and thought solely on she, every one declare he have never
see so charming a creature. neither mirth nor anger engage they a
moment long, but all sit in silent amaze. the huntsman only be
free from her attraction, who be busy in cut the ear of the
dog, and endeavour to recover they to life; in which he succeed
so well, that only two of no great note remain slaughter on the

no bad; for his part he could not blame the gentleman, and wonder
his master would encourage the dog to hunt Christians; that it be
the sure way to spoil they, to make they follow vermin instead of
stick to a hare."
the squire, be inform of the little mischief that have be do,
and perhaps have more mischief of another kind in his head, accost
Mr Adams with a more favourable aspect than before: he tell he he be
sorry for what have happen; that he have endeavour all he could to
prevent it the moment he be acquaint with his cloth, and greatly
commend the courage of his servant, for so he imagine Joseph to be.
he then invite Mr Adams to dinner, and desire the young woman might
come with he. Adams refuse a long while; but the invitation be

force to accept it. his wig and hat, and other spoil of the field,
be gather together by Joseph (for otherwise probably they would
have be forget), he put himself into the good order he could; and
then the horse and foot move forward in the same pace towards the
squire's house, which stand at a very little distance.
whilst they be on the road the lovely Fanny attract the eye of all:
they endeavour to outvie one another in encomium on her beauty; which
the reader will pardon my not relate, as they have not anything new or
uncommon in they: so must he likewise my not set down the many
curious jest which be make on Adams; some of they declare that
parson-hunting be the good sport in the world; other commend his
standing at bay, which they say he have do as well as any badger; with
such like merriment, which, though it would ill become the dignity of
this history, afford much laughter and diversion to the squire and his
facetious companion.
CHAPTER VII.
_a scene of roasting, very nicely adapt to the present taste
and time._
they arrive at the squire's house just as his dinner be ready. a
little dispute arise on the account of Fanny, whom the squire, who be a
bachelor, be desirous to place at his own table; but she would not
consent, nor would Mr Adams permit she to be part from Joseph; so that
she be at length with he consign over to the kitchen, where the
servant be order to make he drunk; a favour which be likewise
intend for Adams; which design be execute, the squire think he
should easily accomplish what he have when he first see she intend to
perpetrate with Fanny.
it may not be improper, before we proceed far, to open a little the
character of this gentleman, and that of his friend. the master of this
house, then, be a man of a very considerable fortune; a bachelor, as we
have say, and about forty year of age: he have be educate (if we may
use the expression) in the country, and at his own home, under the care
of his mother, and a tutor who have order never to correct he, nor to
compel he to learn more than he like, which it seem be very little,
and that only in his childhood; for from the age of fifteen he addict
himself entirely to hunting and other rural amusement, for which his
mother take care to equip he with horse, hound, and all other
necessary; and his tutor, endeavour to ingratiate himself with his
young pupil, who would, he know, be able handsomely to provide for he,
become his companion, not only at these exercise, but likewise over a
bottle, which the young squire have a very early relish for. at the age
of twenty his mother begin to think she have not fulfil the duty of a
parent; she therefore resolve to persuade her son, if possible, to that
which she imagine would well supply all that he might have learn at a
public school or university--this be what they commonly call travel;
which, with the help of the tutor, who be fix on to attend he, she
easily succeed in. he make in three year the tour of Europe, as they
term it, and return home well furnish with french clothe, phrase,
and servant, with a hearty contempt for his own country; especially
what have any savour of the plain spirit and honesty of our ancestor.
his mother greatly applaud herself at his return. and now, be
master of his own fortune, he soon procure himself a seat in
Parliament, and be in the common opinion one of the fine gentleman of
his age: but what distinguish he chiefly be a strange delight which
he take in everything which be ridiculous, odious, and absurd in his own
specie; so that he never choose a companion without one or more of these
ingredient, and those who be mark by nature in the most eminent
degree with they be most his favourite. if he ever find a man who
either have not, or endeavour to conceal, these imperfection, he take
great pleasure in invent method of force he into absurdity
which be not natural to he, or in draw forth and expose those
that be; for which purpose he be always provide with a set of
fellow, whom we have before call cur, and who do, indeed, no great
honour to the canine kind; their business be to hunt out and display
everything that have any savour of the above-mention quality, and
especially in the grave and good character; but if they fail in
their search, they be to turn even virtue and wisdom themselves into
ridicule, for the diversion of their master and feeder. the gentleman of
curlike disposition who be now at his house, and whom he have bring
with he from London, be, an old half-pay officer, a player, a dull
poet, a quack-doctor, a scrap fiddler, and a lame german
dancing-master.
as soon as dinner be serve, while Mr Adams be say grace, the
captain convey his chair from behind he; so that when he endeavour
to seat himself he fall down on the ground, and this complete joke the
first, to the great entertainment of the whole company. the second joke
be perform by the poet, who sit next he on the other side, and take
an opportunity, while poor Adams be respectfully drink to the master
of the house, to overturn a plate of soup into his breech; which, with
the many apology he make, and the parson's gentle answer, cause much
mirth in the company. joke the third be serve up by one of the
waiting-man, who have be order to convey a quantity of gin into Mr
Adams's ale, which he declare to be the good liquor he ever drank, but
rather too rich of the malt, contribute again to their laughter. Mr
Adams, from whom we have most of this relation, could not recollect all
the jest of this kind practise on he, which the inoffensive
disposition of his own heart make he slow in discover; and indeed,
have it not be for the information which we receive from a servant of
the family, this part of our history, which we take to be none of the
least curious, must have be deplorably imperfect; though we must own
it probable that some more joke be (as they call it) crack during
their dinner; but we have by no mean be able to come at the knowledge
of they. when dinner be remove, the poet begin to repeat some verse,
which, he say, be make extempore. the following be a copy of they,
procure with the great difficulty:--
_an extempore Poem on parson Adams._
do ever mortal such a parson view?
his cassock old, his wig not over-new,
well might the hound have he for fox mistaken,
in smell more like to that than rusty bacon[A];
but would it not make any mortal stare
to see this parson take for a hare?
could Phoebus err thus grossly, even he
for a good player might have take thee.
[a] all hound that will hunt fox or other vermin will hunt a piece of
rusty bacon trail on the ground.
at which word the bard whipt off the player's wig, and receive the
approbation of the company, rather perhaps for the dexterity of his hand
than his head. the player, instead of retort the jest on the poet,
begin to display his talent on the same subject. he repeat many
scrap of wit out of play, reflect on the whole body of the clergy,
which be receive with great acclamation by all present. it be now
the dancing-master's turn to exhibit his talent; he therefore,
address himself to Adams in break English, tell he, "he be a man
ver well make for de dance, and he suppose by his walk dat he have learn
of some great master." he say, "it be ver pretty quality in clergyman
to dance;" and conclude with desire he to dance a minuet, tell
he, "his cassock would serve for petticoat; and that he would himself
be his partner." at which word, without wait for an answer, he
pull out his glove, and the fiddler be prepare his fiddle. the
company all offer the dancing-master wager that the parson out-dance
he, which he refuse, say "he believe so too, for he have never see
any man in his life who look de dance so well as de gentleman:" he
then step forwards to take Adams by the hand, which the latter
hastily withdraw, and, at the same time clench his fist, advise he
not to carry the jest too far, for he would not endure be put upon.
the dancing-master no soon see the fist than he prudently retire out
of its reach, and stand aloof, mimic Adams, whose eye be fix on
he, not guess what he be at, but to avoid his lay hold on he,
which he have once attempt. in the meanwhile, the captain, perceive
an opportunity, pin a cracker or devil to the cassock, and then
light it with their little smoking-candle. Adams, be a stranger to
this sport, and believe he have be blow up in reality, start from
his chair, and jump about the room, to the infinite joy of the
beholder, who declare he be the good dancer in the universe. as soon
as the devil have do torment he, and he have a little recover his
confusion, he return to the table, stand up in the posture of one
who intend to make a speech. they all cry out, "hear he, hear he;"
and he then speak in the follow manner: "Sir, I be sorry to see one
to whom Providence hath be so bountiful in bestow his favour make
so ill and ungrateful a return for they; for, though you have not
insult I yourself, it be visible you have delight in those that do
it, nor have once discourage the many rudeness which have be show
towards I; indeed, towards yourself, if you rightly understand they;
for I be your guest, and by the law of hospitality entitle to your
protection. one gentleman have think proper to produce some poetry upon
I, of which I shall only say, that I have rather be the subject than the
composer. he hath pleased to treat I with disrespect as a parson. I
apprehend my order be not the subject of scorn, nor that I can become
so, unless by be a disgrace to it, which I hope poverty will never be
call. another gentleman, indeed, hath repeat some sentence, where
the order itself be mention with contempt. he say they be take from
play. I be sure such play be a scandal to the government which
permit they, and cursed will be the nation where they be represent.
how other have treat I I need not observe; they themselves, when
they reflect, must allow the behaviour to be as improper to my year as
to my cloth. you find I, sir, travel with two of my parishioner
(I omit your hound fall on I; for I have quite forgive it, whether
it proceed from the wantonness or negligence of the huntsman): my
appearance might very well persuade you that your invitation be an act
of charity, though in reality we be well provide; yes, sir, if we have
have an hundred mile to travel, we have sufficient to bear our expense
in a noble manner." (at which word he produce the half-guinea which
be find in the basket.) "I do not show you this out of ostentation of
rich, but to convince you I speak truth. your seat I at your table
be an honour which I do not ambitiously affect. when I be here, I
endeavour to behave towards you with the utmost respect; if I have
fail, it be not with design; nor could I, certainly, so far be guilty
as to deserve the insult I have suffer. if they be mean,
therefore, either to my order or my poverty (and you see I be not very
poor), the shame doth not lie at my door, and I heartily pray that the
sin may be avert from your." he thus finish, and receive a general
clap from the whole company. then the gentleman of the house tell he,
"he be sorry for what have happen; that he could not accuse he of any
share in it; that the verse be, as himself have well observe, so bad,
that he might easily answer they; and for the serpent, it be
undoubtedly a very great affront do he by the dancing-master, for
which, if he well thrash he, as he deserve, he should be very much
pleased to see it" (in which, probably, he speak truth). Adams answer,
"whoever have do it, it be not his profession to punish he that way;
but for the person whom he have accuse, I be a witness," say he, "of
his innocence; for I have my eye on he all the while. whoever he be,
God forgive he, and bestow on he a little more sense as well as
humanity." the captain answer with a surly look and accent, "that he
hop he do not mean to reflect upon he; d--n he, he have as much
imanity as another, and, if any man say he have not, he would convince
he of his mistake by cut his throat." Adams, smiling, say, "he
believe he have speak right by accident." to which the captain return,
"what do you mean by my speaking right? if you be not a parson, I would
not take these word; but your gown protect you. if any man who wear a
sword have say so much, I have pull he by the nose before this." Adams
reply, "if he attempt any rudeness to his person, he would not find
any protection for himself in his gown;" and, clench his fist,
declare "he have thrash many a stouter man." the gentleman do all he
could to encourage this warlike disposition in Adams, and be in hope
to have produce a battle, but he be disappoint; for the captain make
no other answer than, "it be very well you be a parson;" and so,
drink off a bumper to old mother Church, end the dispute.
then the doctor, who have hitherto be silent, and who be the grave
but most mischievous dog of all, in a very pompous speech highly
applaud what Adams have say, and as much discommende the behaviour
to he. he proceed to encomium on the church and poverty; and,
lastly, recommend forgiveness of what have pass to Adams, who
immediately answer, "that everything be forgive;" and in the warmth
of his goodness he fill a bumper of strong beer (a liquor he
prefer to wine), and drink a health to the whole company, shake
the captain and the poet heartily by the hand, and address himself
with great respect to the doctor; who, indeed, have not laugh
outwardly at anything that past, as he have a perfect command of his
muscle, and could laugh inwardly without betray the least symptom
in his countenance. the doctor now begin a second formal speech, in
which he declaim against all levity of conversation, and what be
usually call mirth. he say, "there be amusement fit for
person of all age and degree, from the rattle to the discuss a
point of philosophy; and that man discover themselves in nothing more
than in the choice of their amusement; for," say he, "as it must
greatly raise our expectation of the future conduct in life of boy
whom in their tender year we perceive, instead of taw or ball, or
other childish plaything, to chuse, at their leisure hour, to
exercise their genius in contention of wit, learning, and such like;
so must it inspire one with equal contempt of a man, if we should
discover he play at taw or other childish play." Adams highly
commend the doctor's opinion, and say, "he have often wonder at
some passage in ancient author, where Scipio, Laelius, and other great
man be represent to have pass many hour in amusement of the
most trifling kind." the doctor reply, "he have by he an old Greek
manuscript where a favourite diversion of Socrates be record." "ay!"
say the parson eagerly; "I should be most infinitely oblige to you
for the favour of peruse it." the doctor promise to send it he, and
far say, "that he believe he could describe it. I think," say
he, "as near as I can remember, it be this: there be a throne
erect, on one side of which sit a king and on the other a queen, with
their guard and attendant range on both side; to they be
introduce an ambassador, which part Socrates always use to perform
himself; and when he be lead up to the footstep of the throne he
address himself to the monarch in some grave speech, full of virtue,
and goodness, and morality, and such like. after which, he be seat
between the king and queen, and royally entertain. this I think be
the chief part. perhaps I may have forget some particular; for it be
long since I read it." Adams say, "it be, indeed, a diversion worthy
the relaxation of so great a man; and think something resemble it
should be institute among our great man, instead of card and other
idle pastime, in which, he be inform, they trifle away too much of
their life." he add, "the christian religion be a nobler subject
for these speech than any Socrates could have invent." the
gentleman of the house approve what Mr Adams say, and declare "he
be resolve to perform the ceremony this very evening." to which the
doctor object, as no one be prepare with a speech, "unless," say
he (turn to Adams with a gravity of countenance which would have
deceive a more knowing man), "you have a sermon about you, doctor."
"Sir," say Adams, "I never travel without one, for fear of what may
happen." he be easily prevail on by his worthy friend, as he now
call the doctor, to undertake the part of the ambassador; so that the
gentleman send immediate order to have the throne erect, which be
perform before they have drink two bottle; and, perhaps, the reader
will hereafter have no great reason to admire the nimbleness of the
servant. indeed, to confess the truth, the throne be no more than
this: there be a great tub of water provide, on each side of which
be place two stool raise high than the surface of the tub, and
over the whole be lay a blanket; on these stool be place the king
and queen, namely, the master of the house and the captain. and now the
ambassador be introduce between the poet and the doctor; who, have
read his sermon, to the great entertainment of all present, be lead up
to his place and seat between their majesty. they immediately rise
up, when the blanket, want its support at either end, give way, and
souse Adams over head and ear in the water. the captain make his
escape, but, unluckily, the gentleman himself not be as nimble as he
ought, Adams catch hold of he before he descend from his throne,
and pull he in with he, to the entire secret satisfaction of all
the company. Adams, after duck the squire twice or thrice, leapt out
of the tub, and look sharp for the doctor, whom he would certainly
have convey to the same place of honour; but he have wisely withdraw:
he then search for his crabstick, and have find that, as well as
his fellow traveller, he declare he would not stay a moment long in
such a house. he then depart, without take leave of his host, whom
he have exact a more severe revenge on than he intend; for, as he
do not use sufficient care to dry himself in time, he catch a cold by
the accident which throw he into a fever that have like to have cost
he his life.
CHAPTER VIII.
_which some reader will think too short and other too long._
Adams, and Joseph, who be no less enraged than his friend at the
treatment he meet with, go out with their stick in their hand, and
carry off Fanny, notwithstanding the opposition of the servant, who
do all, without proceed to violence, in their power to detain they.
they walk as fast as they could, not so much from any apprehension of
be pursue as that Mr Adams might, by exercise, prevent any harm from
the water. the gentleman, who have give such order to his servant
concern Fanny that he do not in the least fear she get away, no
soon hear that she be go, than he begin to rave, and immediately
despatch several with order either to bring she back or never return.
the poet, the player, and all but the dancing-master and doctor, go on
this errand.
the night be very dark in which our friend begin their journey;
however, they make such expedition, that they soon arrive at an inn
which be at seven mile' distance. here they unanimously consent to
pass the evening, Mr Adams be now as dry as he be before he have set
out on his embassy.
this inn, which indeed we might call an ale-house, have not the word,
the New Inn, be writ on the sign, afford they no well provision
than bread and cheese and ale; on which, however, they make a very
comfortable meal; for hunger be well than a french cook.
they have no soon sup, than Adams, return thank to the Almighty
for his food, declare he have eat his homely common with much great
satisfaction than his splendid dinner; and express great contempt for
the folly of mankind, who sacrifice their hope of heaven to the
acquisition of vast wealth, since so much comfort be to be find in the
humblest state and the low provision. "very true, sir," say a grave
man who sit smoake his pipe by the fire, and who be a traveller as
well as himself. "I have often be as much surprized as you be, when I
consider the value which mankind in general set on rich, since every
day's experience show we how little be in their power; for what,
indeed, truly desirable, can they bestow on we? can they give beauty to
the deform, strength to the weak, or health to the infirm? surely if
they could we should not see so many ill-favour face haunt the
assembly of the great, nor would such number of feeble wretch
languish in their coach and palace. no, not the wealth of a kingdom
can purchase any paint to dress pale Ugliness in the bloom of that young
maiden, nor any drug to equip Disease with the vigour of that young
man. do not rich bring we to solicitude instead of rest, envy instead
of affection, and danger instead of safety? can they prolong their own
possession, or lengthen his day who enjoy they? so far otherwise, that
the sloth, the luxury, the care which attend they, shorten the life of
million, and bring they with pain and misery to an untimely grave.
where, then, be their value if they can neither embellish nor strengthen
our form, sweeten nor prolong our lives?--again: can they adorn the
mind more than the body? do they not rather swell the heart with vanity,
puff up the cheek with pride, shut our ear to every call of virtue,
and our bowel to every motive of compassion?" "give I your hand,
brother," say Adams, in a rapture, "for I suppose you be a
clergyman."--"No, truly," answer the other (indeed, he be a priest of
the Church of Rome; but those who understand our law will not wonder he
be not over-ready to own it).--"Whatever you be," cry Adams, "you
have speak my sentiment: I believe I have preach every syllable of
your speech twenty time over; for it hath always appear to I easy
for a cable-rope (which by the way be the true rendering of that word we
have translate camel) to go through the eye of a needle than for a rich
man to get into the kingdom of heaven."--"That, sir," say the other,
"will be easily grant you by divine, and be deplorably true; but as
the prospect of our good at a distance doth not so forcibly affect we,
it might be of some service to mankind to be make thoroughly
sensible--which I think they might be with very little serious
attention--that even the blessing of this world be not to be purchase
with rich; a doctrine, in my opinion, not only metaphysically, but, if
I may so say, mathematically demonstrable; and which I have be always
so perfectly convinced of that I have a contempt for nothing so much as
for gold." Adams now begin a long discourse: but as most which he say
occur among many author who have treat this subject, I shall omit
insert it. during its continuance Joseph and Fanny retire to rest,
and the host likewise leave the room. when the english parson have
conclude, the Romish resume the discourse, which he continue with
great bitterness and invective; and at last end by desire Adams to
lend he eighteen-pence to pay his reckoning; promise, if he never
pay he, he might be assure of his prayer. the good man answer that
eighteen-penny would be too little to carry he any very long journey;
that he have half a guinea in his pocket, which he would divide with he.
he then fall to search his pocket, but could find no money; for
indeed the company with whom he din have pass one jest upon he which
we do not then enumerate, and have pick his pocket of all that
treasure which he have so ostentatiously produce.
"bless I!" cry Adams, "I have certainly lose it; I can never have
spend it. Sir, as I be a Christian, I have a whole half-guinea in my
pocket this morning, and have not now a single halfpenny of it leave.
sure the devil must have take it from me!"--"sir," answer the priest,
smile, "you need make no excuse; if you be not willing to lend I
