the money, I be content."--"sir," cry Adams, "if I have the great
sum in the world--aye, if I have ten pound about I--I would bestow it
all to rescue any Christian from distress. I be more vexed at my loss on
your account than my own. be ever anything so unlucky? because I have
no money in my pocket I shall be suspect to be no Christian."--"I be
more unlucky," quoth the other, "if you be as generous as you say; for
really a crown would have make I happy, and convey I in plenty to
the place I be go, which be not above twenty mile off, and where I
can arrive by to-morrow night. I assure you I be not accustomed to
travel pennyless. I be but just arrive in England; and we be force
by a storm in our passage to throw all we have overboard. I donot suspect
but this fellow will take my word for the trifle I owe he; but I hate
to appear so mean as to confess myself without a shilling to such
people; for these, and indeed too many other, know little difference in
their estimation between a beggar and a thief." however, he think he
should deal well with the host that evening than the next morning: he
therefore resolve to set out immediately, notwithstanding the darkness;
and accordingly, as soon as the host return, he communicate to he
the situation of his affair; upon which the host, scratch his head,
answer, "why, I do not know, master; if it be so, and you have no
money, I must trust, I think, though I have rather always have ready
money if I could; but, marry, you look like so honest a gentleman that I
donot fear your pay I if it be twenty time as much." the priest
make no reply, but, take leave of he and Adams as fast as he could,
not without confusion, and perhaps with some distrust of Adams's
sincerity, depart.
he be no soon go than the host fall a-shake his head, and
declare, if he have suspect the fellow have no money, he would not have
draw he a single drop of drink, say he despair of ever see his
face again, for that he look like a confounded rogue.
"rabbit the fellow," cry he, "I think, by his talk so much about
rich, that he have a hundred pound at least in his pocket." Adams chid
he for his suspicion, which, he say, be not become a Christian;
and then, without reflect on his loss, or consider how he himself
should depart in the morning, he retire to a very homely bed, as his
companion have before; however, health and fatigue give they a sweeter
repose than be often in the power of velvet and down to bestow.
CHAPTER IX.
_contain as surprize and bloody adventure as can be find in this
or perhaps any other authentic history._
it be almost morning when Joseph Andrews, whose eye the thought of
his dear Fanny have open, as he lie fondly meditate on that lovely
creature, hear a violent knock at the door over which he lay. he
presently jump out of bed, and, open the window, be ask if there
be no traveller in the house? and presently, by another voice, if two
man and a woman have not take up there their lodge that night? though
he know not the voice, he begin to entertain a suspicion of the
truth--for indeed he have receive some information from one of the
servant of the squire's house of his design--and answer in the
negative. one of the servant, who know the host well, call out to he
by his name just as he have open another window, and ask he the same
question; to which he answer in the affirmative. o ho! say another,
have we find you? and order the host to come down and open his door.
Fanny, who be as wakeful as Joseph, no soon hear all this than she
leap from her bed, and, hastily put on her gown and petticoat,
run as fast as possible to Joseph's room, who then be almost drest. he
immediately let she in, and, embrace she with the most passionate
tenderness, bid her fear nothing, for he would die in her defence. "be
that a reason why I should not fear," say she, "when I should lose what
be dearer to I than the whole world?" Joseph, then kiss her hand,
say, "he could almost thank the occasion which have extort from her a
tenderness she would never indulge he with before." he then run and
wake his bedfellow Adams, who be yet fast asleep, notwithstanding many
call from Joseph; but be no soon make sensible of their danger than
he leap from his bed, without consider the presence of Fanny, who
hastily turn her face from he, and enjoy a double benefit from the
dark, which, as it would have prevent any offence, to an innocence
less pure, or a modesty less delicate, so it conceal even those
blush which be raise in she.
Adams have soon put on all his clothe but his breech, which, in the
hurry, he forget; however, they be pretty well supply by the length
of his other garment; and now, the house-door be open, the
captain, the poet, the player, and three servant come in. the captain
tell the host that two fellow, who be in his house, have run away with
a young woman, and desire to know in which room she lay. the host, who
presently believe the story, direct they, and instantly the captain
and poet, justle one another, run up. the poet, who be the nimble,
enter the chamber first, search the bed, and every other part, but
to no purpose; the bird be fly, as the impatient reader, who might
otherwise have be in pain for she, be before advertise. they then
enquire where the man lay, and be approach the chamber, when
Joseph roar out, in a loud voice, that he would shoot the first man
who offer to attack the door. the captain enquire what fire-arm they
have; to which the host answer, he believe they have none; nay, he be
almost convinced of it, for he have hear one ask the other in the
evening what they should have do if they have be overtake, when they
have no arm; to which the other answer, they would have defend
themselves with their stick as long as they be able, and God would
assist a just cause. this satisfy the captain, but not the poet, who
prudently retreat downstairs, say, it be his business to record
great action, and not to do they. the captain be no soon well
satisfied that there be no fire-arm than, bid defiance to
gunpowder, and swear he love the smell of it, he order the
servant to follow he, and, march boldly up, immediately attempt
to force the door, which the servant soon help he to accomplish.
when it be open, they discover the enemy draw up three deep; Adams
in the front, and Fanny in the rear. the captain tell Adams that if they
would go all back to the house again they should be civilly treat; but
unless they consent he have order to carry the young lady with he,
whom there be great reason to believe they have steal from her parent;
for, notwithstanding her disguise, her air, which she could not conceal,
sufficiently discover her birth to be infinitely superior to theirs.
Fanny, burst into tear, solemnly assure he he be mistaken; that
she be a poor helpless foundling, and have no relation in the world
which she know of; and, throw herself on her knee, beg that he
would not attempt to take she from her friend, who, she be convince,
would die before they would lose she; which Adams confirm with word
not far from amount to an oath. the captain swear he have no leisure
to talk, and, bid they thank themselves for what happen, he
order the servant to fall on, at the same time endeavour to pass
by Adams, in order to lay hold on Fanny; but the parson, interrupt
he, receive a blow from one of they, which, without consider whence
it come, he return to the captain, and give he so dexterous a knock
in that part of the stomach which be vulgarly call the pit, that he
stagger some pace backwards. the captain, who be not accustomed to
this kind of play, and who wisely apprehend the consequence of such
another blow, two of they seem to he equal to a thrust through the
body, draw forth his hanger, as Adams approach he, and be level
a blow at his head, which would probably have silence the preacher for
ever, have not Joseph in that instant lift up a certain huge stone pot
of the chamber with one hand, which six beau could not have lift with
both, and discharge it, together with the content, full in the
captain's face. the uplifted hanger drop from his hand, and he fall
prostrate on the floor with a lumpish noise, and his halfpence rattle
in his pocket; the red liquor which his vein contain, and the white
liquor which the pot contain, run in one stream down his face and his
clothe. nor have Adams quite escape, some of the water have in its
passage shed its honour on his head, and begin to trickle down the
wrinkle or rather furrow of his cheek, when one of the servant,
snatch a mop out of a pail of water, which have already do its duty
in wash the house, push it in the parson's face; yet could not he
bear he down, for the parson, wrest the mop from the fellow with one
hand, with the other bring his enemy as low as the earth, have give
he a stroke over that part of the face where, in some man of pleasure,
the natural and artificial nose be conjoin.
Hitherto, Fortune seem to incline the victory on the traveller' side,
when, accord to her custom, she begin to show the fickleness of her
disposition; for now the host, enter the field, or rather chamber of
battle, fly directly at Joseph, and, dart his head into his stomach
(for he be a stout fellow and an expert boxer), almost stagger he:
but Joseph, step one leg back, do with his left hand so chuck he
under the chin that he reel. the youth be pursue his blow with his
right hand when he receive from one of the servant such a stroke with
a cudgel on his temple, that it instantly deprive he of sense, and he
measure his length on the ground.
Fanny rent the air with her cry, and Adams be come to the
assistance of Joseph; but the two serve-man and the host now fall on
he, and soon subdue he, though he fight like a madman, and look so
black with the impression he have receive from the mop, that Don
Quixote would certainly have take he for an inchanted moor. but now
follow the most tragical part; for the captain be rise again, and,
see Joseph on the floor, and Adams secure, he instantly lay hold on
Fanny, and, with the assistance of the poet and player, who, hear the
battle be over, be now come up, drag she, cry and tear her
hair, from the sight of her Joseph, and, with a perfect deafness to all
her entreaty, carry she downstairs by violence, and fasten she on
the player's horse; and the captain, mount his own, and lead that
on which this poor miserable wretch be, depart, without any more
consideration of her cry than a butcher hath of those of a lamb; for
indeed his thought be entertain only with the degree of favour
which he promise himself from the squire on the success of this
adventure.
the servant, who be order to secure Adams and Joseph as safe as
possible, that the squire might receive no interruption to his design on
poor Fanny, immediately, by the poet's advice, tie Adams to one of the
bed-post, as they do Joseph on the other side, as soon as they could
bring he to himself; and then, leave they together, back to back, and
desire the host not to set they at liberty, nor to go near they, till
he have further order, they depart towards their master; but happen
to take a different road from that which the captain have fall into.
CHAPTER X.
_a discourse between the poet and the player; of no other use in this
history but to divert the reader._
before we proceed any far in this tragedy we shall leave Mr Joseph
and Mr Adams to themselves, and imitate the wise conductor of the
stage, who in the midst of a grave action entertain you with some
excellent piece of satire or humour call a dance. which piece, indeed,
be therefore dance, and not speak, as it be deliver to the audience
by person whose thinking faculty be by most people hold to lie in their
heel; and to whom, as well as hero, who think with their hand,
nature hath only give head for the sake of conformity, and as they be
of use in dancing, to hang their hat on.
the poet, address the player, proceed thus, "as I be say" (for
they have be at this discourse all the time of the engagement
above-stairs), "the reason you have no good new play be evident; it be
from your discouragement of author. gentleman will not write, sir, they
will not write, without the expectation of fame or profit, or perhaps
both. play be like tree, which will not grow without nourishment; but
like mushroom, they shoot up spontaneously, as it be, in a rich soil.
the mus, like vine, may be prune, but not with a hatchet. the town,
like a peevish child, know not what it desire, and be always good
pleased with a rattle. a farce-writer hath indeed some chance for
success: but they have lose all taste for the sublime. though I believe
one reason of their depravity be the badness of the actor. if a man
write like an angel, sir, those fellow know not how to give a
sentiment utterance."--"Not so fast," say the player: "the modern
actor be as good at least as their author, nay, they come nearer
their illustrious predecessor; and I expect a Booth on the stage again,
soon than a Shakespear or an Otway; and indeed I may turn your
observation against you, and with truth say, that the reason no author
be encourage be because we have no good new play."--"i have not
affirm the contrary," say the poet; "but I be surprize you grow so
warm; you cannot imagine yourself interested in this dispute; I hope you
have a well opinion of my taste than to apprehend I squint at
yourself. no, sir, if we have six such actor as you, we should soon
rival the Bettertons and Sandfords of former time; for, without a
compliment to you, I think it impossible for any one to have excel
you in most of your part. Nay, it be solemn truth, and I have hear
many, and all great judge, express as much; and, you will pardon I if
I tell you, I think every time I have see you lately you have
constantly acquire some new excellence, like a snowball. you have
deceive I in my estimation of perfection, and have outdo what I
think inimitable."--"you be as little interested," answer the
player, "in what I have say of other poet; for d--n I if there be
not many stroke, ay, whole scene, in your last tragedy, which at least
equal Shakespear. there be a delicacy of sentiment, a dignity of
expression in it, which I will own many of our gentleman do not do
adequate justice to. to confess the truth, they be bad enough, and I
pity an author who be present at the murder of his work."--"Nay, it be
but seldom that it can happen," return the poet; "the work of most
modern author, like dead-bear child, cannot be murder. it be such
wretched half-beget, half-writ, lifeless, spiritless, low, grovel
stuff, that I almost pity the actor who be oblige to get it by heart,
which must be almost as difficult to remember as word in a language you
donot understand."--"i be sure," say the player, "if the sentence have
little meaning when they be writ, when they be speak they have less.
I know scarce one who ever lay an emphasis right, and much less adapt
his action to his character. I have see a tender lover in an attitude
of fight with his mistress, and a brave hero sue to his enemy with
his sword in his hand. I donot care to abuse my profession, but rot I
if in my heart I be not inclined to the poet's side."--"It be rather
generous in you than just," say the poet; "and, though I hate to speak
ill of any person's production--nay, I never do it, nor will--but yet,
to do justice to the actor, what could Booth or Betterton have make of
such horrible stuff as Fenton's Mariamne, Frowd's philotas, or Mallet's
Eurydice; or those low, dirty, last-die-speech, which a fellow in
the city of Wapping, your Dillo or Lillo, what be his name, call
tragedies?"--"very well," say the player; "and pray what do you think
of such fellow as Quin and delane, or that face-make puppy young
Cibber, that ill-look dog Macklin, or that saucy slut Mrs Clive? what
work would they make with your shakespear, Otways, and lee? how would
those harmonious line of the last come from their tongues?--
"'--no more; for I disdain
all pomp when thou art by: far be the noise
of king and crown from we, whose gentle soul
our kind fate have steer'd another way.
free as the forest bird wewill pair together,
without rememb're who our father be:
fly to the arbor, grot, and flow'ry mead;
there in soft murmur interchange our soul;
together drink the crystal of the stream,
or taste the yellow fruit which autumn yield,
and, when the golden evening call we home,
wing to our downy nest, and sleep till morn.'
"or how would this disdain of Otway--
"'whowould be that foolish sordid thing call'd man?'"
"hold! hold! hold!" say the poet: "do repeat that tender speech in the
third act of my play which you make such a figure in."--"I would
willingly," say the player, "but I have forget it."--"Ay, you be not
quite perfect in it when you play it," cry the poet, "or you would
have have such an applause as be never give on the stage; an applause I
be extremely concerned for your losing."--"sure," say the player, "if
I remember, that be hiss more than any passage in the whole
play."--"Ay, your speak it be hiss," say the poet.--"my speak
it!" say the player.--"i mean your not speak it," say the poet.
"you be out, and then they hiss."--"They hiss, and then I be out,
if I remember," answer the player; "and I must say this for myself,
that the whole audience allow I do your part justice; so donot lay
the damnation of your play to my account."--"i donot know what you mean
by damnation," reply the poet.--"Why, you know it be act but one
night," cry the player.--"no," say the poet, "you and the whole town
be enemy; the pit be all my enemy, fellow that would cut my
throat, if the fear of hang do not restrain they. all taylor, sir,
all taylor."--"Why should the taylor be so angry with you?" cry the
player. "I suppose you donot employ so many in make your clothe."--"I
admit your jest," answer the poet; "but you remember the affair as
well as myself; you know there be a party in the pit and upper gallery
that would not suffer it to be give out again; though much, ay
infinitely, the majority, all the box in particular, be desirous of
it; nay, most of the lady swear they never would come to the house
till it be act again. indeed, I must own their policy be good in not
let it be give out a second time: for the rascal know if it have
go a second night it would have run fifty; for if ever there be
distress in a tragedy--I be not fond of my own performance; but if I
should tell you what the good judge say of it--nor be it entirely
owe to my enemy neither that it do not succeed on the stage as well
as it hath since among the polite reader; for you cannot say it have
justice do it by the performer."--"I think," answer the player,
"the performer do the distress of it justice; for I be sure we be in
distress enough, who be pelt with orange all the last act: we all
imagine it would have be the last act of our life."
the poet, whose fury be now raise, have just attempt to answer when
they be interrupt, and an end put to their discourse, by an
accident, which if the reader be impatient to know, he must skip over
the next chapter, which be a sort of counterpart to this, and contain
some of the good and grave matter in the whole book, be a
discourse between parson Abraham Adams and Mr Joseph Andrews.
CHAPTER XI.
_contain the exhortation of parson Adams to his friend in
affliction; calculate for the instruction and improvement of the
reader._
Joseph no soon come perfectly to himself than, perceive his mistress
go, he bewail her loss with groan which would have pierce any
heart but those which be possess by some people, and be make of a
certain composition not unlike flint in its hardness and other
property; for you may strike fire from they, which will dart through
the eye, but they can never distil one drop of water the same way. his
own, poor youth! be of a soft composition; and at those word, "o my
dear fanny! o my love! shall I never, never see thee more?" his eye
overflow with tear, which would have become any but a hero. in a
word, his despair be more easy to be conceive than related.
Mr Adams, after many groan, sit with his back to Joseph, begin thus
in a sorrowful tone: "you cannot imagine, my good child, that I entirely
blame these first agony of your grief; for, when misfortune attack we
by surprize, it must require infinitely more learning than you be
master of to resist they; but it be the business of a man and a
Christian to summon Reason as quickly as he can to his aid; and she will
presently teach he patience and submission. be comfort, therefore,
child; I say be comfort. it be true, you have lose the prettiest,
kind, loveliest, sweet young woman, one with whom you might have
expect to have live in happiness, virtue, and innocence; by whom you
might have promise yourself many little darling, who would have be
the delight of your youth and the comfort of your age. you have not only
lose she, but have reason to fear the utmost violence which lust and
power can inflict upon she. now, indeed, you may easily raise idea of
horror, which might drive you to despair."--"O I shall run mad!" cry
Joseph. "o that I could but command my hand to tear my eye out and my
flesh off!"--"if you would use they to such purpose, I be glad you
cannot," answer Adams. "I have state your misfortune as strong as I
possibly can; but, on the other side, you be to consider you be a
Christian, that no accident happen to we without the divine permission,
and that it be the duty of a man, and a Christian, to submit. we do not
make ourselves; but the same power which make we rule over we, and we
be absolutely at his disposal; he may do with we what he please, nor
have we any right to complain. a second reason against our complaint be
our ignorance; for, as we know not future event, so neither can we tell
to what purpose any accident tend; and that which at first threaten we
with evil may in the end produce our good. I should indeed have say our
ignorance be twofold (but I have not at present time to divide
properly), for, as we know not to what purpose any event be ultimately
direct, so neither can we affirm from what cause it originally spring.
you be a man, and consequently a sinner; and this may be a punishment
to you for your sin: indeed in this sense it may be esteem as a good,
yea, as the great good, which satisfy the anger of Heaven, and
avert that wrath which cannot continue without our destruction.
thirdly, our impotency of relieve ourselves demonstrate the folly and
absurdity of our complaint: for whom do we resist, or against whom do
we complain, but a power from whose shaft no armour can guard we, no
speed can fly?--a power which leave we no hope but in submission." "o
sir!" cry Joseph, "all this be very true, and very fine, and I could
hear you all day if I be not so grieve at heart as now I be."--"Would
you take physic," say Adams, "when you be well, and refuse it when you
be sick? be not comfort to be administer to the afflict, and not to
those who rejoice or those who be at ease?" "o! you have not speak one
word of comfort to I yet!" return Joseph. "no!" cry Adams; "what be
I then do? what can I say to comfort you?" "o tell I," cry Joseph,
"that Fanny will escape back to my arm, that they shall again enclose
that lovely creature, with all her sweetness, all her untainted
innocence about she!" "why, perhaps you may," cry Adams, "but I cannot
promise you whatbe to come. you must, with perfect resignation, wait the
event: if she be restore to you again, it be your duty to be thankful,
and so it be if she be not. Joseph, if you be wise and truly know your
own interest, you will peaceably and quietly submit to all the
dispensation of Providence, be thoroughly assure that all the
misfortune, how great soever, which happen to the righteous, happen to
they for their own good. Nay, it be not your interest only, but your
duty, to abstain from immoderate grief; which if you indulge, you be
not worthy the name of a Christian." he speak these last word with an
accent a little severer than usual; upon which Joseph beg he not to
be angry, say, he mistake he if he think he deny it be his
duty, for he have know that long ago. "what signify know your duty,
if you do not perform it?" answer Adams. "your knowledge increase
your guilt. o Joseph! I never think you have this stubbornness in your
mind." Joseph reply, "he fancy he misunderstood he; which I assure
you," say he, "you do, if you imagine I endeavour to grieve; upon my
soul I donot." Adams rebuke he for swearing, and then proceed to
enlarge on the folly of grief, tell he, all the wise man and
philosopher, even among the heathen, have write against it, quote
several passage from Seneca, and the Consolation, which, though it be
not Cicero's, be, he say, as good almost as any of his work; and
conclude all by hint that immoderate grief in this case might
incense that power which alone could restore he his fanny. this reason,
or indeed rather the idea which it raise of the restoration of his
mistress, have more effect than all which the parson have say before, and
for a moment abate his agony; but, when his fear sufficiently set
before his eye the danger that poor creature be in, his grief return
again with repeat violence, nor could Adams in the least asswage it;
though it may be doubt in his behalf whether Socrates himself could
have prevail any well.
they remain some time in silence, and groan and sigh issue from
they both; at length Joseph burst out into the follow soliloquy:--
"yes, I will bear my sorrow like a man,
but I must also feel they as a man.
I cannot but remember such thing be,
and be most dear to I."
Adams ask he what stuff that be he repeat? to which he answer,
they be some line he have get by heart out of a play. "ay, there be
nothing but heathenism to be learn from play," reply he. "I never
hear of any play fit for a Christian to read, but Cato and the
Conscious Lovers; and, I must own, in the latter there be some thing
almost solemn enough for a sermon." but we shall now leave they a
little, and enquire after the subject of their conversation.
CHAPTER XII.
_More adventure, which we hope will as much please as surprize
the reader._
neither the facetious dialogue which pass between the poet and the
player, nor the grave and truly solemn discourse of Mr Adams, will, we
conceive, make the reader sufficient amend for the anxiety which he
must have feel on the account of poor Fanny, whom we leave in so
deplorable a condition. we shall therefore now proceed to the relation
of what happen to that beautiful and innocent virgin, after she fall
into the wicked hand of the captain.
the man of war, have convey his charming prize out of the inn a
little before day, make the utmost expedition in his power towards the
squire's house, where this delicate creature be to be offer up a
sacrifice to the lust of a ravisher. he be not only deaf to all her
bewailing and entreaty on the road, but accost her ear with
impurity which, have be never before accustomed to they, she
happily for herself very little understood. at last he change his note,
and attempt to soothe and mollify she, by set forth the splendor
and luxury which would be her fortune with a man who would have the
inclination, and power too, to give she whatever her utmost wish could
desire; and tell she he doubt not but she would soon look kind on
he, as the instrument of her happiness, and despise that pitiful fellow
whom her ignorance only could make she fond of. she answer, she know
not whom he mean; she never be fond of any pitiful fellow. "be you
affront, madam," say he, "at my call he so? but what well can
be say of one in a livery, notwithstanding your fondness for he?" she
return, that she do not understand he, that the man have be her
fellow-servant, and she believe be as honest a creature as any alive;
but as for fondness for men--"I warrant ye," cry the captain, "we
shall find mean to persuade you to be fond; and I advise you to yield
to gentle one, for you may be assure that it be not in your power, by
any struggle whatever, to preserve your virginity two hour long. it
will be your interest to consent; for the squire will be much kind to
you if he enjoy you willingly than by force." at which word she begin
to call aloud for assistance (for it be now open day), but, find
none, she lift her eye to heaven, and supplicate the Divine
assistance to preserve her innocence. the captain tell she, if she
persist in her vociferation, he would find a mean of stop she
mouth. and now the poor wretch, perceive no hope of succour,
abandon herself to despair, and, sigh out the name of Joseph!
Joseph! a river of tear run down her lovely cheek, and wet the
handkerchief which cover her bosom. a horseman now appear in the
road, upon which the captain threaten she violently if she complain;
however, the moment they approach each other she beg he with the
utmost earnestness to relieve a distressed creature who be in the hand
of a ravisher. the fellow stopt at those word, but the captain assure
he it be his wife, and that he be carry her home from her
adulterer, which so satisfy the fellow, who be an old one (and
perhaps a married one too), that he wish he a good journey, and ride
on. he be no soon past than the captain abuse she violently for
break his command, and threaten to gagg she, when two more
horseman, arm with pistol, come into the road just before they. she
again solicit their assistance, and the captain tell the same story as
before. upon which one say to the other, "thatbe a charming wench,
Jack; I wish I have be in the fellow's place, whoever he be." but the
other, instead of answer he, cry out, "Zounds, I know she;" and
then, turn to she, say, "sure you be not Fanny Goodwill?"--"Indeed,
indeed, I be," she cried--"O John, I know you now-Heaven hath send you
to my assistance, to deliver I from this wicked man, who be carry I
away for his vile purpose--o for God's sake rescue I from he!" a
fierce dialogue immediately ensue between the captain and these two
man, who, be both arm with pistol, and the chariot which they
attend be now arrive, the captain see both force and stratagem
be vain, and endeavour to make his escape, in which however he could
not succeed. the gentleman who ride in the chariot order it to stop,
and with an air of authority examine into the merit of the cause; of
which be advertise by Fanny, whose credit be confirm by the
fellow who know she, he order the captain, who be all bloody from his
encounter at the inn, to be convey as a prisoner behind the chariot,
and very gallantly take Fanny into it; for, to say the truth, this
gentleman (who be no other than the celebrated Mr Peter Pounce, and who
precede the Lady Booby only a few mile, by set out early in the
morning) be a very gallant person, and love a pretty girl well than
anything besides his own money or the money of other people.
the chariot now proceed towards the inn, which, as Fanny be inform,
lay in their way, and where it arrive at that very time while the poet
and player be dispute below-stair, and Adams and Joseph be
discourse back to back above; just at that period to which we bring
they both in the two precede chapter the chariot stopt at the door,
and in an instant fanny, leap from it, run up to her Joseph.--O
reader! conceive if thou canst the joy which fire the breast of these
lover on this meeting; and if thy own heart doth not sympathetically
assist thee in this conception, I pity thee sincerely from my own; for
let the hard-hearted villain know this, that there be a pleasure in a
tender sensation beyond any which he be capable of taste.
Peter, be inform by Fanny of the presence of Adams, stopt to see
he, and receive his homage; for, as Peter be an hypocrite, a sort of
people whom Mr Adams never see through, the one pay that respect to his
seeming goodness which the other believe to be pay to his rich;
hence Mr Adams be so much his favourite, that he once lend he four
pound thirteen shilling and sixpence to prevent his go to gaol, on
no great security than a bond and judgment, which probably he would
have make no use of, though the money have not be (as it be) pay
exactly at the time.
it be not perhaps easy to describe the figure of Adams; he have rise in
such a hurry, that he have on neither breech, garter, nor stocking;
nor have he take from his head a red spot handkerchief, which by
night bind his wig, turn inside out, around his head. he have on his
tear cassock and his greatcoat; but, as the remainder of his cassock
hang down below his greatcoat, so do a small stripe of white, or rather
whitish, linen appear below that; to which we may add the several
colour which appear on his face, where a long piss-burn beard serve
to retain the liquor of the stone-pot, and that of a blacker hue which
distil from the mop.--this figure, which Fanny have deliver from his
captivity, be no soon spy by Peter than it disorder the compose
gravity of his muscle; however, he advise he immediately to make
himself clean, nor would accept his homage in that pickle.
the poet and player no soon see the captain in captivity than they
begin to consider of their own safety, of which flight present itself
as the only mean; they therefore both of they mount the poet's horse,
and make the most expeditious retreat in their power.
the host, who well know Mr Pounce and Lady Booby's livery, be not a
little surprized at this change of the scene; nor be his confusion much
help by his wife, who be now just risen, and, have hear from he
the account of what have pass, comfort he with a decent number of
fool and blockhead; ask he why he do not consult she, and tell he
he would never leave follow the nonsensical dictate of his own
numskull till she and her family be ruin.
Joseph, be inform of the captain's arrival, and see his fanny
now in safety, quit she a moment, and, run downstairs, go
directly to he, and strip off his coat, challenge he to fight;
but the captain refuse, say he do not understand box. he then
grasp a cudgel in one hand, and, catch the captain by the collar
with the other, give he a most severe drubbing, and end with tell
he he have now have some revenge for what his dear Fanny have suffer.
when Mr Pounce have a little regale himself with some provision which he
have in his chariot, and Mr Adams have put on the good appearance his
clothe would allow he, Pounce order the captain into his presence,
for he say he be guilty of felony, and the next justice of peace
should commit he; but the servant (whose appetite for revenge be soon
satisfied), be sufficiently content with the drubbing which Joseph
have inflict on he, and which be indeed of no very moderate kind, have
suffer he to go off, which he do, threaten a severe revenge
against Joseph, which I have never hear he think proper to take.
the mistress of the house make her voluntary appearance before Mr
Pounce, and with a thousand curtsy tell he, "she hop his honour
would pardon her husband, who be a very nonsense man, for the sake of
his poor family; that indeed if he could be ruin alone, she should be
very willing of it; for because as why, his worship very well know he
deserve it; but she have three poor small child, who be not capable
to get their own living; and if her husband be send to gaol, they must
all come to the parish; for she be a poor weak woman, continually
a-breeding, and have no time to work for they. she therefore hop his
honour would take it into his worship's consideration, and forgive she
husband this time; for she be sure he never intend any harm to man,
woman, or child; and if it be not for that block-head of his own, the
man in some thing be well enough; for she have have three child by
he in less than three year, and be almost ready to cry out the fourth
time." she would have proceed in this manner much long, have not
Peter stopt her tongue, by tell she he have nothing to say to her
husband nor her neither. so, as Adams and the rest have assure she of
forgiveness, she cry and curtsy out of the room.
Mr Pounce be desirous that Fanny should continue her journey with he
in the chariot; but she absolutely refuse, say she would ride behind
Joseph on a horse which one of Lady Booby's servant have equip he
with. but, alas! when the horse appear, it be find to be no other
than that identical beast which Mr Adams have leave behind he at the inn,
and which these honest fellow, who know he, have redeem. indeed,
whatever horse they have provide for Joseph, they would have prevail
with he to mount none, no, not even to ride before his beloved fanny,
till the parson be supply; much less would he deprive his friend of
the beast which belong to he, and which he know the moment he see,
though Adams do not; however, when he be remind of the affair, and
tell that they have bring the horse with they which he leave behind, he
answer--bless I! and so I do.
Adams be very desirous that Joseph and Fanny should mount this horse,
and declare he could very easily walk home. "if I walk alone," say
he, "I would wage a shilling that the pedestrian outstrip the
equestrian traveller; but, as I intend to take the company of a pipe,
peradventure I may be an hour later." one of the servant whisper
Joseph to take he at his word, and suffer the old put to walk if he
would: this proposal be answer with an angry look and a peremptory
refusal by Joseph, who, catch Fanny up in his arm, aver he would
rather carry her home in that manner, than take away Mr Adams's horse
and permit he to walk on foot.
perhaps, reader, thou hast see a contest between two gentleman, or two
lady, quickly decide, though they have both assert they would not
eat such a nice morsel, and each insist on the other's accept it;
but in reality both be very desirous to swallow it themselves. do not
therefore conclude hence that this dispute would have come to a speedy
decision: for here both party be heartily in earnest, and it be very
probable they would have remain in the inn-yard to this day, have not
the good Peter Pounce put a stop to it; for, find he have no long
hope of satisfy his old appetite with Fanny, and be desirous of
have some one to whom he might communicate his grandeur, he tell the
parson he would convey he home in his chariot. this favour be by
Adams, with many bow and acknowledgment, accept, though he
afterwards say, "he ascend the chariot rather that he might not
offend than from any desire of rid in it, for that in his heart he
prefer the pedestrian even to the vehicular expedition." all matter
be now settle, the chariot, in which ride Adams and Pounce, move
forwards; and Joseph have borrow a pillion from the host, Fanny have
just seat herself thereon, and have lay hold of the girdle which she
lover wear for that purpose, when the wise beast, who conclude that one
at a time be sufficient, that two to one be odd, &c., discover
much uneasiness at his double load, and begin to consider his hinder as
his fore leg, move the direct contrary way to that which be call
forwards. nor could Joseph, with all his horsemanship, persuade he to
advance; but, without have any regard to the lovely part of the lovely
girl which be on his back, he use such agitation, that, have not one
of the man come immediately to her assistance, she have, in plain
English, tumble backwards on the ground. this inconvenience be
presently remedie by an exchange of horse; and then Fanny be again
place on her pillion, on a well-natured and somewhat a well-feed
beast, the parson's horse, find he have no long odd to contend
with, agree to march; and the whole procession set forwards for
Booby-hall, where they arrive in a few hour without anything
remarkable happen on the road, unless it be a curious dialogue
between the parson and the steward: which, to use the language of a late
Apologist, a pattern to all biographer, "wait for the reader in the
next chapter."
CHAPTER XIII.
_a curious dialogue which pass between Mr Abraham Adams and Mr Peter
Pounce, well worth read than all the work of Colley Cibber and
many other._
the chariot have not proceed far before Mr Adams observe it be a very
fine day. "ay, and a very fine country too," answer Pounce.--"I should
think so more," return Adams, "if I have not lately travel over the
Downs, which I take to exceed this and all other prospect in the
universe."--"a fig for prospect!" answer Pounce; "one acre here be
worth ten there; and for my own part, I have no delight in the prospect
of any land but my own."--"sir," say Adams, "you can indulge yourself
with many fine prospect of that kind."--"I thank God I have a little,"
reply the other, "with which I be content, and envy no man: I have a
little, Mr Adams, with which I do as much good as I can." Adams
answer, "that riches without charity be nothing worth; for that they
be a blessing only to he who make they a blessing to other."--"you
and I," say Peter, "have different notion of charity. I own, as it be
generally use, I do not like the word, nor do I think it become one of
us gentleman; it be a mean parson-like quality; though I would not infer
many parson have it neither."--"sir," say Adams, "my definition of
charity be, a generous disposition to relieve the distressed."--"there
be something in that definition," answer Peter, "which I like well
enough; it be, as you say, a disposition, and do not so much consist
in the act as in the disposition to do it. but, alas! Mr Adams, who be
mean by the distressed? believe I, the distress of mankind be
mostly imaginary, and it would be rather folly than goodness to relieve
they."--"sure, sir," reply Adams, "hunger and thirst, cold and
nakedness, and other distress which attend the poor, can never be say
to be imaginary evil."--"How can any man complain of hunger," say
Peter, "in a country where such excellent salad be to be gather in
almost every field? or of thirst, where every river and stream produce
such delicious potation? and as for cold and nakedness, they be evil
introduce by luxury and custom. a man naturally want clothe no more
than a horse or any other animal; and there be whole nation who go
without they; but these be thing perhaps which you, who do not know
the world"--"you will pardon I, sir," return Adams; "I have read of
the Gymnosophists."--"a plague of your jehosaphat!" cry Peter; "the
great fault in our constitution be the provision make for the poor,
except that perhaps make for some other. Sir, I have not an estate
which doth not contribute almost as much again to the poor as to the
land-tax; and I do assure you I expect to come myself to the parish in
the end." to which Adams give a dissent smile, Peter thus
proceed: "I fancy, Mr Adams, you be one of those who imagine I be a
lump of money; for there be many who, I fancy, believe that not only my
pocket, but my whole clothe, be line with bank-bill; but I assure
you, you be all mistaken; I be not the man the world esteem I. if I
can hold my head above water it be all I can. I have injure myself by
purchasing. I have be too liberal of my money. indeed, I fear my heir
will find my affair in a bad situation than they be repute to be.
ah! he will have reason to wish I have love money more and land less.
Pray, my good neighbour, where should I have that quantity of rich the
world be so liberal to bestow on I? where could I possibly, without I
have steal it, acquire such a treasure?" "why, truly," say Adams, "I
have be always of your opinion; I have wonder as well as yourself
with what confidence they could report such thing of you, which have to
I appear as mere impossibility; for you know, sir, and I have often
hear you say it, that your wealth be of your own acquisition; and can
it be credible that in your short time you should have amass such a
heap of treasure as these people will have you worth? indeed, have you
inherit an estate like Sir Thomas Booby, which have descend in your
family for many generation, they might have have a colour for their
assertion." "why, what do they say I be worth?" cry Peter, with a
malicious sneer. "Sir," answer Adams, "I have hear some aver you be
not worth less than twenty thousand pound." at which Peter frown.
"Nay, sir," say Adams, "you ask I only the opinion of other; for my
own part, I have always deny it, nor do I ever believe you could
possibly be worth half that sum." "however, Mr Adams," say he,
squeeze he by the hand, "I would not sell they all I be worth for
double that sum; and as to what you believe, or they believe, I care not
a fig, no not a fart. I be not poor because you think I so, nor because
you attempt to undervalue I in the country. I know the envy of mankind
very well; but I thank Heaven I be above they. it be true, my wealth be
of my own acquisition. I have not an estate, like Sir Thomas Booby, that
have descend in my family through many generation; but I know heir of
such estate who be force to travel about the country like some people
in torn cassock, and might be glad to accept of a pitiful curacy for
what I know. yes, sir, as shabby fellow as yourself, whom no man of my
figure, without that vice of good-nature about he, would suffer to ride
in a chariot with he." "Sir," say Adams, "I value not your chariot of
a rush; and if I have know you have intend to affront I, I would have
walk to the world's end on foot ere I would have accept a place in
it. however, sir, I will soon rid you of that inconvenience;" and, so
say, he open the chariot door, without call to the coachman, and
leapt out into the highway, forget to take his hat along with he;
which, however, Mr Pounce throw after he with great violence. Joseph
and Fanny stopt to bear he company the rest of the way, which be not
above a mile.
BOOK IV.
CHAPTER I.
_the arrival of Lady Booby and the rest at Booby-hall._
the coach and six, in which Lady Booby ride, overtake the other
traveller as they enter the parish. she no soon see Joseph than she
cheek glow with red, and immediately after become as totally pale.
she have in her surprize almost stopt her coach; but recollect herself
timely enough to prevent it. she enter the parish amidst the ringing
of bell and the acclamation of the poor, who be rejoice to see
their patroness return after so long an absence, during which time all
her rent have be draft to London, without a shilling be spend
among they, which tend not a little to their utter impoverishing; for,
if the court would be severely miss in such a city as London, how much
more must the absence of a person of great fortune be feel in a little
country village, for whose inhabitant such a family find a constant
employment and supply; and with the offal of whose table the infirm,
aged, and infant poor be abundantly feed, with a generosity which hath
scarce a visible effect on their benefactor' pocket!
but, if their interest inspire so public a joy into every
countenance, how much more forcibly do the affection which they bear
parson Adams operate upon all who behold his return! they flock
about he like dutiful child round an indulgent parent, and vye
with each other in demonstration of duty and love. the parson on his
side shake every one by the hand, enquire heartily after the health
of all that be absent, of their child, and relation; and expr
a satisfaction in his face which nothing but benevolence make happy by
its object could infuse.
nor do Joseph and Fanny want a hearty welcome from all who see they. in
short, no three person could be more kindly receive, as, indeed, none
ever more deserved to be universally beloved.
Adams carry his fellow-traveller home to his house, where he insist
on their partaking whatever his wife, whom, with his child, he find
in health and joy, could provide:--where we shall leave they enjoy
perfect happiness over a homely meal, to view scene of great
splendour, but infinitely less bliss.
our more intelligent reader will doubtless suspect, by this second
appearance of Lady Booby on the stage, that all be not end by the
dismission of Joseph; and, to be honest with they, they be in the
right: the arrow have pierce deeply than she imagine; nor be the wound
so easily to be cure. the removal of the object soon cool her rage,
but it have a different effect on her love; that depart with his
person, but this remain lurk in her mind with his image. Restless,
interrupted slumber, and confuse horrible dream be her portion the
first night. in the morning, fancy paint she a more delicious scene;
but to delude, not delight she; for, before she could reach the promise
happiness, it vanish, and leave she to curse, not bless, the vision.
she start from her sleep, her imagination be all on fire with the
phantom, when, her eye accidentally glance towards the spot where
yesterday the real Joseph have stand, that little circumstance raise his
idea in the lively colour in her memory. each look, each word, each
gesture rush back on her mind with charm which all his coldness could
not abate. Nay, she impute that to his youth, his folly, his awe, his
religion, to everything but what would instantly have produce contempt,
want of passion for the sex, or that which would have rouse her hatred,
want of like to she.
reflection then hurry she far, and tell she she must see this
beautiful youth no more; nay, suggest to she that she herself have
dismiss he for no other fault than probably that of too violent an
awe and respect for herself; and which she ought rather to have esteem
a merit, the effect of which be besides so easily and surely to have
be remove; she then blame, she curse the hasty rashness of her
temper; her fury be vent all on herself, and Joseph appear innocent
in her eye. her passion at length grow so violent, that it force she
on seek relief, and now she think of recall he: but pride
forbad that; pride, which soon drive all soft passion from her soul,
and represent to she the meanness of he she be fond of. that thought
soon begin to obscure his beauty; contempt succeed next, and then
disdain, which presently introduce her hatred of the creature who have
give she so much uneasiness. these enemy of Joseph have no soon
take possession of her mind than they insinuate to she a thousand
thing in his disfavour; everything but dislike of her person; a thought
which, as it would have be intolerable to bear, she check the moment
it endeavour to arise. revenge come now to her assistance; and she
consider her dismission of he, stript, and without a character, with
the utmost pleasure. she riot in the several kind of misery which she
imagination suggest to she might be his fate; and, with a smile
compose of anger, mirth, and scorn, view he in the rag in which she
fancy have drest he.
Mrs Slipslop, be summon, attend her mistress, who have now in her
own opinion totally subdue this passion. whilst she be dress she
ask if that fellow have be turn away accord to her order.
Slipslop answer, she have tell her ladyship so (as indeed she
had).--"and how do he behave?" reply the lady. "truly, madam," cry
Slipslop, "in such a manner that infect everybody who see he. the
poor lad have but little wage to receive; for he constantly allow his
father and mother half his income; so that, when your ladyship's livery
be stript off, he have not wherewithal to buy a coat, and must have go
naked if one of the footman have not incommodate he with one; and
whilst he be stand in his shirt (and, to say truth, he be an
amorous figure), be tell your ladyship would not give he a
character, he sigh, and say he have do nothing willingly to offend;
that for his part, he should always give your ladyship a good character
wherever he go; and he pray God to bless you; for you be the good
of lady, though his enemy have set you against he. I wish you have
not turn he away; for I believe you have not a faithfuller servant in
the house."--"How come you then," reply the lady, "to advise I to
turn he away?"--"I, madam!" say Slipslop; "I be sure you will do I
the justice to say, I do all in my power to prevent it; but I see your
ladyship be angry; and it be not the business of we upper servant to
hinterfear on these occasion." "and be it not you, audacious wretch!"
cry the lady, "who make I angry? be it not your tittle-tattle, in
which I believe you belye the poor fellow, which incense I against
he? he may thank you for all that hath happen; and so may I for the
loss of a good servant, and one who probably have more merit than all of
you. poor fellow! I be charm with his goodness to his parent. why do
not you tell I of that, but suffer I to dismiss so good a creature
without a character? I see the reason of your whole behaviour now as
well as your complaint; you be jealous of the wench." "I jealous!"
say Slipslop; "I assure you, I look upon myself as his better; I be
not meat for a footman, I hope." these word throw the lady into a
violent passion, and she send Slipslop from her presence, who depart,
toss her nose, and cry, "Marry, come up! there be some people
more jealous than I, I believe." her lady affect not to hear the
word, though in reality she do, and understand they too. now ensue a
second conflict, so like the former, that it might savour of repetition
to relate it minutely. it may suffice to say that Lady Booby find good
reason to doubt whether she have so absolutely conquer her passion as
she have flatter herself; and, in order to accomplish it quite, take a
resolution, more common than wise, to retire immediately into the
country. the reader hath long ago see the arrival of Mrs Slipslop, whom
no pertness could make her mistress resolve to part with; lately, that
of Mr Pounce, her forerunner; and, lastly, that of the lady herself.
the morning after her arrival be Sunday, she go to church, to the
great surprize of everybody, who wonder to see her ladyship, be no
very constant church-woman, there so suddenly upon her journey. Joseph
be likewise there; and I have hear it be remark that she fix her
eye on he much more than on the parson; but this I believe to be only
a malicious rumour. when the prayer be end Mr Adams stand up, and
with a loud voice pronounce, "I publish the bann of marriage between
Joseph Andrews and Frances Goodwill, both of this parish," &c. whether
this have any effect on Lady Booby or no, who be then in her pew, which
the congregation could not see into, I could never discover: but
certain it be that in about a quarter of an hour she stand up, and
direct her eye to that part of the church where the woman sit, and
persist in look that way during the remainder of the sermon in so
scrutinize a manner, and with so angry a countenance, that most of
the woman be afraid she be offend at they. the moment she return
home she send for Slipslop into her chamber, and tell she she wonder
what that impudent fellow Joseph do in that parish? upon which
Slipslop give she an account of her meet Adams with he on the road,
and likewise the adventure with Fanny. at the relation of which the
lady often change her countenance; and when she have hear all, she
order Mr Adams into her presence, to whom she behave as the reader
will see in the next chapter.
CHAPTER II.
_a dialogue between Mr Abraham Adams and the Lady Booby._
Mr Adams be not far off, for he be drink her ladyship's health
below in a cup of her ale. he no soon come before she than she begin
in the follow manner: "I wonder, sir, after the many great
obligation you have have to this family" (with all which the reader hath
in the course of this history be minutely acquaint), "that you will
ungratefully show any respect to a fellow who hath be turn out of it
for his misdeed. nor doth it, I can tell you, sir, become a man of your
character, to run about the country with an idle fellow and wench.
indeed, as for the girl, I know no harm of she. Slipslop tell I she
be formerly breed up in my house, and behave as she ought, till she
hanker after this fellow, and he spoil she. Nay, she may still,
perhaps, do very well, if he will let she alone. you be, therefore,
do a monstrous thing in endeavour to procure a match between these
two people, which will be to the ruin of they both."--"Madam," say
Adams, "if your ladyship will but hear I speak, I protest I never hear
any harm of Mr Joseph Andrews; if I have, I should have correct he for
it; for I never have, nor will, encourage the fault of those under my
care. as for the young woman, I assure your ladyship I have as good an
opinion of she as your ladyship yourself or any other can have. she be
the sweetest-temper, honest, worthy young creature; indeed, as
to her beauty, I do not commend she on that account, though all man
allow she be the handsome woman, gentle or simple, that ever appear
in the parish."--"you be very impertinent," say she, "to talk such
fulsome stuff to I. it be mighty become truly in a clergyman to
trouble himself about handsome woman, and you be a delicate judge of
beauty, no doubt. a man who hath live all his life in such a parish as
this be a rare judge of beauty! ridiculous! beauty indeed! a country
wench a beauty! I shall be sick whenever I hear beauty mention again.
and so this wench be to stock the parish with beauty, I hope. but,
sir, our poor be numerous enough already; I will have no more vagabond
settle here."--"Madam," say Adams, "your ladyship be offend with I,
I protest, without any reason. this couple be desirous to consummate
long ago, and I dissuade they from it; nay, I may venture to say, I
believe I be the sole cause of their delay it."--"Well," say she,
"and you do very wisely and honestly too, notwithstanding she be the
great beauty in the parish."--"and now, madam," continue he, "I only
perform my office to Mr Joseph."--"Pray, donot mister such fellow to
I," cry the lady. "he," say the parson, "with the consent of Fanny,
before my face, put in the bann." "yes," answer the lady, "I suppose
the slut be forward enough; Slipslop tell I how her head run upon
fellow; that be one of her beauty, I suppose. but if they have put in
the bann, I desire you will publish they no more without my
order."--"madam," cry Adams, "if any one put in a sufficient
caution, and assign a proper reason against they, I be willing to
surcease."--"I tell you a reason," say she: "he be a vagabond, and he
shall not settle here, and bring a nest of beggar into the parish; it
will make we but little amend that they will be beauty."--"madam,"
answer Adams, "with the utmost submission to your ladyship, I have be
inform by lawyer Scout that any person who serve a year gain a
settlement in the parish where he serve."--"Lawyer Scout," reply the
lady, "be an impudent coxcomb; I will have no lawyer Scout interfere
with I. I repeat to you again, I will have no more incumbrance bring
on we: so I desire you will proceed no far."--"madam," return
Adams, "I would obey your ladyship in everything that be lawful; but
surely the party be poor be no reason against their marrying. God
forbid there should be any such law! the poor have little share enough
of this world already; it would be barbarous indeed to deny they the
common privilege and innocent enjoyment which nature indulge to the
animal creation."--"Since you understand yourself no well," cry the
lady, "nor the respect due from such as you to a woman of my
distinction, than to affront my ear by such loose discourse, I shall
mention but one short word; it be my order to you that you publish
these bann no more; and if you dare, I will recommend it to your
master, the doctor, to discard you from his service. I will, sir,
notwithstanding your poor family; and then you and the great beauty
in the parish may go and beg together."--"madam," answer Adams, "I
know not what your ladyship mean by the term master and service. I be
in the service of a Master who will never discard I for do my duty;
and if the doctor (for indeed I have never be able to pay for a
licence) think proper to turn I from my cure, God will provide I, I
hope, another. at least, my family, as well as myself, have hand; and
he will prosper, I doubt not, our endeavour to get our bread honestly
with they. whilst my conscience be pure, I shall never fear what man can
do unto I."--"I condemn my humility," say the lady, "for demean
myself to converse with you so long. I shall take other measure; for I
see you be a confederate with they. but the soon you leave I the
well; and I shall give order that my door may no long be open to
you. I will suffer no parson who run about the country with beauty to
be entertain here."--"Madam," say Adams, "I shall enter into no
person' door against their will; but I be assure, when you have
enquire far into this matter, you will applaud, not blame, my
proceeding; and so I humbly take my leave:" which he do with many bow,
or at least many attempt at a bow.
CHAPTER III.
_what pass between the lady and lawyer Scout._
in the afternoon the lady send for Mr Scout, whom she attack most
violently for intermeddle with her servant, which he deny, and
indeed with truth, for he have only assert accidentally, and perhaps
rightly, that a year's service gain a settlement; and so far he own
he might have formerly inform the parson and believe it be law. "I
be resolve," say the lady, "to have no discard servant of mine
settle here; and so, if this be your law, I shall send to another
lawyer." Scout say, "if she send to a hundred lawyer, not one or all
of they could alter the law. the utmost that be in the power of a
lawyer be to prevent the law's take effect; and that he himself could
do for her ladyship as well as any other; and I believe," say he,
"madam, your ladyship, not be conversant in these matter, hath
mistake a difference; for I assert only that a man who serve a year
be settle. now there be a material difference between be settle in
law and settle in fact; and as I affirm generally he be settle, and
law be preferable to fact, my settlement must be understand in law and
not in fact. and suppose, madam, we admit he be settle in law, what
use will they make of it? how doth that relate to fact? he be not
settle in fact; and if he be not settle in fact, he be not an
inhabitant; and if he be not an inhabitant, he be not of this parish;
and then undoubtedly he ought not to be publish here; for Mr Adams
hath tell I your ladyship's pleasure, and the reason, which be a very
good one, to prevent burden we with the poor; we have too many
already, and I think we ought to have an act to hang or transport half
of they. if we can prove in evidence that he be not settle in fact, it
be another matter. what I say to Mr Adams be on a supposition that he
be settle in fact; and indeed, if that be the case, I should
doubt."--"Don't tell I your fact and your if," say the lady; "I
donot understand your gibberish; you take too much upon you, and be
very impertinent, in pretend to direct in this parish; and you shall
be teach well, I assure you, you shall. but as to the wench, I be
resolve she shall not settle here; I will not suffer such beauty as
these to produce child for we to keep."--"beautie, indeed! your
ladyship be pleased to be merry," answer scout.--"mr Adams describe
she so to I," say the lady. "pray, what sort of dowdy be it, Mr
scout?"--"the ugly creature almost I ever beheld; a poor dirty drab,
your ladyship never see such a wretch."--"Well, but, dear Mr Scout, let
she be what she will, these ugly woman will bring child, you know; so
that we must prevent the marriage."--"true, madam," reply Scout, "for
the subsequent marriage co-operate with the law will carry law into
fact. when a man be marry he be settle in fact, and then he be not
removable. I will see Mr Adams, and I make no doubt of prevail with
he. his only objection be, doubtless, that he shall lose his fee; but
that be once make easy, as it shall be, I be confident no far
objection will remain. no, no, it be impossible; but your ladyship cannot
discommend his unwillingness to depart from his fee. every man ought to
have a proper value for his fee. as to the matter in question, if your
ladyship please to employ I in it, I will venture to promise you
success. the law of this land be not so vulgar to permit a mean fellow
to contend with one of your ladyship's fortune. we have one sure card,
which be, to carry he before Justice Frolick, who, upon hear your
ladyship's name, will commit he without any farther question. as for
the dirty slut, we shall have nothing to do with she; for, if we get rid
of the fellow, the ugly jade will--"--"take what measure you please,
good Mr Scout," answer the lady: "but I wish you could rid the parish
of both; for Slipslop tell I such story of this wench, that I abhor
the thought of she; and, though you say she be such an ugly slut, yet
you know, dear Mr Scout, these forward creature, who run after man,
will always find some as forward as themselves; so that, to prevent the
increase of beggar, we must get rid of she."--"your ladyship be very
much in the right," answer Scout; "but I be afraid the law be a little
deficient in give we any such power of prevention; however, the
justice will stretch it as far as he be able, to oblige your ladyship.
to say truth, it be a great blessing to the country that he be in the
commission, for he hath take several poor off our hand that the law
would never lay hold on. I know some justice who think as much of
commit a man to Bridewell as his lordship at 'size would of hang
he; but it would do a man good to see his worship, our justice, commit
a fellow to Bridewell, he take so much pleasure in it; and when once we
ha'um there, we seldom hear any more o'um. hebe either starve or eat up
by vermin in a month's time."--Here the arrival of a visitor put an end
to the conversation, and Mr Scout, have undertake the cause and
promise it success, depart.
this Scout be one of those fellow who, without any knowledge of the
law, or be breed to it, take upon they, in defiance of an act of
Parliament, to act as lawyer in the country, and be call so. they
be the pest of society, and a scandal to a profession, to which indeed
they do not belong, and which owe to such kind of rascallion the
ill-will which weak person bear towards it. with this fellow, to whom a
little before she would not have condescend to have speak, do a
certain passion for Joseph, and the jealousy and the disdain of poor
innocent Fanny, betray the Lady Booby into a familiar discourse, in
which she inadvertently confirm many hint with which Slipslop, whose
gallant he be, have pre-acquainted he; and whence he have take an
opportunity to assert those severe falsehood of little fanny which
possibly the reader might not have be well able to account for if we
have not think proper to give he this information.
CHAPTER IV.
_a short chapter, but very full of matter; particularly the arrival of
Mr Booby and his lady._
all that night, and the next day, the Lady Booby past with the utmost
anxiety; her mind be distract and her soul toss up and down by many
turbulent and opposite passion. she love, hat, pitied, scorn,
admire, despise the same person by fit, which change in a very short
interval. on Tuesday morning, which happen to be a holiday, she go
to church, where, to her surprize, Mr Adams publish the bann again
with as audible a voice as before. it be lucky for she that, as there
be no sermon, she have an immediate opportunity of return home to

Scout hath carry Joseph and Fanny both before the justice. all the
parish be in tear, and say they will certainly be hang; for nobody
know what it be for"--"i suppose they deserve it," say the lady.
"what! dost thou mention such wretch to me?"--"O dear madam," answer
Slipslop, "be it not a pity such a graceless young man should die a
virulent death? I hope the judge will take commensuration on his youth.
as for Fanny, I donot think it signify much what become of she; and
if poor Joseph hath do anything, I could venture to swear she traduce
he to it: few man ever come to a fragrant punishment, but by those
nasty creature, who be a scandal to our sect." the lady be no more
pleased at this news, after a moment's reflection, than Slipslop

she presently repair, have compose her countenance as well as she
could, and be a little satisfied that the wedding would by these
mean be at least interrupt, and that she should have an opportunity
to execute any resolution she might take, for which she see herself
provide with an excellent instrument in Scout.
the Lady Booby apprehend her servant have make a mistake when he
mention Mr Booby's lady; for she have never hear of his marriage: but
how great be her surprize when, at she enter the room, her nephew
present his wife to she; say, "Madam, this be that charming Pamela,

little on some business of consequence; and, as their discourse during
his absence would afford little improvement or entertainment to the
reader, we will leave they for a while to attend Mr Booby.
CHAPTER V.
_contain justice business; curious precedent of deposition, and

_the depusition of James Scout, layer, and Thomas Trotter,
yeoman, take before mee, one of his magesty's justasse of the
piece for Zumersetshire._
"these deponant saith, and first Thomas Trotter for himself
saith, that on the -- of this instant October, be
Sabbath-day, betwin the our of 2 and 4 in the afternoon, he
zeed Joseph Andrews and Francis Goodwill walk akross a certane
felde belunge to layer Scout, and out of the path which lede
thru the say felde, and there he zede Joseph Andrews with a
nife cut one hassel twig, of the value, as he believe, of
three half-pence, or thereabout; and he saith that the say
Francis Goodwill be likewise walk on the grass out of the
say path in the say felde, and do receive and karry in she
hand the said twig, and so be cumfarte, eade, and abatte
to the say Joseph therein. and the say James Scout for
himself say that he verily believe the say twig to be his
own proper twig," &c.
"Jesu!" say the squire, "would you commit two person to Bridewell for

CHAPTER VI.
_of which you be desire to read no more than you like._
the meeting between Joseph and Pamela be not without tear of joy on

one's echo. be he not more worthy of affection than a dirty country
clown, though bear of a family as old as the flood? or an idle worthless
rake, or little puisny beau of quality? and yet these we must condemn
ourselves to, in order to avoid the censure of the world; to shun the
contempt of other, we must ally ourselves to those we despise; we must
prefer birth, title, and fortune, to real merit. it be a tyranny of
custom, a tyranny we must comply with; for we people of fashion be the
slave of custom."--"Marry come up!" say Slipslop, who now know well
which party to take. "if I be a woman of your ladyship's fortune and
quality, I would be a slave to nobody."--"Me," say the lady; "I be
speak if a young woman of fashion, who have see nothing of the world,

who--but if I should reflect I should run mad. my future ease must
