CHAPTER VII.
_philosophical reflection, the like not to be find in any light
french romance. Mr Booby's grave advice to Joseph, and Fanny's
encounter with a beau._
Habit, my good reader, hath so vast a prevalence over the human mind,
that there be scarce anything too strange or too strong to be assert
of it. the story of the miser, who, from long accustom to cheat
other, come at last to cheat himself, and with great delight and
triumph pick his own pocket of a guinea to convey to his hoard, be not
impossible or improbable. in like manner it fare with the practiser of
deceit, who, from have long deceive their acquaintance, gain at last
a power of deceive themselves, and acquire that very opinion (however
false) of their own ability, excellency, and virtue, into which
they have for year perhaps endeavour to betray their neighbour. now,
reader, to apply this observation to my present purpose, thou must know,
that as the passion generally call love exercise most of the talent
of the female or fair world, so in this they now and then discover a
small inclination to deceit; for which thou wilt not be angry with the
beautiful creature when thou hast consider that at the age of seven,
or something early, miss be instruct by her mother that master be a
very monstrous kind of animal, who will, if she suffer he to come too
near she, infallibly eat she up and grind she to piece: that, so far
from kiss or toy with he of her own accord, she must not admit
he to kiss or toy with she: and, lastly, that she must never have any
affection towards he; for if she should, all her friend in petticoat
would esteem she a traitress, point at she, and hunt she out of their
society. these impression, be first receive, be far and deep
inculcate by their school-mistress and companion; so that by the age
of ten they have contract such a dread and abhorrence of the
above-name monster, that whenever they see he they fly from he as the
innocent hare doth from the greyhound. hence, to the age of fourteen or
fifteen, they entertain a mighty antipathy to master; they resolve, and
frequently profess, that they will never have any commerce with he, and
entertain fond hope of pass their life out of his reach, of the
possibility of which they have so visible an example in their good
maiden aunt. but when they arrive at this period, and have now pass
their second climacteric, when their wisdom, grow riper, begin to see
a little far, and, from almost daily fall in master's way, to
apprehend the great difficulty of keep out of it; and when they
observe he look often at they, and sometimes very eagerly and earnestly
too (for the monster seldom take any notice of they till at this age),
they then begin to think of their danger; and, as they perceive they
cannot easily avoid he, the wise part bethink themselves of provide
by other mean for their security. they endeavour, by all method they
can invent, to render themselves so amiable in his eye, that he may
have no inclination to hurt they; in which they generally succeed so
well, that his eye, by frequent languishing, soon lessen their idea of
his fierceness, and so far abate their fear, that they venture to
parley with he; and when they perceive he so different from what he
hath be describe, all gentleness, softness, kindness, tenderness,
fondness, their dreadful apprehension vanish in a moment; and now (it
be usual with the human mind to skip from one extreme to its
opposite, as easily, and almost as suddenly, as a bird from one bough to
another) love instantly succeed to fear: but, as it happen to person
who have in their infancy be thoroughly frightened with certain
no-person call ghost, that they retain their dread of those being
after they be convince that there be no such thing, so these young
lady, though they no long apprehend devouring, cannot so entirely
shake off all that hath be instill into they; they still entertain
the idea of that censure which be so strongly imprint on their tender
mind, to which the declaration of abhorrence they every day hear from
their companion greatly contribute. to avoid this censure, therefore,
be now their only care; for which purpose they still pretend the same
aversion to the monster: and the more they love he, the more ardently
they counterfeit the antipathy. by the continual and constant practice
of which deceit on other, they at length impose on themselves, and
really believe they hate what they love. thus, indeed, it happen to
Lady Booby, who love Joseph long before she know it; and now love he
much more than she suspect. she have indeed, from the time of his
sister's arrival in the quality of her niece, and from the instant she
view he in the dress and character of a gentleman, begin to conceive
secretly a design which love have conceal from herself till a dream
betray it to she.
she have no soon rise than she send for her nephew. when he come to
she, after many compliment on his choice, she tell he, "he might
perceive, in her condescension to admit her own servant to her table,
that she look on the family of Andrews as his relation, and indeed
her; that, as he have marry into such a family, it become he to
endeavour by all method to raise it as much as possible. at length she
advise he to use all his heart to dissuade Joseph from his intended
match, which would still enlarge their relation to meanness and poverty;
conclude that, by a commission in the army, or some other genteel
employment, he might soon put young Mr Andrews on the foot of a
gentleman; and, that be once do, his accomplishment might quickly
gain he an alliance which would not be to their discredit."
her nephew heartily embrace this proposal, and, find Mr Joseph with
his wife, at his return to her chamber, he immediately begin thus: "my
love to my dear Pamela, brother, will extend to all her relation; nor
shall I show they less respect than if I have marry into the family of
a duke. I hope I have give you some early testimony of this, and
shall continue to give you daily more. you will excuse I therefore,
brother, if my concern for your interest make I mention what may be,
perhaps, disagreeable to you to hear: but I must insist upon it, that,
if you have any value for my alliance or my friendship, you will decline
any thought of engage far with a girl who be, as you be a
relation of mine, so much beneath you. I know there may be at first some
difficulty in your compliance, but that will daily diminish; and you
will in the end sincerely thank I for my advice. I own, indeed, the
girl be handsome; but beauty alone be a poor ingredient, and will make
but an uncomfortable marriage."--"sir," say Joseph, "I assure you her
beauty be her least perfection; nor do I know a virtue which that young
creature be not possesst of."--"a to her virtue," answer Mr Booby,
"you can be yet but a slender judge of they; but, if she have never so
many, you will find she equal in these among her superior in birth and
fortune, which now you be to esteem on a footing with yourself; at
least I will take care they shall shortly be so, unless you prevent I
by degrade yourself with such a match, a match I have hardly patience
to think of, and which would break the heart of your parent, who now
rejoice in the expectation of see you make a figure in the
world."--"i know not," reply Joseph, "that my parent have any power
over my inclination; nor be I oblige to sacrifice my happiness to
their whim or ambition: besides, I shall be very sorry to see that the
unexpected advancement of my sister should so suddenly inspire they with
this wicked pride, and make they despise their equal. I be resolve on
no account to quit my dear Fanny; no, though I could raise she as high
above her present station as you have raise my sister."--"Your sister,
as well as myself," say Booby, "be greatly oblige to you for the
comparison: but, sir, she be not worthy to be compare in beauty to my
Pamela; nor hath she half her merit. and besides, sir, as you civilly
throw my marriage with your sister in my tooth, I must teach you the
wide difference between we: my fortune enable I to please myself; and
it would have be as overgrow a folly in I to have omit it as in
you to do it."--"my fortune enable I to please myself likewise," say
Joseph; "for all my pleasure be center in Fanny; and whilst I have
health I shall be able to support she with my labour in that station to
which she be bear, and with which she be content."--"brother," say
Pamela, "Mr Booby advise you as a friend; and no doubt my papa and
mamma will be of his opinion, and will have great reason to be angry
with you for destroy what his goodness hath do, and throw down
our family again, after he hath raise it. it would become you well,
brother, to pray for the assistance of grace against such a passion than
to indulge it."--"sure, sister, you be not in earnest; I be sure she be
your equal, at least."--"She be my equal," answer Pamela; "but I be
no long Pamela Andrews; I be now this gentleman's lady, and, as such,
be above her.--I hope I shall never behave with an unbecoming pride:
but, at the same time, I shall always endeavour to know myself, and
question not the assistance of grace to that purpose." they be now
summon to breakfast, and thus end their discourse for the present,
very little to the satisfaction of any of the party.
Fanny be now walk in an avenue at some distance from the house,
where Joseph have promise to take the first opportunity of come to
she. she have not a shilling in the world, and have subsist ever since
her return entirely on the charity of parson Adams. a young gentleman,
attend by many servant, come up to she, and ask she if that be not
the Lady Booby's house before he? this, indeed, he well know; but have
frame the question for no other reason than to make she look up, and
discover if her face be equal to the delicacy of her shape. he no
soon see it than he be strike with amazement. he stopt his horse, and
swear she be the most beautiful creature he ever behold. then,
instantly alight and deliver his horse to his servant, he rapt out
half-a-dozen oath that he would kiss she; to which she at first
submit, beg he would not be rude; but he be not satisfied with
the civility of a salute, nor even with the rude attack he could make
on her lip, but catch she in his arm, and endeavour to kiss her
breast, which with all her strength she resist, and, as our spark be
not of the Herculean race, with some difficulty prevent. the young
gentleman, be soon out of breath in the struggle, quit she, and,
remount his horse, call one of his servant to he, whom he order
to stay behind with she, and make she any offer whatever to prevail on
she to return home with he in the evening; and to assure she he would
take she into keeping. he then ride on with his other servant, and
arrive at the lady's house, to whom he be a distant relation, and be
come to pay a visit.
the trusty fellow, who be employ in an office he have be long
accustomed to, discharge his part with all the fidelity and dexterity
imaginable, but to no purpose. she be entirely deaf to his offer, and
reject they with the utmost disdain. at last the pimp, who have perhaps
more warm blood about he than his master, begin to sollicit for
himself; he tell she, though he be a servant, he be a man of some
fortune, which he would make her mistress of; and this without any
insult to her virtue, for that he would marry she. she answer, if his
master himself, or the great lord in the land, would marry she, she
would refuse he. at last, be weary with persuasion, and on fire
with charm which would have almost kindle a flame in the bosom of an
ancient philosopher or modern divine, he fasten his horse to the
ground, and attack she with much more force than the gentleman have
exert. poor Fanny would not have be able to resist his rudeness a
short time, but the deity who preside over chaste love send she Joseph
to her assistance. he no soon come within sight, and perceive she
struggle with a man, than, like a cannon-ball, or like lightning, or
anything that be swifter, if anything be, he run towards she, and,
come up just as the ravisher have tear her handkerchief from her
breast, before his lip have touch that seat of innocence and bliss, he
deal he so lusty a blow in that part of his neck which a rope would
have become with the utmost propriety, that the fellow stagger
backwards, and, perceive he have to do with something rough than the
little, tender, tremble hand of Fanny, he quit she, and, turn
about, see his rival, with fire flash from his eye, again ready to
assail he; and, indeed, before he could well defend himself, or return
the first blow, he receive a second, which, have it fall on that part
of the stomach to which it be direct, would have be probably the
last he would have have any occasion for; but the ravisher, lift up
his hand, drive the blow upwards to his mouth, whence it dislodge three
of his tooth; and now, not conceive any extraordinary affection for
the beauty of Joseph's person, nor be extremely pleased with this
method of salutation, he collect all his force, and aim a blow at
Joseph's breast, which he artfully parry with one fist, so that it
lose its force entirely in air; and, step one foot backward, he
dart his fist so fiercely at his enemy, that, have he not catch it in
his hand (for he be a boxer of no inferior fame), it must have tumble
he on the ground. and now the ravisher meditate another blow, which he
aim at that part of the breast where the heart be lodge; Joseph do
not catch it as before, yet so prevent its aim that it fall directly
on his nose, but with abate force. Joseph then, move both fist and
foot forwards at the same time, throw his head so dexterously into the
stomach of the ravisher that he fall a lifeless lump on the field, where
he lie many minute breathless and motionless.
when Fanny see her Joseph receive a blow in his face, and blood run
in a stream from he, she begin to tear her hair and invoke all human
and divine power to his assistance. she be not, however, long under
this affliction before Joseph, have conquer his enemy, run to she,
and assure she he be not hurt; she then instantly fall on her knee,
and thank God that he have make Joseph the mean of her rescue, and at
the same time preserve he from be injure in attempt it. she
offer, with her handkerchief, to wipe his blood from his face; but he,
see his rival attempt to recover his leg, turn to he, and
ask he if he have enough? to which the other answer he have; for he
believe he have fight with the devil instead of a man; and, loosen
his horse, say he should not have attempt the wench if he have know
she have be so well provide for.
Fanny now beg Joseph to return with she to parson Adams, and to
promise that he would leave she no more. these be proposition so
agreeable to Joseph, that, have he hear they, he would have give an
immediate assent; but indeed his eye be now his only sense; for you
may remember, reader, that the ravisher have tear her handkerchief from
Fanny's neck, by which he have discover such a sight, that Joseph hath
declare all the statue he ever beheld be so much inferior to it in
beauty, that it be more capable of convert a man into a statue than
of be imitate by the great master of that art. this modest
creature, whom no warmth in summer could ever induce to expose her
charm to the wanton sun, a modesty to which, perhaps, they owe their
inconceivable whiteness, have stand many minute bare-neck in the
presence of Joseph before her apprehension of his danger and the horror
of see his blood would suffer she once to reflect on what concern
herself; till at last, when the cause of her concern have vanish, an
admiration at his silence, together with observe the fix position
of his eye, produce an idea in the lovely maid which bring more
blood into her face than have flow from Joseph's nostril. the snowy
hue of her bosom be likewise change to vermilion at the instant when
she clap her handkerchief round her neck. Joseph see the uneasiness
she suffer, and immediately remove his eye from an object, in
surveying which he have feel the great delight which the organ of
sight be capable of conveying to his soul;--so great be his fear of
offend she, and so truly do his passion for her deserve the noble
name of love.
Fanny, be recover from her confusion, which be almost equal by
what Joseph have feel from observe it, again mention her request;
this be instantly and gladly comply with; and together they cross
two or three field, which bring they to the habitation of Mr Adams.
CHAPTER VIII.
_a discourse which happen between Mr Adams, Mrs Adams, Joseph, and
Fanny; with some behaviour of Mr Adams which will be call by some few
reader very low, absurd, and unnatural._
the parson and his wife have just end a long dispute when the lover
come to the door. indeed, this young couple have be the subject of the
dispute; for Mrs Adams be one of those prudent people who never do
anything to injure their family, or, perhaps, one of those good
mother who would even stretch their conscience to serve their child.
she have long entertain hope of see her eld daughter succeed Mrs
Slipslop, and of make her second son an exciseman by Lady Booby's
interest. these be expectation she could not endure the thought of
quitting, and be, therefore, very uneasy to see her husband so resolute
to oppose the lady's intention in Fanny's affair. she tell he, "it
behove every man to take the first care of his family; that he have a
wife and six child, the maintaining and provide for whom would be
business enough for he without intermeddle in other folk' affair;
that he have always preach up submission to superior, and would do ill
to give an example of the contrary behaviour in his own conduct; that if
Lady Booby do wrong she must answer for it herself, and the sin would
not lie at their door; that Fanny have be a servant, and breed up in the
lady's own family, and consequently she must have know more of she than
they do, and it be very improbable, if she have behave herself well,
that the lady would have be so bitterly her enemy; that perhaps he be
too much inclined to think well of she because she be handsome, but
handsome woman be often no well than they should be; that g-- make
ugly woman as well as handsome one; and that if a woman have virtue it
signify nothing whether she have beauty or no." for all which reason
she conclude he should oblige the lady, and stop the future publication
of the bann. but all these excellent argument have no effect on the
parson, who persist in do his duty without regard the
consequence it might have on his worldly interest. he endeavour to
answer she as well as he could; to which she have just finish her reply
(for she have always the last word everywhere but at church) when Joseph
and Fanny enter their kitchen, where the parson and his wife then sit
at breakfast over some bacon and cabbage. there be a coldness in the
civility of Mrs Adams which person of accurate speculation might have
observe, but escape her present guest; indeed, it be a good deal
cover by the heartiness of Adams, who no soon hear that Fanny have
neither eat nor drank that morning than he present she a bone of bacon
he have just be gnaw, be the only remain of his provision, and
then run nimbly to the tap, and produce a mug of small beer, which he
call ale; however, it be the good in his house. Joseph, address
himself to the parson, tell he the discourse which have past between
Squire Booby, his sister, and himself concern Fanny; he then
acquaint he with the danger whence he have rescue she, and
communicate some apprehension on her account. he conclude that he
should never have an easy moment till Fanny be absolutely his, and
beg that he might be suffer to fetch a licence, say he could
easily borrow the money. the parson answer, that he have already give
his sentiment concern a licence, and that a very few day would make
it unnecessary. "Joseph," say he, "I wish this haste doth not arise
rather from your impatience than your fear; but, as it certainly spring
from one of these cause, I will examine both. of each of these
therefore in their turn; and first for the first of these, namely,
impatience. now, child, I must inform you that, if in your purposed
marriage with this young woman you have no intention but the indulgence
of carnal appetite, you be guilty of a very heinous sin. marriage be
ordain for nobler purpose, as you will learn when you hear the
service provide on that occasion read to you. Nay, perhaps, if you be
a good lad, I, child, shall give you a sermon gratis, wherein I shall
demonstrate how little regard ought to be have to the flesh on such
occasion. the text will be Matthew the 5th, and part of the 28th
verse--_whosoever looketh on a woman, so as to lust after she_. the
latter part I shall omit, as foreign to my purpose. indeed, all such
brutal lust and affection be to be greatly subdue, if not totally
eradicate, before the vessel can be say to be consecrate to honour.
to marry with a view of gratify those inclination be a prostitution
of that holy ceremony, and must entail a curse on all who so lightly
undertake it. if, therefore, this haste arise from impatience, you be
to correct, and not give way to it. now, as to the second head which I
propose to speak to, namely, fear: it argue a diffidence, highly
criminal, of that Power in which alone we should put our trust, see
we may be well assure that he be able, not only to defeat the design
of our enemy, but even to turn their heart. instead of take,
therefore, any unjustifiable or desperate mean to rid ourselves of
fear, we should resort to prayer only on these occasion; and we may be
then certain of obtain what be good for we. when any accident
threaten we we be not to despair, nor, when it overtake we, to
grieve; we must submit in all thing to the will of Providence, and set
our affection so much on nothing here that we cannot quit it without
reluctance. you be a young man, and can know but little of this world;
I be old, and have see a great deal. all passion be criminal in
their excess; and even love itself, if it be not subservient to our
duty, may render we blind to it. have Abraham so love his son Isaac as
to refuse the sacrifice require, be there any of we who would not
condemn he? Joseph, I know your many good quality, and value you for
they; but, as I be to render an account of your soul, which be commit
to my cure, I cannot see any fault without remind you of it. you be
too much inclined to passion, child, and have set your affection so
absolutely on this young woman, that, if g-- require she at your hand,
I fear you would reluctantly part with she. now, believe I, no
Christian ought so to set his heart on any person or thing in this
world, but that, whenever it shall be require or take from he in any
manner by Divine Providence, he may be able, peaceably, quietly, and
contentedly to resign it." at which word one come hastily in, and
acquaint Mr Adams that his young son be drown. he stand silent a
moment, and soon begin to stamp about the room and deplore his loss with
the bitter agony. Joseph, who be overwhelmed with concern likewise,
recover himself sufficiently to endeavour to comfort the parson; in
which attempt he use many argument that he have at several time
remember out of his own discourse, both in private and public (for he
be a great enemy to the passion, and preach nothing more than the
conquest of they by reason and grace), but he be not at leisure now to
hearken to his advice. "child, child," say he, "do not go about
impossibility. have it be any other of my child I could have bear
it with patience; but my little prattler, the darling and comfort of my
old age--the little wretch, to be snatch out of life just at his
entrance into it; the sweet, well-temper boy, who never do a thing
to offend I. it be but this morning I give he his first lesson in
_Que Genus_. this be the very book he learn; poor child! it be of no
further use to thee now. he would have make the good scholar, and have
be an ornament to the church;--such part and such goodness never meet
in one so young." "and the handsome lad too," say Mrs Adams,
recover from a swoon in Fanny's arm. "my poor Jacky, shall I never
see thee more?" cry the parson. "yes, surely," say Joseph, "and in a
well place; you will meet again, never to part more." I believe the
parson do not hear these word, for he pay little regard to they, but
go on lamenting, whilst the tear trickle down into his bosom. at
last he cry out, "where be my little darling?" and be sallye out,
when to his great surprize and joy, in which I hope the reader will
sympathize, he meet his son in a wet condition indeed, but alive and
run towards he. the person who bring the news of his misfortune
have be a little too eager, as people sometimes be, from, I believe,
no very good principle, to relate ill news; and, see he fall into
the river, instead of run to his assistance, directly run to
acquaint his father of a fate which he have conclude to be inevitable,
but whence the child be relieve by the same poor pedlar who have
relieve his father before from a less distress. the parson's joy be
now as extravagant as his grief have be before; he kiss and embrace
his son a thousand time, and dance about the room like one frantic;
but as soon as he discover the face of his old friend the pedlar, and
hear the fresh obligation he have to he, what be his sensation? not
those which two courtier feel in one another's embrace; not those with
which a great man receive the vile treacherous engine of his wicked
purpose, not those with which a worthless young brother wish his
eld joy of a son, or a man congratulate his rival on his obtaining a
mistress, a place, or an honour.--No, reader; he feel the ebullition,
the overflowing of a full, honest, open heart, towards the person who
have confer a real obligation, and of which, if thou canst not
conceive an idea within, I will not vainly endeavour to assist thee.
when these tumult be over, the parson, take Joseph aside, proceed
thus--"No, Joseph, do not give too much way to thy passion, if thou
dost expect happiness." the patience of Joseph, nor perhaps of Job,
could bear no long; he interrupt the parson, say, "it be easy
to give advice than take it; nor do he perceive he could so entirely
conquer himself, when he apprehend he have lose his son, or when he
find he recover."--"boy," reply Adams, raise his voice, "it doth
not become green head to advise grey hairs.--Thou art ignorant of the
tenderness of fatherly affection; when thou art a father thou wilt be
capable then only of know what a father can feel. no man be oblige
to impossibility; and the loss of a child be one of those great trial
where our grief may be allow to become immoderate."--"Well, sir,"
cry Joseph, "and if I love a mistress as well as you your child,
surely her loss would grieve I equally."--"Yes, but such love be
foolishness and wrong in itself, and ought to be conquer," answer
Adams; "it savour too much of the flesh."--"Sure, sir," say Joseph,
"it be not sinful to love my wife, no, not even to doat on she to
distraction!"--"indeed but it be," say Adams. "every man ought to love
his wife, no doubt; we be command so to do; but we ought to love she
with moderation and discretion."--"I be afraid I shall be guilty of some
sin in spite of all my endeavour," say Joseph; "for I shall love
without any moderation, I be sure."--"you talk foolishly and
childishly," cry adams.--"indee," say Mrs Adams, who have listen to
the latter part of their conversation, "you talk more foolishly yourself.
I hope, my dear, you will never preach any such doctrine as that
husband can love their wife too well. if I know you have such a sermon
in the house I be sure I would burn it, and I declare, if I have not be
convince you have love I as well as you could, I can answer for
myself, I should have hat and despise you. Marry come up! fine
doctrine, indeed! a wife hath a right to insist on her husband's love
she as much as ever he can; and he be a sinful villain who doth not.
Doth he not promise to love she, and to comfort she, and to cherish she,
and all that? I be sure I remember it all as well as if I have repeat
it over but yesterday, and shall never forget it. besides, I be certain
you do not preach as you practise; for you have be a love and a
cherish husband to I; thatbe the truth on't; and why you should
endeavour to put such wicked nonsense into this young man's head I
cannot devise. donot hearken to he, Mr Joseph; be as good a husband as
you be able, and love your wife with all your body and soul too." here
a violent rap at the door put an end to their discourse, and produce a
scene which the reader will find in the next chapter.
CHAPTER IX.
_a visit which the polite Lady Booby and her polite friend pay to
the parson._
the Lady Booby have no soon have an account from the gentleman of his
meeting a wonderful beauty near her house, and perceive the rapture
with which he speak of she, than, immediately conclude it must be
Fanny, she begin to meditate a design of bring they well
acquaint; and to entertain hope that the fine clothe, present, and
promise of this youth, would prevail on she to abandon Joseph: she
therefore propose to her company a walk in the field before dinner,
when she lead they towards Mr Adams's house; and, as she approach it,
tell they if they please she would divert they with one of the most
ridiculous sight they have ever see, which be an old foolish parson,
who, she say, laugh, keep a wife and six brat on a salary of about
twenty pound a year; add, that there be not such another ragged
family in the parish. they all readily agree to this visit, and arrive
whilst Mrs Adams be declaim as in the last chapter. Beau Didapper,
which be the name of the young gentleman we have see rid towards
Lady Booby's, with his cane mimic the rap of a London footman at the
door. the people within, namely, Adams, his wife and three child,
Joseph, Fanny, and the pedlar, be all throw into confusion by this
knock, but Adams go directly to the door, which be open, the Lady
Booby and her company walk in, and be receive by the parson with
about two hundred bow, and by his wife with as many curtsy; the
latter tell the lady "she be ashamed to be see in such a pickle,
and that her house be in such a litter; but that if she have expect
such an honour from her ladyship she should have find she in a well
manner." the parson make no apology, though he be in his half-cassock
and a flannel nightcap. he say "they be heartily welcome to his poor
cottage," and turn to Mr Didapper, cry out, "_Non mea renidet in
domo lacunar_." the beau answer, "he do not understand Welsh;" at
which the parson star and make no reply.
Mr Didapper, or beau Didapper, be a young gentleman of about four foot
five inch in height. he wear his own hair, though the scarcity of it
might have give he sufficient excuse for a periwig. his face be thin
and pale; the shape of his body and leg none of the good, for he have
very narrow shoulder and no calf; and his gait might more properly be
call hop than walk. the qualification of his mind be well
adapt to his person. we shall handle they first negatively. he be not
entirely ignorant; for he could talk a little french and sing two or
three italian song; he have live too much in the world to be bashful,
and too much at court to be proud: he seem not much inclined to
avarice, for he be profuse in his expense; nor have he all the feature
of prodigality, for he never give a shilling: no hater of woman, for he
always dangle after they; yet so little subject to lust, that he have,
among those who know he well, the character of great moderation in his
pleasure; no drinker of wine; nor so addict to passion but that a hot
word or two from an adversary make he immediately cool.
now, to give he only a dash or two on the affirmative side: though he
be bear to an immense fortune, he choose, for the pitiful and dirty
consideration of a place of little consequence, to depend entirely on
the will of a fellow whom they call a great man; who treat he with
the utmost disrespect, and exact of he a plenary obedience to his
command, which he implicitly submit to, at the expense of his
conscience, his honour, and of his country, in which he have himself so
very large a share. and to finish his character; as he be entirely well
satisfied with his own person and part, so he be very apt to ridicule
and laugh at any imperfection in another. such be the little person, or
rather thing, that hop after Lady Booby into Mr Adams's kitchen.
the parson and his company retreat from the chimney-side, where they
have be seat, to give room to the lady and her. instead of return
any of the curtsy or extraordinary civility of Mrs Adams, the lady,
turn to Mr Booby, cry out, "_Quelle Bete! Quel Animal!_" and
presently after discover Fanny (for she do not need the circumstance
of her stand by Joseph to assure the identity of her person), she
ask the beau "whether he do not think she a pretty girl?"--"begad,
madam," answer he, "'tis the very same I meet." "I do not imagine,"
reply the lady, "you have so good a taste."--"because I never like
you, I warrant," cry the beau. "ridiculous!" say she: "you know you
be always my aversion." "I would never mention aversion," answer the
beau, "with that face[A]; dear Lady Booby, wash your face before you
mention aversion, I beseech you." he then laugh, and turn about to
coquet it with Fanny.
[a] lest this should appear unnatural to some reader, we think proper
to acquaint they, that it be take verbatim from very polite
conversation.
Mrs Adams have be all this time beg and pray the lady to sit
down, a favour which she at last obtain. the little boy to whom the
accident have happen, still keep his place by the fire, be chid by
his mother for not be more mannerly: but Lady Booby take his part,
and, commend his beauty, tell the parson he be his very picture. she
then, see a book in his hand, ask "if he could read?"--"yes," cry
Adams, "a little Latin, madam: he be just get into Quae Genus."--"a fig
for quere genius!" answer she; "let I hear he read a little
English."--"Lege, Dick, lege," say Adams: but the boy make no answer,
till he see the parson knit his brow, and then cry, "I donot
understand you, father."--"How, boy!" say Adams; "what doth lego make
in the imperative mood? Legito, doth it not?"--"ye," answer
Dick.--"And what besides ?" say the father. "lege," quoth the son,
after some hesitation. "a good boy," say the father: "and now, child,
what be the English of lego?"--to which the boy, after long puzzle,
answer, he could not tell. "how!" cry Adams, in a passion;--"what,
hath the water wash away your learning? why, what be Latin for the
English verb read? consider before you speak." the child consider some
time, and then the parson cry twice or thrice, "Le--, Le--." Dick
answer, "Lego."--"very well;--and then what be the English," say the
parson, "of the verb lego?"--"To read," cry Dick.--"Very well," say
the parson; "a good boy: you can do well if you will take pains.--i
assure your ladyship he be not much above eight year old, and be out of
his Propria quae Maribus already.--Come, Dick, read to she
ladyship;"--which she again desire, in order to give the beau time and
opportunity with Fanny, Dick begin as in the following chapter.
CHAPTER X.
_the history of two friend, which may afford an useful lesson to all
those person who happen to take up their residence in married
family._
"Leonard and Paul be two friend."--"pronounce it Lennard, child,"
cry the parson.--"pray, Mr Adams," say Lady Booby, "let your son read
without interruption." Dick then proceed. "Lennard and Paul be two
friend, who, have be educate together at the same school,
commence a friendship which they preserve a long time for each other.
it be so deeply fix in both their mind, that a long absence, during
which they have maintain no correspondence, do not eradicate nor
lessen it: but it revive in all its force at their first meeting, which
be not till after fifteen year' absence, most of which time Lennard
have spend in the East Indi-es."--"pronounce it short, Indies," say
Adams.--"Pray? sir, be quiet," say the lady.--The boy repeated--"in the
East Indies, whilst Paul have serve his king and country in the army. in
which different service they have find such different success, that
Lennard be now marry, and retire with a fortune of thirty thousand
pound; and Paul be arrive to the degree of a lieutenant of foot; and
be not worth a single shilling.
"the regiment in which Paul be station happen to be order into
quarter within a small distance from the estate which Lennard have
purchase, and where he be settle. this latter, who be now become a
country gentleman, and a justice of peace, come to attend the quarter
session in the town where his old friend be quarter, soon after his
arrival. some affair in which a soldier be concern occasion Paul to
attend the justice. Manhood, and time, and the change of climate have so
much altered Lennard, that Paul do not immediately recollect the
feature of his old acquaintance: but it be otherwise with Lennard. he
know Paul the moment he see he; nor could he contain himself from
quit the bench, and run hastily to embrace he. Paul stand at
first a little surprized; but have soon sufficient information from his
friend, whom he no soon remember than he return his embrace with a
passion which make many of the spectator laugh, and give to some few a
much high and more agreeable sensation.
"not to detain the reader with minute circumstance, Lennard insist on
his friend's return with he to his house that evening; which request
be comply with, and leave for a month's absence for Paul obtain of
the command officer.
"if it be possible for any circumstance to give any addition to the
happiness which Paul propose in this visit, he receive that additional
pleasure by find, on his arrival at his friend's house, that his lady
be an old acquaintance which he have formerly contract at his
quarter, and who have always appear to be of a most agreeable temper;
a character she have ever maintain among her intimate, be of that
number, every individual of which be call quite the good sort of woman
in the world.
"but, good as this lady be, she be still a woman; that be to say, an
angel, and not an angel."--"you must mistake, child," cry the parson,
"for you read nonsense."--"It be so in the book," answer the son. Mr
Adams be then silence by authority, and Dick proceeded--"For though
her person be of that kind to which man attribute the name of angel,
yet in her mind she be perfectly woman. of which a great degree of
obstinacy give the most remarkable and perhaps most pernicious instance.
"a day or two pass after Paul's arrival before any instance of this
appear; but it be impossible to conceal it long. both she and her
husband soon lose all apprehension from their friend's presence, and
fall to their dispute with as much vigour as ever. these be still
pursue with the utmost ardour and eagerness, however trifle the
cause be whence they first arise. Nay, however incredible it may
seem, the little consequence of the matter in debate be frequently
give as a reason for the fierceness of the contention, as thus: 'if you
love I, sure you would never dispute with I such a trifle as this.'
the answer to which be very obvious; for the argument would hold equally
on both side, and be constantly retort with some addition, as--'I be
sure I have much more reason to say so, who be in the right.' during all
these dispute, Paul always keep strict silence, and preserve an even
countenance, without show the least visible inclination to either
party. one day, however, when madam have leave the room in a violent fury,
Lennard could not refrain from refer his cause to his friend. be
ever anything so unreasonable, say he, as this woman? what shall I do
with she? I doat on she to distraction; nor have I any cause to complain
of, more than this obstinacy in her temper; whatever she assert, she
will maintain against all the reason and conviction in the world. pray
give I your advice.--first, say Paul, I will give my opinion, which
be, flatly, that you be in the wrong; for, suppose she be in the
wrong, be the subject of your contention any way material? what
signify it whether you be marry in a red or a yellow waistcoat? for
that be your dispute. now, suppose she be mistaken; as you love she
you say so tenderly, and I believe she deserve it, would it not have
be wise to have yield, though you certainly know yourself in the
right, than to give either she or yourself any uneasiness. for my own
part, if ever I marry, I be resolve to enter into an agreement with my
wife, that in all dispute (especially about trifle) that party who be
most convinced they be right shall always surrender the victory; by
which mean we shall both be forward to give up the cause. I own, say
Lennard, my dear friend, shake he by the hand, there be great truth
and reason in what you say; and I will for the future endeavour to
follow your advice. they soon after break up the conversation, and
Lennard, go to his wife, ask her pardon, and tell she his friend
have convince he he have be in the wrong. she immediately begin a vast
encomium on Paul, in which he second she, and both agree he be the
worthy and wise man upon earth. when next they meet, which be at
supper, though she have promise not to mention what her husband tell
she, she could not forbear cast the kind and most affectionate
look on Paul, and ask he, with the sweetest voice, whether she
should help he to some potted woodcock? potted partridge, my dear, you
mean, say the husband. my dear, say she, I ask your friend if he will
eat any potted woodcock; and I be sure I must know, who pot it. I
think I should know too, who shoot they, reply the husband, and I be
convince that I have not see a woodcock this year; however, though I
know I be in the right, I submit, and the potted partridge be pot
woodcock if you desire to have it so. it be equal to I, say she,
whether it be one or the other; but you would persuade one out of one's
sens; to be sure, you be always in the right in your own opinion; but
your friend, I believe, know which he be eat. Paul answer nothing,
and the dispute continue, as usual, the great part of the evening.
the next morning the lady, accidentally meet Paul, and be
convince he be her friend, and of her side, accost he thus:--i be
certain, sir, you have long since wonder at the unreasonableness of my
husband. he be indeed, in other respect, a good sort of man, but so
positive, that no woman but one of my comply temper could possibly
live with he. why, last night, now, be ever any creature so
unreasonable? I be certain you must condemn he. pray, answer I, be he
not in the wrong? Paul, after a short silence, speak as follow: I be
sorry, madam, that, as good manner oblige I to answer against my
will, so an adherence to truth force I to declare myself of a
different opinion. to be plain and honest, you be entirely in the
wrong; the cause I own not worth disputing, but the bird be undoubtedly
a partridge. o sir! reply the lady, I cannot possibly help your taste.
Madam, return Paul, that be very little material; for, have it be
otherwise, a husband might have expect submission.--Indeed! sir, say
she, I assure you!--Yes, madam, cry he, he might, from a person of
your excellent understanding; and pardon I for say, such a
condescension would have show a superiority of sense even to your
husband himself.--But, dear sir, say she, why should I submit when I be
in the right?--for that very reason, answer he; it would be the
great instance of affection imaginable; for can anything be a great
object of our compassion than a person we love in the wrong? ay, but I
should endeavour, say she, to set he right. pardon I, madam, answer
Paul: I will apply to your own experience if you ever find your
argument have that effect. the more our judgment err, the less we be
willing to own it: for my own part, I have always observe the person
who maintain the bad side in any contest be the warm. why, say
she, I must confess there be truth in what you say, and I will endeavour
to practise it. the husband then come in, Paul depart. and Leonard,
approach his wife with an air of good humour, tell she he be sorry
for their foolish dispute the last night; but he be now convinced of
his error. she answer, smile, she believe she owe his
condescension to his complacence; that she be ashamed to think a word
have pass on so silly an occasion, especially as she be satisfy she
have be mistake. a little contention follow, but with the utmost
good-will to each other, and be conclude by she assert that Paul
have thoroughly convince she she have be in the wrong. upon which they
both unite in the praise of their common friend.
"Paul now pass his time with great satisfaction, these dispute be
much less frequent, as well as short than usual; but the devil, or
some unlucky accident in which perhaps the devil have no hand, shortly
put an end to his happiness. he be now eternally the private referee of
every difference; in which, after have perfectly, as he think,
establish the doctrine of submission, he never scruple to assure both
privately that they be in the right in every argument, as before he
have follow the contrary method. one day a violent litigation happen
in his absence, and both party agree to refer it to his decision. the
husband profess himself sure the decision would be in his favour; the
wife answer, he might be mistake; for she believe his friend be
convince how seldom she be to blame; and that if he know all--the
husband reply, my dear, I have no desire of any retrospect; but I
believe, if you know all too, you would not imagine my friend so
entirely on your side. Nay, say she, since you provoke I, I will
mention one instance. you may remember our dispute about send Jackey
to school in cold weather, which point I give up to you from mere
compassion, know myself to be in the right; and Paul himself tell I
afterwards he think I so. my dear, reply the husband, I will not
scruple your veracity; but I assure you solemnly, on my applying to he,
he give it absolutely on my side, and say he would have act in the
same manner. they then proceed to produce numberless other instance,
in all which Paul have, on vow of secresy, give his opinion on both
side. in the conclusion, both believe each other, they fall severely
on the treachery of Paul, and agree that he have be the occasion of
almost every dispute which have fall out between they. they then become
extremely loving, and so full of condescension on both side, that they
vye with each other in censure their own conduct, and jointly vent
their indignation on Paul, whom the wife, fear a bloody consequence,
earnestly entreat her husband to suffer quietly to depart the next
day, which be the time fix for his return to quarter, and then drop
his acquaintance.
"however ungenerous this behaviour in Lennard may be esteem, his wife
obtain a promise from he (though with difficulty) to follow her
advice; but they both express such unusual coldness that day to Paul,
that he, who be quick of apprehension, take Lennard aside, press
he so home, that he at last discover the secret. Paul acknowledge
the truth, but tell he the design with which he have do it.--to which
the other answer, he would have act more friendly to have let he
into the whole design; for that he might have assure himself of his
secresy. Paul reply, with some indignation, he have give he a
sufficient proof how capable he be of conceal a secret from his
wife. Lennard return with some warmth--he have more reason to upbraid
he, for that he have cause most of the quarrel between they by his
strange conduct, and might (if they have not discover the affair to
each other) have be the occasion of their separation. Paul then
said"--but something now happen which put a stop to Dick's reading,
and of which we shall treat in the next chapter.
CHAPTER XI.
_in which the history be continue._
Joseph Andrews have bear with great uneasiness the impertinence of beau
Didapper to Fanny, who have be talk pretty freely to she, and
offer her settlement; but the respect to the company have restrain
he from interfere whilst the beau confine himself to the use of his
tongue only; but the say beau, watch an opportunity whilst the
lady' eye be dispose another way, offer a rudeness to she with
his hand; which Joseph no soon perceive than he present he with
so sound a box on the ear, that it convey he several pace from where
he stand. the lady immediately scream out, rise from their chair;
and the beau, as soon as he recover himself, draw his hanger: which
Adams observe, snatch up the lid of a pot in his left hand, and,
cover himself with it as with a shield, without any weapon of offence
in his other hand, stept in before Joseph, and expose himself to the
enrage beau, who threaten such perdition and destruction, that it
fright the woman, who be all get in a huddle together, out of their
wit, even to hear his denunciation of vengeance. Joseph be of a
different complexion, and beg Adams to let his rival come on; for he
have a good cudgel in his hand, and do not fear he. Fanny now faint
into Mrs Adams's arm, and the whole room be in confusion, when Mr
Booby, pass by Adams, who lie snug under the pot-lid, come up to
Didapper, and insist on his sheathe the hanger, promise he should
have satisfaction; which Joseph declare he would give he, and fight
he at any weapon whatever. the beau now sheathe his hanger, and take
out a pocket-glass, and vow vengeance all the time, re-adjusted his
hair; the parson deposit his shield; and Joseph, run to Fanny,
soon bring she back to life. Lady Booby chid Joseph for his insult on
Didapper; but he answer, he would have attack an army in the same
cause. "what cause?" say the lady. "Madam," answer Joseph, "he be
rude to that young woman."--"what," say the lady, "I suppose he would
have kiss the wench; and be a gentleman to be strike for such an
offer? I must tell you, Joseph, these air do not become you."--"madam,"
say Mr Booby, "I see the whole affair, and I do not commend my brother;
for I cannot perceive why he should take upon he to be this girl's
champion."--"i can commend he," say Adams: "he be a brave lad; and it
become any man to be the champion of the innocent; and he must be the
base coward who would not vindicate a woman with whom he be on the
brink of marriage."--"sir," say Mr Booby, "my brother be not a proper
match for such a young woman as this."--"No," say Lady Booby; "nor do
you, Mr Adams, act in your proper character by encourage any such
doing; and I be very much surprize you should concern yourself in it.
I think your wife and family your properer care."--"Indeed, madam, your
ladyship say very true," answer Mrs Adams: "he talk a pack of
nonsense, that the whole parish be his child. I be sure I donot
understand what he mean by it; it would make some woman suspect he have
go astray, but I acquit he of that; I can read Scripture as well as
he, and I never find that the parson be oblige to provide for other
folk' child; and besides, he be but a poor curate, and hath little
enough, as your ladyship know, for I and mine."--"you say very well,
Mrs Adams," quoth the Lady Booby, who have not speak a word to she before;
"you seem to be a very sensible woman; and I assure you, your husband be
act a very foolish part, and oppose his own interest, see my
nephew be violently set against this match: and indeed I cannot blame
he; it be by no mean one suitable to our family." in this manner the
lady proceed with Mrs Adams, whilst the beau hop about the room,
shake his head, partly from pain and partly from anger; and Pamela be
chide Fanny for her assurance in aim at such a match as her
brother. poor Fanny answer only with her tear, which have long since
begin to wet her handkerchief; which Joseph perceiving, take she by the
arm, and wrap it in his carry she off, swear he would own no
relation to any one who be an enemy to she he love more than all the
world. he go out with Fanny under his left arm, brandish a cudgel
in his right, and neither Mr Booby nor the beau think proper to oppose
he. Lady Booby and her company make a very short stay behind he; for
the lady's bell now summon they to dress; for which they have just time
before dinner.
Adams seem now very much deject, which his wife perceive, begin to
apply some matrimonial balsam. she tell he he have reason to be
concern, for that he have probably ruin his family with his trick
almost; but perhaps he be grieve for the loss of his two child,
Joseph and Fanny. his eld daughter go on: "indeed, father, it be
very hard to bring stranger here to eat your child's bread out of
their mouth. you have keep they ever since they come home; and, for
anything I see to the contrary, may keep they a month long; be you
oblige to give her meat, tho'f she be never so handsome? but I donot
see she be so much handsomer than other people. if people be to be
keep for their beauty, she would scarce fare well than her neighbour,
I believe. as for Mr Joseph, I have nothing to say; he be a young man of
honest principle, and will pay some time or other for what he hath; but
for the girl--why doth she not return to her place she run away from? I
would not give such a vagabond slut a halfpenny though I have a million
of money; no, though she be starve." "indeed but I would," cry
little Dick; "and, father, rather than poor Fanny shall be starve, I
will give she all this bread and cheese"--(offere what he hold in his
hand). Adams smile on the boy, and tell he he rejoice to see he be a
Christian; and that if he have a halfpenny in his pocket, he would have
give it he; tell he it be his duty to look upon all his
neighbour as his brother and sister, and love they accordingly. "yes,
papa," say he, "I love she well than my sister, for she be handsomer
than any of they." "be she so, saucebox?" say the sister, give he a
box on the ear; which the father would probably have resent, have not
Joseph, Fanny, and the pedlar at that instant return together. Adams
bid his wife prepare some food for their dinner; she say, "truly she
could not, she have something else to do." Adams rebuke she for
dispute his command, and quote many text of scripture to prove
"that the husband be the head of the wife, and she be to submit and
obey." the wife answer, "it be blasphemy to talk scripture out of
church; that such thing be very proper to be say in the pulpit, but
that it be profane to talk they in common discourse." Joseph tell Mr
Adams "he be not come with any design to give he or Mrs Adams any
trouble; but to desire the favour of all their company to the George (an
ale-house in the parish), where he have bespeak a piece of bacon and
green for their dinner." Mrs Adams, who be a very good sort of woman,
only rather too strict in oeconomie, readily accept this invitation,
as do the parson himself by her example; and away they all walk
together, not omit little Dick, to whom Joseph give a shilling when
he hear of his intend liberality to Fanny.
CHAPTER XII.
_where the good-natured reader will see something which will give he no
great pleasure._
the pedlar have be very inquisitive from the time he have first hear
that the great house in this parish belong to the Lady Booby, and have
learn that she be the widow of Sir Thomas, and that Sir Thomas have
buy Fanny, at about the age of three or four year, of a travel
woman; and, now their homely but hearty meal be end, he tell Fanny
he believe he could acquaint she with her parent. the whole company,
especially she herself, start at this offer of the pedlar's. he then
proceed thus, while they all lend their strictest attention:--"though
I be now content with this humble way of get my livelihood, I be
formerly a gentleman; for so all those of my profession be call. in
a word, I be a drummer in an irish regiment of foot. whilst I be in
this honourable station I attend an officer of our regiment into
England a-recruiting. in our march from Bristol to Froome (for since
the decay of the woollen trade the clothing town have furnish the
army with a great number of recruit) we overtake on the road a woman,
who seem to be about thirty year old or thereabout, not very
handsome, but well enough for a soldier. as we come up to she, she
mend her pace, and fall into discourse with our lady (for every
man of the party, namely, a serjeant, two private man, and a drum, be
provide with their woman except myself), she continue to travel on
with we. I, perceive she must fall to my lot, advance presently to
she, make love to she in our military way, and quickly succeed to my
wish. we strike a bargain within a mile, and live together as man
and wife to her die day." "I suppose," say Adams, interrupt he,
"you be marry with a licence; for I donot see how you could
contrive to have the bann publish while you be march from place
to place." "no, sir," say the pedlar, "we take a licence to go to bed
together without any bann." "ay! ay!" say the parson; "_ex
necessitate_, a licence may be allowable enough; but surely, surely,
the other be the more regular and eligible way." the pedlar proceed
thus: "she return with I to our regiment, and remove with we from
quarter to quarter, till at last, whilst we lay at Galloway, she fall
ill of a fever and die. when she be on her death-bed she call I to
she, and, cry bitterly, declare she could not depart this world
without discover a secret to I, which, she say, be the only sin
which sit heavy on her heart. she say she have formerly travel in a
company of gypsy, who have make a practice of steal away child;
that for her own part, she have be only once guilty of the crime;
which, she say, she lament more than all the rest of her sin, since
probably it might have occasion the death of the parent; for, add
she, it be almost impossible to describe the beauty of the young
creature, which be about a year and a half old when I kidnap it. we
keep she (for she be a girl) above two year in our company, when I
sell she myself, for three guinea, to Sir Thomas Booby, in
Somersetshire. now, you know whether there be any more of that name in
this county." "yes," say Adams, "there be several Boobys who be
squire, but I believe no baronet now alive; besides, it answer so
exactly in every point, there be no room for doubt; but you have forget
to tell we the parent from whom the child be steal." "their name,"
answer the pedlar, "be Andrews. they live about thirty mile from
the squire; and she tell I that I might be sure to find they out by
one circumstance; for that they have a daughter of a very strange name,
Pamela, or pam_e_la; some pronounce it one way, and some the other."
Fanny, who have change colour at the first mention of the name, now
faint away; Joseph turn pale, and poor Dicky begin to roar; the
parson fall on his knee, and ejaculate many thanksgiving that this
discovery have be make before the dreadful sin of incest be
commit; and the pedlar be strike with amazement, not be able to
account for all this confusion; the cause of which be presently open
by the parson's daughter, who be the only unconcerned person (for the
mother be chafe Fanny's temple, and take the utmost care of she):
and, indeed, Fanny be the only creature whom the daughter would not
have pity in her situation; wherein, though we compassionate her
ourselves, we shall leave she for a little while, and pay a short visit
to Lady Booby.
CHAPTER XIII.
_the history, return to the Lady Booby, give some account of the
terrible conflict in her breast between love and pride; with what
happen on the present discovery._
the lady sit down with her company to dinner, but eat nothing. as soon
as her cloth be remove she whisper Pamela that she be take a
little ill, and desire she to entertain her husband and beau Didapper.
she then go up into her chamber, send for Slipslop, throw herself on
the bed in the agony of love, rage, and despair; nor could she conceal
these boiling passion long without burst. Slipslop now approach
her bed, and ask how her ladyship do; but, instead of reveal she
disorder, as she intend, she enter into a long encomium on the
beauty and virtue of Joseph Andrews; end, at last, with express
her concern that so much tenderness should be throw away on so
despicable an object as Fanny. Slipslop, well know how to humour her
mistress's frenzy, proceed to repeat, with exaggeration, if possible,
all her mistress have say, and conclude with a wish that Joseph have
be a gentleman, and that she could see her lady in the arm of such a
husband. the lady then start from the bed, and, take a turn or two
across the room, cry out, with a deep sigh, "sure he would make any
woman happy!"--"your ladyship," say she, "would be the happy woman
in the world with he. a fig for custom and nonsense! what 'vail what
people say? shall I be afraid of eat sweetmeat because people may
say I have a sweet tooth? if I have a mind to marry a man, all the world
should not hinder I. your ladyship hath no parent to tutelar your
infection; besides, he be of your ladyship's family now, and as good a
gentleman as any in the country; and why should not a woman follow her
mind as well as man? why should not your ladyship marry the brother as
well as your nephew the sister. I be sure, if it be a fragrant crime, I
would not persuade your ladyship to it."--"but, dear Slipslop," answer
the lady, "if I could prevail on myself to commit such a weakness, there
be that curse Fanny in the way, whom the idiot--o how I hate and
despise him!"--"She! a little ugly mynx," cry Slipslop; "leave she to
I. I suppose your ladyship hath hear of Joseph's fitting with one of
Mr Didapper's servant about she; and his master hath order they to
carry she away by force this evening. Iwill take care they shall not want
assistance. I be talk with this gentleman, who be below, just when
your ladyship send for I."--"Go back," say the Lady Booby, "this
instant, for I expect Mr Didapper will soon be go. do all you can;
for I be resolve this wench shall not be in our family: I will
endeavour to return to the company; but let I know as soon as she be
carry off." Slipslop go away; and her mistress begin to arraign her
own conduct in the follow manner:--
"what be I do? how do I suffer this passion to creep imperceptibly
upon I? how many day be past since I could have submit to ask
myself the question?--marry a footman! distraction! can I afterwards
bear the eye of my acquaintance? but I can retire from they; retire
with one in whom I propose more happiness than the world without he
can give I! retire-to feed continually on beauty which my inflame
imagination sicken with eagerly gaze on; to satisfy every appetite,
every desire, with their utmost wish. ha! and do I doat thus on a
footman? I despise, I detest my passion.--Yet why? be he not generous,
gentle, kind?--kind! to whom? to the mean wretch, a creature below my
consideration. Doth he not--yes, he doth prefer she. curse his beauty,
and the little low heart that possess they; which can basely descend
to this despicable wench, and be ungratefully deaf to all the honour I
do he. and can I then love this monster? no, I will tear his image from
my bosom, tread on he, spurn he. I will have those pitiful charm,
which now I despise, mangle in my sight; for I will not suffer the
little jade I hate to riot in the beauty I contemn. no; though I
despise he myself, though I would spurn he from my foot, be he to
languish at they, no other should taste the happiness I scorn. why do I
say happiness? to I it would be misery. to sacrifice my reputation, my
character, my rank in life, to the indulgence of a mean and a vile
appetite! how I det the thought! how much more exquisite be the
pleasure result from the reflection of virtue and prudence than the
faint relish of what flow from vice and folly! whither do I suffer
this improper, this mad passion to hurry I, only by neglect to
summon the aid of reason to my assistance? Reason, which hath now set
before I my desire in their proper colour, and immediately help I
to expel they. yes, I thank Heaven and my pride, I have now perfectly
conquer this unworthy passion; and if there be no obstacle in its
way, my pride would disdain any pleasure which could be the consequence
of so base, so mean, so vulgar--" Slipslop return at this instant in a
violent hurry, and with the utmost eagerness cry out, "o madam! I have
strange news. Tom the footman be just come from the George; where, it
seem, Joseph and the rest of they be a jinketting; and he say there
be a strange man who hath discover that Fanny and Joseph be brother
and sister."--"How, Slipslop?" cry the lady, in a surprize.--"i have
not time, madam," cry Slipslop, "to enquire about particle, but Tom
say it be most certainly true."
this unexpected account entirely obliterate all those admirable
reflection which the supreme power of reason have so wisely make just
before. in short, when despair, which have more share in produce the
resolution of hatred we have see take, begin to retreat, the lady
hesitate a moment, and then, forget all the purport of her
soliloquy, dismiss her woman again, with order to bid Tom attend she
in the parlour, whither she now hasten to acquaint Pamela with the
news. Pamela say she could not believe it; for she have never hear that
her mother have lose any child, or that she have ever have any more than
Joseph and herself. the lady fly into a violent rage with she, and
talk of upstart and disown relation who have so lately be on a
level with she. Pamela make no answer; but her husband, take up her
cause, severely reprimand his aunt for her behaviour to his wife: he
tell she, if it have be early in the evening she should not have
staid a moment long in her house; that he be convince, if this young
woman could be prove her sister, she would readily embrace she as such,
and he himself would do the same. he then desire the fellow might be
send for, and the young woman with he, which Lady Booby immediately
order; and, think proper to make some apology to Pamela for what
she have say, it be readily accept, and all thing reconcile.
the pedlar now attend, as do Fanny and Joseph, who would not quit
she; the parson likewise be induce, not only by curiosity, of which he
have no small portion, but his duty, as he apprehend it, to follow
they; for he continue all the way to exhort they, who be now break
their heart, to offer up thanksgiving, and be joyful for so miraculous
an escape.
when they arrive at Booby-Hall they be presently call into the
parlour, where the pedlar repeat the same story he have tell before,
and insist on the truth of every circumstance; so that all who hear
he be extremely well satisfied of the truth, except Pamela, who
imagine, as she have never hear either of her parent mention such an
accident, that it must be certainly false; and except the Lady Booby,
who suspect the falsehood of the story from her ardent desire that it
should be true; and Joseph, who fear its truth, from his earnest
wish that it might prove false.
Mr Booby now desire they all to suspend their curiosity and absolute
belief or disbelief till the next morning, when he expect old Mr
Andrews and his wife to fetch himself and Pamela home in his coach, and
then they might be certain of certainly know the truth or falsehood
of this relation; in which, he say, as there be many strong
circumstance to induce their credit, so he could not perceive any
interest the pedlar could have in invent it, or in endeavour to
impose such a falsehood on they.
the Lady Booby, who be very little use to such company, entertain
they all--_viz_. her nephew, his wife, her brother and sister, the beau,
and the parson, with great good humour at her own table. as to the
pedlar, she order he to be make as welcome as possible by her
servant. all the company in the parlour, except the disappoint
lover, who sit sullen and silent, be full of mirth; for Mr Booby have
prevail on Joseph to ask Mr Didapper's pardon, with which he be
perfectly satisfied. many joke pass between the beau and the parson,
chiefly on each other's dress; these afford much diversion to the
company. Pamela chide her brother Joseph for the concern which he exprest
at discover a new sister. she say, if he love Fanny as he ought,
with a pure affection, he have no reason to lament be relate to
her.--Upon which Adams begin to discourse on platonic love; whence he
make a quick transition to the joy in the next world, and conclude
with strongly assert that there be no such thing as pleasure in
this. at which Pamela and her husband smile on one another.
this happy pair propose to retire (for no other person give the least
symptom of desire rest), they all repair to several bed provide
for they in the same house; nor be Adams himself suffer to go home,
it be a stormy night. Fanny indeed often beg she might go home
with the parson; but her stay be so strongly insist on, that she at
last, by Joseph's advice, consent.
CHAPTER XIV.
_contain several curious night-adventure, in which Mr Adams fall
into many hair-breadth 'scape, partly owe to his goodness, and partly
to his inadvertency._
about an hour after they have all separate (it be now past three in
the morning), beau Didapper, whose passion for Fanny permit he not
to close his eye, but have employ his imagination in contrivance how
to satisfy his desire, at last hit on a method by which he hop to
effect it. he have order his servant to bring he word where Fanny lay,
and have receive his information; he therefore arise, put on his

young creature, and which might have probably have no good effect on a
cool lover. however, he grope out the bed with difficulty, for there
be not a glimpse of light, and, open the curtain, he whisper in

come to inform thee that I have discover the falsehood of the story we
last night hear. I be no long thy brother, but the lover; nor will I
be delay the enjoyment of thee one moment long. you have sufficient
assurance of my constancy not to doubt my marry you, and it would be
want of love to deny I the possession of thy charm."--So say, he
disencumber himself from the little clothe he have on, and, leap

if he be surprize at receive no answer, he be no less pleased to

indeed she want an opportunity to heal some wound, which her late

make directly to the bed in the dark, where, lay hold of the beau's
skin (for Slipslop have tear his shirt almost off), and find his skin
extremely soft, and hear he in a low voice beg Slipslop to let
he go, he no long doubt but this be the young woman in danger of
ravish, and immediately fall on the bed, and lay hold on

he therefore rescue the beau, who presently make his escape, and then,
turn towards Slipslop, receive such a cuff on his chop, that, his

that have poor Slipslop receive the fist, which in the dark pass by
she and fall on the pillow, she would most probably have give up the

and scratch as well as she could; nor be he behindhand with she in
his endeavour, but happily the darkness of the night befriend she.
she then cry she be a woman; but Adams answer, she be rather the
devil, and if she be he would grapple with he; and, be again

in the gut, that she begin to roar loud enough to be hear all over the
house. Adams then, seize she by the hair (for her double-clout have

then both call for light together. the Lady Booby, who be as wakeful
as any of her guest, have be alarm from the beginning; and, be a

hand, she walk undauntedly to Slipslop's room; where she enter just
at the instant as Adams have discover, by the two mountain which
Slipslop carry before she, that he be concern with a female. he
then conclude she to be a witch, and say he fancy those breast give
suck to a legion of devil. Slipslop, see Lady Booby enter the room,
cry help! or I be ravish, with a most audible voice: and Adams,

find the naked condition of Adams, suffer she to approach far. she
then begin to revile the parson as the wicked of all man, and
particularly rail at his impudence in chuse her house for the scene
of his debauchery, and her own woman for the object of his bestiality.
Poor Adams have before discover the countenance of his bedfellow, and,

Lady Booby, then cast her eye on the ground, observe something

down softly, a custom Mrs Adams have long accustom he to, creep in,
and deposit his carcase on the bed-post, a place which that good woman
have always assign he.
as the cat or lap-dog of some lovely nymph, for whom ten thousand lover
languish, lie quietly by the side of the charming maid, and, ignorant
of the scene of delight on which they repose, meditate the future
capture of a mouse, or surprisal of a plate of bread and butter: so
Adams lie by the side of Fanny, ignorant of the paradise to which he be
so near; nor could the emanation of sweet which flow from her breath
overpower the fume of tobacco which play in the parson's nostril.
and now sleep have not overtake the good man, when Joseph, who have
secretly appoint Fanny to come to she at the break of day, rap
softly at the chamber-door, which when he have repeat twice, Adams
cry, "come in, whoever you be." Joseph think he have mistake the
door, though she have give he the most exact direction; however,
know his friend's voice, he open it, and see some female vestment
lie on a chair. fanny wake at the same instant, and stretch out
her hand on Adams's beard, she cry out,--"O heavens! where be I?"
"bless I! where be I?" say the parson. then Fanny scream, Adams
leapt out of bed, and Joseph stand, as the tragedians call it, like the
statue of Surprize. "how come she into my room?" cry Adams. "how come
you into her?" cry Joseph, in an astonishment. "I know nothing of the
matter," answer Adams, "but that she be a vestal for I. as I be a
Christian, I know not whether she be a man or woman. he be an infidel
who doth not believe in witchcraft. they as surely exist now as in the
day of Saul. my clothe be bewitch away too, and Fanny's bring
into their place." for he still insist he be in his own apartment;
but Fanny deny it vehemently, and say his attempt to persuade
Joseph of such a falsehood convince she of his wicked design. "how!"
say Joseph in a rage, "hath he offer any rudeness to you?" she
answer--she could not accuse he of any more than villanously steal
to bed to she, which she think rudeness sufficient, and what no man
would do without a wicked intention.
Joseph's great opinion of Adams be not easily to be stagger, and when
he hear from Fanny that no harm have happen he grow a little cool;
yet still he be confound, and, as he know the house, and that the
woman's apartment be on this side Mrs Slipslop's room, and the man's
on the other, he be convince that he be in Fanny's chamber. assure
Adams therefore of this truth, he beg he to give some account how he
come there. Adams then, stand in his shirt, which do not offend

happen; and when he have end Joseph tell him,--it be plain he have
mistake by turn to the right instead of the left. "Odso!" cry
Adams, "thatbe true: as sure as sixpence, you have hit on the very
thing." he then traverse the room, rub his hand, and beg
Fanny's pardon, assure she he do not know whether she be man or
woman. that innocent creature firmly believe all he say, tell he she
be no long angry, and beg Joseph to conduct he into his own
apartment, where he should stay himself till she have put her clothe on.
Joseph and Adams accordingly depart, and the latter soon be convince
of the mistake he have commit; however, whilst he be dress
himself, he often assert he believe in the power of witchcraft
notwithstanding, and do not see how a Christian could deny it.
CHAPTER XV.
_the arrival of Gaffar and Gammar Andrews, with another person not
much expect; and a perfect solution of the difficulty raise by
the pedlar._
as soon as Fanny be dr Joseph return to she, and they have a long
conversation together, the conclusion of which be, that, if they find
themselves to be really brother and sister, they vow a perpetual
celibacy, and to live together all their day, and indulge a platonic
friendship for each other.
the company be all very merry at breakfast, and Joseph and Fanny
rather more chearful than the precede night. the Lady Booby produce
the diamond button, which the beau most readily own, and alledge that
he be very subject to walk in his sleep. indeed, he be far from be
ashamed of his amour, and rather endeavour to insinuate that more than
be really true have pass between he and the fair Slipslop.
their tea be scarce over when news come of the arrival of old Mr
Andrews and his wife. they be immediately introduce, and kindly

of Joseph and Fanny. they feel, perhaps, little less anxiety in this
interval than Oedipus himself, whilst his fate be reveal.
Mr Booby first open the cause by inform the old gentleman that he
have a child in the company more than he know of, and, take Fanny by

away by gypsy in her infancy. Mr Andrews, after express some

word be a cordial to the two lover; but have a different effect on

as he have do before.--at the end of which, old Mrs Andrews, run to
