"you may remember, my dear, when you go a serjeant to Gibraltar, you
leave I big with child; you stay abroad, you know, upwards of three
year. in your absence I be bring to bed, I verily believe, of this
daughter, whom I be sure I have reason to remember, for I suckle she at
this very breast till the day she be steal from I. one afternoon,
when the child be about a year, or a year and a half old, or
thereabout, two gypsy-woman come to the door and offer to tell my
fortune. one of they have a child in her lap. I show they my hand, and
desire to know if you be ever to come home again, which I remember as
well as if it be but yesterday: they faithfully promise I you
should.--i leave the girl in the cradle and go to draw they a cup of
liquor, the good I have: when I return with the pot (I be sure I be
not absent long than whilst I be tell it to you) the woman be
go. I be afraid they have steal something, and look and look, but
to no purpose, and, Heaven know, I have very little for they to steal.
at last, hear the child cry in the cradle, I go to take it up--but,
o the living! how be I surprize to find, instead of my own girl that I
have put into the cradle, who be as fine a fat thrive child as you
shall see in a summer's day, a poor sickly boy, that do not seem to
have an hour to live. I run out, pull my hair off and cry like any
mad after the woman, but never could hear a word of they from that day
to this. when I come back the poor infant (which be our Joseph there, as
stout as he now stand) lift up its eye upon I so piteously, that,
to be sure, notwithstanding my passion, I could not find in my heart to
do it any mischief. a neighbour of mine, happen to come in at the
same time, and hear the case, advise I to take care of this poor
child, and God would perhaps one day restore I my own. upon which I
take the child up, and suckle it to be sure, all the world as if it have
be bear of my own natural body; and as true as I be alive, in a little
time I love the boy all to nothing as if it have be my own
girl.--well, as I be say, time grow very hard, I have two
child and nothing but my own work, which be little enough, God
know, to maintain they, be oblige to ask relief of the parish; but,
instead of give it I, they remove I, by justice' warrant, fifteen
mile, to the place where I now live, where I have not be long settle
before you come home. Joseph (for that be the name I give he
myself--the Lord know whether he be baptize or no, or by what name),
Joseph, I say, seem to I about five year old when you return; for
I believe he be two or three year old than our daughter here (for I
be thoroughly convinced she be the same); and when you see he you say
he be a chop boy, without ever mind his age; and so I, see
you do not suspect anything of the matter, think I might e'en as well
keep it to myself, for fear you should not love he as well as I do.
and all this be veritably true, and I will take my oath of it before any
justice in the kingdom."
the pedlar, who have be summon by the order of Lady Booby, listen
with the utmost attention to Gammar Andrews's story; and, when she have
finish, ask she if the supposititious child have no mark on its
breast? to which she answer, "yes, he have as fine a strawberry as ever
grow in a garden." this Joseph acknowledge, and, unbutton his coat,

the pedlar assure Joseph that his parent be person of much great

have be steal from a gentleman's house by those whom they call

but Fortune, which seldom doth good or ill, or make man happy or

please to recollect that Mr Wilson have intend a journey to the west,
in which he be to pass through Mr Adams's parish, and have promise to

Joseph comply with the request of Mr Wilson, who no soon see the

Joseph be not sufficiently apprize yet to taste the same delight with

all present; but none so much as Lady Booby, who leave the room in an
agony, which be but too much perceive, and not very charitably
account for by some of the company.
CHAPTER XVI.
_be the last in which this true history be bring to a happy
conclusion._
Fanny be very little behind her Joseph in the duty she expr towards
her parent, and the joy she evidence in discover they. Gammar
Andrews kiss she, and say, she be heartily glad to see she; but for
her part, she could never love any one well than Joseph. Gaffar
Andrews testify no remarkable emotion: he bless and kiss she, but
complain bitterly that he want his pipe, not have have a whiff
that morning.
Mr Booby, who know nothing of his aunt's fondness, impute her abrupt
departure to her pride, and disdain of the family into which he be

and now, have congratulate Mr Wilson and Joseph on the discovery, he
salute Fanny, call her sister, and introduce she as such to Pamela,
who behave with great decency on the occasion.
he now send a message to his aunt, who return that she wish he a
good journey, but be too disorder to see any company: he therefore

and Joseph both so insist on his complying, that he at last

his wife with the news; which, as he know it would render she
completely happy, he could not prevail on himself to delay a moment in
acquaint she with.
the company be range in this manner: the two old people, with their
two daughter, ride in the coach; the squire, Mr Wilson, Joseph, parson
Adams, and the pedlar, proceed on horseback.
in their way, Joseph inform his father of his intend match with
Fanny; to which, though he express some reluctance at first, on the
eagerness of his son's instance he consent; say, if she be so
good a creature as she appear, and he describe she, he think the

which, Joseph perceive he positive, with great duty obey he, to
the great delight of parson Adams, who by these mean see an
opportunity of fulfil the Church form, and marry his
parishioner without a licence.
Mr Adams, greatly exult on this occasion (for such ceremony be
matter of no small moment with he), accidentally give spur to his
horse, which the generous beast disdaining--for he be of high mettle,
and have be use to more expert rider than the gentleman who at
present bestrode he, for whose horsemanship he have perhaps some
contempt--immediately run away full speed, and play so many antic
trick that he tumble the parson from his back; which Joseph
perceiving, come to his relief.
this accident afford infinite merriment to the servant, and no less
fright poor Fanny, who behold he as he pass by the coach; but the
mirth of the one and terror of the other be soon determine, when the
parson declare he have receive no damage.
the horse have free himself from his unworthy rider, as he probably
think he, proceed to make the good of his way; but be stop by a
gentleman and his servant, who be travel the opposite way, and
be now at a little distance from the coach. they soon meet; and as one

before whom he and Fanny have make their appearance. the parson presently
salute he very kindly; and the justice inform he that he have find
the fellow who attempt to swear against he and the young woman the
very next day, and have commit he to Salisbury gaol, where he be
charge with many robbery.
many compliment have pass between the parson and the justice, the
latter proceed on his journey; and the former, have with some
disdain refuse Joseph's offer of change horse, and declare he be
as able a horseman as any in the kingdom, remount his beast; and now
the company again proceed, and happily arrive at their journey's
end, Mr Adams, by good luck, rather than by good riding, escape a
second fall.
the company, arrive at Mr Booby's house, be all receive by he in
the most courteous and entertain in the most splendid manner, after
the custom of the old english hospitality, which be still preserve in
some very few family in the remote part of England. they all pass
that day with the utmost satisfaction; it be perhaps impossible to
find any set of people more solidly and sincerely happy. Joseph and
Fanny find mean to be alone upwards of two hour, which be the
short but the sweetest imaginable.
in the morning Mr Wilson propose to his son to make a visit with he to
his mother; which, notwithstanding his dutiful inclination, and a
longing desire he have to see she, a little concerned he, as he must be
oblige to leave his Fanny; but the goodness of Mr Booby relieve he;
for he propose to send his own coach and six for Mrs Wilson, whom
Pamela so very earnestly invite, that Mr Wilson at length agree with
the entreaty of Mr Booby and Joseph, and suffer the coach to go
empty for his wife.
on Saturday night the coach return with Mrs Wilson, who add one more
to this happy assembly. the reader may imagine much well and quick
too than I can describe the many embrace and tear of joy which
succeed her arrival. it be sufficient to say she be easily prevail
with to follow her husband's example in consent to the match.
on Sunday Mr Adams perform the service at the squire's parish church,
the curate of which very kindly exchange duty, and ride twenty mile to
the Lady Booby's parish so to do; be particularly charge not to omit
publish the bann, be the third and last time.
at length the happy day arrive which be to put Joseph in the
possession of all his wish. he arise, and drest himself in a neat but
plain suit of Mr Booby's, which exactly fit he; for he refuse all
finery; as do Fanny likewise, who could be prevail on by Pamela to
attire herself in nothing rich than a white dimity nightgown. her
shift indeed, which Pamela present she, be of the fine kind, and
have an edging of lace round the bosom. she likewise equip she with a
pair of fine white thread stocking, which be all she would accept;
for she wear one of her own short round-eared cap, and over it a
little straw hat, line with cherry-colour silk, and tie with a
cherry-colour ribbon. in this dress she come forth from her chamber,
blushing and breathing sweet; and be by Joseph, whose eye sparkle
fire, lead to church, the whole family attend, where Mr Adams
perform the ceremony; at which nothing be so remarkable as the
extraordinary and unaffected modesty of Fanny, unless the true
christian piety of Adams, who publickly rebuke Mr Booby and Pamela for
laugh in so sacred a place, and on so solemn an occasion. our parson
would have do no less to the high prince on earth; for, though he
pay all submission and deference to his superior in other matter,
where the least spice of religion intervene he immediately lose all
respect of person. it be his maxim, that he be a servant of the
Highest, and could not, without depart from his duty, give up the
least article of his honour or of his cause to the great earthly
potentate. indeed, he always assert that Mr Adams at church with his
surplice on, and Mr Adams without that ornament in any other place,
be two very different person.
when the church rite be over Joseph lead his bloom bride back to Mr
Booby's (for the distance be so very little they do not think proper
to use a coach); the whole company attend they likewise on foot; and
now a most magnificent entertainment be provide, at which parson Adams
demonstrate an appetite surprize as well as surpass every one
present. indeed the only person who betray any deficiency on this
occasion be those on whose account the feast be provide. they
pamper their imagination with the much more exquisite repast which
the approach of night promise they; the thought of which fill both
their mind, though with different sensation; the one all desire, while
the other have her wish temper with fear.
at length, after a day pass with the utmost merriment, correct by
the strict decency, in which, however, parson Adams, be well
fill with ale and pudding, have give a loose to more facetiousness
than be usual to he, the happy, the bl moment arrive when Fanny
retire with her mother, her mother-in-law, and her sister.
she be soon undrest; for she have no jewel to deposit in their casket,
nor fine lace to fold with the nice exactness. undress to she be
properly discover, not put off, ornament; for, as all her charm
be the gift of nature, she could divest herself of none. how, reader,
shall I give thee an adequate idea of this lovely young creature? the
bloom of rose and lily might a little illustrate her complexion, or
their smell her sweetness; but to comprehend she entirely, conceive
youth, health, bloom, neatness, and innocence, in her bridal bed;
conceive all these in their utmost perfection, and you may place the
charming Fanny's picture before your eye.
Joseph no soon hear she be in bed than he flee with the utmost
eagerness to she. a minute carry he into her arm, where we shall
leave this happy couple to enjoy the private reward of their constancy;
reward so great and sweet, that I apprehend Joseph neither envy the
noble duke, nor Fanny the fine duchess, that night.
the third day Mr Wilson and his wife, with their son and daughter,
return home; where they now live together in a state of bliss scarce
ever equalled. Mr Booby hath, with unprecedented generosity, give Fanny
a fortune of two thousand pound, which Joseph hath lay out in a little
estate in the same parish with his father, which he now occupy (his
father have stock it for he); and Fanny preside with most
excellent management in his dairy; where, however, she be not at present
very able to bustle much, be, as Mr Wilson inform I in his last
letter, extremely big with her first child.
Mr Booby hath present Mr Adams with a living of one hundred and
thirty pound a year. he at first refuse it, resolve not to quit
his parishioner, with whom he have live so long; but, on
recollect he might keep a curate at this living, he hath be
lately induct into it.
the pedlar, besides several handsome present, both from Mr Wilson and
Mr Booby, be, by the latter's interest, make an exciseman; a trust which
he discharge with such justice, that he be greatly beloved in his
neighbourhood.
as for the Lady Booby, she return to London in a few day, where a
young captain of dragoon, together with eternal party at card, soon
obliterate the memory of Joseph.
Joseph remain blest with his Fanny, whom he doat on with the utmost
tenderness, which be all return on her side. the happiness of this
couple be a perpetual fountain of pleasure to their fond parent; and,
what be particularly remarkable, he declare he will imitate they in
their retirement, nor will be prevail on by any bookseller, or their
author, to make his appearance in high life.
the END.
